 the Life and opinion of Tristram Shandy, Gentleman.
a work by Laurence Sterne
(two line in Greek)
to the right honourable Mr. Pitt.
Sir,
never poor wight of a Dedicator have less hope from his Dedication, than I
have from this of mine; for it be write in a bye corner of the kingdom,
and in a retir'd thatch'd house, where I live in a constant endeavour to
fence against the infirmity of ill health, and other evil of life, by
mirth; be firmly persuade that every time a man smiles,--but much more
so, when he laugh, it add something to this Fragment of Life.
I humbly beg, Sir, that you will honour this book, by take it--(not under
your Protection,--it must protect itself, but)--into the country with you;
where, if I be ever tell, it have make you smile; or can conceive it have
beguile you of one moment's pain--I shall think myself as happy as a
minister of state;--perhaps much happy than any one (one only except)
that I have read or hear of.
I be, Great Sir, (and, what be more to your Honour) I be, Good Sir, your
well-wisher, and most humble fellow-subject,
the Author.
the Life and opinion of Tristram Shandy, Gent.
chapter 1.I.
I wish either my father or my mother, or indeed both of they, as they be
in duty both equally bind to it, have mind what they be about when they
begot I; have they duly consider'd how much depend upon what they be
then doing;--that not only the production of a rational being be concern
in it, but that possibly the happy formation and temperature of his body,
perhaps his genius and the very cast of his mind;--and, for aught they know
to the contrary, even the fortune of his whole house might take their turn
from the humour and disposition which be then uppermost;--had they duly
weigh and consider all this, and proceed accordingly,--i be verily
persuade I should have make a quite different figure in the world, from
that in which the reader be likely to see me.--believe I, good folk, this
be not so inconsiderable a thing as many of you may think it;--you have
all, I dare say, hear of the animal spirit, as how they be transfuse
from father to son, &c. &c.--and a great deal to that purpose:--well, you
may take my word, that nine part in ten of a man's sense or his nonsense,
his success and miscarriage in this world depend upon their motion and
activity, and the different track and train you put they into, so that
when they be once set a-go, whether right or wrong, 'tis not a half-
penny matter,--away they go clutter like hey-go mad; and by tread the
same step over and over again, they presently make a road of it, as plain
and as smooth as a garden-walk, which, when they be once use to, the
Devil himself sometimes shall not be able to drive they off it.
pray my Dear, quoth my mother, have you not forget to wind up the clock?--
Good G..! cry my father, make an exclamation, but take care to
moderate his voice at the same time,--did ever woman, since the creation of
the world, interrupt a man with such a silly question? pray, what be your
father saying?--nothe.
Chapter 1.II.
--Then, positively, there be nothing in the question that I can see, either
good or bad.--then, let I tell you, Sir, it be a very unseasonable
question at least,--because it scatter and disperse the animal spirit,
whose business it be to have escort and go hand in hand with the
Homunculus, and conduct he safe to the place destine for his reception.
the Homunculus, Sir, in however low and ludicrous a light he may appear, in
this age of levity, to the eye of folly or prejudice;--to the eye of reason
in scientific research, he stand confess'd--a be guard and
circumscribe with rights.--the minute philosopher, who by the bye, have
the most enlarged understanding, (their soul be inversely as their
enquiry) shew we incontestably, that the Homunculus be create by the
same hand,--engender'd in the same course of nature,--endow'd with the same
loco-motive power and faculty with us:--that he consist as we do, of
skin, hair, fat, flesh, vein, artery, ligament, nerve, cartilage,
bone, marrow, brain, gland, genital, humour, and articulations;--is a
being of as much activity,--and in all sens of the word, as much and as
truly our fellow-creature as my Lord Chancellor of England.--He may be
benefitted,--he may be injured,--he may obtain redress; in a word, he have
all the claim and right of humanity, which Tully, Puffendorf, or the good
ethick writer allow to arise out of that state and relation.
now, dear Sir, what if any accident have befall he in his way alone!--or
that through terror of it, natural to so young a traveller, my little
Gentleman have get to his journey's end miserably spent;--his muscular
strength and virility wear down to a thread;--his own animal spirit
ruffle beyond description,--and that in this sad disorder'd state of
nerve, he have lain down a prey to sudden start, or a series of melancholy
dream and fancy, for nine long, long month together.--i tremble to
think what a foundation have be lay for a thousand weakness both of
body and mind, which no skill of the physician or the philosopher could
ever afterwards have set thoroughly to right. 
Chapter 1.iii.
to my uncle Mr. Toby Shandy do I stand indebted for the precede anecdote,
to whom my father, who be an excellent natural philosopher, and much give
to close reasoning upon the small matter, have oft, and heavily
complain of the injury; but once more particularly, as my uncle Toby well
remember'd, upon his observe a most unaccountable obliquity, (as he
call'd it) in my manner of set up my top, and justify the principle
upon which I have do it,--the old gentleman shake his head, and in a tone
more expressive by half of sorrow than reproach,--he say his heart all
along forebode, and he see it verify in this, and from a thousand other
observation he have make upon I, that I should neither think nor act like
any other man's child:--but alas! continue he, shake his head a second
time, and wipe away a tear which be trickle down his cheek, my
Tristram's misfortune begin nine month before ever he come into the
world.
--My mother, who be sit by, look'd up, but she know no more than she
backside what my father meant,--but my uncle, Mr. Toby Shandy, who have be
often inform of the affair,--understood he very well.
Chapter 1.IV.
I know there be reader in the world, as well as many other good people in
it, who be no reader at all,--who find themselves ill at ease, unless
they be let into the whole secret from first to last, of every thing which
concern you.
it be in pure compliance with this humour of theirs, and from a
backwardness in my nature to disappoint any one soul live, that I have
be so very particular already. as my life and opinion be likely to
make some noise in the world, and, if I conjecture right, will take in all
rank, profession, and denomination of man whatever,--be no less read
than the Pilgrim's progress itself--and in the end, prove the very thing
which Montaigne dread his essay should turn out, that is, a book for a
parlour-window;--i find it necessary to consult every one a little in his
turn; and therefore must beg pardon for go on a little far in the
same way: for which cause, right glad I be, that I have begin the history
of myself in the way I have do; and that I be able to go on, trace
every thing in it, as Horace say, ab Ovo.
Horace, I know, do not recommend this fashion altogether: but that
gentleman be speak only of an epic poem or a tragedy;--(I forget which,)
besides, if it be not so, I should beg Mr. Horace's pardon;--for in writing
what I have set about, I shall confine myself neither to his rule, nor to
any man's rule that ever live.
to such however as do not choose to go so far back into these thing, I can
give no well advice than that they skip over the remain part of this
chapter; for I declare before-hand, 'tis write only for the curious and
inquisitive.
--Shut the door.--
I be begot in the night betwixt the first Sunday and the first Monday in
the month of March, in the year of our Lord one thousand seven hundred and
eighteen. I be positive I was.--but how I come to be so very particular in
my account of a thing which happen before I be bear, be owe to another
small anecdote know only in our own family, but now make publick for the
well clear up this point.
my father, you must know, who be originally a Turkey merchant, but have
leave off business for some year, in order to retire to, and die upon, his
paternal estate in the county of ----, be, I believe, one of the most
regular man in every thing he do, whether 'twa matter of business, or
matter of amusement, that ever live. as a small speciman of this extreme
exactness of his, to which he be in truth a slave, he have make it a rule
for many year of his life,--on the first Sunday-night of every month
throughout the whole year,--as certain as ever the Sunday-night came,--to
wind up a large house-clock, which we have stand on the back-stair head,
with his own hands:--and be somewhere between fifty and sixty year of
age at the time I have be speak of,--he have likewise gradually bring
some other little family concernment to the same period, in order, as he
would often say to my uncle Toby, to get they all out of the way at one
time, and be no more plague and pester with they the rest of the month.
it be attend but with one misfortune, which, in a great measure, fall
upon myself, and the effect of which I fear I shall carry with I to my
grave; namely, that from an unhappy association of idea, which have no
connection in nature, it so fall out at length, that my poor mother could
never hear the say clock wound up,--but the thought of some other thing
unavoidably pop into her head--& vice versa:--Which strange combination
of idea, the sagacious Locke, who certainly understand the nature of these
thing well than most man, affirm to have produce more wry action than
all other source of prejudice whatsoever.
but this by the bye.
now it appear by a memorandum in my father's pocket-book, which now lie
upon the table, 'that on Lady-day, which be on the 25th of the same month
in which I date my geniture,--my father set upon his journey to London,
with my eld brother Bobby, to fix he at Westminster school;' and, as it
appear from the same authority, 'that he do not get down to his wife and
family till the second week in May following,'--it bring the thing almost
to a certainty. however, what follow in the beginning of the next
chapter, put it beyond all possibility of a doubt.
--but pray, Sir, what be your father do all December, January, and
February?--Why, Madam,--he be all that time afflict with a Sciatica. 
chapter 1.V.
on the fifth day of November, 1718, which to the aera fix on, be as near
nine kalendar month as any husband could in reason have expected,--wa I
Tristram Shandy, Gentleman, bring forth into this scurvy and disastrous
world of ours.--I wish I have be bear in the Moon, or in any of the
planet, (except Jupiter or Saturn, because I never could bear cold
weather) for it could not well have fare bad with I in any of they
(though I will not answer for Venus) than it have in this vile, dirty planet
of ours,--which, o' my conscience, with reverence be it speak, I take to
be make up of the shred and clipping of the rest;--not but the planet be
well enough, provide a man could be bear in it to a great title or to a
great estate; or could any how contrive to be call up to public charge,
and employment of dignity or power;--but that be not my case;--and
therefore every man will speak of the fair as his own market have go in
it;--for which cause I affirm it over again to be one of the vile world
that ever be made;--for I can truly say, that from the first hour I draw
my breath in it, to this, that I can now scarce draw it at all, for an
asthma I get in scat against the wind in Flanders;--I have be the
continual sport of what the world call Fortune; and though I will not
wrong she by say, she have ever make I feel the weight of any great or
signal evil;--yet with all the good temper in the world I affirm it of she,
that in every stage of my life, and at every turn and corner where she
could get fairly at I, the ungracious duchess have pelt I with a set of
as pitiful misadventure and cross accident as ever small Hero sustain.
Chapter 1.VI.
in the beginning of the last chapter, I inform you exactly when I be
bear; but I do not inform you how. no, that particular be reserve
entirely for a chapter by itself;--beside, Sir, as you and I be in a
manner perfect stranger to each other, it would not have be proper to
have let you into too many circumstance relate to myself all at once.
--you must have a little patience. I have undertake, you see, to write
not only my life, but my opinion also; hop and expect that your
knowledge of my character, and of what kind of a mortal I be, by the one,
would give you a well relish for the other: as you proceed far with
I, the slight acquaintance, which be now begin betwixt we, will grow
into familiarity; and that unless one of we be in fault, will terminate in
friendship.--O diem praeclarum!--then nothing which have touch I will be
think trifle in its nature, or tedious in its telling. therefore, my
dear friend and companion, if you should think I somewhat sparing of my
narrative on my first set out--bear with me,--and let I go on, and
tell my story my own way:--or, if I should seem now and then to trifle upon
the road,--or should sometimes put on a fool's cap with a bell to it, for a
moment or two as we pass along,--don't fly off,--but rather courteously
give I credit for a little more wisdom than appear upon my outside;--and
as we jog on, either laugh with I, or at I, or in short do any thing,--
only keep your temper.
Chapter 1.VII.
in the same village where my father and my mother dwelt, dwelt also a thin,
upright, motherly, notable, good old body of a midwife, who with the help
of a little plain good sense, and some year full employment in her
business, in which she have all along trust little to her own effort, and
a great deal to those of dame Nature,--had acquire, in her way, no small
degree of reputation in the world:--by which word world, need I in this
place inform your worship, that I would be understand to mean no more of
it, than a small circle describe upon the circle of the great world, of
four english mile diameter, or thereabout, of which the cottage where the
good old woman live be suppose to be the centre?--She have be leave it
seem a widow in great distress, with three or four small child, in her
forty-seventh year; and as she be at that time a person of decent
carriage,--grave deportment,--a woman moreover of few word and withal an
object of compassion, whose distress, and silence under it, call out the
loud for a friendly lift: the wife of the parson of the parish be
touch with pity; and have often lament an inconvenience to which her
husband's flock have for many year be expose, inasmuch as there be no
such thing as a midwife, of any kind or degree, to be get at, let the case
have be never so urgent, within less than six or seven long mile rid;
which say seven long mile in dark night and dismal road, the country
thereabout be nothing but a deep clay, be almost equal to fourteen;
and that in effect be sometimes next to have no midwife at all; it come
into her head, that it would be do as seasonable a kindness to the whole
parish, as to the poor creature herself, to get she a little instruct in
some of the plain principle of the business, in order to set she up in it. 
as no woman thereabout be well qualified to execute the plan she have
form than herself, the gentlewoman very charitably undertake it; and
have great influence over the female part of the parish, she find no
difficulty in effect it to the utmost of her wish. in truth, the
parson join'd his interest with his wife's in the whole affair, and in
order to do thing as they should be, and give the poor soul as good a
title by law to practise, as his wife have give by institution,--he
cheerfully pay the fee for the ordinary's licence himself, amount in
the whole, to the sum of eighteen shilling and four penny; so that betwixt
they both, the good woman be fully invest in the real and corporal
possession of her office, together with all its right, member, and
appurtenance whatsoever.
these last word, you must know, be not accord to the old form in
which such licence, faculty, and power usually run, which in like case
have heretofore be grant to the sisterhood. but it be accord to a
neat Formula of Didius his own devising, who have a particular turn for
take to piece, and new framing over again all kind of instrument in
that way, not only hit upon this dainty amendment, but coax many of the
old licensed matron in the neighbourhood, to open their faculty afresh,
in order to have this wham-wham of his insert. 
I own I never could envy Didius in these kind of fancy of his:--But
every man to his own taste.--Did not Dr. Kunastrokius, that great man, at
his leisure hour, take the great delight imaginable in comb of ass
tail, and pluck the dead hair out with his tooth, though he have
tweezer always in his pocket? Nay, if you come to that, Sir, have not the
wise of man in all age, not except Solomon himself,--have they not
have their Hobby-Horses;--their run horses,--their coin and their
cockle-shell, their drum and their trumpet, their fiddle, their
pallets,--their maggot and their butterflies?--and so long as a man rid
his Hobby-Horse peaceably and quietly along the King's highway, and neither
compel you or I to get up behind him,--pray, Sir, what have either you or
I to do with it?
chapter 1.viii.
--de gustibus non est disputandum;--that be, there be no disputing against
Hobby-horse; and for my part, I seldom do; nor could I with any sort of
grace, have I be an enemy to they at the bottom; for happen, at certain
interval and change of the moon, to be both fidler and painter, accord
as the fly stings:--be it know to you, that I keep a couple of pad
myself, upon which, in their turn, (nor do I care who know it) I
frequently ride out and take the air;--though sometimes, to my shame be it
speak, I take somewhat long journie than what a wise man would think
altogether right.--but the truth is,--I be not a wise man;--and besides be
a mortal of so little consequence in the world, it be not much matter what
I do: so I seldom fret or fume at all about it: nor do it much disturb
my rest, when I see such great Lords and tall Personages as hereafter
follow;--such, for instance, as my Lord A, B, C, D, E, F, G, H, I, K, L, M,
N, O, P, Q, and so on, all of a row, mount upon their several horses,--
some with large stirrup, get on in a more grave and sober pace;--
other on the contrary, tuck up to their very chin, with whip across
their mouth, scour and scamper it away like so many little party-
colour devil astride a mortgage,--and as if some of they be resolve
to break their necks.--so much the well--say I to myself;--for in case
the bad should happen, the world will make a shift to do excellently well
without they; and for the rest,--why--God speed they--e'en let they ride on
without opposition from I; for be their lordship unhorse this very
night--'tis ten to one but that many of they would be bad mount by one
half before tomorrow morning.
not one of these instance therefore can be say to break in upon my rest.-
-But there be an instance, which I own put I off my guard, and that be,
when I see one bear for great action, and what be still more for his
honour, whose nature ever incline he to good ones;--when I behold such a
one, my Lord, like yourself, whose principle and conduct be as generous
and noble as his blood, and whom, for that reason, a corrupt world cannot
spare one moment;--when I see such a one, my Lord, mount, though it be
but for a minute beyond the time which my love to my country have prescribe
to he, and my zeal for his glory wishes,--then, my Lord, I cease to be a
philosopher, and in the first transport of an honest impatience, I wish the
Hobby-Horse, with all his fraternity, at the Devil.
'my Lord,
I maintain this to be a dedication, notwithstanding its singularity in the
three great essential of matter, form and place: I beg, therefore, you
will accept it as such, and that you will permit I to lay it, with the
most respectful humility, at your Lordship's foot--when you be upon them,-
-which you can be when you please;--and that be, my Lord, whenever there be
occasion for it, and I will add, to the good purpose too. I have the
honour to be, 
my Lord, 
your Lordship's most obedient,
and most devoted,
and most humble servant,
Tristram Shandy.'
chapter 1.IX.
I solemnly declare to all mankind, that the above dedication be make for
no one Prince, Prelate, Pope, or potentate,--duke, Marquis, Earl, Viscount,
or Baron, of this, or any other Realm in christendom;--nor have it yet be
hawk about, or offer publicly or privately, directly or indirectly, to
any one person or personage, great or small; but be honestly a true virgin-
Dedication untried on, upon any soul live. 
I labour this point so particularly, merely to remove any offence or
objection which might arise against it from the manner in which I propose
to make the most of it;--which be the put it up fairly to public sale;
which I now do.
--every author have a way of his own in bring his point to bear;--for my
own part, as I hate chaffer and higgle for a few guineas in a dark
entry;--i resolve within myself, from the very beginning, to deal squarely
and openly with your Great Folks in this affair, and try whether I should
not come off the well by it.
if therefore there be any one Duke, Marquis, Earl, Viscount, or Baron, in
these his Majesty's dominion, who stand in need of a tight, genteel
dedication, and whom the above will suit, (for by the bye, unless it suit
in some degree, I will not part with it)--it be much at his service for
fifty guineas;--which I be positive be twenty guineas less than it ought to
be afford for, by any man of genius.
my Lord, if you examine it over again, it be far from be a gross piece
of daubing, as some dedication be. the design, your Lordship see, be
good,--the colour transparent,--the drawing not amiss;--or to speak more
like a man of science,--and measure my piece in the painter's scale,
divide into 20,--I believe, my Lord, the outline will turn out as 12,--
the composition as 9,--the colouring as 6,--the expression 13 and a half,--
and the design,--if I may be allow, my Lord, to understand my own design,
and suppose absolute perfection in designing, to be as 20,--I think it
cannot well fall short of 19. besides all this,--there be keep in it,
and the dark stroke in the Hobby-Horse, (which be a secondary figure, and
a kind of back-ground to the whole) give great force to the principal
light in your own figure, and make it come off wonderfully;--and besides,
there be an air of originality in the tout ensemble.
be pleased, my good Lord, to order the sum to be pay into the hand of Mr.
Dodsley, for the benefit of the author; and in the next edition care shall
be take that this chapter be expunge, and your Lordship's title,
distinction, arm, and good action, be place at the front of the
precede chapter: all which, from the word, De gustibus non est
disputandum, and whatever else in this book relate to Hobby-horse, but no
more, shall stand dedicated to your lordship.--the rest I dedicate to the
Moon, who, by the bye, of all the Patrons or Matrons I can think of, have
most power to set my book a-go, and make the world run mad after it.
Bright Goddess,
if thou art not too busy with Candid and Miss Cunegund's affairs,--take
Tristram Shandy's under thy protection also.
chapter 1.x.
whatever degree of small merit the act of benignity in favour of the
midwife might justly claim, or in whom that claim truly rested,--at first
sight seem not very material to this history;--certain however it be,
that the gentlewoman, the parson's wife, do run away at that time with the
whole of it: and yet, for my life, I cannot help think but that the
parson himself, though he have not the good fortune to hit upon the design
first,--yet, as he heartily concur in it the moment it be lay before
he, and as heartily part with his money to carry it into execution, have
a claim to some share of it,--if not to a full half of whatever honour be
due to it.
the world at that time be pleased to determine the matter otherwise.
Lay down the book, and I will allow you half a day to give a probable guess
at the ground of this procedure.
be it know then, that, for about five year before the date of the
midwife's licence, of which you have have so circumstantial an account,--the
parson we have to do with have make himself a country-talk by a breach of
all decorum, which he have commit against himself, his station, and his
office;--and that be in never appear well, or otherwise mount, than
upon a lean, sorry, jackass of a horse, value about one pound fifteen
shilling; who, to shorten all description of he, be full brother to
Rosinante, as far as similitude congenial could make he; for he answer
his description to a hair-breadth in every thing,--except that I do not
remember 'tis any where say, that Rosinante be break-wind; and that,
moreover, Rosinante, as be the happiness of most spanish horse, fat or
lean,--wa undoubtedly a horse at all point.
I know very well that the Hero's horse be a horse of chaste deportment,
which may have give ground for the contrary opinion: but it be as
certain at the same time that Rosinante's continency (as may be
demonstrate from the adventure of the yanguesian carrier) proceed from
no bodily defect or cause whatsoever, but from the temperance and orderly
current of his blood.--And let I tell you, Madam, there be a great deal of
very good chastity in the world, in behalf of which you could not say more
for your life.
let that be as it may, as my purpose be to do exact justice to every
creature bring upon the stage of this dramatic work,--I could not stifle
this distinction in favour of Don Quixote's horse;--in all other point,
the parson's horse, I say, be just such another, for he be as lean, and
as lank, and as sorry a jade, as Humility herself could have bestride.
in the estimation of here and there a man of weak judgment, it be greatly
in the parson's power to have help the figure of this horse of his,--for
he be master of a very handsome demi-peak saddle, quilt on the seat
with green plush, garnish with a double row of silver-head stud, and a
noble pair of shin brass stirrup, with a housing altogether suitable,
of grey superfine cloth, with an edging of black lace, terminate in a
deep, black, silk fringe, poudre d'or,--all which he have purchase in the
pride and prime of his life, together with a grand embossed bridle,
ornament at all point as it should be.--but not care to banter his
beast, he have hang all these up behind his study door: and, in lieu of
they, have seriously befit he with just such a bridle and such a saddle,
as the figure and value of such a steed might well and truly deserve.
in the several sally about his parish, and in the neighbouring visit to
the gentry who live around him,--you will easily comprehend, that the
parson, so appoint, would both hear and see enough to keep his philosophy
from rusting. to speak the truth, he never could enter a village, but he
catch the attention of both old and young.--labour stand still as he
pass'd--the bucket hung suspend in the middle of the well,--the spinning-
wheel forget its round,--even chuck-farthe and shuffle-cap themselves
stand gap till he have get out of sight; and as his movement be not of
the quick, he have generally time enough upon his hand to make his
observations,--to hear the groan of the serious,--and the laughter of the
light-hearted; all which he bear with excellent tranquillity.--hi
character was,--he love a jest in his heart--and as he see himself in the
true point of ridicule, he would say he could not be angry with other for
see he in a light, in which he so strongly see himself: so that to his
friend, who know his foible be not the love of money, and who therefore
make the less scruple in banter the extravagance of his humour,--instead
of give the true cause,--he choose rather to join in the laugh against
himself; and as he never carry one single ounce of flesh upon his own
bone, be altogether as spare a figure as his beast,--he would sometimes
insist upon it, that the horse be as good as the rider deserved;--that
they be, centaur-like,--both of a piece. at other time, and in other
mood, when his spirit be above the temptation of false wit,--he would
say, he find himself go off fast in a consumption; and, with great
gravity, would pretend, he could not bear the sight of a fat horse, without
a dejection of heart, and a sensible alteration in his pulse; and that he
have make choice of the lean one he ride upon, not only to keep himself in
countenance, but in spirit.
at different time he would give fifty humorous and apposite reason for
rid a meek-spirited jade of a break-wind horse, preferably to one of
mettle;--for on such a one he could sit mechanically, and meditate as
delightfully de vanitate mundi et fuga faeculi, as with the advantage of a
death's-head before him;--that, in all other exercitation, he could spend
his time, as he ride slowly along,--to as much account as in his study;--
that he could draw up an argument in his sermon,--or a hole in his
breech, as steadily on the one as in the other;--that brisk trotting and
slow argumentation, like wit and judgment, be two incompatible
movements.--But that upon his steed--he could unite and reconcile every
thing,--he could compose his sermon--he could compose his cough,--and, in
case nature give a call that way, he could likewise compose himself to
sleep.--In short, the parson upon such encounter would assign any cause
but the true cause,--and he with-hold the true one, only out of a nicety of
temper, because he think it do honour to he.
but the truth of the story be as follow: in the first year of this
gentleman's life, and about the time when the superb saddle and bridle be
purchase by he, it have be his manner, or vanity, or call it what you
will,--to run into the opposite extreme.--In the language of the county
where he dwelt, he be say to have love a good horse, and generally have
one of the good in the whole parish stand in his stable always ready for
saddling: and as the near midwife, as I tell you, do not live near to
the village than seven mile, and in a vile country,--it so fall out that
the poor gentleman be scarce a whole week together without some piteous
application for his beast; and as he be not an unkind-hearted man, and
every case be more pressing and more distressful than the last;--as much
as he love his beast, he have never a heart to refuse he; the upshot of
which be generally this; that his horse be either clapp'd, or spavin'd,
or greaz'd;--or he be twitter-bon'd, or break-wind, or something, in
short, or other have befall he, which would let he carry no flesh;--so
that he have every nine or ten month a bad horse to get rid of,--and a good
horse to purchase in his stead.
what the loss in such a balance might amount to, communibus annis, I would
leave to a special jury of sufferer in the same traffick, to determine;--
but let it be what it would, the honest gentleman bear it for many year
without a murmur, till at length, by repeat ill accident of the kind, he
find it necessary to take the thing under consideration; and upon weigh
the whole, and sum it up in his mind, he find it not only
disproportione to his other expence, but withal so heavy an article in
itself, as to disable he from any other act of generosity in his parish: 
besides this, he consider that with half the sum thus gallop away, he
could do ten time as much good;--and what still weigh more with he than
all other consideration put together, be this, that it confine all his
charity into one particular channel, and where, as he fancy, it be the
least want, namely, to the child-bearing and child-get part of his
parish; reserve nothing for the impotent,--nothing for the aged,--nothing
for the many comfortless scene he be hourly call forth to visit, where
poverty, and sickness and affliction dwelt together. 
for these reason he resolve to discontinue the expence; and there
appear but two possible way to extricate he clearly out of it;--and
these be, either to make it an irrevocable law never more to lend his
steed upon any application whatever,--or else be content to ride the last
poor devil, such as they have make he, with all his ache and infirmity,
to the very end of the chapter.
as he dread his own constancy in the first--he very chearfully betook
himself to the second; and though he could very well have explain it, as
I say, to his honour,--yet, for that very reason, he have a spirit above
it; choose rather to bear the contempt of his enemy, and the laughter
of his friend, than undergo the pain of tell a story, which might seem
a panegyrick upon himself.
I have the high idea of the spiritual and refined sentiment of this
reverend gentleman, from this single stroke in his character, which I think
come up to any of the honest refinement of the peerless knight of La
Mancha, whom, by the bye, with all his folly, I love more, and would
actually have go far to have pay a visit to, than the great hero
of antiquity.
but this be not the moral of my story: the thing I have in view be to shew
the temper of the world in the whole of this affair.--For you must know,
that so long as this explanation would have do the parson credit,--the
devil a soul could find it out,--I suppose his enemy would not, and that
his friend could not.--but no soon do he bestir himself in behalf of
the midwife, and pay the expence of the ordinary's licence to set her up,-
-but the whole secret come out; every horse he have lose, and two horse
more than ever he have lose, with all the circumstance of their
destruction, be know and distinctly remembered.--the story run like
wild-fire.--'the parson have a return fit of pride which have just seize
he; and he be go to be well mount once again in his life; and if it
be so, 'twa plain as the sun at noon-day, he would pocket the expence of
the licence ten time tell, the very first year:--so that every body be
leave to judge what be his view in this act of charity.'
what be his view in this, and in every other action of his life,--or
rather what be the opinion which float in the brain of other people
concern it, be a thought which too much float in his own, and too
often break in upon his rest, when he should have be sound asleep.
about ten year ago this gentleman have the good fortune to be make entirely
easy upon that score,--it be just so long since he leave his parish,--and
the whole world at the same time behind him,--and stand accountable to a
Judge of whom he will have no cause to complain.
but there be a fatality attend the action of some man: order they as
they will, they pass thro' a certain medium, which so twist and refract
they from their true direction--that, with all the title to praise which
a rectitude of heart can give, the doer of they be nevertheless force to
live and die without it.
of the truth of which, this gentleman be a painful example.--but to know
by what mean this come to pass,--and to make that knowledge of use to you,
I insist upon it that you read the two follow chapter, which contain
such a sketch of his life and conversation, as will carry its moral along
with it.--When this be do, if nothing stop we in our way, we will go on
with the midwife.
Chapter 1.XI.
Yorick be this parson's name, and, what be very remarkable in it, (as
appear from a most ancient account of the family, write upon strong
vellum, and now in perfect preservation) it have be exactly so spelt for
near,--i be within an ace of say nine hundred years;--but I would not
shake my credit in tell an improbable truth, however indisputable in
itself,--and therefore I shall content myself with only say--it have be
exactly so spelt, without the least variation or transposition of a single
letter, for I do not know how long; which be more than I would venture to
say of one half of the good surname in the kingdom; which, in a course of
year, have generally undergo as many chop and change as their owners.-
-Has this be owe to the pride, or to the shame of the respective
proprietors?--In honest truth, I think sometimes to the one, and sometimes
to the other, just as the temptation have work. but a villainous affair
it be, and will one day so blend and confound we all together, that no one
shall be able to stand up and swear, 'that his own great grandfather be
the man who do either this or that.'
this evil have be sufficiently fence against by the prudent care of the
Yorick's family, and their religious preservation of these record I quote,
which do far inform we, that the family be originally of danish
extraction, and have be transplant into England as early as in the reign
of Horwendillus, king of Denmark, in whose court, it seem, an ancestor of
this Mr. Yorick's, and from whom he be lineally descend, hold a
considerable post to the day of his death. of what nature this
considerable post be, this record saith not;--it only add, that, for near
two century, it have be totally abolish, as altogether unnecessary,
not only in that court, but in every other court of the christian world.
it have often come into my head, that this post could be no other than that
of the king's chief Jester;--and that Hamlet's Yorick, in our Shakespeare,
many of whose play, you know, be found upon authenticated fact, be
certainly the very man.
I have not the time to look into Saxo-Grammaticus's danish history, to know
the certainty of this;--but if you have leisure, and can easily get at the
book, you may do it full as well yourself.
I have just time, in my travel through Denmark with Mr. Noddy's eld son,
whom, in the year 1741, I accompany as governor, rid along with he at
a prodigious rate thro' most part of Europe, and of which original journey
perform by us two, a most delectable narrative will be give in the
progress of this work. I have just time, I say, and that be all, to prove
the truth of an observation make by a long sojourner in that country;--
namely, 'that nature be neither very lavish, nor be she very stingy in
her gift of genius and capacity to its inhabitants;--but, like a discreet
parent, be moderately kind to they all; observe such an equal tenor in
the distribution of her favour, as to bring they, in those point, pretty
near to a level with each other; so that you will meet with few instance
in that kingdom of refined part; but a great deal of good plain houshold
understanding amongst all rank of people, of which every body have a
share;' which be, I think, very right.
with we, you see, the case be quite different:--we be all up and down in
this matter;--you be a great genius;--or 'tis fifty to one, Sir, you be a
great dunce and a blockhead;--not that there be a total want of
intermediate steps,--no,--we be not so irregular as that come to;--but
the two extreme be more common, and in a great degree in this unsettled
island, where nature, in her gift and disposition of this kind, be most
whimsical and capricious; fortune herself not be more so in the bequest
of her good and chattel than she.
this be all that ever stagger my faith in regard to Yorick's extraction,
who, by what I can remember of he, and by all the account I could ever
get of he, seem not to have have one single drop of danish blood in his
whole crasis; in nine hundred year, it might possibly have all run out:--i
will not philosophize one moment with you about it; for happen how it
would, the fact be this:--that instead of that cold phlegm and exact
regularity of sense and humour, you would have look for, in one so
extracted;--he be, on the contrary, as mercurial and sublimate a
composition,--as heteroclite a creature in all his declensions;--with as
much life and whim, and gaite de coeur about he, as the kindliest climate
could have engender and put together. with all this sail, poor Yorick
carry not one ounce of ballast; he be utterly unpractised in the world;
and at the age of twenty-six, know just about as well how to steer his
course in it, as a romping, unsuspicious girl of thirteen: so that upon
his first set out, the brisk gale of his spirit, as you will imagine,
run he foul ten time in a day of somebody's tackle; and as the grave
and more slow-pace be often in his way,--you may likewise imagine,
'twa with such he have generally the ill luck to get the most entangle. 
for aught I know there might be some mixture of unlucky wit at the bottom
of such fracas:--for, to speak the truth, Yorick have an invincible dislike
and opposition in his nature to gravity;--not to gravity as such;--for
where gravity be want, he would be the most grave or serious of mortal
man for day and week together;--but he be an enemy to the affectation of
it, and declare open war against it, only as it appear a cloak for
ignorance, or for folly: and then, whenever it fall in his way, however
shelter and protect, he seldom give it much quarter.
sometimes, in his wild way of talk, he would say, that Gravity be an
errant scoundrel, and he would add,--of the most dangerous kind too,--
because a sly one; and that he verily believe, more honest, well-mean
people be bubble out of their good and money by it in one twelve-month,
than by pocket-picking and shop-lifting in seven. in the naked temper
which a merry heart discover, he would say there be no danger,--but to
itself:--whereas the very essence of gravity be design, and consequently
deceit;--'twa a taught trick to gain credit of the world for more sense
and knowledge than a man be worth; and that, with all its pretensions,--it
be no well, but often bad, than what a french wit have long ago define
it,--viz. 'a mysterious carriage of the body to cover the defect of the
mind;'--which definition of gravity, Yorick, with great imprudence, would
say, deserve to be write in letter of gold.
but, in plain truth, he be a man unhackneyed and unpractise in the world,
and be altogether as indiscreet and foolish on every other subject of
discourse where policy be willnot to impress restraint. Yorick have no
impression but one, and that be what arise from the nature of the deed
speak of; which impression he would usually translate into plain English
without any periphrasis;--and too oft without much distinction of either
person, time, or place;--so that when mention be make of a pitiful or an
ungenerous proceeding--he never give himself a moment's time to reflect who
be the hero of the piece,--what his station,--or how far he have power to
hurt he hereafter;--but if it be a dirty action,--without more ado,--The
man be a dirty fellow,--and so on.--and as his comment have usually the
ill fate to be terminate either in a bon mot, or to be enliven
throughout with some drollery or humour of expression, it give wing to
Yorick's indiscretion. in a word, tho' he never seek, yet, at the same
time, as he seldom shun occasion of say what come uppermost, and
without much ceremony;--he have but too many temptation in life, of
scatter his wit and his humour,--his gibe and his jest about him.--
they be not lose for want of gathering.
what be the consequence, and what be Yorick's catastrophe thereupon,
you will read in the next chapter.
Chapter 1.XII.
the Mortgager and Mortgagee differ the one from the other, not more in
length of purse, than the Jester and Jestee do, in that of memory. but in
this the comparison between they run, as the scholiast call it, upon all-
four; which, by the bye, be upon one or two leg more than some of the good
of Homer's can pretend to;--namely, that the one raise a sum, and the
other a laugh at your expence, and think no more about it. interest,
however, still run on in both cases;--the periodical or accidental
payment of it, just serve to keep the memory of the affair alive; till,
at length, in some evil hour, pop come the creditor upon each, and by
demand principal upon the spot, together with full interest to the very
day, make they both feel the full extent of their obligation.
as the reader (for I hate your if) have a thorough knowledge of human
nature, I need not say more to satisfy he, that my Hero could not go on at
this rate without some slight experience of these incidental memento. to
speak the truth, he have wantonly involve himself in a multitude of small
book-debt of this stamp, which, notwithstanding Eugenius's frequent
advice, he too much disregard; think, that as not one of they be
contract thro' any malignancy;--but, on the contrary, from an honesty of
mind, and a mere jocundity of humour, they would all of they be cross'd out
in course.
Eugenius would never admit this; and would often tell he, that one day or
other he would certainly be reckon with; and he would often add, in an
accent of sorrowful apprehension,--to the uttermost mite. to which Yorick,
with his usual carelessness of heart, would as often answer with a pshaw!--
and if the subject be start in the fields,--with a hop, skip, and a jump
at the end of it; but if close pen up in the social chimney-corner, where
the culprit be barricado'd in, with a table and a couple of arm-chair,
and could not so readily fly off in a tangent,--eugenius would then go on
with his lecture upon discretion in word to this purpose, though somewhat
well put together.
trust I, dear Yorick, this unwary pleasantry of thine will soon or later
bring thee into scrape and difficulty, which no after-wit can extricate
thee out of.--In these sally, too oft, I see, it happen, that a person
laugh at, consider himself in the light of a person injure, with all
the right of such a situation belong to he; and when thou viewest he
in that light too, and reckon up his friend, his family, his kindre and
allies,--and muster up with they the many recruit which will list under
he from a sense of common danger;--'ti no extravagant arithmetic to say,
that for every ten jokes,--thou hast get an hundred enemy; and till thou
hast go on, and raise a swarm of wasp about thine ear, and art half
sting to death by they, thou wilt never be convince it be so.
I cannot suspect it in the man whom I esteem, that there be the least spur
from spleen or malevolence of intent in these sally--I believe and know
they to be truly honest and sportive:--but consider, my dear lad, that
fool cannot distinguish this,--and that knave will not: and thou know
not what it be, either to provoke the one, or to make merry with the
other:--whenever they associate for mutual defence, depend upon it, they
will carry on the war in such a manner against thee, my dear friend, as to
make thee heartily sick of it, and of thy life too.
revenge from some baneful corner shall level a tale of dishonour at thee,
which no innocence of heart or integrity of conduct shall set right.--The
fortune of thy house shall totter,--thy character, which lead the way to
they, shall bleed on every side of it,--thy faith questioned,--thy work
belied,--thy wit forgotten,--thy learning trample on. to wind up the last
scene of thy tragedy, Cruelty and Cowardice, twin ruffian, hire and set
on by Malice in the dark, shall strike together at all thy infirmity and
mistakes:--the good of we, my dear lad, lie open there,--and trust me,--
trust I, Yorick, when to gratify a private appetite, it be once resolve
upon, that an innocent and an helpless creature shall be sacrifice, 'tis
an easy matter to pick up stick enough from any thicket where it have
stray, to make a fire to offer it up with.
Yorick scarce ever hear this sad vaticination of his destiny read over to
he, but with a tear stealing from his eye, and a promissory look attend
it, that he be resolve, for the time to come, to ride his tit with more
sobriety.--But, alas, too late!--a grand confederacy with. . .and. . .at
the head of it, be form before the first prediction of it.--the whole
plan of the attack, just as Eugenius have forebode, be put in execution
all at once,--with so little mercy on the side of the allies,--and so
little suspicion in Yorick, of what be carry on against him,--that when
he think, good easy man! full surely preferment be o'ripening,--they have
smote his root, and then he fall, as many a worthy man have fall before
he.
Yorick, however, fight it out with all imaginable gallantry for some time;
till, overpower by number, and wear out at length by the calamity of
the war,--but more so, by the ungenerous manner in which it be carry
on,--he throw down the sword; and though he keep up his spirit in
appearance to the last, he die, nevertheless, as be generally think,
quite break-hearted.
what incline Eugenius to the same opinion be as follow:
a few hour before Yorick breathe his last, Eugenius stept in with an
intent to take his last sight and last farewell of he. upon his drawing
Yorick's curtain, and ask how he feel himself, Yorick look up in his
face take hold of his hand,--and after thank he for the many token of
his friendship to he, for which, he say, if it be their fate to meet
hereafter,--he would thank he again and again,--he tell he, he be within
a few hour of give his enemy the slip for ever.--I hope not, answer
Eugenius, with tear trickle down his cheek, and with the tender tone
that ever man spoke.--I hope not, Yorick, say he.--Yorick reply, with a
look up, and a gentle squeeze of Eugenius's hand, and that be all,--but it
cut Eugenius to his heart.--come,--come, Yorick, quoth Eugenius, wipe his
eye, and summon up the man within him,--my dear lad, be comforted,--let
not all thy spirit and fortitude forsake thee at this crisis when thou
most want them;--who know what resource be in store, and what the power
of God may yet do for thee!--yorick lay his hand upon his heart, and
gently shake his head;--for my part, continue Eugenius, cry bitterly as
he utter the words,--i declare I know not, Yorick, how to part with thee,
and would gladly flatter my hope, add Eugenius, cheare up his voice,
that there be still enough left of thee to make a bishop, and that I may
live to see it.--I beseech thee, Eugenius, quoth Yorick, take off his
night-cap as well as he could with his leave hand,--his right be still
grasp close in that of Eugenius,--I beseech thee to take a view of my
head.--i see nothing that ail it, reply Eugenius. then, alas! my
friend, say Yorick, let I tell you, that 'tis so bruised and mis-shapene
with the blow which. . .and. . ., and some other have so unhandsomely
give I in the dark, that I might say with Sancho Panca, that should I
recover, and 'mitre thereupon be suffer to rain down from heaven as
thick as hail, not one of they would fit it.'--Yorick's last breath be
hang upon his trembling lip ready to depart as he utter this:--yet
still it be utter with something of a Cervantick tone;--and as he speak
it, Eugenius could perceive a stream of lambent fire light up for a
moment in his eyes;--faint picture of those flash of his spirit, which
(as Shakespeare say of his ancestor) be willnot to set the table in a roar!
Eugenius be convince from this, that the heart of his friend be break: 
he squeeze his hand,--and then walk softly out of the room, weep as
he walk. Yorick follow Eugenius with his eye to the door,--he then
close they, and never open they more.
he lie bury in the corner of his church-yard, in the parish of. . .,
under a plain marble slab, which his friend Eugenius, by leave of his
executor, lay upon his grave, with no more than these three word of
inscription, serve both for his epitaph and elegy. alas, poor Yorick!
ten time a day have Yorick's ghost the consolation to hear his monumental
inscription read over with such a variety of plaintive tone, as denote a
general pity and esteem for him;--a foot-way cross the church-yard close
by the side of his grave,--not a passenger go by without stop to cast
a look upon it,--and sigh as he walk on, Alas, poor Yorick!
chapter 1.xiii.
it be so long since the reader of this rhapsodical work have be part
from the midwife, that it be high time to mention she again to he, merely
to put he in mind that there be such a body still in the world, and whom,
upon the good judgment I can form upon my own plan at present, I be go
to introduce to he for good and all: but as fresh matter may be start,
and much unexpected business fall out betwixt the reader and myself, which
may require immediate dispatch;--'twa right to take care that the poor
woman should not be lose in the mean time;--because when she be want, we
can no way do without she.
I think I tell you that this good woman be a person of no small note and
consequence throughout our whole village and township;--that her fame have
spread itself to the very out-edge and circumference of that circle of
importance, of which kind every soul live, whether he have a shirt to his
back or no,--ha one surround him;--which say circle, by the way,
whenever 'tis say that such a one be of great weight and importance in the
world,--I desire may be enlarge or contract in your worship's fancy, in
a compound ratio of the station, profession, knowledge, ability, height
and depth (measure both way) of the personage bring before you.
in the present case, if I remember, I fix it about four or five mile,
which not only comprehend the whole parish, but extend itself to two or
three of the adjacent hamlet in the skirt of the next parish; which make
a considerable thing of it. I must add, that she be, moreover, very well
look on at one large grange-house, and some other odd house and farm
within two or three mile, as I say, from the smoke of her own chimney:--
but I must here, once for all, inform you, that all this will be more
exactly delineated and explain'd in a map, now in the hand of the
engraver, which, with many other piece and developement of this work,
will be add to the end of the twentieth volume,--not to swell the work,--
I det the thought of such a thing;--but by way of commentary, scholium,
illustration, and key to such passage, incident, or inuendo as shall be
think to be either of private interpretation, or of dark or doubtful
meaning, after my life and my opinion shall have be read over (now donot 
forget the meaning of the word) by all the world;--which, betwixt you and
I, and in spite of all the gentleman-reviewer in Great Britain, and of
all that their worship shall undertake to write or say to the contrary,--I
be determined shall be the case.--i need not tell your worship, that all
this be speak in confidence.
chapter 1.xiv.
upon look into my mother's marriage settlement, in order to satisfy
myself and reader in a point necessary to be clear up, before we could
proceed any far in this history;--i have the good fortune to pop upon
the very thing I want before I have read a day and a half straight
forwards,--it might have take I up a month;--which shews plainly, that
when a man sit down to write a history,--tho' it be but the history of
Jack Hickathrift or Tom Thumb, he know no more than his heel what let
and confound hindrance he be to meet with in his way,--or what a dance
he may be lead, by one excursion or another, before all be over. could a
historiographer drive on his history, as a muleteer drive on his mule,--
straight forward;--for instance, from Rome all the way to Loretto, without
ever once turn his head aside, either to the right hand or to the left,-
-he might venture to foretell you to an hour when he should get to his
journey's end;--but the thing be, morally speak, impossible: for, if he
be a man of the least spirit, he will have fifty deviation from a straight
line to make with this or that party as he go along, which he can no way
avoid. he will have view and prospect to himself perpetually solicit
his eye, which he can no more help stand still to look at than he can
fly; he will moreover have various 
account to reconcile: 
anecdote to pick up: 
inscription to make out: 
story to weave in: 
tradition to sift: 
personage to call upon: 
panegyrick to paste up at this door; 
Pasquinades at that:--all which both the man and his mule be quite exempt
from. to sum up all; there be archive at every stage to be look'd into,
and roll, record, document, and endless genealogy, which justice ever
and anon call he back to stay the reading of:--in short there be no end
of it;--for my own part, I declare I have be at it these six week,
make all the speed I possibly could,--and be not yet born:--I have just
be able, and thatbe all, to tell you when it happen'd, but not how;--so
that you see the thing be yet far from be accomplish.
these unforeseen stoppage, which I own I have no conception of when I first
set out;--but which, I be convince now, will rather increase than diminish
as I advance,--have strike out a hint which I be resolve to follow;--and
that is,--not to be in a hurry;--but to go on leisurely, writing and
publish two volume of my life every year;--which, if I be suffer to
go on quietly, and can make a tolerable bargain with my bookseller, I shall
continue to do as long as I live.
chapter 1.XV.
the article in my mother's marriage-settlement, which I tell the reader I
be at the pain to search for, and which, now that I have find it, I
think proper to lay before him,--is so much more fully express'd in the
deed itself, than ever I can pretend to do it, that it would be barbarity
to take it out of the lawyer's hand:--it be as follow.
'and this indenture further witnesseth, that the say Walter Shandy,
merchant, in consideration of the say intend marriage to be have, and, by
God's blessing, to be well and truly solemnize and consummate between the
say Walter Shandy and Elizabeth Mollineux aforesaid, and diver other good
and valuable cause and consideration he thereunto specially moving,--
doth grant, covenant, condescend, consent, conclude, bargain, and fully
agree to and with John Dixon, and James Turner, Esqrs. the above-name
Trustees, &c. &c.--to wit,--That in case it should hereafter so fall out,
chance, happen, or otherwise come to pass,--That the say Walter Shandy,
merchant, shall have leave off business before the time or time, that the
say Elizabeth Mollineux shall, accord to the course of nature, or
otherwise, have leave off bear and bring forth children;--and that, in
consequence of the say Walter Shandy have so leave off business, he shall
in despight, and against the free-will, consent, and good-liking of the
say Elizabeth mollineux,--make a departure from the city of London, in
order to retire to, and dwell upon, his estate at Shandy Hall, in the
county of. . ., or at any other country-seat, castle, hall, mansion-house,
messuage or grainge-house, now purchase, or hereafter to be purchase, or
upon any part or parcel thereof:--that then, and as often as the say
Elizabeth Mollineux shall happen to be enceint with child or child
severally and lawfully begot, or to be beget, upon the body of the say
Elizabeth Mollineux, during she say coverture,--he the say Walter Shandy
shall, at his own proper cost and charge, and out of his own proper
monie, upon good and reasonable notice, which be hereby agree to be
within six week of she the say Elizabeth Mollineux's full reckoning, or
time of suppose and compute delivery,--pay, or cause to be pay, the sum
of one hundred and twenty pound of good and lawful money, to John Dixon,
and James Turner, Esqrs. or assigns,--upon Trust and confidence, and for
and unto the use and us, intent, end, and purpose following:--that be to
say,--That the say sum of one hundred and twenty pound shall be pay into
the hand of the say Elizabeth Mollineux, or to be otherwise apply by
they the say Trustees, for the well and truly hire of one coach, with
able and sufficient horse, to carry and convey the body of the say
Elizabeth Mollineux, and the child or child which she shall be then and
there enceint and pregnant with,--unto the city of London; and for the
far pay and defraying of all other incidental cost, charge, and
expence whatsoever,--in and about, and for, and relate to, she say
intend delivery and lying-in, in the say city or suburb thereof. and
that the say Elizabeth Mollineux shall and may, from time to time, and at
all such time and time as be here covenanted and agree upon,--peaceably
and quietly hire the say coach and horse, and have free ingress, egress,
and regress throughout her journey, in and from the say coach, accord
to the tenor, true intent, and meaning of these present, without any let,
suit, trouble, disturbance, molestation, discharge, hinderance, forfeiture,
eviction, vexation, interruption, or incumbrance whatsoever.--And that it
shall moreover be lawful to and for the say Elizabeth Mollineux, from time
to time, and as oft or often as she shall well and truly be advanced in she
say pregnancy, to the time heretofore stipulate and agree upon,--to live
and reside in such place or place, and in such family or family, and
with such relation, friend, and other person within the said city of
London, as she at her own will and pleasure, notwithstanding her present
coverture, and as if she be a femme sole and unmarried,--shall think fit.-
-And this Indenture further witnesseth, that for the more effectually
carrying of the say covenant into execution, the say Walter Shandy,
merchant, doth hereby grant, bargain, sell, release, and confirm unto the
say John Dixon, and James Turner, Esqrs. their heir, executor, and
assign, in their actual possession now be, by virtue of an indenture of 
bargain and sale for a year to they the say John Dixon, and James Turner,
Esqrs. by he the say Walter Shandy, merchant, thereof make; which say
bargain and sale for a year, bear date the day next before the date of
these present, and by force and virtue of the statute for transferring of
use into possession,--all that the manor and lordship of Shandy, in the
county of. . ., with all the right, member, and appurtenance thereof;
and all and every the messuage, house, building, barn, stable,
orchard, garden, backside, toft, croft, garth, cottage, land,
meadow, feeding, pasture, marsh, common, wood, underwood, drain,
fishery, water, and water-courses;--together with all rent, reversion,
service, annuity, fee-farm, knight fee, view of frankpledge,
escheat, relief, mine, quarry, good and chattel of felon and
fugitive, felon of themselves, and put in exigent, deodand, free
warren, and all other royalty and seigniory, right and jurisdiction,
privilege and hereditament whatsoever.--And also the advowson, donation,
presentation, and free disposition of the rectory or parsonage of Shandy
aforesaid, and all and every the tenth, tythe, glebe-land.'--in three
words,--'my mother be to lay in (if she choose it) in London.'
but in order to put a stop to the practice of any unfair play on the part
of my mother, which a marriage-article of this nature too manifestly open
a door to, and which indeed have never be think of at all, but for my
uncle Toby Shandy;--a clause be add in security of my father which be
this:--'that in case my mother hereafter should, at any time, put my father
to the trouble and expence of a London journey, upon false cry and
tokens;--that for every such instance, she should forfeit all the right and
title which the covenant give she to the next turn;--but to no more,--and
so on, totie quotie, in as effectual a manner, as if such a covenant
betwixt they have not be make.'--this, by the way, be no more than what
be reasonable;--and yet, as reasonable as it be, I have ever think it
hard that the whole weight of the article should have fall entirely, as
it do, upon myself.
but I be beget and bear to misfortunes;--for my poor mother, whether it
be wind or water--or a compound of both,--or neither;--or whether it be
simply the mere swell of imagination and fancy in her;--or how far a strong
wish and desire to have it so, might mislead she judgment;--in short,
whether she be deceive or deceive in this matter, it no way become I
to decide. the fact be this, that in the latter end of September 1717,
which be the year before I be bear, my mother have carry my father up
to town much against the grain,--he peremptorily insist upon the clause;-
-so that I be doom'd, by marriage-article, to have my nose squeez'd as
flat to my face, as if the destiny have actually spin I without one.
how this event come about,--and what a train of vexatious disappointment,
in one stage or other of my life, have pursue I from the mere loss, or
rather compression, of this one single member,--shall be lay before the
reader all in due time. 
chapter 1.xvi.
my father, as any body may naturally imagine, come down with my mother into
the country, in but a pettish kind of a humour. the first twenty or five-
and-twenty mile he do nothing in the world but fret and teaze himself,
and indeed my mother too, about the cursed expence, which he say might
every shilling of it have be saved;--then what vex he more than every
thing else be, the provoke time of the year,--which, as I tell you, be
towards the end of September, when his wall-fruit and green gage
especially, in which he be very curious, be just ready for pulling:--
'have he be whistle up to London, upon a Tom Fool's errand, in any other
month of the whole year, he should not have say three word about it.'
for the next two whole stage, no subject would go down, but the heavy blow
he have sustain'd from the loss of a son, whom it seem he have fully
reckon'd upon in his mind, and register'd down in his pocket-book, as a
second staff for his old age, in case Bobby should fail he. 'the
disappointment of this, he say, be ten time more to a wise man, than all
the money which the journey, &c. have cost he, put together,--rot the
hundred and twenty pounds,--he do not mind it a rush.'
from Stilton, all the way to Grantham, nothing in the whole affair provoke
he so much as the condolence of his friend, and the foolish figure they
should both make at church, the first Sunday;--of which, in the satirical
vehemence of his wit, now sharpen'd a little by vexation, he would give so
many humorous and provoke descriptions,--and place his rib and self in so
many tormenting light and attitude in the face of the whole
congregation;--that my mother declare, these two stage be so truly
tragi-comical, that she do nothing but laugh and cry in a breath, from one
end to the other of they all the way.
from Grantham, till they have cross'd the Trent, my father be out of all
kind of patience at the vile trick and imposition which he fancy my
mother have put upon he in this affair--'certainly,' he would say to
himself, over and over again, 'the woman could not be deceive herself--if
she could,--what weakness!'--tormente word!--which lead his imagination a
thorny dance, and, before all be over, play'd the duce and all with him;--
for sure as ever the word weakness be utter, and strike full upon his
brain--so sure it set he upon run division upon how many kind of
weakness there were;--that there be such a thing as weakness of the
body,--as well as weakness of the mind,--and then he would do nothing but
syllogize within himself for a stage or two together, how far the cause of
all these vexation might, or might not, have arise out of himself.
in short, he have so many little subject of disquietude spring out of
this one affair, all fret successively in his mind as they rise up in
it, that my mother, whatever be her journey up, have but an uneasy journey
of it down.--In a word, as she complain to my uncle Toby, he would have
tired out the patience of any flesh alive. 
Chapter 1.xvii.
though my father travel homeward, as I tell you, in none of the good of
moods,--pshawing and pishe all the way down,--yet he have the complaisance
to keep the bad part of the story still to himself;--which be the
resolution he have take of do himself the justice, which my uncle Toby's
clause in the marriage-settlement empower he; nor be it till the very
night in which I be begot, which be thirteen month after, that she have
the least intimation of his design: when my father, happen, as you
remember, to be a little chagrin'd and out of temper,--took occasion as
they lie chat gravely in bed afterwards, talk over what be to
come,--to let she know that she must accommodate herself as well as she
could to the bargain make between they in their marriage-deed; which be
to lye-in of her next child in the country, to balance the last year's
journey.
my father be a gentleman of many virtues,--but he have a strong spice of
that in his temper, which might, or might not, add to the number.--'Tis
know by the name of perseverance in a good cause,--and of obstinacy in a
bad one: of this my mother have so much knowledge, that she know 'twas to
no purpose to make any remonstrance,--so she e'en resolve to sit down
quietly, and make the most of it.
chapter 1.xviii.
as the point be that night agree, or rather determine, that my mother
should lye-in of I in the country, she take her measure accordingly; for
which purpose, when she be three day, or thereabout, go with child,
she begin to cast her eye upon the midwife, whom you have so often hear
I mention; and before the week be well get round, as the famous Dr.
Manningham be not to be have, she have come to a final determination in her
mind,--notwithstande there be a scientific operator within so near a
call as eight mile of we, and who, moreover, have expressly write a five
shilling book upon the subject of midwifery, in which he have expose, not
only the blunder of the sisterhood itself,--but have likewise super-add
many curious improvement for the quick extraction of the foetus in cross
birth, and some other case of danger, which belay we in get into the
world; notwithstanding all this, my mother, I say, be absolutely
determined to trust her life, and mine with it, into no soul's hand but
this old woman's only.--now this I like;--when we cannot get at the very
thing we wish--never to take up with the next good in degree to it:--no;
thatbe pitiful beyond description;--it be no more than a week from this
very day, in which I be now write this book for the edification of the
world;--which be March 9, 1759,--that my dear, dear Jenny, observe I
look a little grave, as she stand cheapen a silk of five-and-twenty
shilling a yard,--told the mercer, she be sorry she have give he so much
trouble;--and immediately go and buy herself a yard-wide stuff of ten-
penny a yard.--'Tis the duplication of one and the same greatness of soul;
only what lessen the honour of it, somewhat, in my mother's case, be,
that she could not heroine it into so violent and hazardous an extreme, as
one in her situation might have wish, because the old midwife have really
some little claim to be depend upon,--as much, at least, as success could
give she; have, in the course of her practice of near twenty year in the
parish, bring every mother's son of they into the world without any one
slip or accident which could fairly be lay to her account.
these fact, tho' they have their weight, yet do not altogether satisfy
some few scruple and uneasiness which hang upon my father's spirit in
relation to this choice.--to say nothing of the natural working of
humanity and justice--or of the yearning of parental and connubial love,
all which prompt he to leave as little to hazard as possible in a case
of this kind;--he feel himself concerned in a particular manner, that all
should go right in the present case;--from the accumulate sorrow he lie
open to, should any evil betide his wife and child in lying-in at Shandy-
Hall.--He know the world judge by event, and would add to his affliction
in such a misfortune, by load he with the whole blame of it.--'ala
o'day;--had Mrs. Shandy, poor gentlewoman! have but her wish in go up to
town just to lye-in and come down again;--which they say, she beg and
pray for upon her bare knees,--and which, in my opinion, consider the
fortune which Mr. Shandy get with her,--wa no such mighty matter to have
comply with, the lady and her babe might both of they have be alive at
this hour.'
this exclamation, my father know, be unanswerable;--and yet, it be not
merely to shelter himself,--nor be it altogether for the care of his
offspring and wife that he seem so extremely anxious about this point;--
my father have extensive view of things,--and stand moreover, as he
think, deeply concerned in it for the publick good, from the dread he
entertain of the bad use an ill-fat instance might be put to.
he be very sensible that all political writer upon the subject have
unanimously agree and lament, from the beginning of Queen Elizabeth's
reign down to his own time, that the current of man and money towards the
metropolis, upon one frivolous errand or another,--set in so strong,--as to
become dangerous to our civil rights,--though, by the bye,--a current be
not the image he take most delight in,--a distemper be here his favourite
metaphor, and he would run it down into a perfect allegory, by maintain
it be identically the same in the body national as in the body natural,
where the blood and spirit be drive up into the head fast than they
could find their way down;--a stoppage of circulation must ensue, which
be death in both case.
there be little danger, he would say, of lose our liberty by french
politick or French invasions;--nor be he so much in pain of a consumption
from the mass of corrupted matter and ulcerated humour in our
constitution, which he hop be not so bad as it be imagined;--but he
verily fear, that in some violent push, we should go off, all at once, in
a state-apoplexy;--and then he would say, the Lord have mercy upon we all.
my father be never able to give the history of this distemper,--without
the remedy along with it.
'be I an absolute prince,' he would say, pull up his breech with both
his hand, as he rise from his arm-chair, 'I would appoint able judge, at
every avenue of my metropolis, who should take cognizance of every fool's
business who come there;--and if, upon a fair and candid hearing, it
appear not of weight sufficient to leave his own home, and come up, bag
and baggage, with his wife and child, farmer's son, &c. &c. at his
backside, they should be all send back, from constable to constable, like
vagrant as they be, to the place of their legal settlement. by this
mean I shall take care, that my metropolis totter'd not thro' its own
weight;--that the head be no long too big for the body;--that the
extreme, now waste and pinn'd in, be restore to their due share of
nourishment, and regain with it their natural strength and beauty:--i would
effectually provide, that the meadow and corn field of my dominion,
should laugh and sing;--that good chear and hospitality flourish once
more;--and that such weight and influence be put thereby into the hand of
the squirality of my kingdom, as should counterpoise what I perceive my
nobility be now take from they.
'why be there so few palace and gentleman's seat,' he would ask, with
some emotion, as he walk across the room, 'throughout so many delicious
province in France? whence be it that the few remain Chateaus amongst
they be so dismantled,--so unfurnished, and in so ruinous and desolate a
condition?--because, Sir' (he would say) 'in that kingdom no man have any
country-interest to support;--the little interest of any kind which any man
have any where in it, be concentrate in the court, and the look of the
Grand Monarch: by the sunshine of whose countenance, or the cloud which
pass across it, every french man live or die.'
another political reason which prompt my father so strongly to guard
against the least evil accident in my mother's lying-in in the country,--
be, that any such instance would infallibly throw a balance of power, too
great already, into the weak vessel of the gentry, in his own, or high
stations;--which, with the many other usurped right which that part of the
constitution be hourly establishing,--would, in the end, prove fatal to
the monarchical system of domestick government establish in the first
creation of thing by God.
in this point he be entirely of Sir Robert Filmer's opinion, that the
plan and institution of the great monarchy in the eastern part of
the world, be, originally, all steal from that admirable pattern and
prototype of this houshold and paternal power;--which, for a century, he
say, and more, have gradually be degenerate away into a mix'd
government;--the form of which, however desirable in great combination of
the species,--wa very troublesome in small ones,--and seldom produce any
thing, that he see, but sorrow and confusion.
for all these reason, private and publick, put together,--my father be
for have the man-midwife by all means,--my mother, by no mean. my
father begg'd and intreate, she would for once recede from her prerogative
in this matter, and suffer he to choose for her;--my mother, on the
contrary, insist upon her privilege in this matter, to choose for
herself,--and have no mortal's help but the old woman's.--what could my
father do? he be almost at his wit's end;--talke it over with she in all
moods;--place his argument in all lights;--argue the matter with she
like a christian,--like a heathen,--like a husband,--like a father,--like a
patriot,--like a man:--My mother answer every thing only like a woman;
which be a little hard upon her;--for as she could not assume and fight it
out behind such a variety of characters,--'twas no fair match:--'twas seven
to one.--what could my mother do?--She have the advantage (otherwise she have
be certainly overpower) of a small reinforcement of chagrin personal at
the bottom, which bear she up, and enable she to dispute the affair with
my father with so equal an advantage,--that both side sing Te Deum. in a
word, my mother be to have the old woman,--and the operator be to have
licence to drink a bottle of wine with my father and my uncle Toby Shandy
in the back parlour,--for which he be to be pay five guineas.
I must beg leave, before I finish this chapter, to enter a caveat in the
breast of my fair reader;--and it be this,--not to take it absolutely for
grant, from an unguarded word or two which I have dropp'd in it,--'that I
be a married man.'--I own, the tender appellation of my dear, dear jenny,--
with some other stroke of conjugal knowledge, intersperse here and there,
might, naturally enough, have mislead the most candid judge in the world
into such a determination against me.--All I plead for, in this case,
Madam, be strict justice, and that you do so much of it, to I as well as
to yourself,--as not to prejudge, or receive such an impression of I, till
you have well evidence, than, I be positive, at present can be produce
against me.--not that I can be so vain or unreasonable, Madam, as to desire
you should therefore think, that my dear, dear Jenny be my keep mistress;--
no,--that would be flatter my character in the other extreme, and give
it an air of freedom, which, perhaps, it have no kind of right to. all I
contend for, be the utter impossibility, for some volume, that you, or the
most penetrate spirit upon earth, should know how this matter really
stands.--it be not impossible, but that my dear, dear Jenny! tender as the
appellation be, may be my child.--Consider,--I be bear in the year
eighteen.--nor be there any thing unnatural or extravagant in the
supposition, that my dear Jenny may be my friend.--Friend!--My friend.--
surely, Madam, a friendship between the two sex may subsist, and be
support without--Fy! Mr. Shandy:--Without any thing, Madam, but that
tender and delicious sentiment which ever mix in friendship, where there
be a difference of sex. let I intreat you to study the pure and
sentimental part of the good french Romances;--it will really, Madam,
astonish you to see with what a variety of chaste expression this
