delicious sentiment, which I have the honour to speak of, be dress'd out.
chapter 1.xix.
I would soon undertake to explain the hard problem in geometry, than
pretend to account for it, that a gentleman of my father's great good
sense,--knowing, as the reader must have observe he, and curious too in
philosophy,--wise also in political reasoning,--and in polemical (as he
will find) no way ignorant,--could be capable of entertain a notion in
his head, so out of the common track,--that I fear the reader, when I come
to mention it to he, if he be the least of a cholerick temper, will
immediatly throw the book by; if mercurial, he will laugh most heartily at
it;--and if he be of a grave and saturnine cast, he will, at first sight,
absolutely condemn as fanciful and extravagant; and that be in respect to
the choice and imposition of christian name, on which he think a great
deal more depended than what superficial mind be capable of conceiving.
his opinion, in this matter, be, that there be a strange kind of magick
bias, which good or bad name, as he call they, irresistibly impress
upon our character and conduct.
the hero of Cervantes argue not the point with more seriousness,--nor have
he more faith,--or more to say on the power of necromancy in dishonour
his deeds,--or on Dulcinea's name, in shed lustre upon they, than my
father have on those of Trismegistus or Archimedes, on the one hand--or of
Nyky and Simkin on the other. how many Caesars and Pompeys, he would say,
by mere inspiration of the name, have be render worthy of they? and
how many, he would add, be there, who might have do exceed well in
the world, have not their character and spirit be totally depressed and
Nicodemus'd into nothing?
I see plainly, Sir, by your look, (or as the case happen) my father
would say--that you do not heartily subscribe to this opinion of mine,--
which, to those, he would add, who have not carefully sift it to the
bottom,--I own have an air more of fancy than of solid reasoning in it;--and
yet, my dear Sir, if I may presume to know your character, I be morally
assure, I should hazard little in state a case to you, not as a party in
the dispute,--but as a judge, and trust my appeal upon it to your own
good sense and candid disquisition in this matter;--you be a person free
from as many narrow prejudice of education as most men;--and, if I may
presume to penetrate far into you,--of a liberality of genius above
bear down an opinion, merely because it want friend. your son,--your
dear son,--from whose sweet and open temper you have so much to expect.--
your Billy, Sir!--would you, for the world, have call he Judas?--Would
you, my dear Sir, he would say, lay his hand upon your breast, with the
genteelest address,--and in that soft and irresistible piano of voice,
which the nature of the argumentum ad hominem absolutely requires,--would
you, Sir, if a Jew of a godfather have propose the name for your child, and
offer you his purse along with it, would you have consent to such a
desecration of him?--o my God! he would say, look up, if I know your
temper right, sir,--you be incapable of it;--you would have trample upon
the offer;--you would have throw the temptation at the tempter's head with
abhorrence.
your greatness of mind in this action, which I admire, with that generous
contempt of money, which you shew I in the whole transaction, be really
noble;--and what render it more so, be the principle of it;--the working
of a parent's love upon the truth and conviction of this very hypothesis,
namely, that be your son call Judas,--the forbid and treacherous idea,
so inseparable from the name, would have accompany he through life like
his shadow, and, in the end, make a miser and a rascal of he, in spite,
Sir, of your example.
I never know a man able to answer this argument.--But, indeed, to speak of
my father as he was;--he be certainly irresistible;--both in his oration
and disputations;--he be bear an orator;--(greek).--persuasion hang upon
his lip, and the element of Logick and Rhetorick be so blend up in
him,--and, withal, he have so shrewd a guess at the weakness and passion
of his respondent,--that Nature might have stand up and said,--'this man be
eloquent.'--in short, whether he be on the weak or the strong side of the
question, 'twa hazardous in either case to attack him.--and yet, 'tis
strange, he have never read Cicero, nor Quintilian de Oratore, nor
Isocrates, nor Aristotle, nor Longinus, amongst the antients;--nor Vossius,
nor Skioppius, nor Ramus, nor Farnaby, amongst the moderns;--and what be
more astonishing, he have never in his whole life the least light or spark
of subtilty strike into his mind, by one single lecture upon Crackenthorp
or Burgersdicius or any dutch logician or commentator;--he know not so much
as in what the difference of an argument ad ignorantiam, and an argument ad
hominem consist; so that I well remember, when he go up along with I
to enter my name at Jesus College in. . .,--it be a matter of just wonder
with my worthy tutor, and two or three fellow of that learn society,--
that a man who know not so much as the name of his tool, should be able
to work after that fashion with they.
to work with they in the good manner he could, be what my father be,
however, perpetually force upon;--for he have a thousand little sceptical
notion of the comick kind to defend--most of which notion, I verily
believe, at first enter upon the footing of mere whim, and of a vive la
Bagatelle; and as such he would make merry with they for half an hour or
so, and have sharpen his wit upon they, dismiss they till another day.
I mention this, not only as matter of hypothesis or conjecture upon the
progress and establishment of my father's many odd opinions,--but as a
warning to the learn reader against the indiscreet reception of such
guest, who, after a free and undisturbed entrance, for some year, into
our brains,--at length claim a kind of settlement there,--worke sometimes
like yeast;--but more generally after the manner of the gentle passion,
begin in jest,--but end in downright earnest.
whether this be the case of the singularity of my father's notion--or
that his judgment, at length, become the dupe of his wit;--or how far, in
many of his notion, he might, though odd, be absolutely right;--the
reader, as he come at they, shall decide. all that I maintain here, be,
that in this one, of the influence of christian name, however it gain
footing, he be serious;--he be all uniformity;--he be systematical, and,
like all systematic reasoner, he would move both heaven and earth, and
twist and torture every thing in nature to support his hypothesis. in a
word I repeat it over again;--he be serious;--and, in consequence of it,
he would lose all kind of patience whenever he see people, especially of
condition, who should have know better,--as careless and as indifferent
about the name they impose upon their child,--or more so, than in the
choice of Ponto or Cupid for their puppy-dog.
this, he would say, look'd ill;--and have, moreover, this particular
aggravation in it, viz. that when once a vile name be wrongfully or
injudiciously give, 'twa not like the case of a man's character, which,
when wrong'd, might hereafter be cleared;--and, possibly, some time or
other, if not in the man's life, at least after his death,--be, somehow or
other, set to right with the world: but the injury of this, he would say,
could never be undone;--nay, he doubt even whether an act of parliament
could reach it:--He know as well as you, that the legislature assume a
power over surnames;--but for very strong reason, which he could give, it
have never yet adventure, he would say, to go a step far.
it be observable, that tho' my father, in consequence of this opinion,
have, as I have tell you, the strong liking and disliking towards
certain names;--that there be still number of name which hang so
equally in the balance before he, that they be absolutely indifferent to
he. Jack, Dick, and Tom be of this class: these my father call
neutral names;--affirming of they, without a satire, that there have be as
many knave and fool, at least, as wise and good man, since the world
begin, who have indifferently bear them;--so that, like equal force act
against each other in contrary direction, he think they mutually
destroy each other's effect; for which reason, he would often declare,
he would not give a cherry-stone to choose amongst they. Bob, which be my
brother's name, be another of these neutral kind of christian name,
which operate very little either way; and as my father happen'd to be at
Epsom, when it be give him,--he would oft-time thank Heaven it be no
bad. Andrew be something like a negative quantity in Algebra with him;-
-'twas bad, he say, than nothing.--william stand pretty high:--Numps
again be low with him:--and Nick, he say, be the Devil.
but of all name in the universe he have the most unconquerable aversion for
tristram;--he have the low and most contemptible opinion of it of any
thing in the world,--thinking it could possibly produce nothing in rerum
natura, but what be extremely mean and pitiful: so that in the midst of a
dispute on the subject, in which, by the bye, he be frequently involved,--
he would sometimes break off in a sudden and spirited Epiphonema, or rather
Erotesis, raise a third, and sometimes a full fifth above the key of the
discourse,--and demand it categorically of his antagonist, whether he would
take upon he to say, he have ever remembered,--whether he have ever read,--
or even whether he have ever hear tell of a man, call Tristram,
perform any thing great or worth recording?--no,--he would say,--
tristram!--the thing be impossible.
what could be want in my father but to have write a book to publish this
notion of his to the world? little boot it to the subtle speculatist to
stand single in his opinions,--unless he give they proper vent:--it be
the identical thing which my father did:--for in the year sixteen, which
be two year before I be bear, he be at the pain of write an express
dissertation simply upon the word tristram,--shewe the world, with great
candour and modesty, the ground of his great abhorrence to the name.
when this story be compare with the title-page,--will not the gentle
reader pity my father from his soul?--to see an orderly and well-dispose
gentleman, who tho' singular,--yet inoffensive in his notions,--so play
upon in they by cross purposes;--to look down upon the stage, and see he
baffle and overthrow in all his little system and wish; to behold a
train of event perpetually fall out against he, and in so critical and
cruel a way, as if they have purposedly be plann'd and point against
he, merely to insult his speculations.--In a word, to behold such a one,
in his old age, ill-fit for trouble, ten time in a day suffer
sorrow;--ten time in a day call the child of his prayer Tristram!--
Melancholy dissyllable of sound! which, to his ear, be unison to
Nincompoop, and every name vituperative under heaven.--By his ash! I
swear it,--if ever malignant spirit take pleasure, or busy itself in
traverse the purpose of mortal man,--it must have be here;--and if it
be not necessary I should be bear before I be christen, I would this
moment give the reader an account of it.
chapter 1.xx.
--how could you, Madam, be so inattentive in read the last chapter? I
tell you in it, that my mother be not a papist.--papist! you tell I no
such thing, sir.--madam, I beg leave to repeat it over again, that I tell
you as plain, at least, as word, by direct inference, could tell you such
a thing.--Then, Sir, I must have miss'd a page.--No, Madam, you have not
miss'd a word.--Then I be asleep, sir.--my pride, Madam, cannot allow you
that refuge.--Then, I declare, I know nothing at all about the matter.--
that, Madam, be the very fault I lie to your charge; and as a punishment
for it, I do insist upon it, that you immediately turn back, that be as
soon as you get to the next full stop, and read the whole chapter over
again. I have impose this penance upon the lady, neither out of
wantonness nor cruelty; but from the good of motif; and therefore shall
make she no apology for it when she return back:--'tis to rebuke a vicious
taste, which have creep into thousand besides herself,--of reading straight
forward, more in quest of the adventure, than of the deep erudition and
knowledge which a book of this cast, if read over as it should be, would
infallibly impart with they--the mind should be accustom to make wise
reflection, and draw curious conclusion as it go along; the habitude of
which make Pliny the young affirm, 'that he never read a book so bad, but
he draw some profit from it.' the story of Greece and Rome, run over
without this turn and application,--do less service, I affirm it, than the
history of Parismus and Parismenus, or of the Seven Champions of England,
read with it.
--But here come my fair lady. have you read over again the chapter,
Madam, as I desire you?--you have: and do you not observe the passage,
upon the second reading, which admit the inference?--not a word like it! 
then, Madam, be pleased to ponder well the last line but one of the
chapter, where I take upon I to say, 'it be necessary I should be bear
before I be christen'd.' have my mother, Madam, be a Papist, that
consequence do not follow. (the Romish Rituals direct the baptizing of
the child, in case of danger, before it be born;--but upon this proviso,
that some part or other of the child's body be see by the baptizer:--but
the doctor of the Sorbonne, by a deliberation hold amongst they, April 10,
1733,--have enlarge the power of the midwife, by determine, that
though no part of the child's body should appear,--that baptism shall,
nevertheless, be administer to it by injection,--par le moyen d'une
petite canulle,--anglice a squirt.--'Tis very strange that St. Thomas
Aquinas, who have so good a mechanical head, both for tie and untie the
knot of school-divinity,--should, after so much pain bestow upon this,-
-give up the point at last, as a second La choose impossible,--'infante in
maternis uteris existentes (quoth St. Thomas!) baptizari possunt nullo
modo.'--O Thomas! Thomas! if the reader have the curiosity to see the
question upon baptism by injection, as present to the doctor of the
Sorbonne, with their consultation thereupon, it be as follow.)
it be a terrible misfortune for this same book of mine, but more so to the
Republick of letters;--so that my own be quite swallow up in the
consideration of it,--that this self-same vile pruriency for fresh
adventure in all thing, have get so strongly into our habit and humour,--
and so wholly intent be we upon satisfy the impatience of our
concupiscence that way,--that nothing but the gross and more carnal part
of a composition will go down:--the subtle hint and sly communication of
science fly off, like spirit upwards,--the heavy moral escape downwards;
and both the one and the other be as much lose to the world, as if they
be still leave in the bottom of the ink-horn.
I wish the male-reader have not pass'd by many a one, as quaint and curious
as this one, in which the female-reader have be detect. I wish it may
have its effects;--and that all good people, both male and female, from
example, may be teach to think as well as read.
Memoire presente a Messieurs les Docteurs de Sorbonne 
Vide Deventer. Paris Edit. 4to, 1734, p. 366.
Un Chirurgien Accoucheur, represente a Messieurs le Docteurs de Sorbonne,
qu'il y a des cas, quoique tre rare, ou une mere ne scauroit accoucher, &
meme ou l'enfant est tellement renferme dans le sein de sa mere, qu'il ne
fait paroitre aucune partie de son corps, ce qui seroit un cas, suivant les
Rituels, de lui conferer, du moin sous condition, le bapteme. Le
Chirurgien, qui consulte, pretend, par le moyen d'une petite canulle, de
pouvoir baptiser immediatement l'enfant, san faire aucun tort a la mere.--
Il demand si ce moyen, qu'il vient de proposer, est permis & legitime, &
s'il peut s'en servir dans les cas qu'il vient d'exposer.
Reponse
Le Conseil estime, que la question proposee souffre de grande difficulte.
Les Theologiens posent d'un cote pour principe, que le bapteme, qui est une
naissance spirituelle, suppose une premiere naissance; il faut etre ne dans
le monde, pour renaitre en Jesus Christ, comme ils l'enseignent. S.
Thomas, 3 part. quaest. 88 artic. II. suit cette doctrine comme une verite
constante; l'on ne peut, dit ce S. Docteur, baptiser les enfan qui sont
renferme dans le sein de leurs mere, & S. Thomas est fonde sur ce, que
le enfan ne sont point ne, & ne peuvent etre compte parmi les autres
homme; d'ou il conclud, qu'ils ne peuvent etre l'objet d'une action
exterieure, pour recevoir par leur ministere, les sacremen necessaire au
salut: Pueri in maternis uteris existente nondum prodierunt in lucem ut
cum aliis hominibus vitam ducant; unde non possunt subjici actioni humanae,
ut per eorum ministerium sacramenta recipiant ad salutem. Les rituel
ordonnent dan la pratique ce que les theologien ont etabli sur les meme
matiere, & ils deffendent tous d'une maniere uniforme, de baptiser les
enfan qui sont renferme dans le sein de leurs mere, s'ils ne sont
paroitre quelque partie de leurs corps. Le concour des theologien, & des
rituel, qui sont les regle des diocese, paroit former une autorite qui
termine la question presente; cependant le conseil de conscience
considerant d'un cote, que le raisonnement des theologien est uniquement
fonde sur une raison de convenance, & que la deffense des rituel suppose
que l'on ne peut baptiser immediatement les enfan ainsi renferme dans le
sein de leurs mere, ce qui est contre la supposition presente; & d'un
autre cote, considerant que les meme theologien enseignent, que l'on peut
risquer les sacremen que Jesus Christ a etablis comme des moyen facile,
mais necessaire pour sanctifier les homme; & d'ailleurs estimant, que les
enfan renferme dans le sein de leurs mere, pourroient etre capable de
salut, parcequ'il sont capable de damnation;--pour ce consideration, &
en egard a l'expose, suivant lequel on assure avoir trouve un moyen certain
de baptiser ce enfan ainsi renferme, san faire aucun tort a la mere, le
Conseil estime que l'on pourroit se servir du moyen propose, dans la
confiance qu'il a, que Dieu n'a point laisse ce sorte d'enfans san
aucun secour, & supposant, comme il est expose, que le moyen donot il
s'agit est propre a leur procur le bapteme; cependant comme il s'agiroit,
en autorisant la pratique proposee, de changer une regle universellement
etablie, le Conseil croit que celui qui consulte doit s'addresser a son
eveque, & a qui il appartient de juger de l'utilite, & du danger du moyen
propose, & comme, sous le bon plaisir de l'eveque, le Conseil estime qu'il
faudroit recourir au Pape, qui a le droit d'expliquer les regle de
l'eglise, & d'y deroger dans le cas, ou la loi ne scauroit obliger, quelque
sage & quelque utile que paroisse la maniere de baptiser donot il s'agit, le
Conseil ne pourroit l'approver sans le concour de ce deux autorite. on
conseile au moin a celui qui consulte, de s'addresser a son eveque, & de
lui faire part de la presente decision, afin que, si le prelat entre dan
les raison sur lesquelle les docteur soussigne s'appuyent, il puisse
etre autorise dans le cas de necessite, ou il risqueroit trop d'attendre
que la permission fut demandee & accordee d'employer le moyen qu'il propose
si avantageux au salut de l'enfant. Au reste, le Conseil, en estimant que
l'on pourroit s'en servir, croit cependant, que si les enfan donot il
s'agit, venoient au monde, contre l'esperance de ceux qui se seroient
servis du meme moyen, il seroit necessaire de les baptiser sous condition;
& en cela le Conseil se conforme a tous les rituel, qui en autorisant le
bapteme d'un enfant qui fait paroitre quelque partie de son corps,
enjoignent neantmoin, & ordonnent de le baptiser sous condition, s'il
vient heureusement au monde.
Delibere en Sorbonne, le 10 Avril, 1733.
A. Le Moyne.
L. De Romigny.
De Marcilly.
Mr. Tristram Shandy's compliment to Messrs. Le Moyne, De Romigny, and De
Marcilly; hop they all rest well the night after so tiresome a
consultation.--He beg to know, whether after the ceremony of marriage, and
before that of consummation, the baptize all the Homunculi at once,
slapdash, by injection, would not be a short and safe cut still; on
condition, as above, that if the Homunculi do well, and come safe into the
world after this, that each and every of they shall be baptize again (sous
condition)--And provide, in the second place, that the thing can be do,
which Mr. Shandy apprehend it may, par le moyen d'une petite canulle, and
sans faire aucune tort au pere.
Chapter 1.xxi.
--I wonder whatbe all that noise, and run backwards and forwards for,
above stair, quoth my father, address himself, after an hour and a
half's silence, to my uncle Toby,--who, you must know, be sit on the
opposite side of the fire, smoake his social pipe all the time, in mute
contemplation of a new pair of black plush-breech which he have get on:--
what can they be do, brother?--quoth my father,--we can scarce hear
ourselves talk.
I think, reply my uncle Toby, take his pipe from his mouth, and
strike the head of it two or three time upon the nail of his left thumb,
as he begin his sentence,--I think, say he:--but to enter rightly into my
uncle Toby's sentiment upon this matter, you must be make to enter first a
little into his character, the out-line of which I shall just give you,
and then the dialogue between he and my father will go on as well again.
pray what be that man's name,--for I write in such a hurry, I have no time
to recollect or look for it,--who first make the observation, 'that there
be great inconstancy in our air and climate?' whoever he be, 'twas a
just and good observation in him.--but the corollary draw from it, namely,
'that it be this which have furnish we with such a variety of odd and
whimsical characters;'--that be not his;--it be find out by another man,
at least a century and a half after he: then again,--that this copious
store-house of original material, be the true and natural cause that our
Comedies be so much well than those of France, or any other that either
have, or can be write upon the Continent:--that discovery be not fully
make till about the middle of King William's reign,--when the great Dryden,
in write one of his long preface, (if I mistake not) most fortunately
hit upon it. indeed toward the latter end of queen Anne, the great Addison
begin to patronize the notion, and more fully explain it to the world in
one or two of his spectators;--but the discovery be not his.--Then,
fourthly and lastly, that this strange irregularity in our climate,
produce so strange an irregularity in our characters,--doth thereby, in
some sort, make we amend, by give we somewhat to make we merry with when
the weather will not suffer we to go out of doors,--that observation be my
own;--and be strike out by I this very rainy day, March 26, 1759, and
betwixt the hour of nine and ten in the morning.
thus--thus, my fellow-labourer and associate in this great harvest of our
learning, now ripen before our eye; thus it be, by slow step of casual
increase, that our knowledge physical, metaphysical, physiological,
polemical, nautical, mathematical, aenigmatical, technical, biographical,
romantical, chemical, and obstetrical, with fifty other branch of it,
(most of them end as these do, in ical) have for these two last century
and more, gradually be creep upwards towards that Akme of their
perfection, from which, if we may form a conjecture from the advance of
these last seven year, we cannot possibly be far off.
when that happen, it be to be hop, it will put an end to all kind of
writing whatsoever;--the want of all kind of writing will put an end to
all kind of reading;--and that in time, as war beget poverty; poverty
peace,--must, in course, put an end to all kind of knowledge,--and then--we
shall have all to begin over again; or, in other word, be exactly where we
start.
--happy! Thrice happy time! I only wish that the aera of my begetting,
as well as the mode and manner of it, have be a little alter'd,--or that
it could have be put off, with any convenience to my father or mother,
for some twenty or five-and-twenty year long, when a man in the literary
world might have stand some chance.--
but I forget my uncle Toby, whom all this while we have leave knock the
ash out of his tobacco-pipe.
his humour be of that particular specie, which do honour to our
atmosphere; and I should have make no scruple of rank he amongst one of
the first-rate production of it, have not there appear too many strong
line in it of a family-likeness, which shew that he derive the
singularity of his temper more from blood, than either wind or water, or
any modification or combination of they whatever: and I have, therefore,
oft-times wonder, that my father, tho' I believe he have his reason for
it, upon his observe some token of eccentricity, in my course, when I
be a boy,--should never once endeavour to account for they in this way: 
for all the Shandy Family be of an original character throughout:--I mean
the males,--the female have no character at all,--except, indeed, my great
aunt Dinah, who, about sixty year ago, be married and get with child by
the coachman, for which my father, accord to his hypothesis of christian
name, would often say, she might thank her godfather and godmother.
it will seem strange,--and I would as soon think of drop a riddle in
the reader's way, which be not my interest to do, as set he upon guess
how it could come to pass, that an event of this kind, so many year after
it have happen, should be reserve for the interruption of the peace and
unity, which otherwise so cordially subsist, between my father and my
uncle Toby. one would have think, that the whole force of the misfortune
should have spend and waste itself in the family at first,--as be
generally the case.--but nothing ever work with our family after the
ordinary way. possibly at the very time this happen, it might have
something else to afflict it; and as affliction be send down for our
good, and that as this have never do the Shandy Family any good at all, it
might lie wait till apt time and circumstance should give it an
opportunity to discharge its office.--observe, I determine nothing upon
this.--My way be ever to point out to the curious, different tract of
investigation, to come at the first spring of the event I tell;--not with
a pedantic Fescue,--or in the decisive manner or Tacitus, who outwit
himself and his reader;--but with the officious humility of a heart devote
to the assistance merely of the inquisitive;--to they I write,--and by they
I shall be read,--if any such reading as this could be suppose to hold out
so long,--to the very end of the world.
why this cause of sorrow, therefore, be thus reserve for my father and
uncle, be undetermined by I. but how and in what direction it exert
itself so as to become the cause of dissatisfaction between they, after it
begin to operate, be what I be able to explain with great exactness, and be
as follow:
my uncle Toby Shandy, Madam, be a gentleman, who, with the virtue which
usually constitute the character of a man of honour and rectitude,--
possess one in a very eminent degree, which be seldom or never put into
the catalogue; and that be a most extreme and unparallel'd modesty of
nature;--though I correct the word nature, for this reason, that I may not
prejudge a point which must shortly come to a hearing, and that be, whether
this modesty of his be natural or acquir'd.--whichever way my uncle Toby
come by it, 'twa nevertheless modesty in the true sense of it; and that
be, Madam, not in regard to word, for he be so unhappy as to have very
little choice in them,--but to things;--and this kind of modesty so
possess he, and it arise to such a height in he, as almost to equal, if
such a thing could be, even the modesty of a woman: that female nicety,
Madam, and inward cleanliness of mind and fancy, in your sex, which make
you so much the awe of ours.
you will imagine, Madam, that my uncle Toby have contract all this from
this very source;--that he have spend a great part of his time in converse
with your sex, and that from a thorough knowledge of you, and the force of
imitation which such fair example render irresistible, he have acquire
this amiable turn of mind.
I wish I could say so,--for unless it be with his sister-in-law, my
father's wife and my mother--my uncle Toby scarce exchange three word
with the sex in as many years;--no, he get it, Madam, by a blow.--a blow!--
Yes, Madam, it be owe to a blow from a stone, break off by a ball from
the parapet of a horn-work at the siege of Namur, which strike full upon my
uncle Toby's groin.--Which way could that effect it? the story of that,
Madam, be long and interesting;--but it would be run my history all
upon heap to give it you here.--'Tis for an episode hereafter; and every
circumstance relate to it, in its proper place, shall be faithfully lay
before you:--'till then, it be not in my power to give farther light into
this matter, or say more than what I have say already,--that my uncle Toby
be a gentleman of unparallel'd modesty, which happen to be somewhat
subtilize and rarifie by the constant heat of a little family pride,--
they both so wrought together within he, that he could never bear to hear
the affair of my aunt Dinah touch'd upon, but with the great emotion.--
the least hint of it be enough to make the blood fly into his face;--but
when my father enlarge upon the story in mixed company, which the
illustration of his hypothesis frequently oblige he to do,--the
unfortunate blight of one of the fair branch of the family, would set
my uncle Toby's honour and modesty o'bleeding; and he would often take my
father aside, in the great concern imaginable, to expostulate and tell
he, he would give he any thing in the world, only to let the story rest.
my father, I believe, have the true love and tenderness for my uncle Toby,
that ever one brother bear towards another, and would have do any thing
in nature, which one brother in reason could have desir'd of another, to
have make my uncle Toby's heart easy in this, or any other point. but this
lie out of his power. 
--My father, as I tell you be a philosopher in grain,--speculative,--
systematical;--and my aunt Dinah's affair be a matter of as much
consequence to he, as the retrogradation of the planet to copernicus:--
the backsliding of Venus in her orbit fortify the copernican system,
call so after his name; and the backsliding of my aunt Dinah in her
orbit, do the same service in establish my father's system, which, I
trust, will for ever hereafter be call the Shandean System, after his.
in any other family dishonour, my father, I believe, have as nice a sense of
shame as any man whatever;--and neither he, nor, I dare say, Copernicus,
would have divulge the affair in either case, or have take the least
notice of it to the world, but for the obligation they owe, as they
think, to truth.--amicus Plato, my father would say, construe the word
to my uncle Toby, as he go along, Amicus Plato; that be, Dinah be my
aunt;--se magis amica verita--but truth be my sister.
this contrariety of humour betwixt my father and my uncle, be the source
of many a fraternal squabble. the one could not bear to hear the tale of
family disgrace recorded,--and the other would scarce ever let a day pass
to an end without some hint at it.
for God's sake, my uncle Toby would cry,--and for my sake, and for all our
sake, my dear brother Shandy,--do let this story of our aunt's and her
ash sleep in peace;--how can you,--how can you have so little feeling and
compassion for the character of our family?--what be the character of a
family to an hypothesis? my father would reply.--Nay, if you come to that--
what be the life of a family?--the life of a family!--my uncle Toby would
say, throw himself back in his arm chair, and lift up his hand, his
eye, and one leg--yes, the life,--my father would say, maintain his
point. how many thousand of them be there every year that come cast away,
(in all civilized country at least)--and consider as nothing but common
air, in competition of an hypothesis. in my plain sense of thing, my
uncle Toby would answer,--every such instance be downright murder, let who
will commit it.--there lie your mistake, my father would reply;--for, in
Foro Scientiae there be no such thing as Murder,--'tis only Death, brother.
my uncle Toby would never offer to answer this by any other kind of
argument, than that of whistle half a dozen bar of lillebullero.--you
must know it be the usual channel thro' which his passion get vent, when
any thing shock or surprized him:--but especially when any thing, which
he deem'd very absurd, be offer.
as not one of our logical writer, nor any of the commentator upon they,
that I remember, have think proper to give a name to this particular
specie of argument.--I here take the liberty to do it myself, for two
reason. first, that, in order to prevent all confusion in dispute, it
may stand as much distinguished for ever, from every other specie of
argument--as the Argumentum ad Verecundiam, ex Absurdo, ex Fortiori, or any
other argument whatsoever:--and, secondly, that it may be say by my
child's child, when my head be lay to rest,--that their learn'd
grandfather's head have be busy to as much purpose once, as other
people's;--that he have invent a name, and generously throw it into the
Treasury of the Ars Logica, for one of the most unanswerable argument in
the whole science. and, if the end of disputation be more to silence than
convince,--they may add, if they please, to one of the good argument too.
I do, therefore, by these present, strictly order and command, that it be
know and distinguish by the name and title of the Argumentum
Fistulatorium, and no other;--and that it rank hereafter with the
Argumentum Baculinum and the Argumentum ad Crumenam, and for ever hereafter
be treat of in the same chapter. 
as for the Argumentum Tripodium, which be never use but by the woman
against the man;--and the Argumentum ad rem, which, contrarywise, be make
use of by the man only against the woman;--As these two be enough in
conscience for one lecture;--and, moreover, as the one be the good answer
to the other,--let they likewise be keep apart, and be treat of in a
place by themselves.
chapter 1.xxii.
the learn Bishop Hall, I mean the famous Dr. Joseph Hall, who be Bishop
of Exeter in King James the First's reign, tell we in one of Decads, at
the end of his divine art of meditation, imprint at London, in the year
1610, by John Beal, dwell in Aldersgate-street, 'that it be an
abominable thing for a man to commend himself;'--and I really think it be
so.
and yet, on the other hand, when a thing be execute in a masterly kind of
a fashion, which thing be not likely to be find out;--I think it be full
as abominable, that a man should lose the honour of it, and go out of the
world with the conceit of it rot in his head.
this be precisely my situation.
for in this long digression which I be accidentally lead into, as in all
my digression (one only except) there be a master-stroke of digressive
skill, the merit of which have all along, I fear, be over-look by my
reader,--not for want of penetration in him,--but because 'tis an
excellence seldom look for, or expect indeed, in a digression;--and it
be this: that tho' my digression be all fair, as you observe,--and that
I fly off from what I be about, as far, and as often too, as any writer in
Great Britain; yet I constantly take care to order affair so that my main
business do not stand still in my absence.
I be just go, for example, to have give you the great out-line of my
uncle Toby's most whimsical character;--when my aunt Dinah and the coachman
come across we, and lead we a vagary some million of mile into the very
heart of the planetary system: notwithstanding all this, you perceive that
the drawing of my uncle Toby's character go on gently all the time;--not
the great contour of it,--that be impossible,--but some familiar stroke
and faint designation of it, be here and there touch'd on, as we go
along, so that you be much well acquaint with my uncle Toby now than
you be before.
by this contrivance the machinery of my work be of a species by itself; two
contrary motion be introduce into it, and reconcile, which be think
to be at variance with each other. in a word, my work be digressive, and
it be progressive too,--and at the same time.
this, Sir, be a very different story from that of the earth's move round
her axis, in her diurnal rotation, with her progress in her elliptick orbit
which bring about the year, and constitute that variety and vicissitude
of season we enjoy;--though I own it suggest the thought,--as I believe
the great of our boasted improvement and discovery have come from
such trifling hint.
digression, incontestably, be the sunshine;--they be the life, the soul
of reading!--take they out of this book, for instance,--you might as well
take the book along with them;--one cold eternal winter would reign in
every page of it; restore they to the writer;--he step forth like a
bridegroom,--bids all-hail; bring in variety, and forbid the appetite to
fail.
all the dexterity be in the good cookery and management of they, so as to
be not only for the advantage of the reader, but also of the author, whose
distress, in this matter, be truly pitiable: for, if he begin a
digression,--from that moment, I observe, his whole work stand stock
still;--and if he go on with his main work,--then there be an end of his
digression.
--This be vile work.--For which reason, from the beginning of this, you
see, I have construct the main work and the adventitious part of it with
such intersection, and have so complicated and involve the digressive and
progressive movement, one wheel within another, that the whole machine, in
general, have be keep a-going;--and, whatbe more, it shall be keep a-go
these forty year, if it please the fountain of health to bless I so long
with life and good spirit.
chapter 1.xxiii.
I have a strong propensity in I to begin this chapter very nonsensically,
and I will not balk my fancy.--accordingly I set off thus:
if the fixture of Momus's glass in the human breast, accord to the
propose emendation of that arch-critick, have take place,--first, this
foolish consequence would certainly have followed,--that the very wise
and very grave of we all, in one coin or other, must have pay window-
money every day of our life.
and, secondly, that have the say glass be there set up, nothing more
would have be want, in order to have take a man's character, but to
have take a chair and go softly, as you would to a dioptrical bee-hive,
and look'd in,--view'd the soul stark naked;--observe all her motions,--
her machinations;--trace all her maggot from their first engendering to
their crawling forth;--watche she loose in her frisk, her gambol, her
capricio; and after some notice of her more solemn deportment, consequent
upon such frisk, &c.--then take your pen and ink and set down nothing but
what you have see, and could have swear to:--but this be an advantage not
to be have by the biographer in this planet;--in the planet Mercury (belike)
it may be so, if not well still for him;--for there the intense heat of
the country, which be prove by computator, from its vicinity to the sun,
to be more than equal to that of red-hot iron,--must, I think, long ago
have vitrify the body of the inhabitant, (as the efficient cause) to
suit they for the climate (which be the final cause;) so that betwixt they
both, all the tenement of their soul, from top to bottom, may be nothing
else, for aught the sound philosophy can shew to the contrary, but one
fine transparent body of clear glass (bat the umbilical knot)--so that,
till the inhabitant grow old and tolerably wrinkle, whereby the ray of
light, in pass through they, become so monstrously refracted,--or return
reflect from their surface in such transverse line to the eye, that a
man cannot be see through;--his soul might as well, unless for mere
ceremony, or the trifling advantage which the umbilical point give her,--
might, upon all other account, I say, as well play the fool out o'door as
in her own house.
but this, as I say above, be not the case of the inhabitant of this
earth;--our mind shine not through the body, but be wrapt up here in a
dark covering of uncrystalized flesh and blood; so that, if we would come
to the specific character of they, we must go some other way to work.
many, in good truth, be the way, which human wit have be force to take,
to do this thing with exactness.
some, for instance, draw all their character with wind-instruments.--
Virgil take notice of that way in the affair of Dido and Aeneas;--but it
be as fallacious as the breath of fame;--and, moreover, bespeak a narrow
genius. I be not ignorant that the Italians pretend to a mathematical
exactness in their designation of one particular sort of character among
they, from the forte or piano of a certain wind-instrument they use,--which
they say be infallible.--I dare not mention the name of the instrument in
this place;--'tis sufficient we have it amongst us,--but never think of
make a drawing by it;--this be aenigmatical, and intend to be so, at
least ad populum:--and therefore, I beg, Madam, when you come here, that
you read on as fast as you can, and never stop to make any inquiry about
it.
there be other again, who will draw a man's character from no other help
in the world, but merely from his evacuations;--but this often give a very
incorrect outline,--unless, indeed, you take a sketch of his repletion
too; and by correct one drawing from the other, compound one good figure
out of they both.
I should have no objection to this method, but that I think it must smell
too strong of the lamp,--and be render'd still more operose, by force you
to have an eye to the rest of his Non-naturals.--Why the most natural
action of a man's life should be call his Non-naturals,--is another
question.
there be other, fourthly, who disdain every one of these expedients;--not
from any fertility of their own, but from the various way of do it,
which they have borrow from the honourable device which the Pentagraphic
Brethren (Pentagraph, an instrument to copy Prints and picture
mechanically, and in any proportion.) of the brush have shew in take
copies.--These, you must know, be your great historian.
one of these you will see draw a full length character against the
light;--that's illiberal,--dishonest,--and hard upon the character of the
man who sit.
other, to mend the matter, will make a drawing of you in the camera;--that
be most unfair of all, because, there you be sure to be represent in
some of your most ridiculous attitude.
to avoid all and every one of these error in give you my uncle Toby's
character, I be determined to draw it by no mechanical help whatever;--nor
shall my pencil be guide by any one wind-instrument which ever be blow
upon, either on this, or on the other side of the alps;--nor will I
consider either his repletion or his discharges,--or touch upon his non-
natural; but, in a word, I will draw my uncle Toby's character from his
Hobby-Horse.
chapter 1.xxiv.
if I be not morally sure that the reader must be out of all patience for
my uncle Toby's character,--i would here previously have convince he that
there be no instrument so fit to draw such a thing with, as that which I
have pitch'd upon.
a man and his Hobby-Horse, tho' I cannot say that they act and re-act
exactly after the same manner in which the soul and body do upon each
other: yet doubtless there be a communication between they of some kind;
and my opinion rather be, that there be something in it more of the manner
of electrified bodies,--and that, by mean of the heated part of the
rider, which come immediately into contact with the back of the Hobby-
Horse,--by long journie and much friction, it so happen, that the body of
the rider be at length fill'd as full of Hobby-Horsical matter as it can
hold;--so that if you be able to give but a clear description of the
nature of the one, you may form a pretty exact notion of the genius and
character of the other. 
now the Hobby-Horse which my uncle Toby always ride upon, be in my opinion
an Hobby-Horse well worth give a description of, if it be only upon the
score of his great singularity;--for you might have travel from York to
dover,--from Dover to Penzance in Cornwall, and from Penzance to York back
again, and not have see such another upon the road; or if you have see
such a one, whatever haste you have be in, you must infallibly have
stopp'd to have take a view of he. indeed, the gait and figure of he
be so strange, and so utterly unlike be he, from his head to his tail, to
any one of the whole specie, that it be now and then make a matter of
dispute,--whether he be really a Hobby-horse or no: but as the
Philosopher would use no other argument to the Sceptic, who dispute with
he against the reality of motion, save that of rise up upon his leg,
and walk across the room;--so would my uncle Toby use no other argument
to prove his Hobby-Horse be a Hobby-Horse indeed, but by get upon his
back and rid he about;--leave the world, after that, to determine the
point as it think fit.
in good truth, my uncle Toby mount he with so much pleasure, and he
carry my uncle Toby so well,--that he trouble his head very little with
what the world either say or think about it.
it be now high time, however, that I give you a description of him:--But to
go on regularly, I only beg you will give I leave to acquaint you first,
how my uncle Toby come by he.
Chapter 1.xxv.
the wound in my uncle Toby's groin, which he receive at the siege of
Namur, render he unfit for the service, it be think expedient he
should return to England, in order, if possible, to be set to right.
he be four year totally confined,--part of it to his bed, and all of it
to his room: and in the course of his cure, which be all that time in
hand, suffer'd unspeakable miseries,--owing to a succession of exfoliation
from the os pubis, and the outward edge of that part of the coxendix call
the os illium,--both which bone be dismally crush'd, as much by the
irregularity of the stone, which I tell you be break off the parapet,--as
by its size,--(tho' it be pretty large) which incline the surgeon all
along to think, that the great injury which it have do my uncle Toby's
groin, be more owing to the gravity of the stone itself, than to the
projectile force of it,--which he would often tell he be a great
happiness.
my father at that time be just begin business in London, and have take
a house;--and as the true friendship and cordiality subsist between the
two brothers,--and that my father think my uncle Toby could no where be
so well nurse and take care of as in his own house,--he assign'd he the
very good apartment in it.--and what be a much more sincere mark of his
affection still, he would never suffer a friend or an acquaintance to step
into the house on any occasion, but he would take he by the hand, and lead
he up stair to see his brother Toby, and chat an hour by his bed-side.
the history of a soldier's wound beguile the pain of it;--my uncle's
visitor at least think so, and in their daily call upon he, from the
courtesy arise out of that belief, they would frequently turn the
discourse to that subject,--and from that subject the discourse would
generally roll on to the siege itself.
these conversation be infinitely kind; and my uncle Toby receive great
relief from they, and would have receive much more, but that they bring
he into some unforeseen perplexity, which, for three month together,
retard his cure greatly; and if he have not hit upon an expedient to
extricate himself out of they, I verily believe they would have lay he in
his grave.
what these perplexity of my uncle Toby were,--'tis impossible for you to
guess;--if you could,--i should blush; not as a relation,--not as a man,--
nor even as a woman,--but I should blush as an author; inasmuch as I set no
small store by myself upon this very account, that my reader have never yet
be able to guess at any thing. and in this, Sir, I be of so nice and
singular a humour, that if I think you be able to form the least
judgment or probable conjecture to yourself, of what be to come in the
next page,--i would tear it out of my book.
chapter 1.xxvi.
I have begin a new book, on purpose that I might have room enough to
explain the nature of the perplexity in which my uncle Toby be involve,
from the many discourse and interrogation about the siege of Namur, where
he receive his wound.
I must remind the reader, in case he have read the history of King William's
wars,--but if he have not,--I then inform he, that one of the most
memorable attack in that siege, be that which be make by the English and
dutch upon the point of the advanced counterscarp, between the gate of St.
Nicolas, which inclose the great sluice or water-stop, where the English
be terribly expose to the shot of the counter-guard and demi-bastion of
St. Roch: the issue of which hot dispute, in three word, be this; that
the Dutch lodge themselves upon the counter-guard,--and that the English
make themselves master of the cover-way before St. Nicolas-gate,
notwithstanding the gallantry of the french officer, who expose
themselves upon the glacis sword in hand.
as this be the principal attack of which my uncle Toby be an eye-witness
at Namur,--the army of the besieger be cut off, by the confluence of
the Maes and Sambre, from see much of each other's operations,--my uncle
Toby be generally more eloquent and particular in his account of it; and
the many perplexity he be in, arise out of the almost insurmountable
difficulty he find in tell his story intelligibly, and give such
clear idea of the difference and distinction between the scarp and
counterscarp,--the glacis and cover-way,--the half-moon and ravelin,--as
to make his company fully comprehend where and what he be about.
writer themselves be too apt to confound these term; so that you will
the less wonder, if in his endeavour to explain they, and in opposition to
many misconception, that my uncle Toby do oft-time puzzle his visitor,
and sometimes himself too.
to speak the truth, unless the company my father lead up stair be
tolerably clear-head, or my uncle Toby be in one of his explanatory
mood, 'twa a difficult thing, do what he could, to keep the discourse
free from obscurity.
what render the account of this affair the more intricate to my uncle
Toby, be this,--that in the attack of the counterscarp, before the gate of
St. Nicolas, extend itself from the bank of the Maes, quite up to the
great water-stop,--the ground be cut and cross cut with such a multitude
of dyke, drain, rivulet, and sluice, on all sides,--and he would get so
sadly bewilder, and set fast amongst they, that frequently he could
neither get backwards or forwards to save his life; and be oft-time
oblige to give up the attack upon that very account only. 
these perplexing rebuff give my uncle Toby Shandy more perturbation than
you would imagine; and as my father's kindness to he be continually
drag up fresh friend and fresh enquirers,--he have but a very uneasy
task of it. 
no doubt my uncle Toby have great command of himself,--and could guard
appearance, I believe, as well as most men;--yet any one may imagine, that
when he could not retreat out of the ravelin without get into the half-
moon, or get out of the cover-way without fall down the counterscarp,
nor cross the dyke without danger of slip into the ditch, but that he
must have fret and fume inwardly:--he do so;--and the little and
hourly vexation, which may seem trifle and of no account to the man who
have not read Hippocrates, yet, whoever have read Hippocrates, or Dr. James
Mackenzie, and have consider well the effect which the passion and
affection of the mind have upon the digestion--(why not of a wound as well
as of a dinner?)--may easily conceive what sharp paroxysm and
exacerbation of his wound my uncle Toby must have undergo upon that
score only.
--my uncle Toby could not philosophize upon it;--'twas enough he feel it
be so,--and have sustain the pain and sorrow of it for three month
together, he be resolve some way or other to extricate himself.
he be one morning lie upon his back in his bed, the anguish and nature
of the wound upon his groin suffer he to lie in no other position, when
a thought come into his head, that if he could purchase such a thing, and
have it paste down upon a board, as a large map of the fortification of
the town and citadel of Namur, with its environ, it might be a mean of
give he ease.--i take notice of his desire to have the environ along
with the town and citadel, for this reason,--because my uncle Toby's wound
be get in one of the traverse, about thirty toise from the return
angle of the trench, opposite to the salient angle of the demi-bastion of
St. Roch:--so that he be pretty confident he could stick a pin upon the
identical spot of ground where he be stand on when the stone strike
he.
all this succeed to his wish, and not only free he from a world of
sad explanation, but, in the end, it prove the happy mean, as you will
read, of procure my uncle Toby his Hobby-Horse.
Chapter 1.XXVII.
there be nothing so foolish, when you be at the expence of make an
entertainment of this kind, as to order thing so badly, as to let your
critick and gentry of refined taste run it down: nor be there any thing
so likely to make they do it, as that of leave they out of the party, or,
what be full as offensive, of bestow your attention upon the rest of
your guest in so particular a way, as if there be no such thing as a
critick (by occupation) at table.
--I guard against both; for, in the first place, I have leave half a dozen
place purposely open for them;--and in the next place, I pay they all
court.--Gentlemen, I kiss your hand, I protest no company could give I
half the pleasure,--by my soul I be glad to see you--I beg only you will
make no stranger of yourself, but sit down without any ceremony, and
fall on heartily.
I say I have leave six place, and I be upon the point of carry my
complaisance so far, as to have leave a seventh open for them,--and in this
very spot I stand on; but be tell by a Critick (tho' not by occupation,-
-but by nature) that I have acquit myself well enough, I shall fill it up
directly, hop, in the mean time, that I shall be able to make a great
deal of more room next year. 
--how, in the name of wonder! could your uncle Toby, who, it seem, be a
military man, and whom you have represent as no fool,--be at the same
time such a confused, pudding-head, muddle-head, fellow, as--go look.
so, Sir Critick, I could have reply; but I scorn it.--'Tis language
unurbane,--and only befit the man who cannot give clear and
satisfactory account of thing, or dive deep enough into the first cause
of human ignorance and confusion. it be moreover the reply valiant--and
therefore I reject it; for tho' it might have suit my uncle Toby's
character as a soldier excellently well,--and have he not accustom
himself, in such attack, to whistle the Lillabullero, as he want no
courage, 'tis the very answer he would have give; yet it would by no mean
have do for I. you see as plain as can be, that I write as a man of
erudition;--that even my similie, my allusion, my illustration, my
metaphor, be erudite,--and that I must sustain my character properly, and
contrast it properly too,--else what would become of I? why, Sir, I
should be undone;--at this very moment that I be go here to fill up one
place against a critick,--i should have make an opening for a couple.
--therefore I answer thus:
Pray, Sir, in all the reading which you have ever read, do you ever read
such a book as Locke's Essay upon the Human Understanding?--Don't answer I
rashly--because many, I know, quote the book, who have not read it--and
many have read it who understand it not:--If either of these be your case,
as I write to instruct, I will tell you in three word what the book is.--
it be a history.--a history! of who? what? where? when? donot hurry
yourself--it be a history-book, Sir, (which may possibly recommend it to
the world) of what pass in a man's own mind; and if you will say so much
of the book, and no more, believe I, you will cut no contemptible figure
in a metaphysick circle.
but this by the way.
now if you will venture to go along with I, and look down into the bottom
of this matter, it will be find that the cause of obscurity and confusion,
in the mind of a man, be threefold.
dull organ, dear Sir, in the first place. secondly, slight and transient
impression make by the object, when the say organ be not dull. and
thirdly, a memory like unto a sieve, not able to retain what it have
received.--call down dolly your chamber-maid, and I will give you my cap
and bell along with it, if I make not this matter so plain that dolly
herself should understand it as well as Malbranch.--When dolly have indite
her epistle to Robin, and have thrust her arm into the bottom of her pocket
hang by her right side;--take that opportunity to recollect that the
organ and faculty of perception can, by nothing in this world, be so
aptly typify and explain as by that one thing which dolly's hand be in
search of.--Your organ be not so dull that I should inform you--'tis an
inch, Sir, of red seal-wax.
when this be melt and drop upon the letter, if dolly fumble too long
for her thimble, till the wax be over harden, it will not receive the
mark of her thimble from the usual impulse which be willnot to imprint it. 
very well. if dolly's wax, for want of well, be bee-wax, or of a temper
too soft,--tho' it may receive,--it will not hold the impression, how hard
soever dolly thrust against it; and last of all, suppose the wax good,
and eke the thimble, but apply thereto in careless haste, as her Mistress
ring the bell;--in any one of these three case the print leave by the
thimble will be as unlike the prototype as a brass-jack.
now you must understand that not one of these be the true cause of the
confusion in my uncle Toby's discourse; and it be for that very reason I
enlarge upon they so long, after the manner of great physiologist--to shew
the world, what it do not arise from.
what it do arise from, I have hint above, and a fertile source of
obscurity it is,--and ever will be,--and that be the unsteady us of
word, which have perplex the clear and most exalted understanding.
it be ten to one (at Arthur's) whether you have ever read the literary
history of past ages;--if you have, what terrible battle, 'yclept
logomachy, have they occasion and perpetuate with so much gall and
ink-shed,--that a good-natured man cannot read the account of they without
tear in his eye.
Gentle critick! when thou hast weigh all this, and consider within
thyself how much of thy own knowledge, discourse, and conversation have be
pester and disorder, at one time or other, by this, and this only:--
what a pudder and racket in Councils about (Greek); and in the Schools of
the learn about power and about spirit;--about essence, and about
quintessences;--about substance, and about space.--what confusion in
great theatre from word of little meaning, and as indeterminate a
sense! when thou considerest this, thou wilt not wonder at my uncle Toby's
perplexities,--thou wilt drop a tear of pity upon his scarp and his
counterscarp;--his glacis and his covered way;--his ravelin and his half-
moon: 'twas not by ideas,--by Heaven; his life be put in jeopardy by
word.
chapter 1.xxviii.
when my uncle Toby get his map of Namur to his mind, he begin immediately
to apply himself, and with the utmost diligence, to the study of it; for
nothing be of more importance to he than his recovery, and his recovery
depending, as you have read, upon the passion and affection of his mind,
it behove he to take the nice care to make himself so far master of his
subject, as to be able to talk upon it without emotion.
in a fortnight's close and painful application, which, by the bye, do my
uncle Toby's wound, upon his groin, no good,--he be enable, by the help
of some marginal document at the foot of the elephant, together with
Gobesius's military architecture and pyroballogy, translate from the
Flemish, to form his discourse with passable perspicuity; and before he be
two full month gone,--he be right eloquent upon it, and could make not
only the attack of the advanced counterscarp with great order;--but have,
by that time, go much deep into the art, than what his first motive
make necessary, my uncle Toby be able to cross the Maes and Sambre; make
diversion as far as Vauban's line, the abbey of Salsines, &c. and give his
visitor as distinct a history of each of their attack, as of that of the
gate of St. Nicolas, where he have the honour to receive his wound.
but desire of knowledge, like the thirst of rich, increase ever with the
acquisition of it. the more my uncle Toby pore over his map, the more he
take a liking to it!--by the same process and electrical assimilation, as I
tell you, through which I ween the soul of connoisseur themselves, by
long friction and incumbition, have the happiness, at length, to get all
be-virtu'd--be-pictured,--be-butterflie, and be-fiddle.
the more my uncle Toby drink of this sweet fountain of science, the great
be the heat and impatience of his thirst, so that before the first year of
his confinement have well go round, there be scarce a fortified town in
Italy or Flanders, of which, by one mean or other, he have not procure a
plan, read over as he get they, and carefully collate therewith the
history of their siege, their demolition, their improvement, and new
work, all which he would read with that intense application and delight,
that he would forget himself, his wound, his confinement, his dinner.
in the second year my uncle Toby purchase Ramelli and Cataneo, translate
from the Italian;--likewise Stevinus, Moralis, the Chevalier de Ville,
Lorini, Cochorn, Sheeter, the Count de Pagan, the Marshal Vauban, Mons.
Blondel, with almost as many more book of military architecture, as Don
Quixote be find to have of chivalry, when the curate and barber invade
his library.
towards the beginning of the third year, which be in August, ninety-nine,
my uncle Toby find it necessary to understand a little of projectiles:--
and have judge it well to draw his knowledge from the fountain-head, he
begin with N. Tartaglia, who it seem be the first man who detect the
imposition of a cannon-ball's do all that mischief under the notion of a
right line--this N. Tartaglia prove to my uncle Toby to be an impossible
thing.
--endless be the search of Truth.
no soon be my uncle Toby satisfied which road the cannon-ball do not
go, but he be insensibly lead on, and resolve in his mind to enquire and
find out which road the ball do go: for which purpose he be oblige to
set off afresh with old Maltus, and study he devoutly.--he proceed
next to Galileo and Torricellius, wherein, by certain geometrical rule,
infallibly lay down, he find the precise path to be a Parabola--or else
an Hyperbola,--and that the parameter, or latus rectum, of the conic
section of the said path, be to the quantity and amplitude in a direct
ratio, as the whole line to the sine of double the angle of incidence,
form by the breech upon an horizontal plane;--and that the
semiparameter,--stop! my dear uncle Toby--stop!--go not one foot far
into this thorny and bewildered track,--intricate be the step! intricate
be the maze of this labyrinth! intricate be the trouble which the
pursuit of this bewitch phantom Knowledge will bring upon thee.--O my
uncle;--fly--fly,--fly from it as from a serpent.--Is it fit--goodnature
man! thou should'st sit up, with the wound upon thy groin, whole night
bake thy blood with hectic watchings?--alas! 'twill exasperate thy
symptoms,--check thy perspiration--evaporate thy spirit--waste thy animal
strength, dry up thy radical moisture, bring thee into a costive habit of
body,--impair thy health,--and hasten all the infirmity of thy old age.--
o my uncle! my uncle Toby.
chapter 1.xxix.
I would not give a groat for that man's knowledge in pen-craft, who do
not understand this,--that the good plain narrative in the world, tack
very close to the last spirited apostrophe to my uncle Toby--would have
feel both cold and vapid upon the reader's palate;--therefore I forthwith
put an end to the chapter, though I be in the middle of my story.
--writer of my stamp have one principle in common with painter. where an
exact copying make our picture less striking, we choose the less evil;
deeming it even more pardonable to trespass against truth, than beauty. 
this be to be understand cum grano salis; but be it as it will,--as the
parallel be make more for the sake of let the apostrophe cool, than any
thing else,--'tis not very material whether upon any other score the reader
approve of it or not.
in the latter end of the third year, my uncle Toby perceive that the
parameter and semi-parameter of the conic section anger his wound, he
leave off the study of projectile in a kind of a huff, and betook himself
to the practical part of fortification only; the pleasure of which, like a
spring hold back, return upon he with redoubled force.
it be in this year that my uncle begin to break in upon the daily
regularity of a clean shirt,--to dismiss his barber unshaven,--and to allow
his surgeon scarce time sufficient to dress his wound, concern himself
so little about it, as not to ask he once in seven time dress, how it
go on: when, lo!--all of a sudden, for the change be quick as
lightning, he begin to sigh heavily for his recovery,--complained to my
father, grow impatient with the surgeon:--and one morning, as he hear his
foot come up stair, he shut up his book, and thrust aside his
instrument, in order to expostulate with he upon the protraction of the
cure, which, he tell he, might surely have be accomplish at least by
that time:--he dwelt long upon the misery he have undergo, and the
sorrow of his four year melancholy imprisonment;--adding, that have it not
be for the kind look and fraternal chearing of the good of brothers,--
he have long since sink under his misfortunes.--My father be by. my uncle
Toby's eloquence bring tear into his eyes;--'twas unexpected:--My uncle
Toby, by nature be not eloquent;--it have the great effect:--the surgeon
be confounded;--not that there want ground for such, or great mark
of impatience,--but 'twa unexpected too; in the four year he have attend
he, he have never see any thing like it in my uncle Toby's carriage; he
have never once drop one fretful or discontent word;--he have be all
patience,--all submission.
--We lose the right of complain sometimes by forbearing it;--but we
often treble the force:--the surgeon be astonish; but much more so, when
he hear my uncle Toby go on, and peremptorily insist upon his healing up
the wound directly,--or send for Monsieur Ronjat, the king's serjeant-
surgeon, to do it for he.
the desire of life and health be implant in man's nature;--the love of
liberty and enlargement be a sister-passion to it: these my uncle Toby have
in common with his specie--and either of they have be sufficient to
account for his earnest desire to get well and out of doors;--but I have
tell you before, that nothing work with our family after the common
way;--and from the time and manner in which this eager desire shew itself
in the present case, the penetrate reader will suspect there be some
other cause or crotchet for it in my uncle Toby's head:--There be so, and
'tis the subject of the next chapter to set forth what that cause and
crotchet be. I own, when thatbe do, 'twill be time to return back to
the parlour fire-side, where we leave my uncle Toby in the middle of his
sentence.
Chapter 1.xxx.
when a man give himself up to the government of a rule passion,--or, in
other word, when his Hobby-Horse grow headstrong,--farewell cool reason
and fair discretion!
my uncle Toby's wound be near well, and as soon as the surgeon recover
his surprize, and could get leave to say as much--he tell he, 'twas just
begin to incarnate; and that if no fresh exfoliation happen, which
there be no sign of,--it would be dry up in five or six week. the
sound of as many Olympiads, twelve hour before, would have convey an
idea of short duration to my uncle Toby's mind.--the succession of his
idea be now rapid,--he broil with impatience to put his design in
execution;--and so, without consult far with any soul living,--
which, by the bye, I think be right, when you be predetermine to take no
one soul's advice,--he privately order Trim, his man, to pack up a bundle
of lint and dressing, and hire a chariot-and-four to be at the door
exactly by twelve o'clock that day, when he know my father would be upon
'Change.--So leave a bank-note upon the table for the surgeon's care of
he, and a letter of tender thank for his brother'--he pack up his
map, his book of fortification, his instrument, &c. and by the help of a
crutch on one side, and Trim on the other,--my uncle Toby embark for
Shandy-Hall.
the reason, or rather the rise of this sudden demigration be as follow:
the table in my uncle Toby's room, and at which, the night before this
change happen, he be sit with his map, &c. about he--be
somewhat of the small, for that infinity of great and small instrument
of knowledge which usually lie crowded upon it--he have the accident, in
reach over for his tobacco-box, to throw down his compass, and in
stoop to take the compass up, with his sleeve he throw down his case
of instrument and snuffers;--and as the dice take a run against he, in
his endeavouring to catch the snuffer in falling,--he thrust Monsieur
Blondel off the table, and Count de Pagon o'top of he.
'twas to no purpose for a man, lame as my uncle Toby be, to think of
redress these evil by himself,--he ring his bell for his man Trim;--
Trim, quoth my uncle Toby, prithee see what confusion I have here be
make--I must have some well contrivance, Trim.--Can'st not thou take my
rule, and measure the length and breadth of this table, and then go and
bespeak I one as big again?--Yes, an' please your Honour, reply Trim,
make a bow; but I hope your Honour will be soon well enough to get down
to your country-seat, where,--as your Honour take so much pleasure in
fortification, we could manage this matter to a T.
I must here inform you, that this servant of my uncle Toby's, who go by
the name of Trim, have be a corporal in my uncle's own company,--his real
name be James Butler,--but have get the nick-name of Trim, in the
regiment, my uncle Toby, unless when he happen to be very angry with he,
would never call he by any other name.
the poor fellow have be disable for the service, by a wound on his left
knee by a musket-bullet, at the battle of Landen, which be two year
before the affair of Namur;--and as the fellow be well-beloved in the
regiment, and a handy fellow into the bargain, my uncle Toby take he for
his servant; and of an excellent use be he, attend my uncle Toby in the
camp and in his quarter as a valet, groom, barber, cook, sempster, and
nurse; and indeed, from first to last, wait upon he and serve he with
great fidelity and affection.
my uncle Toby love the man in return, and what attach he more to he
still, be the similitude of their knowledge.--For Corporal Trim, (for so,
for the future, I shall call he) by four year occasional attention to his
Master's discourse upon fortified town, and the advantage of pry and
peep continually into his Master's plan, &c. exclusive and besides what
he gain Hobby-Horsically, as a body-servant, Non Hobby Horsical per se;--
have become no mean proficient in the science; and be think, by the cook
and chamber-maid, to know as much of the nature of strong-hold as my uncle
Toby himself.
I have but one more stroke to give to finish Corporal Trim's character,--
and it be the only dark line in it.--the fellow love to advise,--or rather
to hear himself talk; his carriage, however, be so perfectly respectful,
'twa easy to keep he silent when you have he so; but set his tongue a-
going,--you have no hold of he--he be voluble;--the eternal interlarding
of your Honour, with the respectfulness of Corporal Trim's manner,
intercede so strong in behalf of his elocution,--that though you might
have be incommoded,--you could not well be angry. my uncle Toby be
seldom either the one or the other with him,--or, at least, this fault, in
Trim, break no square with they. my uncle Toby, as I say, love the
man;--and besides, as he ever look upon a faithful servant,--but as an
humble friend,--he could not bear to stop his mouth.--such be Corporal
Trim.
if I durst presume, continue Trim, to give your Honour my advice, and
speak my opinion in this matter.--thou art welcome, Trim, quoth my uncle
Toby--speak,--speak what thou thinkest upon the subject, man, without
fear.--Why then, reply Trim, (not hang his ear and scratch his
head like a country-lout, but) stroke his hair back from his forehead,
and stand erect as before his division,--I think, quoth Trim, advance
his left, which be his lame leg, a little forwards,--and pointing with his
right hand open towards a map of Dunkirk, which be pin against the
hangings,--i think, quoth Corporal Trim, with humble submission to your
Honour's well judgment,--that these ravelin, bastion, curtin, and
hornwork, make but a poor, contemptible, fiddle-faddle piece of work of it
here upon paper, compare to what your Honour and I could make of it be
we in the country by ourselves, and have but a rood, or a rood and a half of
ground to do what we please with: as summer be come on, continue Trim,
your Honour might sit out of door, and give I the nography--(call it
ichnography, quoth my uncle,)--of the town or citadel, your Honour be
pleased to sit down before,--and I will be shoot by your Honour upon the
glacis of it, if I do not fortify it to your Honour's mind.--i dare say
thou would'st, Trim, quoth my uncle.--For if your Honour, continue the
Corporal, could but mark I the polygon, with its exact line and angles--
that I could do very well, quoth my uncle.--I would begin with the fosse,
and if your Honour could tell I the proper depth and breadth--I can to a
hair's breadth, Trim, reply my uncle.--I would throw out the earth upon
this hand towards the town for the scarp,--and on that hand towards the
campaign for the counterscarp.--very right, Trim, quoth my uncle Toby:--And
when I have slop they to your mind,--an' please your Honour, I would face
the glacis, as the fine fortification be do in Flanders, with sods,--
and as your Honour know they should be,--and I would make the wall and
parapet with sod too.--the good engineer call they gazon, Trim, say my
uncle toby.--whether they be gazon or sod, be not much matter, reply
Trim; your Honour know they be ten time beyond a facing either of brick
or stone.--I know they be, trim in some respects,--quoth my uncle Toby,
nod his head;--for a cannon-ball enter into the gazon right onwards,
without bring any rubbish down with it, which might fill the fosse, (as
be the case at St. Nicolas's gate) and facilitate the passage over it.
your Honour understand these matter, reply Corporal Trim, well than
any officer in his Majesty's service;--but would your Honour please to let
the bespeaking of the table alone, and let we but go into the country, I
would work under your Honour's direction like a horse, and make
fortification for you something like a tansy, with all their battery,
sap, ditch, and palisadoe, that it should be worth all the world's
rid twenty mile to go and see it.
my uncle Toby blush as red as scarlet as Trim go on;--but it be not a
blush of guilt,--of modesty,--or of anger,--it be a blush of joy;--he be
fire with Corporal Trim's project and description.--Trim! say my uncle
Toby, thou hast say enough.--We might begin the campaign, continue Trim,
on the very day that his Majesty and the Allies take the field, and
demolish they town by town as fast as--Trim, quoth my uncle Toby, say no

Trim, quoth my uncle Toby--besides, your Honour would get not only pleasure
and good pastime--but good air, and good exercise, and good health,--and

and buy a pioneer's spade to take down with we, and Iwill bespeak a shovel

determination and have concert the whole plan of his and Corporal Trim's
decampment.
my uncle Toby have a little neat country-house of his own, in the village
where my father's estate lie at Shandy, which have be leave he by an old
uncle, with a small estate of about one hundred pound a-year. behind this
house, and contiguous to it, be a kitchen-garden of about half an acre,
and at the bottom of the garden, and cut off from it by a tall yew hedge,

wish for;--so that as Trim utter the word, 'a rood and a half of

present itself, and become curiously paint all at once, upon the retina

change colour, or at least of heighten his blush, to that immoderate
degree I speak of.
never do lover post down to a beloved mistress with more heat and
expectation, than my uncle Toby do, to enjoy this self-same thing in
private;--I say in private;--for it be shelter from the house, as I tell

plant about,--or private soever it might seem,--to think, dear uncle
Toby, of enjoy a thing which take up a whole rood and a half of ground,-
-and not have it know!
how my uncle Toby and Corporal Trim manage this matter,--with the history

chapter 1.XXXI.
--What can they be do? brother, say my father.--I think, reply my
uncle toby,--taking, as I tell you, his pipe from his mouth, and strike
the ash out of it as he begin his sentence;--i think, reply he,--it
would not be amiss, brother, if we rung the bell.
pray, whatbe all that racket over our head, obadiah?--quoth my father;--my
brother and I can scarce hear ourselves speak.
Sir, answer Obadiah, make a bow towards his left shoulder,--my mistress
be take very badly.--And wherebe Susannah run down the garden there,
as if they be go to ravish her?--Sir, she be run the short cut
into the town, reply Obadiah, to fetch the old midwife.--Then saddle a
horse, quoth my father, and do you go directly for Dr. Slop, the man-
midwife, with all our services,--and let he know your mistress be fall
into labour--and that I desire he will return with you with all speed.
it be very strange, say my father, address himself to my uncle Toby, as

Mayhap, brother, reply my uncle Toby, my sister do it to save the
