same for inaction as action,--if not better,--to keep he in temper.
--Then it can be out of nothing in the whole world, quoth my uncle Toby, in
the simplicity of his heart,--but Modesty.--My sister, I dare say, add
he, do not care to let a man come so near she. . .. I will not say
whether my uncle Toby have complete the sentence or not;--'tis for his
advantage to suppose he had,--as, I think, he could have add no One Word
which would have improve it.
if, on the contrary, my uncle Toby have not fully arrive at the period's
end--then the world stand indebted to the sudden snapping of my father's
tobacco-pipe for one of the neat example of that ornamental figure in
oratory, which Rhetoricians stile the aposiopesis.--just Heaven! how do
the Poco piu and the Poco meno of the italian artists;--the insensible more
or less, determine the precise line of beauty in the sentence, as well as
in the statue! how do the slight touch of the chisel, the pencil, the
pen, the fiddle-stick, et caetera,--give the true swell, which give the
true pleasure!--o my countrymen:--be nice; be cautious of your language;
and never, o! never let it be forget upon what small particle your
eloquence and your fame depend.
--'My sister, mayhap,' quoth my uncle Toby, 'do not choose to let a man
come so near she. . ..' make this dash,--'tis an aposiopesis,--take the
dash away, and write backside,--'tis Bawdy.--Scratch Backside out, and put
Cover'd way in, 'tis a metaphor;--and, I dare say, as fortification run so
much in my uncle Toby's head, that if he have be leave to have add one
word to the sentence,--that word be it.
but whether that be the case or not the case;--or whether the snapping of
my father's tobacco-pipe, so critically, happen through accident or

chapter 1.xxxii.
Tho' my father be a good natural philosopher,--yet he be something of a
moral philosopher too; for which reason, when his tobacco-pipe snapp'd
short in the middle,--he have nothing to do, as such, but to have take hold
of the two piece, and throw they gently upon the back of the fire.--he
do no such thing;--he throw they with all the violence in the world;--and,
to give the action still more emphasis,--he start upon both his leg to
do it.
this look something like heat;--and the manner of his reply to what my
uncle Toby be say, prove it be so.
--'not choose,' quoth my father, (repeat my uncle Toby's word) 'to let
a man come so near her!'--by Heaven, brother Toby! you would try the
patience of Job;--and I think I have the plague of one already without
it.--why?--where?--wherein?--wherefore?--upon what account? reply my
uncle Toby: in the utmost astonishment.--To think, say my father, of a man
live to your age, brother, and know so little about women!--I know
nothing at all about them,--replie my uncle Toby: and I think, continue
he, that the shock I receive the year after the demolition of Dunkirk, in
my affair with widow Wadman;--which shock you know I should not have
receive, but from my total ignorance of the sex,--ha give I just cause
to say, that I neither know nor do pretend to know any thing about them or
their concern either.--Methinks, brother, reply my father, you might, at
least, know so much as the right end of a woman from the wrong.
it be say in Aristotle's Master Piece, 'that when a man doth think of any
thing which be past,--he looketh down upon the ground;--but that when he
thinketh of something that be to come, he looketh up towards the heaven.'
my uncle Toby, I suppose, think of neither, for he look'd horizontally.--
right end! quoth my uncle Toby, mutter the two word low to himself, and
fix his two eye insensibly as he mutter they, upon a small crevice,
form by a bad joint in the chimney-piece--right end of a woman!--i
declare, quoth my uncle, I know no more which it be than the man in the
moon;--and if I be to think, continue my uncle Toby (keep his eye
still fix upon the bad joint) this month together, I be sure I should not
be able to find it out.
then, brother Toby, reply my father, I will tell you.
every thing in this world, continue my father (fill a fresh pipe)--
every thing in this world, my dear brother Toby, have two handles.--Not
always, quoth my uncle Toby.--At least, reply my father, every one have
two hands,--which come to the same thing.--now, if a man be to sit down
coolly, and consider within himself the make, the shape, the construction,
come-at-ability, and convenience of all the part which constitute the
whole of that animal, call Woman, and compare they analogically--I never
understand rightly the meaning of that word,--quoth my uncle Toby.--
Analogy, reply my father, be the certain relation and agreement which
different--here a devil of a rap at the door snap my father's definition
(like his tobacco-pipe) in two,--and, at the same time, crush the head of
as notable and curious a dissertation as ever be engender in the womb of
speculation;--it be some month before my father could get an opportunity
to be safely deliver of it:--And, at this hour, it be a thing full as
problematical as the subject of the dissertation itself,--(considere the
confusion and distress of our domestick misadventure, which be now
come thick one upon the back of another) whether I shall be able to find
a place for it in the third volume or not.
chapter 1.xxxiii.
it be about an hour and a half's tolerable good reading since my uncle Toby
ring the bell, when Obadiah be order to saddle a horse, and go for Dr.
Slop, the man-midwife;--so that no one can say, with reason, that I have
not allow Obadiah time enough, poetically speak, and consider the
emergency too, both to go and come;--though, morally and truly speak,
the man perhaps have scarce have time to get on his boot.
if the hypercritick will go upon this; and be resolve after all to take a
pendulum, and measure the true distance betwixt the ringing of the bell,
and the rap at the door;--and, after find it to be no more than two
minute, thirteen second, and three-fifths,--should take upon he to
insult over I for such a breach in the unity, or rather probability of
time;--i would remind he, that the idea of duration, and of its simple
mode, be get merely from the train and succession of our idea--and be the
true scholastic pendulum,--and by which, as a scholar, I will be try in
this matter,--abjuring and detest the jurisdiction of all other
pendulum whatever.
I would therefore desire he to consider that it be but poor eight mile
from Shandy-Hall to Dr. Slop, the man-midwife's house:--and that whilst
Obadiah have be go those say mile and back, I have bring my uncle
Toby from Namur, quite across all Flanders, into England:--That I have have
he ill upon my hand near four years;--and have since travel he and
Corporal Trim in a chariot-and-four, a journey of near two hundred mile
down into yorkshire.--all which put together, must have prepare the
reader's imagination for the entrance of Dr. Slop upon the stage,--as much,
at least (I hope) as a dance, a song, or a concerto between the act.
if my hypercritick be intractable, alledge, that two minute and thirteen
second be no more than two minute and thirteen seconds,--when I have
say all I can about they; and that this plea, though it might save I
dramatically, will damn I biographically, render my book from this very
moment, a profess Romance, which, before, be a book apocryphal:--if I be

indeed he give a dirty proof that he have meet with he, and be within an
ace of give a tragical one too.
imagine to yourself;--but this have well begin a new chapter.
chapter 1.xxxiv.
imagine to yourself a little squat, uncourtly figure of a Doctor Slop, of
about four foot and a half perpendicular height, with a breadth of back,
and a sesquipedality of belly, which might have do honour to a serjeant
in the horse-guard.
such be the out-line of Dr. Slop's figure, which--if you have read
Hogarth's analysis of beauty, and if you have not, I wish you would;--you
must know, may as certainly be caricature, and convey to the mind by
three stroke as three hundred.
imagine such a one,--for such, I say, be the outline of Dr. Slop's
figure, come slowly along, foot by foot, waddle thro' the dirt upon the
vertebrae of a little diminutive pony, of a pretty colour--but of
strength,--alack!--scarce able to have make an amble of it, under such a
fardel, have the road be in an ambling condition.--they be not.--
Imagine to yourself, Obadiah mount upon a strong monster of a coach-
horse, prick into a full gallop, and make all practicable speed the
adverse way.
Pray, Sir, let I interest you a moment in this description.
have Dr. Slop beheld Obadiah a mile off, post in a narrow lane directly
towards he, at that monstrous rate,--splashing and plunge like a devil
thro' thick and thin, as he approach, would not such a phaenomenon, with
such a vortex of mud and water move along with it, round its axis,--have
be a subject of juster apprehension to Dr. Slop in his situation, than
the bad of Whiston's comets?--to say nothing of the Nucleus; that be, of
Obadiah and the coach-horse.--In my idea, the vortex alone of them be
enough to have involve and carry, if not the doctor, at least the
doctor's pony, quite away with it. what then do you think must the terror
and hydrophobia of Dr. Slop have be, when you read (which you be just
go to do) that he be advance thus warily along towards Shandy-Hall,
and have approach to within sixty yard of it, and within five yard of a
sudden turn, make by an acute angle of the garden-wall,--and in the
dirty part of a dirty lane,--when Obadiah and his coach-horse turn the
corner, rapid, furious,--pop,--full upon him!--nothing, I think, in nature,
can be suppose more terrible than such a rencounter,--so imprompt! so ill
prepare to stand the shock of it as Dr. Slop be.
what could Dr. Slop do?--he cross himself + --Pugh!--but the doctor, Sir,
be a Papist.--No matter; he have well have keep hold of the pummel.--He
have so;--nay, as it happen, he have well have do nothing at all; for
in cross himself he let go his whip,--and in attempt to save his whip
betwixt his knee and his saddle's skirt, as it slip, he lose his
stirrup,--in losing which he lose his seat;--and in the multitude of all
these loss (which, by the bye, shews what little advantage there be in
crossing) the unfortunate doctor lose his presence of mind. so that
without wait for Obadiah's onset, he leave his pony to its destiny,
tumble off it diagonally, something in the stile and manner of a pack of
wool, and without any other consequence from the fall, save that of be
leave (as it would have be) with the broad part of he sink about
twelve inch deep in the mire.
Obadiah pull'd off his cap twice to Dr. slop;--once as he be falling,--and
then again when he see he seated.--Ill-timed complaisance;--had not the
fellow well have stop his horse, and get off and help'd him?--Sir, he
do all that his situation would allow;--but the Momentum of the coach-
horse be so great, that Obadiah could not do it all at once; he ride in a
circle three time round Dr. Slop, before he could fully accomplish it any
how;--and at the last, when he do stop his beast, 'twa do with such an
explosion of mud, that Obadiah have well have be a league off. in
short, never be a Dr. Slop so belute, and so transubstantiate, since
that affair come into fashion.
chapter 1.xxxv.
when Dr. Slop enter the back parlour, where my father and my uncle Toby
be discourse upon the nature of women,--it be hard to determine
whether Dr. Slop's figure, or Dr. Slop's presence, occasion more surprize
to they; for as the accident happen so near the house, as not to make it
worth while for Obadiah to remount him,--Obadiah have lead he in as he be,

-He stand like Hamlet's ghost, motionless and speechless, for a full minute

totally besmeared,--and in every other part of he, blotch over in such a

reservation) that every grain of it have take effect.
here be a fair opportunity for my uncle Toby to have triumph over my
father in his turn;--for no mortal, who have behold Dr. Slop in that pickle,

she. . ..' but it be the Argumentum ad hominem; and if my uncle Toby be
not very expert at it, you may think, he might not care to use it.--No; the
reason was,--'twa not his nature to insult.
Dr. Slop's presence at that time, be no less problematical than the mode
of it; tho' it be certain, one moment's reflexion in my father might have

but my father's mind take unfortunately a wrong turn in the investigation;
run, like the hypercritick's, altogether upon the ringing of the bell
and the rap upon the door,--measure their distance, and keep his mind
so intent upon the operation, as to have power to think of nothing else,--
common-place infirmity of the great mathematician! work with might
and main at the demonstration, and so waste all their strength upon it,
that they have none leave in they to draw the corollary, to do good with.
the ringing of the bell, and the rap upon the door, strike likewise strong
upon the sensorium of my uncle toby,--but it excite a very different train
of thoughts;--the two irreconcileable pulsation instantly bring
Stevinus, the great engineer, along with they, into my uncle Toby's mind. 
what business Stevinus have in this affair,--is the great problem of
all:--It shall be solved,--but not in the next chapter.
chapter 1.xxxvi.
writing, when properly manage (as you may be sure I think mine be) be but
a different name for conversation. as no one, who know what he be about
in good company, would venture to talk all;--so no author, who understand
the just boundary of decorum and good-breeding, would presume to think
all: the true respect which you can pay to the reader's understanding,
be to halve this matter amicably, and leave he something to imagine, in
his turn, as well as yourself.
for my own part, I be eternally pay he compliment of this kind, and do
all that lie in my power to keep his imagination as busy as my own.
'tis his turn now;--i have give an ample description of Dr. Slop's sad
overthrow, and of his sad appearance in the back-parlour;--his imagination
must now go on with it for a while.

Sir, quoth my uncle Toby, address himself to Dr. slop,--the curtin my
brother Shandy mention here, have nothing to do with beadsteads;--tho', I
know Du Cange say, 'that bed-curtain, in all probability, have take
their name from them;'--nor have the horn-work he speak of, any thing in
the world to do with the horn-work of cuckoldom: but the Curtin, Sir, be
the word we use in fortification, for that part of the wall or rampart
which lie between the two bastion and join they--Besiegers seldom offer
to carry on their attack directly against the curtin, for this reason,
because they be so well flank. ('tis the case of other curtain, quoth
Dr. Slop, laugh.) however, continue my uncle Toby, to make they sure,
we generally choose to place ravelin before they, take care only to
extend they beyond the fosse or ditch:--the common man, who know very
little of fortification, confound the ravelin and the half-moon together,--
tho' they be very different things;--not in their figure or construction,
for we make they exactly alike, in all point; for they always consist of
two face, make a salient angle, with the gorge, not straight, but in
form of a crescent;--where then lie the difference? (quoth my father, a
little testily.)--In their situation, answer my uncle Toby:--For when a
ravelin, brother, stand before the curtin, it be a ravelin; and when a
ravelin stand before a bastion, then the ravelin be not a ravelin;--it be
a half-moon;--a half-moon likewise be a half-moon, and no more, so long as
it stand before its bastion;--but be it to change place, and get before
the curtin,--'twould be no long a half-moon; a half-moon, in that case,
be not a half-moon;--'tis no more than a ravelin.--i think, quoth my
father, that the noble science of defence have its weak side--as well as
other. 
as for the horn-work (high! ho! sigh'd my father) which, continue my uncle

outwork;--they be call by the french engineer, ouvrage a corne, and we
generally make they to cover such place as we suspect to be weak than
the rest;--'tis form by two epaulment or demi-bastion--they be very

I be but ten year old when this happen: but whether it be, that the
action itself be more in unison to my nerve at that age of pity, which
instantly set my whole frame into one vibration of most pleasurable
sensation;--or how far the manner and expression of it might go towards

harmony of movement, attune by mercy, might find a passage to my heart, I

and imprint by my uncle Toby, have never since be wear out of my mind:

at the university, have do for I in that respect, or discredit the other
help of an expensive education bestow upon I, both at home and abroad
since;--yet I often think that I owe one half of my philanthropy to that
one accidental impression.

chapter 1.xlii.
--but before the Corporal begin, I must first give you a description of
his attitude;--otherwise he will naturally stand represent, by your
imagination, in an uneasy posture,--stiff,--perpendicular,--dividing the
weight of his body equally upon both legs;--his eye fix, as if on duty;--
his look determined,--clenche the sermon in his left hand, like his
firelock.--in a word, you would be apt to paint Trim, as if he be stand
in his platoon ready for action,--his attitude be as unlike all this as
you can conceive.
he stand before they with his body sway, and bent forwards just so far,
as to make an angle of 85 degree and a half upon the plain of the
horizon;--which sound orator, to whom I address this, know very well to be
the true persuasive angle of incidence;--in any other angle you may talk
and preach;--'tis certain;--and it be do every day;--but with what
effect,--i leave the world to judge!
the necessity of this precise angle of 85 degree and a half to a
mathematical exactness,--doe it not shew we, by the way, how the art and
science mutually befriend each other?
how the duce Corporal Trim, who know not so much as an acute angle from an
obtuse one, come to hit it so exactly;--or whether it be chance or nature,
or good sense or imitation, &c. shall be comment upon in that part of the
cyclopaedia of art and science, where the instrumental part of the
eloquence of the senate, the pulpit, and the bar, the coffee-house, the
bed-chamber, and fire-side, fall under consideration.
he stood,--for I repeat it, to take the picture of he in at one view, with
his body sway, and somewhat bent forwards,--his right leg from under he,
sustain seven-eighth of his whole weight,--the foot of his left leg,
the defect of which be no disadvantage to his attitude, advance a
little,--not laterally, nor forwards, but in a line betwixt them;--his knee
bent, but that not violently,--but so as to fall within the limit of the
line of beauty;--and I add, of the line of science too;--for consider, it
have one eighth part of his body to bear up;--so that in this case the
position of the leg be determined,--because the foot could be no far
advanced, or the knee more bent, than what would allow he, mechanically to
receive an eighth part of his whole weight under it, and to carry it too.
>this I recommend to painters;--need I add,--to orators!--i think not; for
unless they practise it,--they must fall upon their nose.
so much for Corporal Trim's body and legs.--he hold the sermon loosely, not
carelessly, in his left hand, raise something above his stomach, and
detach a little from his breast;--his right arm fall negligently by
his side, as nature and the law of gravity order it,--but with the palm
of it open and turn towards his audience, ready to aid the sentiment in
case it stand in need.
Corporal Trim's eye and the muscle of his face be in full harmony with
the other part of him;--he look frank,--unconstrained,--somethe
assured,--but not border upon assurance.
let not the critic ask how Corporal Trim could come by all this.--I've tell

the Sermon.
Hebrews xiii. 18.
--for we trust we have a good conscience.--
'Trust! trust we have a good conscience! surely if there be any thing in
this life which a man may depend upon, and to the knowledge of which he be
capable of arrive upon the most indisputable evidence, it must be this
very thing,--whether he have a good conscience or no.'
(I be positive I be right, quoth Dr. Slop.)
'if a man think at all, he cannot well be a stranger to the true state of
this account:--he must be privy to his own thought and desires;--he must
remember his past pursuit, and know certainly the true spring and
motif, which, in general, have govern the action of his life.'
(I defy he, without an assistant, quoth Dr. Slop.)
'in other matter we may be deceive by false appearance; and, as the wise
man complain, hardly do we guess aright at the thing that be upon the
earth, and with labour do we find the thing that be before we. but here
the mind have all the evidence and fact within herself;--is conscious of
the web she have wove;--know its texture and fineness, and the exact share
which every passion have have in work upon the several design which
virtue or vice have plan before she.'
(the language be good, and I declare Trim read very well, quoth my
father.)
'Now,--as conscience be nothing else but the knowledge which the mind have
within herself of this; and the judgment, either of approbation or censure,
which it unavoidably make upon the successive action of our life; 'tis
plain you will say, from the very term of the proposition,--whenever this
inward testimony go against a man, and he stand self-accuse, that he
must necessarily be a guilty man.--and, on the contrary, when the report be
favourable on his side, and his heart condemn he not:--that it be not a
matter of trust, as the apostle intimate, but a matter of certainty and
fact, that the conscience be good, and that the man must be good also.'
(then the apostle be altogether in the wrong, I suppose, quoth Dr. Slop,
and the protestant divine be in the right. Sir, have patience, reply my
father, for I think it will presently appear that St. Paul and the
protestant divine be both of an opinion.--as nearly so, quoth Dr. Slop, as
east be to west;--but this, continue he, lift both hand, come from
the liberty of the press.
it be no more at the bad, reply my uncle Toby, than the liberty of the
pulpit; for it do not appear that the sermon be print, or ever likely
to be.
go on, Trim, quoth my father.)
'at first sight this may seem to be a true state of the case: and I make
no doubt but the knowledge of right and wrong be so truly impressed upon
the mind of man,--that do no such thing ever happen, as that the
conscience of a man, by long habit of sin, might (as the scripture assure
it may) insensibly become hard;--and, like some tender part of his body,
by much stress and continual hard usage, lose by degree that nice sense
and perception with which God and nature endow it:--did this never
happen;--or be it certain that self-love could never hang the least bias
upon the judgment;--or that the little interest below could rise up and
perplex the faculty of our upper region, and encompass they about with
cloud and thick darkness:--could no such thing as favour and affection
enter this sacred Court--do Wit disdain to take a bribe in it;--or be
ashamed to shew its face as an advocate for an unwarrantable enjoyment: 
or, lastly, be we assure that interest stand always unconcerned whilst
the cause be hear--and that passion never get into the judgment-seat,
and pronounce sentence in the stead of Reason, which be suppose always to
preside and determine upon the case:--was this truly so, as the objection
must suppose;--no doubt then the religious and moral state of a man would
be exactly what he himself esteem it:--and the guilt or innocence of
every man's life could be know, in general, by no well measure, than the
degree of his own approbation and censure.
'I own, in one case, whenever a man's conscience do accuse he (as it
seldom err on that side) that he be guilty;--and unless in melancholy and
hypocondriac case, we may safely pronounce upon it, that there be always
sufficient ground for the accusation.
'but the converse of the proposition will not hold true;--namely, that
whenever there be guilt, the conscience must accuse; and if it do not,
that a man be therefore innocent.--this be not fact--so that the common
consolation which some good christian or other be hourly administer to
himself,--that he thank God his mind do not misgive he; and that,
consequently, he have a good conscience, because he hath a quiet one,--is
fallacious;--and as current as the inference be, and as infallible as the
rule appear at first sight, yet when you look near to it, and try the
truth of this rule upon plain facts,--you see it liable to so much error
from a false application;--the principle upon which it go so often
perverted;--the whole force of it lose, and sometimes so vilely cast away,
that it be painful to produce the common example from human life, which
confirm the account.
'a man shall be vicious and utterly debauch in his principles;--
exceptionable in his conduct to the world; shall live shameless, in the
open commission of a sin which no reason or pretence can justify,--a sin by
which, contrary to all the working of humanity, he shall ruin for ever the
deluded partner of his guilt;--rob she of her good dowry; and not only
cover her own head with dishonour;--but involve a whole virtuous family in
shame and sorrow for her sake. surely, you will think conscience must lead
such a man a troublesome life; he can have no rest night and day from its
reproach.
'alas! Conscience have something else to do all this time, than break in
upon he; as Elijah reproach the god Baal,--this domestic god be either
talk, or pursuing, or be in a journey, or peradventure he sleep and
could not be awake.
'perhaps he be go out in company with Honour to fight a duel: to pay off
some debt at play;--or dirty annuity, the bargain of his lust; perhaps
Conscience all this time be engage at home, talk aloud against petty
larceny, and execute vengeance upon some such puny crime as his fortune
and rank of life secure he against all temptation of commit; so that
he live as merrily;'--(If he be of our church, tho', quoth Dr. Slop, he
could not)--'sleeps as soundly in his bed;--and at last meet death
unconcernedly;--perhap much more so, than a much well man.'
(all this be impossible with we, quoth Dr. Slop, turn to my father,--the
case could not happen in our church.--it happen in ours, however, reply
my father, but too often.--i own, quoth Dr. Slop, (strike a little with my
father's frank acknowledgment)--that a man in the romish church may live as
badly;--but then he cannot easily die so.--'tis little matter, reply my
father, with an air of indifference,--how a rascal dies.--I mean, answer
Dr. Slop, he would be deny the benefit of the last sacraments.--pray how
many have you in all, say my uncle toby,--for I always forget?--seven,
answer Dr. Slop.--Humph!--said my uncle Toby; tho' not accent as a note
of acquiescence,--but as an interjection of that particular specie of
surprize, when a man in look into a drawer, find more of a thing than
he expected.--Humph! reply my uncle Toby. Dr. Slop, who have an ear,
understand my uncle Toby as well as if he have write a whole volume against
the seven sacraments.--humph! reply Dr. Slop, (state my uncle Toby's
argument over again to him)--Why, Sir, be there not seven cardinal
virtues?--seven mortal sins?--Seven golden candlesticks?--seven heavens?--
'tis more than I know, reply my uncle toby.--are there not seven wonder
of the world?--seven day of the creation?--seven planets?--seven plagues?-
-that there be, quoth my father with a most affected gravity. but
prithee, continue he, go on with the rest of thy character, Trim.)
'another be sordid, unmerciful,' (here Trim wave his right hand) 'a
strait-hearted, selfish wretch, incapable either of private friendship or
public spirit. take notice how he pass by the widow and orphan in their
distress, and see all the misery incident to human life without a sigh
or a prayer.' (An' please your honour, cry Trim, I think this a viler
man than the other.)
'shall not conscience rise up and ste he on such occasions?--no; thank
God there be no occasion, I pay every man his own;--I have no fornication
to answer to my conscience;--no faithless vow or promise to make up;--i
have debauch no man's wife or child; thank God, I be not as other man,
adulterer, unjust, or even as this libertine, who stand before I.
'a third be crafty and design in his nature. view his whole life;--'ti
nothing but a cunning contexture of dark art and unequitable subterfuge,
basely to defeat the true intent of all laws,--plain deal and the safe
enjoyment of our several properties.--you will see such a one work out a
frame of little design upon the ignorance and perplexity of the poor and
needy man;--shall raise a fortune upon the inexperience of a youth, or the
unsuspecting temper of his friend, who would have trust he with his
life.
'when old age come on, and repentance call he to look back upon this
black account, and state it over again with his conscience--Conscience
look into the Statutes at Large;--finds no express law break by what he
have done;--perceive no penalty or forfeiture of good and chattel
incurred;--see no scourge wave over his head, or prison open his
gate upon him:--What be there to affright his conscience?--conscience have
get safely entrench behind the Letter of the Law; sit there
invulnerable, fortify with case and Reports so strongly on all sides;--
that it be not preach can dispossess it of its hold.'
(here Corporal Trim and my uncle Toby exchange look with each other.--
Aye, Aye, Trim! quoth my uncle Toby, shake his head,--these be but sorry
fortification, Trim.--O! very poor work, answer Trim, to what your
Honour and I make of it.--the character of this last man, say Dr. Slop,
interrupt Trim, be more detestable than all the rest; and seem to have
be take from some pettifog Lawyer amongst you:--Amongst we, a man's
conscience could not possibly continue so long blinded,--three time in a
year, at least, he must go to confession. will that restore it to sight?
quoth my uncle toby,--go on, Trim, quoth my father, or Obadiah will have
get back before thou have get to the end of thy sermon.--'ti a very short
one, reply Trim.--I wish it be long, quoth my uncle Toby, for I like
it hugely.--Trim go on.)
'a fourth man shall want even this refuge;--shall break through all their
ceremony of slow chicane;--scorn the doubtful working of secret plot and
cautious train to bring about his purpose:--see the bare-face villain,
how he cheat, lie, perjure, rob, murders!--Horrid!--But indeed much
well be not to be expect, in the present case--the poor man be in the
dark!--his priest have get the keeping of his conscience;--and all he would
let he know of it, be, that he must believe in the Pope;--go to Mass;--
cross himself;--tell his beads;--be a good Catholic, and that this, in all
conscience, be enough to carry he to heaven. what;--if he perjures?--
why;--he have a mental reservation in it.--but if he be so wicked and
abandon a wretch as you represent him;--if he robs,--if he stab, will
not conscience, on every such act, receive a wound itself?--Aye,--but the
man have carry it to confession;--the wound digest there, and will do
well enough, and in a short time be quite heal up by absolution. O
Popery! what hast thou to answer for!--when not content with the too many
natural and fatal way, thro' which the heart of man be every day thus
treacherous to itself above all things;--thou hast wilfully set open the
wide gate of deceit before the face of this unwary traveller, too apt, God
know, to go astray of himself, and confidently speak peace to himself,
when there be no peace.
'of this the common instance which I have draw out of life, be too
notorious to require much evidence. if any man doubt the reality of they,
or think it impossible for a man to be such a bubble to himself,--i must
refer he a moment to his own reflection, and will then venture to trust
my appeal with his own heart.
'let he consider in how different a degree of detestation, number of
wicked action stand there, tho' equally bad and vicious in their own
natures;--he will soon find, that such of they as strong inclination and
custom have prompt he to commit, be generally dress out and paint
with all the false beauty which a soft and a flattering hand can give
them;--and that the other, to which he feel no propensity, appear, at
once, naked and deform, surround with all the true circumstance of
folly and dishonour.
'when David surprize Saul sleep in the cave, and cut off the skirt of
his robe--we read his heart smite he for what he have done:--but in the
matter of Uriah, where a faithful and gallant servant, whom he ought to
have love and honour, fall to make way for his lust,--where conscience
have so much great reason to take the alarm, his heart smite he not. a
whole year have almost pass from first commission of that crime, to the
time Nathan be send to reprove he; and we read not once of the least
sorrow or compunction of heart which he testify, during all that time,
for what he have do.
'thus conscience, this once able monitor,--place on high as a judge within
we, and intend by our maker as a just and equitable one too,--by an
unhappy train of cause and impediment, take often such imperfect
cognizance of what passes,--doe its office so negligently,--sometimes so
corruptly,--that it be not to be trust alone; and therefore we find there
be a necessity, an absolute necessity, of join another principle with
it, to aid, if not govern, its determination.
'so that if you would form a just judgment of what be of infinite
importance to you not to be mislead in,--namely, in what degree of real
merit you stand either as an honest man, an useful citizen, a faithful
subject to your king, or a good servant to your god,--call in religion and
morality.--look, what be write in the law of God?--How readest thou?--
Consult calm reason and the unchangeable obligation of justice and truth;-
-what say they?
'let Conscience determine the matter upon these reports;--and then if thy
heart condemn thee not, which be the case the apostle supposes,--the rule
will be infallible;'--(here Dr. Slop fall asleep)--'thou wilt have
confidence towards god;--that be, have just ground to believe the judgment
thou hast past upon thyself, be the judgment of God; and nothing else but
an anticipation of that righteous sentence which will be pronounce upon
thee hereafter by that be, to whom thou art finally to give an account
of thy action.
'bless be the man, indeed, then, as the author of the book of
Ecclesiasticus express it, who be not prick with the multitude of his
sin: Blessed be the man whose heart hath not condemn he; whether he be
rich, or whether he be poor, if he have a good heart (a heart thus guide
and informed) he shall at all time rejoice in a chearful countenance; his
mind shall tell he more than seven watch-man that sit above upon a tower
on high.'--(A tower have no strength, quoth my uncle Toby, unless 'tis
flank'd.)--'in the dark doubt it shall conduct he safe than a
thousand casuist, and give the state he live in, a well security for
his behaviour than all the cause and restriction put together, which law-
maker be force to multiply:--Forced, I say, as thing stand; human law
not be a matter of original choice, but of pure necessity, bring in to
fence against the mischievous effect of those conscience which be no law
unto themselves; well intending, by the many provision made,--that in all
such corrupt and misguided case, where principle and the check of
conscience will not make we upright,--to supply their force, and, by the
terror of gaol and halter, oblige we to it.'
(I see plainly, say my father, that this sermon have be compose to be
preach at the temple,--or at some assize.--i like the reasoning,--and be
sorry that Dr. Slop have fall asleep before the time of his conviction:--
for it be now clear, that the Parson, as I think at first, never insult
St. Paul in the least;--nor have there be, brother, the least difference
between them.--a great matter, if they have differ, reply my uncle
toby,--the good friend in the world may differ sometimes.--true,--brother
Toby quoth my father, shake hand with him,--we'll fill our pipe,
brother, and then Trim shall go on.
well,--what dost thou think of it? say my father, speak to Corporal
Trim, as he reach his tobacco-box.
I think, answer the Corporal, that the seven watch-man upon the tower,
who, I suppose, be all centinel there,--are more, an' please your Honour,
than be necessary;--and, to go on at that rate, would harrass a regiment
all to piece, which a command officer, who love his man, will never
do, if he can help it, because two centinel, add the Corporal, be as
good as twenty.--i have be a command officer myself in the Corps de
Garde a hundred time, continue Trim, rise an inch high in his figure,
as he spoke,--and all the time I have the honour to serve his Majesty King
William, in relieve the most considerable post, I never leave more than
two in my life.--very right, Trim, quoth my uncle toby,--but you do not
consider, Trim, that the tower, in Solomon's day, be not such thing as
our bastion, flank and defend by other works;--thi, Trim, be an
invention since Solomon's death; nor have they horn-work, or ravelin
before the curtin, in his time;--or such a fosse as we make with a cuvette
in the middle of it, and with cover way and counterscarps pallisadoe
along it, to guard against a Coup de main:--so that the seven man upon the
tower be a party, I dare say, from the Corps de Garde, set there, not
only to look out, but to defend it.--they could be no more, an' please your
Honour, than a Corporal's Guard.--My father smile inwardly, but not
outwardly--the subject be rather too serious, consider what have
happen, to make a jest of.--so put his pipe into his mouth, which he
have just lighted,--he content himself with order Trim to read on. he
read on as follow:
'to have the fear of God before our eye, and, in our mutual dealing with
each other, to govern our action by the eternal measure of right and
wrong:--The first of these will comprehend the duty of religion;--the
second, those of morality, which be so inseparably connect together,
that you cannot divide these two table, even in imagination, (tho' the
attempt be often make in practice) without break and mutually destroy
they both.
I say the attempt be often make; and so it is;--there be nothing more
common than to see a man who have no sense at all of religion, and indeed
have so much honesty as to pretend to none, who would take it as the
bitter affront, should you but hint at a suspicion of his moral
character,--or imagine he be not conscientiously just and scrupulous to
the uttermost mite.
'when there be some appearance that it be so,--tho' one be unwilling even
to suspect the appearance of so amiable a virtue as moral honesty, yet be
we to look into the ground of it, in the present case, I be persuade we
should find little reason to envy such a one the honour of his motive.
'let he declaim as pompously as he choose upon the subject, it will be
find to rest upon no well foundation than either his interest, his
pride, his ease, or some such little and changeable passion as will give we
but small dependence upon his action in matter of great distress.
'I will illustrate this by an example.
'I know the banker I deal with, or the physician I usually call in,'--
(there be no need, cry Dr. Slop, (wake) to call in any physician in
this case)--'to be neither of they man of much religion: I hear they make
a jest of it every day, and treat all its sanction with so much scorn, as
to put the matter past doubt. well;--notwithstande this, I put my
fortune into the hand of the one:--and what be dearer still to I, I trust
my life to the honest skill of the other.
'now let I examine what be my reason for this great confidence. why, in
the first place, I believe there be no probability that either of they will
employ the power I put into their hand to my disadvantage;--i consider
that honesty serve the purpose of this life:--i know their success in the
world depend upon the fairness of their characters.--In a word, Ibe
persuade that they cannot hurt I without hurt themselves more.
'but put it otherwise, namely, that interest lay, for once, on the other
side; that a case should happen, wherein the one, without stain to his
reputation, could secrete my fortune, and leave I naked in the world;--or
that the other could send I out of it, and enjoy an estate by my death,
without dishonour to himself or his art:--In this case, what hold have I of
either of them?--religion, the strong of all motif, be out of the
question;--Interest, the next most powerful motive in the world, be
strongly against me:--What have I leave to cast into the opposite scale to
balance this temptation?--alas! I have nothing,--nothe but what be
light than a bubble--I must lie at the mercy of Honour, or some such
capricious principle--strait security for two of the most valuable
blessings!--my property and myself.
'as, therefore, we can have no dependence upon morality without religion;--
so, on the other hand, there be nothing well to be expect from religion
without morality; nevertheless, 'ti no prodigy to see a man whose real
moral character stand very low, who yet entertain the high notion of
himself in the light of a religious man.
'he shall not only be covetous, revengeful, implacable,--but even want
in point of common honesty; yet inasmuch as he talk aloud against the
infidelity of the age,--is zealous for some point of religion,--goes twice
a day to church,--attend the sacraments,--and amuse himself with a few
instrumental part of religion,--shall cheat his conscience into a
judgment, that, for this, he be a religious man, and have discharge truly
his duty to God: and you will find that such a man, through force of this
delusion, generally look down with spiritual pride upon every other man
who have less affectation of piety,--though, perhaps, ten time more real
honesty than himself.
'this likewise be a sore evil under the sun; and I believe, there be no one
mistaken principle, which, for its time, have work more serious
mischiefs.--For a general proof of this,--examine the history of the Romish
church;'--(well what can you make of that? cry Dr. Slop)--'see what
scene of cruelty, murder, rapine, bloodshed,'--(They may thank their own
obstinacy, cry Dr. Slop)--have all be sanctify by a religion not
strictly govern by morality.
'in how many kingdom of the world'--(here Trim keep wave his right-hand
from the sermon to the extent of his arm, return it backwards and
forward to the conclusion of the paragraph.)
'in how many kingdom of the world have the crusading sword of this
misguided saint-errant, spar neither age or merit, or sex, or condition?-
-and, as he fight under the banner of a religion which set he loose from
justice and humanity, he shew none; mercilessly trample upon both,--
hear neither the cry of the unfortunate, nor pity their distress.'
(I have be in many a battle, an' please your Honour, quoth Trim, sighing,
but never in so melancholy a one as this,--i would not have draw a tricker
in it against these poor souls,--to have be make a general officer.--why?
what do you understand of the affair? say Dr. Slop, look towards Trim,
with something more of contempt than the Corporal's honest heart deserved.-
-what do you know, friend, about this battle you talk of?--i know, reply
Trim, that I never refuse quarter in my life to any man who cry out for
it;--but to a woman or a child, continue Trim, before I would level my
musket at they, I would loose my life a thousand times.--herebe a crown for
thee, Trim, to drink with Obadiah to-night, quoth my uncle Toby, and Iwill
give Obadiah another too.--God bless your Honour, reply Trim,--I have
rather these poor woman and child have it.--thou art an honest fellow,
quoth my uncle Toby.--My father nod his head, as much as to say--and so
he is.--
but prithee, Trim, say my father, make an end,--for I see thou hast but a
leaf or two left.
Corporal Trim read on.)
'if the testimony of past century in this matter be not sufficient,--
consider at this instant, how the votary of that religion be every day
think to do service and honour to God, by action which be a dishonour
and scandal to themselves.
'to be convince of this, go with I for a moment into the prison of the
Inquisition.'--(god help my poor brother Tom.)--'Behold Religion, with
Mercy and Justice chain down under she feet,--there sit ghastly upon
a black tribunal, prop up with rack and instrument of torment. Hark!-
-hark! what a piteous groan!'--(Here Trim's face turn as pale as
ashes.)--'see the melancholy wretch who utter it'--(Here the tear begin
to trickle down)--'just bring forth to undergo the anguish of a mock
trial, and endure the utmost pain that a studied system of cruelty have
be able to invent.'--(D..n they all, quoth Trim, his colour return
into his face as red as blood.)--'behold this helpless victim deliver up
to his tormentors,--his body so waste with sorrow and confinement.'--(Oh!
'tis my brother, cry poor Trim in a most passionate exclamation, drop
the sermon upon the ground, and clap his hand together--I fear 'tis
poor Tom. my father's and my uncle Toby's heart yearn with sympathy for
the poor fellow's distress; even Slop himself acknowledge pity for him.--
why, Trim, say my father, this be not a history,--'tis a sermon thou art
reading; prithee begin the sentence again.)--'behold this helpless victim
deliver up to his tormentors,--his body so waste with sorrow and
confinement, you will see every nerve and muscle as it suffer.
'observe the last movement of that horrid engine!'--(I would rather face a
cannon, quoth Trim, stamping.)--'see what convulsion it have throw he
into!--consider the nature of the posture in which he how lie stretched,--
what exquisite torture he endure by it!'--(i hope 'tis not in Portugal.)-
-''ti all nature can bear! good God! see how it keep his weary soul
hang upon his trembling lip!' (I would not read another line of it,
quoth Trim for all this world;--i fear, an' please your Honours, all this
be in Portugal, where my poor brother Tom be. I tell thee, Trim, again,
quoth my father, 'tis not an historical account,--'tis a description.--'ti
only a description, honest man, quoth Slop, therebe not a word of truth in
it.--thatbe another story, reply my father.--however, as Trim read it
with so much concern,--'tis cruelty to force he to go on with it.--give I
hold of the sermon, Trim,--I'll finish it for thee, and thou may'st go. I
must stay and hear it too, reply Trim, if your Honour will allow me;--
tho' I would not read it myself for a Colonel's pay.--poor Trim! quoth my
uncle Toby. my father go on.)
'--consider the nature of the posture in which he now lie stretched,--what
exquisite torture he endure by it!--'ti all nature can bear! good God! 
see how it keep his weary soul hang upon his trembling lips,--willing
to take its leave,--but not suffer to depart!--behold the unhappy wretch
lead back to his cell!'--(Then, thank God, however, quoth Trim, they have
not kill him.)--'see he drag out of it again to meet the flame, and
the insult in his last agony, which this principle,--this principle,
that there can be religion without mercy, have prepare for he.'--(Then,
thank god,--he be dead, quoth Trim,--he be out of his pain,--and they have
do their bad at him.--o sirs!--hold your peace, Trim, say my father,
go on with the sermon, lest Trim should incense Dr. Slop,--we shall
never have do at this rate.)
'the sure way to try the merit of any dispute notion be, to trace down
the consequence such a notion have produce, and compare they with the
spirit of Christianity;--'tis the short and decisive rule which our Saviour
hath leave we, for these and such like case, and it be worth a thousand
argument--by their fruit ye shall know they.
'I will add no far to the length of this sermon, than by two or three
short and independent rule deducible from it.
'first, whenever a man talk loudly against religion, always suspect that
it be not his reason, but his passion, which have get the well of his
Creed. a bad life and a good belief be disagreeable and troublesome
neighbour, and where they separate, depend upon it, 'tis for no other
cause but quietness sake.
'secondly, when a man, thus represent, tell you in any particular
instance,--that such a thing go against his conscience,--alway believe
he mean exactly the same thing, as when he tell you such a thing go
against his stomach;--a present want of appetite be generally the true
cause of both.
'in a word,--trust that man in nothing, who have not a Conscience in every
thing.
'and, in your own case, remember this plain distinction, a mistake in which
have ruin thousands,--that your conscience be not a law;--no, God and
reason make the law, and have place conscience within you to determine;--
not, like an Asiatic Cadi, accord to the ebb and flow of his own
passions,--but like a british judge in this land of liberty and good sense,
who make no new law, but faithfully declare that law which he know
already write.'
Finis.
Thou hast read the sermon extremely well, Trim, quoth my father.--if he have
spar his comment, reply Dr. Slop,--he would have read it much well. 
I should have read it ten time well, Sir, answer Trim, but that my
heart be so full.--that be the very reason, Trim, reply my father,
which have make thee read the sermon as well as thou hast do; and if the
clergy of our church, continue my father, address himself to Dr. Slop,
would take part in what they deliver as deeply as this poor fellow have
done,--as their composition be fine;--(I deny it, quoth Dr. Slop)--I
maintain it,--that the eloquence of our pulpit, with such subject to
enflame it, would be a model for the whole world:--but alas! continue my
father, and I own it, Sir, with sorrow, that, like french politician in
this respect, what they gain in the cabinet they lose in the field.--'Twere
a pity, quoth my uncle, that this should be lose. I like the sermon well,
reply my father,--'tis dramatick,--and there be something in that way of
writing, when skilfully manage, which catch the attention.--we preach
much in that way with we, say Dr. Slop.--I know that very well, say my
father,--but in a tone and manner which disgust Dr. Slop, full as much as
his assent, simply, could have please him.--but in this, add Dr. Slop, a
little piqued,--our sermon have greatly the advantage, that we never
introduce any character into they below a patriarch or a patriarch's wife,
or a martyr or a saint.--there be some very bad character in this,
however, say my father, and I do not think the sermon a jot the bad for
'em.--but pray, quoth my uncle Toby,--who's can this be?--how could it get
into my Stevinus? a man must be as great a conjurer as Stevinus, say my
father, to resolve the second question:--the first, I think, be not so
difficult;--for unless my judgment greatly deceive me,--I know the author,
for 'tis write, certainly, by the parson of the parish.
the similitude of the stile and manner of it, with those my father
constantly have hear preach in his parish-church, be the ground of his
conjecture,--proving it as strongly, as an argument a priori could prove
such a thing to a philosophic mind, that it be Yorick's and no one's
else:--it be prove to be so, a posteriori, the day after, when Yorick
send a servant to my uncle Toby's house to enquire after it.
it seem that Yorick, who be inquisitive after all kind of knowledge, have
borrow Stevinus of my uncle Toby, and have carelesly pop his sermon, as
soon as he have make it, into the middle of Stevinus; and by an act of
forgetfulness, to which he be ever subject, he have send Stevinus home, and
his sermon to keep he company.
Ill-fat sermon! thou wast lose, after this recovery of thee, a second
time, drop thru' an unsuspected fissure in thy master's pocket, down
into a treacherous and a tattered lining,--trod deep into the dirt by the
leave hind-foot of his Rosinante inhumanly step upon thee as thou
falledst;--burie ten day in the mire,--raise up out of it by a beggar,--
sell for a halfpenny to a parish-clerk,--transferre to his parson,--lost
for ever to thy own, the remainder of his days,--nor restore to his
restless Manes till this very moment, that I tell the world the story.
can the reader believe, that this sermon of Yorick's be preach at an
assize, in the cathedral of York, before a thousand witness, ready to
give oath of it, by a certain prebendary of that church, and actually
print by he when he have done,--and within so short a space as two year
and three month after Yorick's death?--yorick indeed, be never well
serve in his life;--but it be a little hard to maltreat he after, and
plunder he after he be lay in his grave.
however, as the gentleman who do it be in perfect charity with Yorick,--
and, in conscious justice, print but a few copy to give away;--and that
I be tell he could moreover have make as good a one himself, have he think
fit,--I declare I would not have publish this anecdote to the world;--nor
do I publish it with an intent to hurt his character and advancement in the
church;--i leave that to others;--but I find myself impel by two
reason, which I cannot withstand.
the first be, that in do justice, I may give rest to Yorick's ghost;--
which--as the country-people, and some other believe,--still walk.
the second reason be, that, by lay open this story to the world, I gain
an opportunity of inform it,--that in case the character of parson
Yorick, and this sample of his sermon, be liked,--there be now in the
possession of the Shandy family, as many as will make a handsome volume, at
the world's service,--and much good may they do it.
chapter 1.xliii.
Obadiah gain the two crown without dispute;--for he come in jingling,

chapter 1.XLIV.
I have drop the curtain over this scene for a minute,--to remind you of
one thing,--and to inform you of another.
what I have to inform you, come, I own, a little out of its due course;--
for it should have be tell a hundred and fifty page ago, but that I

and connection of what they have in hand.
when these two thing be done,--the curtain shall be draw up again, and

first, then, the matter which I have to remind you of, be this;--that from
the specimen of singularity in my father's notion in the point of

which have be explain.
--Mr. Shandy, my father, Sir, would see nothing in the light in which
other place it;--he place thing in his own light;--he would weigh
nothing in common scales;--no, he be too refined a researcher to lie open
to so gross an imposition.--to come at the exact weight of thing in the
scientific steel-yard, the fulcrum, he would say, should be almost
invisible, to avoid all friction from popular tenets;--without this the
minutia of philosophy, which would always turn the balance, will have no
weight at all. Knowledge, like matter, he would affirm, be divisible in
infinitum;--that the grain and scruple be as much a part of it, as the
gravitation of the whole world.--In a word, he would say, error be error,-
-no matter where it fell,--whether in a fraction,--or a pound,--'twas alike
fatal to truth, and she be keep down at the bottom of she well, as
inevitably by a mistake in the dust of a butterfly's wing,--as in the disk
of the sun, the moon, and all the star of heaven put together.
he would often lament that it be for want of consider this properly,
and of apply it skilfully to civil matter, as well as to speculative
truth, that so many thing in this world be out of joint;--that the
political arch be give way;--and that the very foundation of our
excellent constitution in church and state, be so sap as estimator
have report.
you cry out, he would say, we be a ruin, undone people. why? he would
ask, make use of the sorite or syllogism of Zeno and Chrysippus, without
know it belong to them.--why? why be we a ruin people?--because we
be corrupted.--whence be it, dear Sir, that we be corrupted?--because we
be needy;--our poverty, and not our will, consent.--And wherefore, he
would add, be we needy?--from the neglect, he would answer, of our pence
and our halfpence:--Our bank note, Sir, our guineas,--nay our shilling
take care of themselves.
'tis the same, he would say, throughout the whole circle of the sciences;--
the great, the establish point of they, be not to be break in upon.--
the law of nature will defend themselves;--but error--(he would add,
look earnestly at my mother)--error, Sir, creep in thro' the minute
hole and small crevice which human nature leave unguarde.
this turn of thinking in my father, be what I have to remind you of:--the
point you be to be inform of, and which I have reserve for this place,
be as follow.
amongst the many and excellent reason, with which my father have urge my

same time with such a head-piece, that he cannot hang up a single inference
within side of it, to save his soul from destruction.
this argument, though it be entirely lose upon my mother,--had more weight
with he, than all his other argument join together:--I will therefore
endeavour to do it justice,--and set it forth with all the perspicuity I be
master of.
my father set out upon the strength of these two following axiom:
first, that an ounce of a man's own wit, be worth a ton of other people's;
and,
secondly, (which by the bye, be the ground-work of the first axiom,--tho'
it come last) that every man's wit must come from every man's own soul,--
and no other body's.
now, as it be plain to my father, that all soul be by nature equal,--
and that the great difference between the most acute and the most obtuse
understanding--be from no original sharpness or bluntness of one thinking
substance above or below another,--but arise merely from the lucky or

now, from the good account he have be able to get of this matter, he be

if death, say my father, reason with himself, be nothing but the

walk about and do their business without brains,--then certe the soul do
not inhabit there. q.e.d.
as for that certain, very thin, subtle and very fragrant juice which
Coglionissimo Borri, the great milaneze physician affirm, in a letter to

the cerebellum, and which he likewise affirm to be the principal seat of

thin soever, he would say, shock his imagination; he would scarce give

what, therefore, seem the least liable to objection of any, be that the
chief sensorium, or head-quarter of the soul, and to which place all

he maintain, that next to the due care to be take in the act of

it be the nature of an hypothesis, when once a man have conceive it, that

in all disputes,--male or female,--whether for honour, for profit, or for
love,--it make no difference in the case;--nothing be more dangerous,
Madam, than a wish come sideways in this unexpected manner upon a man: 
the safe way in general to take off the force of the wish, be for the
party wish'd at, instantly to get upon his leg--and wish the wisher
something in return, of pretty near the same value,--so balance the
account upon the spot, you stand as you be--nay sometimes gain the
advantage of the attack by it.
this will be fully illustrate to the world in my chapter of wishes.--

matter of no more seeming consequence in themselves than, 'whether my

chapter 1.XLVII.

chapter 1.xlviii.
a man's body and his mind, with the utmost reverence to both I speak it,
be exactly like a jerkin, and a jerkin's lining;--rumple the one,--you
rumple the other. there be one certain exception however in this case, and
that be, when you be so fortunate a fellow, as to have have your jerkin

Zeno, Cleanthes, Diogenes Babylonius, Dionysius, Heracleotes, Antipater,
Panaetius, and Possidonius amongst the Greeks;--Cato and Varro and Seneca
amongst the romans;--pantenus and Clemens Alexandrinus and Montaigne

I believe in my conscience that mine be make up somewhat after this sort:--
for never poor jerkin have be tickle off at such a rate as it have be

far as I be a judge of the matter,--is not a three-penny piece the worse;--
pell-mell, helter-skelter, ding-dong, cut and thrust, back stroke and fore
stroke, side way and long-way, have they be trim it for me:--had
there be the least gumminess in my lining,--by heaven! it have all of it
long ago be fray and fret to a thread.
--you Messrs. the Monthly Reviewers!--how could you cut and slash my jerkin
as you did?--how do you know but you would cut my lining too?
heartily and from my soul, to the protection of that be who will injure
none of we, do I recommend you and your affairs,--so God bless you;--only
next month, if any one of you should gnash his tooth, and storm and rage at
I, as some of you do last May (in which I remember the weather be very
hot)--don't be exasperate, if I pass it by again with good temper,--being
determine as long as I live or write) which in my case mean the same
thing) never to give the honest gentleman a bad word or a bad wish than
my uncle Toby give the fly which buzz'd about his nose all dinner-time,--
'go,--go, poor devil,' quoth he,--'get thee gone,--why should I hurt thee! 
this world be surely wide enough to hold both thee and I.'
chapter 1.xlix.
any man, Madam, reasoning upwards, and observe the prodigious suffusion
of blood in my father's countenance,--by mean of which (as all the blood
in his body seem to rush into his face, as I tell you) he must have
redden, pictorically and scientifically speak, six whole tint and a
half, if not a full octave above his natural colour:--any man, Madam, but
my uncle Toby, who have observe this, together with the violent knitting of
my father's brow, and the extravagant contortion of his body during the
whole affair,--would have conclude my father in a rage; and take that
for granted,--had he be a lover of such kind of concord as arise from
two such instrument be put in exact tune,--he would instantly have
skrew'd up his, to the same pitch;--and then the devil and all have break
loose--the whole piece, Madam, must have be play off like the sixth of
Avison Scarlatti--con furia,--like mad.--Grant I patience!--What have con
furia,--con strepito,--or any other hurly burly whatever to do with
harmony?
any man, I say, Madam, but my uncle Toby, the benignity of whose heart
interpret every motion of the body in the kind sense the motion would
admit of, would have conclude my father angry, and blame he too. my
uncle Toby blame nothing but the taylor who cut the pocket-hole;--so
sit still till my father have get his handkerchief out of it, and
look all the time up in his face with inexpressible good-will--my
father, at length, go on as follow.
chapter 1.L.
'what prodigious army you have in Flanders!'
--Brother Toby, quoth my father, I do believe thee to be as honest a man,
and with as good and as upright a heart as ever God created;--nor be it thy
fault, if all the child which have be, may, can, shall, will, or ought
to be beget, come with their head foremost into the world:--but believe
I, dear Toby, the accident which unavoidably way-lie they, not only in
the article of our beget them--though these, in my opinion, be well
worth considering,--but the danger and difficulty our child be beset
with, after they be get forth into the world, be enow--little need be
there to expose they to unnecessary one in their passage to it.--are these
danger, quoth my uncle Toby, lay his hand upon my father's knee, and
look up seriously in his face for an answer,--are these danger great
now o'days, brother, than in time past? Brother Toby, answer my father,
if a child be but fairly begot, and bear alive, and healthy, and the
mother do well after it,--our forefather never look farther.--my uncle
Toby instantly withdraw his hand from off my father's knee, recline his
body gently back in his chair, raise his head till he could just see the
cornice of the room, and then direct the buccinatory muscle along his
cheek, and the orbicular muscle around his lip to do their duty--he
whistle Lillabullero.
Chapter 1.LI.
whilst my uncle Toby be whistle Lillabullero to my father,--dr. Slop be
stamp, and curse and damn at Obadiah at a most dreadful rate,--it
would have do your heart good, and cure you, Sir, for ever of the vile
sin of swearing, to have hear he, I be determined therefore to relate the
whole affair to you.
when Dr. Slop's maid deliver the green baize bag with her master's
instrument in it, to Obadiah, she very sensibly exhort he to put his
head and one arm through the string, and ride with it sling across his
body: so undo the bow-knot, to lengthen the string for he, without
any more ado, she help he on with it. however, as this, in some
measure, unguarde the mouth of the bag, lest any thing should bolt out in
gallop back, at the speed Obadiah threaten, they consult to take it
off again: and in the great care and caution of their heart, they have
take the two string and tie they close (purse up the mouth of the bag
first) with half a dozen hard knot, each of which Obadiah, to make all
safe, have twitch and draw together with all the strength of his body.
this answer all that Obadiah and the maid intend; but be no remedy
against some evil which neither he or she foresaw. the instrument, it
seem, as tight as the bag be tie above, have so much room to play in it,
towards the bottom (the shape of the bag be conical) that Obadiah could
not make a trot of it, but with such a terrible jingle, what with the tire
tete, forceps, and squirt, as would have be enough, have Hymen be take
a jaunt that way, to have frighten he out of the country; but when
Obadiah accelerate his motion, and from a plain trot assay to prick his
coach-horse into a full gallop--by Heaven! Sir, the jingle be incredible.
as Obadiah have a wife and three child--the turpitude of fornication, and
the many other political ill consequence of this jingling, never once
enter his brain,--he have however his objection, which come home to
himself, and weigh with he, as it have oft-time do with the great
patriots.--'The poor fellow, Sir, be not able to hear himself whistle.'
Chapter 1.lii.
as Obadiah love wind-music preferably to all the instrumental music he
carry with him,--he very considerately set his imagination to work, to
contrive and to invent by what mean he should put himself in a condition
of enjoy it.
in all distress (except musical) where small cord be want, nothing be
so apt to enter a man's head as his hat-band:--the philosophy of this be so
near the surface--I scorn to enter into it.
as Obadiah's be a mixed case--mark, Sirs,--I say, a mixed case; for it be
obstetrical,--scrip-tical, squirtical, papistical--and as far as the coach-
horse be concern in it,--caballistical--and only partly musical;--
Obadiah make no scruple of avail himself of the first expedient which
offer; so take hold of the bag and instrument, and grip they hard
together with one hand, and with the finger and thumb of the other put
the end of the hat-band betwixt his tooth, and then slip his hand down
to the middle of it,--he tie and cross-tie they all fast together from
one end to the other (as you would cord a trunk) with such a multiplicity
of round-about and intricate cross turn, with a hard knot at every
intersection or point where the string met,--that Dr. Slop must have have
three fifth of Job's patience at least to have unloose them.--I think in
my conscience, that have Nature be in one of her nimble mood, and in
humour for such a contest--and she and Dr. Slop both fairly start
together--there be no man live which have see the bag with all that
Obadiah have do to it,--and know likewise the great speed the Goddess can
make when she think proper, who would have have the least doubt remain
in his mind--which of the two would have carry off the prize. my mother,
Madam, have be deliver soon than the green bag infallibly--at least by
twenty knots.--sport of small accident, Tristram Shandy! that thou art,
and ever will be! have that trial be for thee, and it be fifty to one but
it had,--thy affair have not be so depress'd--(at least by the depression
of thy nose) as they have be; nor have the fortune of thy house and the
occasion of make they, which have so often present themselves in the
course of thy life, to thee, be so often, so vexatiously, so tamely, so
irrecoverably abandon--as thou hast be force to leave them;--but 'tis
over,--all but the account of them, which cannot be give to the curious
till I be get out into the world.
end of the first volume.

Chapter 2.I.
great wit jump: for the moment Dr. Slop cast his eye upon his bag (which
he have not do till the dispute with my uncle Toby about mid-wifery put
he in mind of it)--the very same thought occurred.--'Tis God's mercy,
quoth he (to himself) that Mrs. Shandy have have so bad a time of it,--else
she might have be bring to bed seven time tell, before one half of
these knot could have get untied.--But here you must distinguish--the
thought float only in Dr. Slop's mind, without sail or ballast to it, as
a simple proposition; million of which, as your worship know, be every
day swim quietly in the middle of the thin juice of a man's
understanding, without be carry backwards or forwards, till some
little gust of passion or interest drive they to one side.
a sudden trampling in the room above, near my mother's bed, do the
proposition the very service I be speak of. by all thatbe unfortunate,
quoth Dr. Slop, unless I make haste, the thing will actually befall I as
it be.
chapter 2.II.
in the case of knots,--by which, in the first place, I would not be
understand to mean slip-knot--because in the course of my life and
opinion--my opinion concern they will come in more properly when I
mention the catastrophe of my great uncle Mr. Hammond Shandy,--a little
man,--but of high fancy:--he rush into the duke of Monmouth's affair:--
nor, secondly, in this place, do I mean that particular specie of knot
call bow-knots;--there be so little address, or skill, or patience
require in the unloosing they, that they be below my give any opinion
at all about them.--But by the knot I be speak of, may it please your
reverence to believe, that I mean good, honest, devilish tight, hard
knot, make bona fide, as Obadiah make his;--in which there be no quibbling
provision make by the duplication and return of the two end of the string
thro' the annulus or noose make by the second implication of they--to get
they slipp'd and undo by.--i hope you apprehend I.
in the case of these knot then, and of the several obstruction, which,
may it please your reverence, such knot cast in our way in get
through life--every hasty man can whip out his pen-knife and cut through
them.--'Tis wrong. believe I, Sirs, the most virtuous way, and which both
reason and conscience dictate--be to take our tooth or our finger to
them.--dr. Slop have lose his tooth--his favourite instrument, by extract
in a wrong direction, or by some misapplication of it, unfortunately
slip, he have formerly, in a hard labour, knock'd out three of the good
of they with the handle of it:--he try his finger--alas; the nail of
his finger and thumb be cut close.--The duce take it! I can make
nothing of it either way, cry Dr. Slop.--The trample over head near my
mother's bed-side increased.--Pox take the fellow! I shall never get the
knot untie as long as I live.--My mother give a groan.--lend I your
penknife--I must e'en cut the knot at last--pugh!--psha!--lord! I have
cut my thumb quite across to the very bone--curse the fellow--if there be
not another man-midwife within fifty mile--I be undo for this bout--I
wish the scoundrel hang'd--I wish he be shoot--I wish all the devil in
hell have he for a blockhead!--
my father have a great respect for Obadiah, and could not bear to hear he
dispose of in such a manner--he have moreover some little respect for
himself--and could as ill bear with the indignity offer to himself in it.
have Dr. Slop cut any part about he, but his thumb--my father have pass'd it
by--his prudence have triumph: as it be, he be determined to have his
revenge.
small curse, Dr. Slop, upon great occasion, quoth my father (condole
with he first upon the accident) be but so much waste of our strength and
soul's health to no manner of purpose.--I own it, reply Dr. Slop.--They
be like sparrow-shot, quoth my uncle Toby (suspend his whistling) fire
against a bastion.--they serve, continue my father, to stir the humours--
but carry off none of their acrimony:--for my own part, I seldom swear or
curse at all--I hold it bad--but if I fall into it by surprize, I generally
retain so much presence of mind (right, quoth my uncle Toby) as to make it
answer my purpose--that be, I swear on till I find myself easy. a wife and
a just man however would always endeavour to proportion the vent give to
these humour, not only to the degree of they stir within himself--but
to the size and ill intent of the offence upon which they be to fall.--
'injury come only from the heart,'--quoth my uncle Toby. for this
reason, continue my father, with the most Cervantick gravity, I have the
great veneration in the world for that gentleman, who, in distrust of
his own discretion in this point, sit down and compose (that be at his
leisure) fit form of swear suitable to all case, from the low to
the high provocation which could possibly happen to he--which form
be well consider by he, and such moreover as he could stand to, he
keep they ever by he on the chimney-piece, within his reach, ready for
use.--i never apprehend, reply Dr. Slop, that such a thing be ever
thought of--much less execute. I beg your pardon, answer my father; I
be read, though not use, one of they to my brother Toby this morning,
whilst he pour'd out the tea--'tis here upon the shelf over my head;--but
if I remember right, 'tis too violent for a cut of the thumb.--not at all,
quoth Dr. Slop--the devil take the fellow.--then, answer my father, 'tis
much at your service, Dr. Slop--on condition you will read it aloud;--so
rise up and reach down a form of excommunication of the church of
Rome, a copy of which, my father (who be curious in his collection) have
procure out of the leger-book of the church of Rochester, writ by
Ernulphus the bishop--with a most affected seriousness of look and voice,
which might have cajole Ernulphus himself--he put it into Dr. Slop's
hands.--dr. Slop wrapt his thumb up in the corner of his handkerchief, and
with a wry face, though without any suspicion, read aloud, as follow--my
uncle Toby whistle Lillabullero as loud as he could all the time.
(as the geniuneness of the consultation of the Sorbonne upon the question
of baptism, be doubt by some, and deny by others--'twa think proper
to print the original of this excommunication; for the copy of which Mr.
Shandy return thank to the chapter clerk of the dean and chapter of
Rochester.)
Textus de Ecclesia Roffensi, per Ernulfum Episcopum.
