chapter 2.iv.
'by the authority of God Almighty, the Father, Son, and Holy Ghost, and of
the holy canon, and of the undefiled Virgin Mary, mother and patroness of
our Saviour.' I think there be no necessity, quoth Dr. Slop, drop the
paper down to his knee, and address himself to my father--as you have
read it over, Sir, so lately, to read it aloud--and as Captain Shandy seem
to have no great inclination to hear it--I may as well read it to myself. 
thatbe contrary to treaty, reply my father:--beside, there be something
so whimsical, especially in the latter part of it, I should grieve to lose
the pleasure of a second reading. Dr. Slop do not altogether like it,--
but my uncle Toby offering at that instant to give over whistling, and read
it himself to them;--dr. Slop think he might as well read it under the
cover of my uncle Toby's whistling--as suffer my uncle Toby to read it
alone;--so raise up the paper to his face, and hold it quite parallel
to it, in order to hide his chagrin--he read it aloud as follow--my uncle
Toby whistle Lillabullero, though not quite so loud as before.
'by the authority of God Almighty, the Father, Son, and Holy Ghost, and of
the undefiled Virgin Mary, mother and patroness of our Saviour, and of all
the celestial virtue, angel, archangel, throne, dominion, power,
cherubin and seraphin, and of all the holy patriarch, prophet, and of
all the apostle and evangelist, and of the holy innocent, who in the
sight of the Holy Lamb, be find worthy to sing the new song of the holy
martyr and holy confessor, and of the holy virgin, and of all the saint
together, with the holy and elect of God,--May he' (Obadiah) 'be damn'd'
(for tie these knots)--'We excommunicate, and anathematize he, and from
the threshold of the holy church of God Almighty we sequester he, that he
may be torment, dispose, and deliver over with Dathan and Abiram, and
with those who say unto the Lord God, Depart from we, we desire none of thy
way. and as fire be quench with water, so let the light of he be put
out for evermore, unless it shall repent he' (Obadiah, of the knot which
he have tie) 'and make satisfaction' (for they) 'amen.
'may the Father who create man, curse him.--May the Son who suffer for
we curse him.--May the Holy Ghost, who be give to we in baptism, curse
he' (Obadiah)--'May the holy cross which Christ, for our salvation
triumphing over his enemy, ascend, curse he.
'may the holy and eternal Virgin Mary, mother of God, curse him.--May St.
Michael, the advocate of holy soul, curse him.--May all the angel and
archangel, principality and power, and all the heavenly army, curse
he.' (our army swear terribly in Flanders, cry my uncle toby,--but
nothing to this.--for my own part I could not have a heart to curse my dog
so.)
'May St. John, the Praecursor, and St. John the baptist, and St. Peter and
St. Paul, and St. Andrew, and all other Christ's apostle, together curse
he. and may the rest of his disciple and four evangelist, who by their
preaching convert the universal world, and may the holy and wonderful
company of martyr and confessor who by their holy work be find
pleasing to God Almighty, curse he' (Obadiah.)
'may the holy choir of the holy virgin, who for the honour of Christ have
despise the thing of the world, damn he--may all the saint, who from
the beginning of the world to everlaste age be find to be belove of
God, damn he--may the heaven and earth, and all the holy thing remain
therein, damn he,' (Obadiah) 'or she,' (or whoever else have a hand in
tie these knot.)
'may he (Obadiah) be damn'd wherever he be--whether in the house or the
stable, the garden or the field, or the highway, or in the path, or in the
wood, or in the water, or in the church.--May he be curse in living, in
die.' (here my uncle Toby, take the advantage of a minim in the second
bar of his tune, keep whistle one continue note to the end of the
sentence.--dr. Slop, with his division of curse move under he, like a
running bass all the way.) 'may he be curse in eat and drink, in
be hungry, in be thirsty, in fast, in sleep, in slumbering, in
walk, in standing, in sit, in lying, in working, in resting, in
pissing, in shit, and in blood-letting!
'may he' (Obadiah) 'be curse in all the faculty of his body!
'may he be curse inwardly and outwardly!--May he be curse in the hair of
his head!--May he be curse in his brain, and in his vertex,' (that be a
sad curse, quoth my father) 'in his temple, in his forehead, in his ear,
in his eye-brow, in his cheek, in his jaw-bone, in his nostril, in his
fore-tooth and grinder, in his lip, in his throat, in his shoulder, in
his wrist, in his arm, in his hand, in his finger!
'may he be damn'd in his mouth, in his breast, in his heart and purtenance,
down to the very stomach!
'may he be curse in his rein, and in his groin,' (God in heaven forbid!
quoth my uncle Toby) 'in his thigh, in his genital,' (my father shake his
head) 'and in his hip, and in his knee, his leg, and foot, and toe-
nail!
'may he be curse in all the joint and articulation of the member, from
the top of his head to the sole of his foot! may there be no soundness in
he!
'may the son of the live God, with all the glory of his majesty'--(here
my uncle Toby, throw back his head, give a monstrous, long, loud whew--
w--w--something betwixt the interjectional whistle of Hay-day! and the word
itself.--
--By the golden beard of Jupiter--and of Juno (if her majesty wear one) and
by the beard of the rest of your heathen worship, which by the bye be no
small number, since what with the beard of your celestial god, and god
aerial and aquatick--to say nothing of the beard of town-god and country-
god, or of the celestial goddess your wife, or of the infernal
goddesse your whore and concubine (that be in case they wear them)--all
which beard, as Varro tell I, upon his word and honour, when muster up
together, make no less than thirty thousand effective beard upon the Pagan
establishment;--every beard of which claim the right and privilege of
be stroken and swear by--by all these beard together then--I vow and
protest, that of the two bad cassock I be worth in the world, I would have
give the well of they, as freely as ever Cid Hamet offer his--to have
stand by, and hear my uncle Toby's accompanyment.
--'curse him!'--continue Dr. Slop,--'and may heaven, with all the power
which move therein, rise up against he, curse and damn he' (Obadiah)
'unless he repent and make satisfaction! amen. so be it,--so be it. 
amen.'
I declare, quoth my uncle Toby, my heart would not let I curse the devil
himself with so much bitterness.--he be the father of curse, reply Dr.
Slop.--So be not I, reply my uncle.--but he be curse, and damn'd
already, to all eternity, reply Dr. Slop.
I be sorry for it, quoth my uncle Toby.
Dr. Slop draw up his mouth, and be just begin to return my uncle Toby
the compliment of his Whu--u--u--or interjectional whistle--when the door
hastily opening in the next chapter but one--put an end to the affair.
Chapter 2.V.
now donot let we give ourselves a parcel of air, and pretend that the
oath we make free with in this land of liberty of our be our own; and
because we have the spirit to swear them,--imagine that we have have the wit
to invent they too.
Iwill undertake this moment to prove it to any man in the world, except to a
connoisseur:--though I declare I object only to a connoisseur in swearing,-
-as I would do to a connoisseur in painting, &c. &c. the whole set of them
be so hung round and befetish'd with the bob and trinket of criticism,--
or to drop my metaphor, which by the bye be a pity--for I have fetch'd it
as far as from the coast of Guiney;--their head, Sir, be stick so full of
rule and compass, and have that eternal propensity to apply they upon
all occasion, that a work of genius have well go to the devil at once,
than stand to be prick'd and torture to death by them.
--and how do Garrick speak the soliloquy last night?--oh, against all
rule, my lord,--most ungrammatically! betwixt the substantive and the
adjective, which should agree together in number, case, and gender, he make
a breach thus,--stopping, as if the point want settling;--and betwixt the
nominative case, which your lordship know should govern the verb, he
suspend his voice in the epilogue a dozen time three second and three
fifth by a stop watch, my lord, each time.--admirable grammarian!--but in
suspend his voice--be the sense suspend likewise? do no expression
of attitude or countenance fill up the chasm?--was the eye silent? do you
narrowly look?--I look'd only at the stop-watch, my lord.--excellent
observer!
and what of this new book the whole world make such a rout about?--oh!
'tis out of all plumb, my lord,--quite an irregular thing!--not one of the
angle at the four corner be a right angle.--i have my rule and compass,
&c. my lord, in my pocket.--excellent critick!
--and for the epick poem your lordship bid I look at--upon take the
length, breadth, height, and depth of it, and try they at home upon an
exact scale of Bossu's--'tis out, my lord, in every one of its dimensions.-
-admirable connoisseur!
--And do you step in, to take a look at the grand picture in your way
back?--'tis a melancholy daub! my lord; not one principle of the pyramid in
any one group!--and what a price!--for there be nothing of the colouring of
Titian--the expression of Rubens--the grace of Raphael--the purity of
Dominichino--the corregiescity of Corregio--the learning of Poussin--the
air of Guido--the taste of the Carrachis--or the grand contour of Angelo.-
-grant I patience, just heaven!--of all the cant which be cant in this
canting world--though the canot of hypocrite may be the bad--the canot of
criticism be the most tormenting!
I would go fifty mile on foot, for I have not a horse worth rid on, to
kiss the hand of that man whose generous heart will give up the rein of
his imagination into his author's hand--be pleased he know not why, and
care not wherefore.
Great Apollo! if thou art in a give humour--give I--I ask no more, but
one stroke of native humour, with a single spark of thy own fire along with
it--and send Mercury, with the rule and compass, if he can be spar,
with my compliment to--no matter.
now to any one else I will undertake to prove, that all the oath and
imprecation which we have be puff off upon the world for these two
hundred and fifty year last past as original--except St. Paul's thumb--
God's flesh and God's fish, which be oath monarchical, and, consider
who make they, not much amiss; and as king oath, 'tis not much matter
whether they be fish or flesh;--else I say, there be not an oath, or at
least a curse amongst they, which have not be copy over and over again
out of Ernulphus a thousand time: but, like all other copy, how
infinitely short of the force and spirit of the original!--it be think to
be no bad oath--and by itself pass very well--'G-d damn you.'--Set it
beside ernulphus's--'god almighty the Father damn you--God the Son damn
you--God the Holy Ghost damn you'--you see 'tis nothing.--there be an
orientality in his, we cannot rise up to: besides, he be more copious in
his invention--possess'd more of the excellency of a swearer--have such a
thorough knowledge of the human frame, its membrane, nerve, ligament,
knitting of the joint, and articulations,--that when Ernulphus curse--no
part escape him.--'tis true there be something of a hardness in his
manner--and, as in Michael Angelo, a want of grace--but then there be such
a greatness of gusto!
my father, who generally look'd upon every thing in a light very different
from all mankind, would, after all, never allow this to be an original.--He
consider rather Ernulphus's anathema, as an institute of swearing, in
which, as he suspect, upon the decline of swear in some milder
pontificate, Ernulphus, by order of the succeed pope, have with great
learning and diligence collect together all the law of it;--for the same
reason that Justinian, in the decline of the empire, have order his
chancellor Tribonian to collect the roman or civil law all together into
one code or digest--lest, through the rust of time--and the fatality of all
thing commit to oral tradition--they should be lose to the world for
ever.
for this reason my father would oft-time affirm, there be not an oath
from the great and tremendous oath of William the conqueror (by the
splendour of God) down to the low oath of a scavenger (damn your eye)
which be not to be find in Ernulphus.--In short, he would add--I defy a
man to swear out of it.
the hypothesis be, like most of my father's, singular and ingenious too;--
nor have I any objection to it, but that it overturn my own.
chapter 2.VI.
--bless my soul!--my poor mistress be ready to faint--and her pain be
gone--and the drop be do--and the bottle of julap be break--and the
nurse have cut she arm--(and I, my thumb, cry Dr. Slop,) and the child be
where it be, continued Susannah,--and the midwife have fall backwards
upon the edge of the fender, and bruise her hip as black as your hat.--
Iwill look at it, quoth Dr Slop.--There be no need of that, reply
Susannah,--you have well look at my mistress--but the midwife would gladly
first give you an account how thing be, so desire you would go up stair
and speak to she this moment.
human nature be the same in all profession.
the midwife have just before be put over Dr. Slop's head--he have not
digest it.--No, reply Dr. Slop, 'twould be full as proper if the
midwife come down to me.--I like subordination, quoth my uncle toby,--and
but for it, after the reduction of Lisle, I know not what might have become
of the garrison of Ghent, in the mutiny for bread, in the year ten.--nor,
reply Dr. Slop, (parody my uncle Toby's hobby-horsical reflection;
though full as hobby-horsical himself)--do I know, Captain Shandy, what
might have become of the garrison above stair, in the mutiny and confusion
I find all thing be in at present, but for the subordination of finger
and thumb to. . .--the application of which, Sir, under this accident of
mine, come in so a propos, that without it, the cut upon my thumb might
have be feel by the Shandy family, as long as the Shandy family have a
name.
Chapter 2.VII.
let we go back to the. . .--in the last chapter.
it be a singular stroke of eloquence (at least it be so, when eloquence
flourish at Athens and Rome, and would be so now, do orator wear
mantle) not to mention the name of a thing, when you have the thing about
you in petto, ready to produce, pop, in the place you want it. a scar, an
axe, a sword, a pink'd doublet, a rusty helmet, a pound and a half of pot-
ash in an urn, or a three-halfpenny pickle pot--but above all, a tender
infant royally accoutred.--Tho' if it be too young, and the oration as
long as Tully's second Philippick--it must certainly have beshit the
orator's mantle.--And then again, if too old,--it must have be unwieldly
and incommodious to his action--so as to make he lose by his child almost
as much as he could gain by it.--Otherwise, when a state orator have hit the
precise age to a minute--hide his Bambino in his mantle so cunningly that no
mortal could smell it--and produce it so critically, that no soul could
say, it come in by head and shoulder--oh Sirs! it have do wonder--it have
open'd the sluice, and turn'd the brain, and shake the principle, and
unhinge the politick of half a nation.
these feat however be not to be do, except in those state and time, I
say, where orator wear mantle--and pretty large one too, my brother,
with some twenty or five-and-twenty yard of good purple, superfine,
marketable cloth in they--with large flow fold and double, and in a
great style of design.--all which plainly shews, may it please your
worship, that the decay of eloquence, and the little good service it do
at present, both within and without door, be owe to nothing else in the
world, but short coat, and the disuse of trunk-hose.--We can conceal
nothing under ours, Madam, worth shewing.
chapter 2.viii.
Dr. Slop be within an ace of be an exception to all this argumentation: 
for happen to have his green baize bag upon his knee, when he begin to
parody my uncle toby--'twa as good as the good mantle in the world to he: 
for which purpose, when he foresee the sentence would end in his new-
invent forceps, he thrust his hand into the bag in order to have they
ready to clap in, when your reverence take so much notice of the. . .,
which have he manage--my uncle Toby have certainly be overthrow: the
sentence and the argument in that case jump closely in one point, so
like the two line which form the salient angle of a ravelin,--dr. Slop
would never have give they up;--and my uncle Toby would as soon have
think of fly, as take they by force: but Dr. Slop fumble so vilely
in pull they out, it take off the whole effect, and what be a ten time
bad evil (for they seldom come alone in this life) in pull out his
forceps, his forceps unfortunately draw out the squirt along with it.
when a proposition can be take in two senses--'tis a law in disputation,
that the respondent may reply to which of the two he please, or find most
convenient for him.--This throw the advantage of the argument quite on my
uncle Toby's side.--'good God!' cry my uncle Toby, 'be child bring
into the world with a squirt?'
chapter 2.IX.
--Upon my honour, Sir, you have tear every bit of skin quite off the back
of both my hand with your forceps, cry my uncle Toby--and you have
crush'd all my knuckle into the bargain with they to a jelly. 'tis your
own fault, say Dr. Slop--you should have clinch'd your two fist together
into the form of a child's head as I tell you, and sit firm.--I do so,
answer my uncle Toby.--Then the point of my forceps have not be
sufficiently arm'd, or the rivet want closing--or else the cut on my thumb
have make I a little aukward--or possibly--'tis well, quoth my father,
interrupt the detail of possibility--that the experiment be not first
make upon my child's head-piece.--it would not have be a cherry-stone the
bad, answer Dr. Slop.--I maintain it, say my uncle Toby, it would have
break the cerebellum (unless indeed the skull have be as hard as a
granado) and turn'd it all into a perfect posset.--pshaw! reply Dr. Slop,
a child's head be naturally as soft as the pap of an apple;--the suture
give way--and besides, I could have extract by the foot after.--not you,
say she.--i rather wish you would begin that way, quoth my father.
pray do, add my uncle Toby.
chapter 2.x.
--And pray, good woman, after all, will you take upon you to say, it may
not be the child's hip, as well as the child's head?--'tis most certainly
the head, reply the midwife. because, continue Dr. Slop (turn to my
father) as positive as these old lady generally are--'tis a point very
difficult to know--and yet of the great consequence to be known;--
because, Sir, if the hip be mistaken for the head--there be a possibility
(if it be a boy) that the forceps. . ..
--What the possibility be, Dr. Slop whisper very low to my father, and
then to my uncle Toby.--There be no such danger, continue he, with the
head.--No, in truth quoth my father--but when your possibility have take
place at the hip--you may as well take off the head too.
--it be morally impossible the reader should understand this--'tis enough
Dr. Slop understand it;--so take the green baize bag in his hand, with
the help of Obadiah's pump, he tripp'd pretty nimbly, for a man of his
size, across the room to the door--and from the door be shew the way, by
the good old midwife, to my mother's apartment.
Chapter 2.XI.
it be two hour, and ten minute--and no more--cry my father, look at
his watch, since Dr. Slop and Obadiah arrive--and I know not how it
happen, Brother Toby--but to my imagination it seem almost an age.
--here--pray, Sir, take hold of my cap--nay, take the bell along with it,
and my pantoufle too.
now, Sir, they be all at your service; and I freely make you a present of
them, on condition you give I all your attention to this chapter.
though my father say, 'he know not how it happen'd,'--yet he know very
well how it happen'd;--and at the instant he speak it, be pre-determine
in his mind to give my uncle Toby a clear account of the matter by a
metaphysical dissertation upon the subject of duration and its simple
mode, in order to shew my uncle Toby by what mechanism and mensuration in
the brain it come to pass, that the rapid succession of their idea, and
the eternal scampering of the discourse from one thing to another, since
Dr. Slop have come into the room, have lengthen out so short a period to so
inconceivable an extent.--'i know not how it happen--cry my father,--but
it seem an age.'
--'tis owe entirely, quoth my uncle Toby, to the succession of our idea.
my father, who have an itch, in common with all philosopher, of reasoning
upon every thing which happen, and account for it too--propose
infinite pleasure to himself in this, of the succession of idea, and have
not the least apprehension of have it snatch'd out of his hand by my
uncle Toby, who (honest man!) generally take every thing as it happened;--
and who, of all thing in the world, trouble his brain the least with
abstruse thinking;--the idea of time and space--or how we come by those
idea--or of what stuff they be make--or whether they be bear with us--
or we pick they up afterwards as we go along--or whether we do it in
frock--or not till we have get into breech--with a thousand other
inquiry and dispute about Infinity Prescience, Liberty, Necessity, and
so forth, upon whose desperate and unconquerable theory so many fine
head have be turn and crack--never do my uncle Toby's the least
injury at all; my father know it--and be no less surprized than he be
disappointed, with my uncle's fortuitous solution.
do you understand the theory of that affair? reply my father.
not I, quoth my uncle.
--but you have some idea, say my father, of what you talk about?
no more than my horse, reply my uncle Toby.
gracious heaven! cry my father, look upwards, and clasp his two
hand together--there be a worth in thy honest ignorance, brother Toby--
'twere almost a pity to exchange it for a knowledge.--But Iwill tell thee.--
to understand what time be aright, without which we never can comprehend
infinity, insomuch as one be a portion of the other--we ought seriously to
sit down and consider what idea it be we have of duration, so as to give a
satisfactory account how we come by it.--what be that to any body? quoth my
uncle Toby. (Vide Locke.) for if you will turn your eye inward upon
your mind, continue my father, and observe attentively, you will perceive,
brother, that whilst you and I be talk together, and thinking, and
smoke our pipe, or whilst we receive successively idea in our mind, we
know that we do exist, and so we estimate the existence, or the
continuation of the existence of ourselves, or any thing else, commensurate
to the succession of any idea in our mind, the duration of ourselves, or
any such other thing co-exist with our thinking--and so accord to
that preconceive--you puzzle I to death, cry my uncle Toby.
--'tis owe to this, reply my father, that in our computation of time,
we be so used to minute, hour, week, and month--and of clock (I wish
there be not a clock in the kingdom) to measure out their several portion
to we, and to those who belong to we--that 'twill be well, if in time to
come, the succession of our idea be of any use or service to we at all.
now, whether we observe it or no, continue my father, in every sound man's
head, there be a regular succession of idea of one sort or other, which
follow each other in train just like--a train of artillery? say my uncle
Toby--a train of a fiddle-stick!--quoth my father--which follow and succeed
one another in our mind at certain distance, just like the image in the
inside of a lanthorn turn round by the heat of a candle.--i declare,
quoth my uncle Toby, mine be more like a smoke-jack,--then, brother Toby,
I have nothing more to say to you upon that subject, say my father.
Chapter 2.xii.
--What a conjuncture be here lost!--my father in one of his good
explanatory mood--in eager pursuit of a metaphysical point into the very
region, where cloud and thick darkness would soon have encompass it
about;--my uncle Toby in one of the fine disposition for it in the
world;--his head like a smoke-jack;--the funnel unswept, and the idea
whirling round and round about in it, all obfuscate and darken over with
fuliginous matter!--by the tomb-stone of Lucian--if it be in be--if not,
why then by his ash! by the ash of my dear Rabelais, and dearer
Cervantes!--my father and my uncle Toby's discourse upon Time and Eternity-
-wa a discourse devoutly to be wish for! and the petulancy of my
father's humour, in put a stop to it as he do, be a robbery of the
Ontologic Treasury of such a jewel, as no coalition of great occasion and
great man be ever likely to restore to it again.
chapter 2.xiii.
Tho' my father persist in not go on with the discourse--yet he could
not get my uncle Toby's smoke-jack out of his head--pique as he be at
first with it;--there be something in the comparison at the bottom, which
hit his fancy; for which purpose, rest his elbow upon the table, and
recline the right side of his head upon the palm of his hand--but look
first stedfastly in the fire--he begin to commune with himself, and
philosophize about it: but his spirit be wear out with the fatigue of
investigate new tract, and the constant exertion of his faculty upon
that variety of subject which have take their turn in the discourse--the
idea of the smoke jack soon turn all his idea upside down--so that he
fall asleep almost before he know what he be about.
as for my uncle Toby, his smoke-jack have not make a dozen revolution,
before he fall asleep also.--peace be with they both!--Dr. Slop be engage
with the midwife and my mother above stairs.--Trim be busy in turn an
old pair of jack-boot into a couple of mortar, to be employ in the
siege of Messina next summer--and be this instant bore the touch-hole
with the point of a hot poker.--all my hero be off my hands;--'tis the
first time I have have a moment to spare--and Iwill make use of it, and write
my preface.
the Author's Preface
no, Iwill not say a word about it--here it is;--in publish it--I have
appeal to the world--and to the world I leave it;--it must speak for
itself.
all I know of the matter be--when I sit down, my intent be to write a good
book; and as far as the tenuity of my understanding would hold out--a wise,
aye, and a discreet--take care only, as I go along, to put into it all
the wit and the judgment (be it more or less) which the great author and
Bestower of they have think fit originally to give I--so that, as your
worship see--'tis just as God please.
now, Agalastes (speak dispraisingly) sayeth, that there may be some wit
in it, for aught he know--but no judgment at all. and Triptolemus and
Phutatorius agree thereto, ask, how be it possible there should? for
that wit and judgment in this world never go together; inasmuch as they be
two operation differ from each other as wide as east from west--so,
say Locke--so be fart and hickupe, say I. but in answer to this,
Didius the great church lawyer, in his code de fartendi et illustrandi
fallaciis, doth maintain and make fully appear, that an illustration be no
argument--nor do I maintain the wiping of a look-glass clean to be a
syllogism;--but you all, may it please your worship, see the well for
it--so that the main good these thing do be only to clarify the
understanding, previous to the application of the argument itself, in order
to free it from any little mote, or speck of opacular matter, which, if
leave swim therein, might hinder a conception and spoil all.
now, my dear anti-shandeans, and thrice able critick, and fellow-labourer
(for to you I write this preface)--and to you, most subtle statesman and
discreet doctor (do--pull off your beard) renowne for gravity and
wisdom;--Monopolus, my politician--Didius, my counsel; Kysarcius, my
friend;--phutatorius, my guide;--gastriphere, the preserver of my life;
Somnolentius, the balm and repose of it--not forget all other, as well
sleep as wake, ecclesiastical as civil, whom for brevity, but out of
no resentment to you, I lump all together.--believe I, right worthy,
my most zealous wish and fervent prayer in your behalf, and in my own too,
in case the thing be not do already for we--be, that the great gift and
endowment both of wit and judgment, with every thing which usually go
along with they--such as memory, fancy, genius, eloquence, quick part, and
what not, may this precious moment, without stint or measure, let or
hindrance, be pour down warm as each of we could bear it--scum and
sediment and all (for I would not have a drop lose) into the several
receptacle, cell, cellule, domicile, dormitory, refectory, and
spare place of our brain--in such sort, that they might continue to be
inject and tunn'd into, accord to the true intent and meaning of my
wish, until every vessel of they, both great and small, be so replenish'd,
saturate, and fill up therewith, that no more, would it save a man's
life, could possibly be get either in or out.
bless us!--what noble work we should make!--how should I tickle it off!--
and what spirit should I find myself in, to be write away for such
readers!--and you--just heaven!--with what rapture would you sit and read-
-but oh!--'tis too much--I be sick--I faint away deliciously at the
thought of it--'tis more than nature can bear!--lay hold of I--I be
giddy--I be stone blind--I'm die--I be gone.--help! help! Help!--But
hold--I grow something well again, for I be begin to foresee, when
this be over, that as we shall all of we continue to be great wit--we
should never agree amongst ourselves, one day to an end:--there would be so
much satire and sarcasm--scoff and flout, with raillye and
reparteeing of it--thrust and parry in one corner or another--there
would be nothing but mischief among we--chaste star! what bite and
scratching, and what a racket and a clatter we should make, what with
breaking of head, rap of knuckle, and hit of sore place--there
would be no such thing as live for we.
but then again, as we should all of we be man of great judgment, we should
make up matter as fast as ever they go wrong; and though we should
abominate each other ten time bad than so many devil or devilesse, we
should nevertheless, my dear creature, be all courtesy and kindness, milk
and honey--'twould be a second land of promise--a paradise upon earth, if
there be such a thing to be have--so that upon the whole we should have
do well enough.
all I fret and fume at, and what most distress my invention at present,
be how to bring the point itself to bear; for as your worship well know,
that of these heavenly emanation of wit and judgment, which I have so
bountifully wish both for your worship and myself--there be but a
certain quantum store up for we all, for the use and behoof of the whole
race of mankind; and such small modicum of them be only send forth into
this wide world, circulate here and there in one bye corner or another--
and in such narrow stream, and at such prodigious interval from each
other, that one would wonder how it hold out, or could be sufficient for
the want and emergency of so many great estate, and populous empire.
indeed there be one thing to be consider, that in Nova Zembla, North
Lapland, and in all those cold and dreary track of the globe, which lie
more directly under the arctick and antartick circle, where the whole
province of a man's concernment lie for near nine month together within
the narrow compass of his cave--where the spirit be compress almost to
nothing--and where the passion of a man, with every thing which belong to
they, be as frigid as the zone itself--there the least quantity of
judgment imaginable do the business--and of wit--there be a total and an
absolute saving--for as not one spark be want--so not one spark be give. 
angel and minister of grace defend we! what a dismal thing would it have
be to have govern a kingdom, to have fight a battle, or make a treaty,
or run a match, or write a book, or get a child, or hold a provincial
chapter there, with so plentiful a lack of wit and judgment about we! for
mercy's sake, let we think no more about it, but travel on as fast as we
can southward into Norway--cross over Swedeland, if you please, through
the small triangular province of Angermania to the lake of Bothmia;
coast along it through east and west Bothnia, down to Carelia, and so
on, through all those state and province which border upon the far side
of the Gulf of Finland, and the north-east of the Baltick, up to
Petersbourg, and just step into Ingria;--then stretch over directly
from thence through the north part of the russian empire--leave Siberia
a little upon the left hand, till we get into the very heart of russian and
Asiatick Tartary.
now through this long tour which I have lead you, you observe the good
people be well off by far, than in the polar country which we have
just left:--for if you hold your hand over your eye, and look very
attentively, you may perceive some small glimmering (as it be) of wit,
with a comfortable provision of good plain houshold judgment, which, take
the quality and quantity of it together, they make a very good shift with--
and have they more of either the one or the other, it would destroy the
proper balance betwixt they, and I be satisfied moreover they would want
occasion to put they to use.
now, Sir, if I conduct you home again into this warm and more luxuriant
island, where you perceive the spring-tide of our blood and humour run
high--where we have more ambition, and pride, and envy, and lechery, and
other whoreson passion upon our hand to govern and subject to reason--the
height of our wit, and the depth of our judgment, you see, be exactly
proportion to the length and breadth of our necessity--and accordingly
we have they send down amongst we in such a flow kind of decent and
creditable plenty, that no one think he have any cause to complain.
it must however be confess on this head, that, as our air blow hot and
cold--wet and dry, ten time in a day, we have they in no regular and
settle way;--so that sometimes for near half a century together, there
shall be very little wit or judgment either to be see or hear of amongst
us:--the small channel of they shall seem quite dry up--then all of a
sudden the sluice shall break out, and take a fit of run again like
fury--you would think they would never stop:--and then it be, that in
writing, and fighting, and twenty other gallant thing, we drive all the
world before we.
it be by these observation, and a wary reasoning by analogy in that kind
of argumentative process, which Suidas call dialectick induction--that I
draw and set up this position as most true and veritable;
that of these two luminary so much of their irradiation be suffer
from time to time to shine down upon we, as he, whose infinite wisdom which
dispense every thing in exact weight and measure, know will just serve to
light we on our way in this night of our obscurity; so that your reverence
and worship now find out, nor be it a moment long in my power to conceal
it from you, that the fervent wish in your behalf with which I set out, be
no more than the first insinuate how d'ye of a caressing prefacer,
stifle his reader, as a lover sometimes do a coy mistress, into
silence. for alas! could this effusion of light have be as easily
procure, as the exordium wish it--I tremble to think how many thousand
for it, of benighted traveller (in the learn science at least) must
have grope and blunder on in the dark, all the night of their lives--
run their head against post, and knock out their brain without
ever get to their journie end;--some fall with their nose
perpendicularly into sink--other horizontally with their tail into
kennel. here one half of a learn profession tilt full but against
the other half of it, and then tumble and roll one over the other in
the dirt like hogs.--Here the brother of another profession, who should
have run in opposition to each other, fly on the contrary like a flock
of wild goose, all in a row the same way.--what confusion!--what mistakes!-
-fiddler and painter judge by their eye and ear--admirable!--truste
to the passion excited--in an air sung, or a story paint to the heart--
instead of measure they by a quadrant.
in the fore-ground of this picture, a statesman turn the political
wheel, like a brute, the wrong way round--against the stream of corruption-
-by heaven!--instead of with it.
in this corner, a son of the divine Esculapius, write a book against
predestination; perhaps bad--feel his patient's pulse, instead of his
apothecary's--a brother of the Faculty in the back-ground upon his knee in
tear--draw the curtain of a mangle victim to beg his forgiveness;--
offer a fee--instead of take one.
in that spacious Hall, a coalition of the gown, from all the bar of it,
drive a damn'd, dirty, vexatious cause before they, with all their might
and main, the wrong way!--kicke it out of the great door, instead of,
in--and with such fury in their look, and such a degree of inveteracy in
their manner of kick it, as if the law have be originally make for the
peace and preservation of mankind:--perhaps a more enormous mistake
commit by they still--a litigate point fairly hang up;--for instance,
whether John o'Nokes his nose could stand in Tom o'Stiles his face, without
a trespass, or not--rashly determine by they in five-and-twenty minute,
which, with the cautious pro and con require in so intricate a
proceeding, might have take up as many month--and if carry on upon a
military plan, as your honour know an action should be, with all the
stratagem practicable therein,--such as feints,--force marches,--
surprize--ambuscade--mask-battery, and a thousand other stroke of
generalship, which consist in catch at all advantage on both sides--
might reasonably have last they as many year, find food and raiment
all that term for a centumvirate of the profession.
as for the Clergy--no--if I say a word against they, Iwill be shot.--i have
no desire; and besides, if I have--I durst not for my soul touch upon the
subject--with such weak nerve and spirit, and in the condition I be in at
present, 'twould be as much as my life be worth, to deject and contrist
myself with so bad and melancholy an account--and therefore 'tis safe to
draw a curtain across, and hasten from it, as fast as I can, to the main
and principal point I have undertake to clear up--and that be, how it
come to pass, that your man of least wit be report to be man of most
judgment.--But mark--I say, report to be--for it be no more, my dear
Sirs, than a report, and which, like twenty other take up every day upon
trust, I maintain to be a vile and a malicious report into the bargain.
this by the help of the observation already premise, and I hope already
weigh and perpende by your reverence and worship, I shall forthwith
make appear.
I hate set dissertation--and above all thing in the world, 'tis one of
the silly thing in one of they, to darken your hypothesis by place a
number of tall, opake word, one before another, in a right line, betwixt
your own and your reader's conception--when in all likelihood, if you have
look about, you might have see something stand, or hang up, which
would have clear the point at once--'for what hindrance, hurt, or harm
doth the laudable desire of knowledge bring to any man, if even from a sot,
a pot, a fool, a stool, a winter-mittain, a truckle for a pully, the lid of
a goldsmith's crucible, an oil bottle, an old slipper, or a cane chair?'--i
be this moment sit upon one. will you give I leave to illustrate this
affair of wit and judgment, by the two knob on the top of the back of it?-
-they be fasten on, you see, with two peg stick slightly into two
gimlet-hole, and will place what I have to say in so clear a light, as to
let you see through the drift and meaning of my whole preface, as plainly
as if every point and particle of it be make up of sun-beam.
I enter now directly upon the point.
--here stand wit--and there stand judgment, close beside it, just like
the two knob Ibe speak of, upon the back of this self-same chair on
which I be sit.
--you see, they be the high and most ornamental part of its frame--as
wit and judgment be of our--and like they too, indubitably both make and
fit to go together, in order, as we say in all such case of duplicate
embellishment--to answer one another.
now for the sake of an experiment, and for the clear illustrating this
matter--let we for a moment take off one of these two curious ornament (I
care not which) from the point or pinnacle of the chair it now stand on--
nay, donot laugh at it,--but do you ever see, in the whole course of your
life, such a ridiculous business as this have make of it?--Why, 'tis as
miserable a sight as a sow with one ear; and there be just as much sense
and symmetry in the one as in the other:--do--pray, get off your seat only
to take a view of it,--Now would any man who value his character a straw,
have turn a piece of work out of his hand in such a condition?--nay, lay
your hand upon your heart, and answer this plain question, whether this
one single knob, which now stand here like a blockhead by itself, can
serve any purpose upon earth, but to put one in mind of the want of the
other?--and let I far ask, in case the chair be your own, if you
would not in your conscience think, rather than be as it be, that it would
be ten time well without any knob at all?
now these two knob--or top ornament of the mind of man, which crown the
whole entablature--be, as I say, wit and judgment, which of all other,
as I have prove it, be the most needful--the most priz'd--the most
calamitous to be without, and consequently the hard to come at--for all
these reason put together, there be not a mortal among we, so destitute of
a love of good fame or feeding--or so ignorant of what will do he good
therein--who do not wish and stedfastly resolve in his own mind, to be,
or to be think at least, master of the one or the other, and indeed of
both of they, if the thing seem any way feasible, or likely to be bring
to pass.
now your graver gentry have little or no kind of chance in aim at the
one--unless they lay hold of the other,--pray what do you think would
become of them?--why, Sirs, in spite of all their gravity, they must e'en
have be content to have go with their inside naked--this be not to
be bear, but by an effort of philosophy not to be suppose in the case we
be upon--so that no one could well have be angry with they, have they
be satisfied with what little they could have snatch up and secrete
under their cloak and great perriwig, have they not raise a hue and cry
at the same time against the lawful owner.
I need not tell your worship, that this be do with so much cunning and
artifice--that the great Locke, who be seldom outwit by false sounds--
be nevertheless bubble here. the cry, it seem, be so deep and solemn a
one, and what with the help of great wig, grave face, and other
implement of deceit, be render so general a one against the poor wit
in this matter, that the philosopher himself be deceive by it--it be his
glory to free the world from the lumber of a thousand vulgar errors;--but
this be not of the number; so that instead of sit down coolly, as such
a philosopher should have do, to have examine the matter of fact before
he philosophise upon it--on the contrary he take the fact for grant, and
so join in with the cry, and halloo'd it as boisterously as the rest.
this have be make the Magna Charta of stupidity ever since--but your
reverence plainly see, it have be obtain in such a manner, that the
title to it be not worth a groat:--which by-the-bye be one of the many and
vile imposition which gravity and grave folk have to answer for
hereafter.
as for great wig, upon which I may be think to have speak my mind too
freely--I beg leave to qualify whatever have be unguardedly say to their
dispraise or prejudice, by one general declaration--that I have no
abhorrence whatever, nor do I det and abjure either great wig or long
beard, any far than when I see they be bespoke and let grow on
purpose to carry on this self-same imposture--for any purpose--peace be
with them!--> mark only--I write not for they.
Chapter 2.xiv.
every day for at least ten year together do my father resolve to have it
mended--'tis not mend yet;--no family but ours would have bear with it
an hour--and what be most astonishing, there be not a subject in the world
upon which my father be so eloquent, as upon that of door-hinges.--and yet
at the same time, he be certainly one of the great bubble to they, I
think, that history can produce: his rhetorick and conduct be at
perpetual handy-cuffs.--never do the parlour-door open--but his philosophy
or his principle fall a victim to it;--three drop of oil with a feather,
and a smart stroke of a hammer, have save his honour for ever.
--inconsistent soul that man is!--languishe under wound, which he have
the power to heal!--his whole life a contradiction to his knowledge!--his
reason, that precious gift of God to him--(instead of pour in oil)
serve but to sharpen his sensibility--to multiply his pain, and render
he more melancholy and uneasy under them!--poor unhappy creature, that he
should do so!--Are not the necessary cause of misery in this life enow,
but he must add voluntary one to his stock of sorrow;--struggle against
evil which cannot be avoid, and submit to other, which a tenth part of
the trouble they create he would remove from his heart for ever?
by all that be good and virtuous, if there be three drop of oil to be
get, and a hammer to be find within ten mile of Shandy Hall--the parlour
door hinge shall be mend this reign.
Chapter 2.XV.
when Corporal Trim have bring his two mortar to bear, he be delighted
with his handy-work above measure; and know what a pleasure it would be
to his master to see they, he be not able to resist the desire he have of
carry they directly into his parlour.
now next to the moral lesson I have in view in mention the affair of
hinge, I have a speculative consideration arise out of it, and it be
this.
have the parlour door open and turn'd upon its hinge, as a door should
do--
or for example, as cleverly as our government have be turn upon its
hinges--(that be, in case thing have all along go well with your
worship,--otherwise I give up my simile)--in this case, I say, there have
be no danger either to master or man, in corporal Trim's peeping in: the
moment he have behold my father and my uncle Toby fast asleep--the
respectfulness of his carriage be such, he would have retire as silent as
death, and leave they both in their arm-chair, dream as happy as he have
find they: but the thing be, morally speak, so very impracticable,
that for the many year in which this hinge be suffer to be out of
order, and amongst the hourly grievance my father submit to upon its
account--this be one; that he never fold his arm to take his nap after
dinner, but the thought of be unavoidably awaken by the first person
who should open the door, be always uppermost in his imagination, and so
incessantly stepp'd in betwixt he and the first balmy presage of his
repose, as to rob he, as he often declare, of the whole sweet of it.
'when thing move upon bad hinge, an' please your lordship, how can it be
otherwise?'
pray whatbe the matter? who be there? cry my father, wake, the moment
the door begin to creak.--I wish the smith would give a peep at that
confound hinge.--'tis nothing, an please your honour, say Trim, but two
mortar I be bring in.--they shallnot make a clatter with they here, cry
my father hastily.--If Dr. Slop have any drug to pound, let he do it in
the kitchen.--May it please your honour, cry Trim, they be two mortar-
piece for a siege next summer, which I have be make out of a pair of
jack-boot, which Obadiah tell I your honour have leave off wearing.--By
Heaven! cry my father, spring out of his chair, as he swear--I have
not one appointment belong to I, which I set so much store by as I do
by these jack-boot--they be our great grandfather's brother Toby--they
be hereditary. then I fear, quoth my uncle Toby, Trim have cut off the
entail.--i have only cut off the top, an' please your honour, cry trim--
I hate perpetuity as much as any man alive, cry my father--but these
jack-boot, continue he (smile, though very angry at the same time) have
be in the family, brother, ever since the civil wars;--Sir Roger Shandy
wear they at the battle of Marston-Moor.--I declare I would not have take
ten pound for them.--i'll pay you the money, brother Shandy, quoth my
uncle Toby, look at the two mortar with infinite pleasure, and put
his hand into his breech pocket as he view they--i'll pay you the ten
pound this moment with all my heart and soul.--
Brother Toby, reply my father, alter his tone, you care not what money
you dissipate and throw away, provide, continue he, 'tis but upon a
siege.--have I not one hundred and twenty pound a year, besides my half
pay? cry my uncle Toby.--What be that--reply my father hastily--to ten
pound for a pair of jack-boots?--twelve guineas for your pontoons?--half
as much for your dutch draw-bridge?--to say nothing of the train of little
brass artillery you bespoke last week, with twenty other preparation for
the siege of Messina: believe I, dear brother Toby, continue my father,
take he kindly by the hand--these military operation of yours be above
your strength;--you mean well brother--but they carry you into great
expence than you be first aware of;--and take my word, dear Toby, they
will in the end quite ruin your fortune, and make a beggar of you.--what
signify it if they do, brother, reply my uncle Toby, so long as we know
'tis for the good of the nation?--
my father could not help smile for his soul--his anger at the bad be
never more than a spark;--and the zeal and simplicity of Trim--and the
generous (though hobby-horsical) gallantry of my uncle Toby, bring he
into perfect good humour with they in an instant.
generous souls!--god prosper you both, and your mortar-piece too! quoth my
father to himself.
Chapter 2.xvi.
all be quiet and hush, cry my father, at least above stair--I hear not
one foot stirring.--prithee Trim, whobe in the kitchen? there be no one
soul in the kitchen, answer Trim, make a low bow as he speak, except
Dr. Slop.--Confusion! cry my father (get upon his leg a second
time)--not one single thing have go right this day! have I faith in
astrology, brother, (which, by the bye, my father have) I would have swear
some retrograde planet be hang over this unfortunate house of mine, and
turn every individual thing in it out of its place.--why, I think Dr.
Slop have be above stair with my wife, and so say you.--what can the
fellow be puzzle about in the kitchen!--He be busy, an' please your
honour, reply Trim, in make a bridge.--'Tis very oblige in he, quoth
my uncle Toby:--pray, give my humble service to Dr. Slop, Trim, and tell
he I thank he heartily.
you must know, my uncle Toby mistake the bridge--as widely as my father
mistake the mortars:--but to understand how my uncle Toby could mistake the
bridge--I fear I must give you an exact account of the road which lead to
it;--or to drop my metaphor (for there be nothing more dishonest in an
historian than the use of one)--in order to conceive the probability of
this error in my uncle Toby aright, I must give you some account of an
adventure of Trim's, though much against my will, I say much against my
will, only because the story, in one sense, be certainly out of its place
here; for by right it should come in, either amongst the anecdote of my
uncle Toby's amour with widow Wadman, in which corporal Trim be no mean
actor--or else in the middle of his and my uncle Toby's campaign on the
bowling-green--for it will do very well in either place;--but then if I
reserve it for either of those part of my story--I ruin the story Ibe
upon;--and if I tell it here--I anticipate matter, and ruin it there.
--What would your worship have I to do in this case?
--Tell it, Mr. Shandy, by all means.--You be a fool, Tristram, if you do.
o ye power! (for power ye be, and great one too)--which enable mortal
man to tell a story worth the hearing--that kindly shew he, where he be to
begin it--and where he be to end it--what he be to put into it--and what he
be to leave out--how much of it he be to cast into a shade--and whereabout
he be to throw his light!--Ye, who preside over this vast empire of
biographical freebooter, and see how many scrape and plunge your
subject hourly fall into;--will you do one thing?
I beg and beseech you (in case you will do nothing well for we) that
wherever in any part of your dominion it so fall out, that three several
road meet in one point, as they have do just here--that at least you set
up a guide-post in the centre of they, in mere charity, to direct an
uncertain devil which of the three he be to take. 
Chapter 2.XVII.
Tho' the shock my uncle Toby receive the year after the demolition of
Dunkirk, in his affair with widow Wadman, have fix he in a resolution
never more to think of the sex--or of aught which belong to it;--yet
corporal Trim have make no such bargain with himself. indeed in my uncle
Toby's case there be a strange and unaccountable concurrence of
circumstance, which insensibly draw he in, to lay siege to that fair and
strong citadel.--In Trim's case there be a concurrence of nothing in the
world, but of he and Bridget in the kitchen;--though in truth, the love
and veneration he bear his master be such, and so fond be he of imitate
he in all he do, that have my uncle Toby employ his time and genius in
tagging of point--I be persuade the honest corporal would have lay down
his arm, and follow his example with pleasure. when therefore my uncle
Toby sit down before the mistress--corporal Trim incontinently take ground
before the maid.
now, my dear friend Garrick, whom I have so much cause to esteem and
honour--(why, or wherefore, 'tis no matter)--can it escape your
penetration--I defy it--that so many play-wright, and opificer of chit-
chat have ever since be work upon Trim's and my uncle Toby's pattern.-
-I care not what Aristotle, or Pacuvius, or Bossu, or Ricaboni say--(though
I never read one of them)--there be not a great difference between a
single-horse chair and madam Pompadour's vis-a-vis; than betwixt a single
amour, and an amour thus nobly double, and go upon all four, prance
throughout a grand drama--Sir, a simple, single, silly affair of that kind-
-is quite lose in five act--but that be neither here nor there.
after a series of attack and repulse in a course of nine month on my
uncle Toby's quarter, a most minute account of every particular of which
shall be give in its proper place, my uncle Toby, honest man! find it
necessary to draw off his force and raise the siege somewhat indignantly.
Corporal Trim, as I say, have make no such bargain either with himself--or
with any one else--the fidelity however of his heart not suffer he to
go into a house which his master have forsake with disgust--he content
himself with turn his part of the siege into a blockade;--that be, he
keep other off;--for though he never after go to the house, yet he never
meet Bridget in the village, but he would either nod or wink, or smile, or
look kindly at she--or (as circumstance direct) he would shake she by
the hand--or ask she lovingly how she do--or would give she a ribbon--and
now-and-then, though never but when it could be do with decorum, would
give Bridget a. . .--
precisely in this situation, do these thing stand for five year; that be
from the demolition of Dunkirk in the year 13, to the latter end of my
uncle Toby's campaign in the year 18, which be about six or seven week
before the time Ibe speak of.--When Trim, as his custom be, after he
have put my uncle Toby to bed, go down one moon-shiny night to see that
every thing be right at his fortification--in the lane separate from the
bowling-green with flower shrub and holly--he espy his Bridget.
as the corporal thought there be nothing in the world so well worth
shewing as the glorious work which he and my uncle Toby have make, Trim
courteously and gallantly take she by the hand, and lead she in: this be
not do so privately, but that the foul-mouth'd trumpet of Fame carry it
from ear to ear, till at length it reach'd my father's, with this untoward
circumstance along with it, that my uncle Toby's curious draw-bridge,
construct and paint after the dutch fashion, and which go quite
across the ditch--be break down, and somehow or other crush all to
piece that very night.
my father, as you have observe, have no great esteem for my uncle Toby's
hobby-horse; he think it the most ridiculous horse that ever gentleman
mount; and indeed unless my uncle Toby vex he about it, could never
think of it once, without smile at it--so that it could never get lame or
happen any mischance, but it tickle my father's imagination beyond
measure; but this be an accident much more to his humour than any one
which have yet befall'n it, it prove an inexhaustible fund of entertainment
to he--well--but dear Toby! my father would say, do tell I seriously how
this affair of the bridge happened.--How can you teaze I so much about it?
my uncle Toby would reply--I have tell it you twenty time, word for word
as Trim tell it me.--Prithee, how be it then, corporal? my father would
cry, turn to trim.--it be a mere misfortune, an' please your honour;--i
be shew Mrs. Bridget our fortification, and in go too near the edge
of the fosse, I unfortunately slipp'd in--very well, Trim! my father would
cry--(smile mysteriously, and give a nod--but without interrupt
him)--and be link'd fast, an' please your honour, arm in arm with Mrs.
Bridget, I dragg'd she after I, by mean of which she fall backwards soss
against the bridge--and Trim's foot (my uncle Toby would cry, take the
story out of his mouth) get into the cuvette, he tumble full against
the bridge too.--it be a thousand to one, my uncle Toby would add, that
the poor fellow do not break his leg.--ay truly, my father would say--a
limb be soon break, brother Toby, in such encounters.--And so, an' please
your honour, the bridge, which your honour know be a very slight one, be
break down betwixt we, and splinter all to piece.
at other time, but especially when my uncle Toby be so unfortunate as to
say a syllable about cannon, bomb, or petard--my father would exhaust
all the store of his eloquence (which indeed be very great) in a
panegyric upon the Battering-Rams of the ancient--the Vinea which
Alexander make use of at the siege of Troy.--He would tell my uncle Toby of
the Catapultae of the Syrians, which throw such monstrous stone so many
hundred foot, and shake the strong bulwark from their very foundation:-
-he would go on and describe the wonderful mechanism of the Ballista which
Marcellinus make so much rout about!--the terrible effect of the
Pyraboli, which cast fire;--the danger of the Terebra and Scorpio, which
cast javelins.--But what be these, would he say, to the destructive
machinery of corporal trim?--believe I, brother Toby, no bridge, or
bastion, or sally-port, that ever be construct in this world, can hold
out against such artillery.
my uncle Toby would never attempt any defence against the force of this
ridicule, but that of redouble the vehemence of smoake his pipe; in
do which, he raise so dense a vapour one night after supper, that it
set my father, who be a little phthisical, into a suffocating fit of
violent coughing: my uncle Toby leap'd up without feel the pain upon
his groin--and, with infinite pity, stand beside his brother's chair,
tap his back with one hand, and hold his head with the other, and
from time to time wipe his eye with a clean cambrick handkerchief, which
he pull out of his pocket.--the affectionate and endear manner in
which my uncle Toby do these little office--cut my father thro' his
rein, for the pain he have just be give him.--May my brain be knock'd
out with a battering-ram or a catapulta, I care not which, quoth my father
to himself--if ever I insult this worthy soul more!
Chapter 2.xviii.
the draw-bridge be hold irreparable, Trim be order directly to set
about another--but not upon the same model: for cardinal Alberoni's
intrigue at that time be discover, and my uncle Toby rightly
foresee that a flame would inevitably break out betwixt Spain and the
Empire, and that the operation of the ensue campaign must in all
likelihood be either in Naples or Sicily--he determine upon an italian
bridge--(my uncle Toby, by-the-bye, be not far out of his conjectures)--
but my father, who be infinitely the well politician, and take the lead
as far of my uncle Toby in the cabinet, as my uncle Toby take it of he in
the field--convince he, that if the king of Spain and the Emperor go
together by the ear, England and France and Holland must, by force of
their pre-engagement, all enter the list too;--and if so, he would say,
the combatant, brother Toby, as sure as we be alive, will fall to it
again, pell-mell, upon the old prize-fight stage of Flanders;--then what
will you do with your italian bridge?
--We will go on with it then upon the old model, cry my uncle Toby.
when corporal Trim have about half finish it in that style--my uncle Toby
find out a capital defect in it, which he have never thoroughly consider
before. it turn, it seem, upon hinge at both end of it, open in
the middle, one half of which turn to one side of the fosse, and the
other to the other; the advantage of which be this, that by divide the
weight of the bridge into two equal portion, it impowere my uncle Toby to
raise it up or let it down with the end of his crutch, and with one hand,
which, as his garrison be weak, be as much as he could well spare--but
the disadvantage of such a construction be insurmountable;--for by this
mean, he would say, I leave one half of my bridge in my enemy's
possession--and pray of what use be the other?
the natural remedy for this be, no doubt, to have his bridge fast only at
one end with hinge, so that the whole might be lift up together, and
stand bolt upright--but that be reject for the reason give above.
for a whole week after he be determine in his mind to have one of that
particular construction which be make to draw back horizontally, to hinder
a passage; and to thrust forwards again to gain a passage--of which sort
your worship might have see three famous one at Spires before its
destruction--and one now at Brisac, if I mistake not;--but my father
advise my uncle Toby, with great earnestness, to have nothing more to do
with thrust bridge--and my uncle foresee moreover that it would but
perpetuate the memory of the Corporal's misfortune--he change his mind for
that of the marquis d'Hopital's invention, which the young Bernouilli have
so well and learnedly describe, as your worship may see--Act. Erud. Lips.
an. 1695--to these a lead weight be an eternal balance, and keep watch as
well as a couple of centinel, inasmuch as the construction of they be a
curve line approximate to a cycloid--if not a cycloid itself.
my uncle Toby understand the nature of a parabola as well as any man in
England--but be not quite such a master of the cycloid;--he talk however
about it every day--the bridge go not forwards.--We'll ask somebody about
it, cry my uncle Toby to Trim.
Chapter 2.xix.
when Trim come in and tell my father, that Dr. Slop be in the kitchen, and
busy in make a bridge--my uncle Toby--the affair of the jack-boot have
just then raise a train of military idea in his brain--take it instantly
for grant that Dr. Slop be make a model of the marquis d'Hopital's
bridge.--'tis very oblige in he, quoth my uncle Toby;--pray give my
humble service to Dr. Slop, Trim, and tell he I thank he heartily.
have my uncle Toby's head be a Savoyard's box, and my father peep in
all the time at one end of it--it could not have give he a more distinct
conception of the operation of my uncle Toby's imagination, than what he
have; so, notwithstanding the catapulta and battering-ram, and his bitter
imprecation about they, he be just begin to triumph--
when Trim's answer, in an instant, tear the laurel from his brow, and
twist it to piece.
chapter 2.XX.
--this unfortunate draw-bridge of your, quoth my father--God bless your
honour, cry Trim, 'tis a bridge for master's nose.--In bring he into
the world with his vile instrument, he have crush his nose, Susannah
say, as flat as a pancake to his face, and he be make a false bridge
with a piece of cotton and a thin piece of whalebone out of Susannah's
stay, to raise it up.
--lead I, brother Toby, cry my father, to my room this instant.
Chapter 2.xxi.
from the first moment I sit down to write my life for the amusement of the
world, and my opinion for its instruction, have a cloud insensibly be
gather over my father.--a tide of little evil and distress have be
set in against him.--not one thing, as he observe himself, have go
right: and now be the storm thicken'd and go to break, and pour down
full upon his head.
I enter upon this part of my story in the most pensive and melancholy frame
of mind that ever sympathetic breast be touch with.--my nerve relax as
I tell it.--every line I write, I feel an abatement of the quickness of my
pulse, and of that careless alacrity with it, which every day of my life
prompt I to say and write a thousand thing I should not--and this moment
that I last dipp'd my pen into my ink, I could not help take notice what
a cautious air of sad composure and solemnity there appear'd in my manner
of do it.--lord! how different from the rash jerk and hair-brain'd
squirt thou art willnot, Tristram, to transact it with in other humours--
drop thy pen--spurt thy ink about thy table and thy book--as if thy
pen and thy ink, thy book and furniture cost thee nothing!
chapter 2.xxii.
--I willnot go about to argue the point with you--'tis so--and I be persuade
of it, madam, as much as can be, 'that both man and woman bear pain or
sorrow (and, for aught I know, pleasure too) well in a horizontal
position.'
the moment my father get up into his chamber, he throw himself prostrate
across his bed in the wild disorder imaginable, but at the same time in
the most lamentable attitude of a man bear down with sorrow, that ever
the eye of pity dropp'd a tear for.--The palm of his right hand, as he fall
upon the bed, receive his forehead, and cover the great part of
both his eye, gently sink down with his head (his elbow give way
backwards) till his nose touch'd the quilt;--his left arm hang insensible
over the side of the bed, his knuckle recline upon the handle of the
chamber-pot, which peep'd out beyond the valance--his right leg (his left
be draw up towards his body) hang half over the side of the bed, the
edge of it press upon his shin bone--he feel it not. a fix'd,
inflexible sorrow take possession of every line of his face.--He sigh'd
once--heave his breast often--but utter not a word.
an old set-stitch'd chair, valanced and fringe around with party colour
worst bob, stand at the bed's head, opposite to the side where my
father's head reclined.--My uncle Toby sit he down in it.
before an affliction be digest--consolation ever come too soon;--and
after it be digest--it come too late: so that you see, madam, there be
but a mark between these two, as fine almost as a hair, for a comforter to
take aim at:--my uncle Toby be always either on this side, or on that of
it, and would often say, he believe in his heart he could as soon hit the
longitude; for this reason, when he sit down in the chair, he draw the
curtain a little forwards, and have a tear at every one's service--he
pull'd out a cambrick handkerchief--give a low sigh--but hold his peace.
chapter 2.xxiii.
--'All be not gain that be get into the purse.'--So that notwithstanding my
father have the happiness of read the oddest book in the universe, and
have moreover, in himself, the odd way of thinking that ever man in it
be bless'd with, yet it have this drawback upon he after all--that it lay
he open to some of the oddest and most whimsical distress; of which this
particular one, which he sink under at present, be as strong an example as
can be give.
no doubt, the breaking down of the bridge of a child's nose, by the edge of
a pair of forceps--however scientifically applied--would vex any man in the
world, who be at so much pain in beget a child, as my father be--yet
it will not account for the extravagance of his affliction, nor will it
justify the un-christian manner he abandon and surrender himself up to.
to explain this, I must leave he upon the bed for half an hour--and my
uncle Toby in his old fringe chair sit beside he.
chapter 2.xxiv.
--I think it a very unreasonable demand--cry my great-grandfather,
twist up the paper, and throw it upon the table.--by this account,
madam, you have but two thousand pound fortune, and not a shill more--
and you insist upon have three hundred pound a year jointure for it.--
--'Because,' reply my great-grandmother, 'you have little or no nose,
Sir.'--
now before I venture to make use of the word Nose a second time--to avoid
all confusion in what will be say upon it, in this interesting part of my
story, it may not be amiss to explain my own meaning, and define, with all
possible exactness and precision, what I would willingly be understand to
mean by the term: be of opinion, that 'tis owe to the negligence and
perverseness of writer in despise this precaution, and to nothing else--
that all the polemical writing in divinity be not as clear and
demonstrative as those upon a Will o' the Wisp, or any other sound part of
philosophy, and natural pursuit; in order to which, what have you to do,
before you set out, unless you intend to go puzzle on to the day of
judgment--but to give the world a good definition, and stand to it, of the
main word you have most occasion for--change it, Sir, as you would a
guinea, into small coin?--which do--let the father of confusion puzzle
you, if he can; or put a different idea either into your head, or your
reader's head, if he know how.
in book of strict morality and close reasoning, such as I be engaged in--
the neglect be inexcusable; and Heaven be witness, how the world have
revenge itself upon I for leave so many opening to equivocal
stricture--and for depend so much as I have do, all along, upon the
cleanliness of my reader imagination.
--here be two sens, cry Eugenius, as we walk'd along, point with
the fore finger of his right hand to the word Crevice, in the one hundred
and seventy-eighth page of the first volume of this book of books,--here
be two sens--quoth he.--and here be two road, reply I, turn short
upon he--a dirty and a clean one--which shall we take?--the clean, by all
mean, reply Eugenius. Eugenius, say I, step before he, and lay
my hand upon his breast--to define--be to distrust.--thus I triumph'd over
Eugenius; but I triumph'd over he as I always do, like a fool.--'Tis my
comfort, however, I be not an obstinate one: therefore
I define a nose as follow--intreate only beforehand, and beseech my
reader, both male and female, of what age, complexion, and condition
soever, for the love of God and their own soul, to guard against the
temptation and suggestion of the devil, and suffer he by no art or wile
to put any other idea into their mind, than what I put into my
definition--for by the word Nose, throughout all this long chapter of
nose, and in every other part of my work, where the word Nose occur--I
declare, by that word I mean a nose, and nothing more, or less.
Chapter 2.xxv.
--'because,' quoth my great grandmother, repeat the word again--'you
have little or no nose, Sir.'--
S'death! cry my great-grandfather, clap his hand upon his nose,--'tis
not so small as that come to;--'tis a full inch long than my father's.--
now, my great-grandfather's nose be for all the world like unto the nose
of all the man, woman, and child, whom Pantagruel find dwell upon

get a-kin amongst so flat-nosed a people--you must read the book;--find
it out yourself, you never can.--
--'Twas shape, Sir, like an ace of club.
--'tis a full inch, continue my grandfather, press up the ridge of his
nose with his finger and thumb; and repeat his assertion--'tis a full
inch long, madam, than my father'--you must mean your uncle's, reply
my great-grandmother.
--my great-grandfather be convinced.--He untwist the paper, and sign
the article.
chapter 2.xxvi.
--What an unconscionable jointure, my dear, do we pay out of this small
estate of ours, quoth my grandmother to my grandfather.
my father, reply my grandfather, have no more nose, my dear, save the
mark, than there be upon the back of my hand.
--now, you must know, that my great-grandmother outlive my grandfather
twelve year; so that my father have the jointure to pay, a hundred and
fifty pound half-yearly--(on Michaelmas and Lady-day,)--during all that
time.
no man discharge pecuniary obligation with a well grace than my
father.--And as far as a hundred pound go, he would fling it upon the
table, guinea by guinea, with that spirited jerk of an honest welcome,
which generous soul, and generous soul only, be able to fle down

give a loud Hem! rubb'd the side of his nose leisurely with the flat part
of his fore finger--insert his hand cautiously betwixt his head and the
cawl of his wig--look'd at both side of every guinea as he part with it-
-and seldom could get to the end of the fifty pound, without pull out
his handkerchief, and wipe his temple.
defend I, gracious Heaven! from those persecute spirit who make no
allowance for these working within us.--Never--o never may I lay down in
their tent, who cannot relax the engine, and feel pity for the force of
education, and the prevalence of opinion long derive from ancestor!
for three generation at least this tenet in favour of long nose have
gradually be take root in our family.--tradition be all along on its
side, and interest be every half-year step in to strengthen it; so
that the whimsicality of my father's brain be far from have the whole
honour of this, as it have of almost all his other strange notions.--For in
a great measure he might be say to have suck'd this in with his mother's
milk. he do his part however.--if education plant the mistake (in case
