he would often declare, in speak his thought upon the subject, that he
do not conceive how the great family in England could stand it out
against an uninterrupted succession of six or seven short noses.--And for
the contrary reason, he would generally add, that it must be one of the
great problem in civil life, where the same number of long and jolly
nose, follow one another in a direct line, do not raise and hoist it
up into the good vacancy in the kingdom.--He would often boast that the
Shandy family rank'd very high in king Harry the VIIIth's time, but owe
its rise to no state engine--he would say--but to that only;--but that,
like other family, he would add--it have feel the turn of the wheel, and
have never recover the blow of my great-grandfather's nose.--it be an ace
of club indeed, he would cry, shake his head--and as vile a one for an
unfortunate family as ever turn'd up trump.
--Fair and softly, gentle reader!--where be thy fancy carry thee!--if
there be truth in man, by my great-grandfather's nose, I mean the external
organ of smelling, or that part of man which stand prominent in his face--
and which painter say, in good jolly nose and well-proportion face,
should comprehend a full third--that be, measure downward from the
setting on of the hair.
--What a life of it have an author, at this pass!
chapter 2.xxvii.
it be a singular blessing, that nature have form'd the mind of man with the
same happy backwardness and renitency against conviction, which be observe
in old dogs--'of not learn new trick.'
what a shuttlecock of a fellow would the great philosopher that ever
exist be whisk'd into at once, do he read such book, and observe such
fact, and think such thought, as would eternally be make he change
side!
now, my father, as I tell you last year, detest all this--he pick'd up an
opinion, Sir, as a man in a state of nature pick up an apple.--it become
his own--and if he be a man of spirit, he would lose his life rather than
give it up.
I be aware that Didius, the great civilian, will contest this point; and
cry out against I, whence come this man's right to this apple? ex
confesso, he will say--thing be in a state of nature--the apple, be as
much Frank's apple as John's. pray, Mr. Shandy, what patent have he to shew
for it? and how do it begin to be his? be it, when he set his heart upon
it? or when he gather it? or when he chew'd it? or when he roast it? or
when he peel'd, or when he bring it home? or when he digested?--or when
he--?--for 'tis plain, Sir, if the first pick up of the apple, make it
not his--that no subsequent act could.
Brother Didius, Tribonius will answer--(now Tribonius the civilian and
church lawyer's beard be three inch and a half and three eighth
long than Didius his beard--I'm glad he take up the cudgel for I, so I
give myself no farth trouble about the answer.)--Brother Didius,
Tribonius will say, it be a decreed case, as you may find it in the
fragment of Gregorius and Hermogines's code, and in all the code from
Justinian's down to the code of Louis and Des Eaux--that the sweat of a
man's brow, and the exsudation of a man's brain, be as much a man's own
property as the breech upon his backside;--which say exsudation, &c.
be dropp'd upon the say apple by the labour of find it, and pick
it up; and be moreover indissolubly waste, and as indissolubly annex'd,
by the picker up, to the thing pick'd up, carry home, roasted, peel'd,
eaten, digest, and so on;--'tis evident that the gatherer of the apple,
in so do, have mix'd up something which be his own, with the apple which
be not his own, by which mean he have acquire a property;--or, in other
word, the apple be John's apple.
by the same learn chain of reason my father stand up for all his
opinion; he have spar no pain in pick they up, and the more they lay
out of the common way, the well still be his title.--No mortal claim
they; they have cost he moreover as much labour in cooking and digesting as
in the case above, so that they might well and truly be say to be of his
own good and chattels.--accordingly he hold fast by them, both by tooth and
claw--would fly to whatever he could lay his hand on--and, in a word,
would intrench and fortify they round with as many circumvallation and
breast-work, as my uncle Toby would a citadel.
there be one plaguy rub in the way of this--the scarcity of material to
make any thing of a defence with, in case of a smart attack; inasmuch as
few man of great genius have exercise their part in write book upon the
subject of great nose: by the trotting of my lean horse, the thing be
incredible! and I be quite lose in my understanding, when I be consider
what a treasure of precious time and talent together have be waste upon
bad subject--and how many million of book in all language and in all
possible type and binding, have be fabricate upon point not half so
much tend to the unity and peace-making of the world. what be to be
have, however, he set the great store by; and though my father would oft-
time sport with my uncle Toby's library--which, by-the-bye, be ridiculous
enough--yet at the very same time he do it, he collect every book and
treatise which have be systematically write upon nose, with as much care
as my honest uncle Toby have do those upon military architecture.--'tis
true, a much less table would have hold they--but that be not thy
transgression, my dear uncle.--
here--but why here--rather than in any other part of my story--I be not
able to tell:--but here it be--my heart stop I to pay to thee, my dear
uncle Toby, once for all, the tribute I owe thy goodness.--Here let I
thrust my chair aside, and kneel down upon the ground, whilst I be pour
forth the warm sentiment of love for thee, and veneration for the
excellency of thy character, that ever virtue and nature kindle in a
nephew's bosom.--peace and comfort rest for evermore upon thy head!--Thou
enviedst no man's comfort--insultedst no man's opinion--Thou blackenedst
no man's character--devouredst no man's bread: gently, with faithful Trim
behind thee, didst thou amble round the little circle of thy pleasure,
jostle no creature in thy way:--for each one's sorrow, thou hadst a
tear,--for each man's need, thou hadst a shilling.
whilst I be worth one, to pay a weeder--thy path from thy door to thy
bowl-green shall never be grow up.--whilst there be a rood and a half
of land in the Shandy family, thy fortification, my dear uncle Toby, shall
never be demolish'd.

my father's collection be not great, but to make amend, it be curious;

chapter 2.xxix.
of all the tract my father be at the pain to procure and study in

now donot let Satan, my dear girl, in this chapter, take advantage of any

chapter 2.xxx.
'Nihil I paenitet hujus nasi,' quoth Pamphagus;--that is--'My nose have
be the making of I.'--'Nec est cur poeniteat,' reply Cocles; that be,
'how the duce should such a nose fail?'
the doctrine, you see, be lay down by Erasmus, as my father wish it,
with the utmost plainness; but my father's disappointment be, in find
nothing more from so able a pen, but the bare fact itself; without any of
that speculative subtilty or ambidexterity of argumentation upon it, which
Heaven have bestow'd upon man on purpose to investigate truth, and fight for
she on all sides.--My father pish'd and pugh'd at first most terribly--'tis
worth something to have a good name. as the dialogue be of Erasmus, my
father soon come to himself, and read it over and over again with great
application, study every word and every syllable of it thro' and thro'
in its most strict and literal interpretation--he could still make nothing
of it, that way. Mayhap there be more mean, than be say in it, quoth my
father.--learned man, brother Toby, donot write dialogue upon long nose
for nothing.--I'll study the mystick and the allegorick sense--here be some
room to turn a man's self in, brother.
my father read on.--
now I find it needful to inform your reverence and worship, that besides
the many nautical us of long nose enumerate by Erasmus, the dialogist
affirmeth that a long nose be not without its domestic convenience also;
for that in a case of distress--and for want of a pair of bellow, it will
do excellently well, ad ixcitandum focum (to stir up the fire.)
Nature have be prodigal in her gift to my father beyond measure, and have
sow the seed of verbal criticism as deep within he, as she have do the
seed of all other knowledge--so that he have get out his penknife, and be
try experiment upon the sentence, to see if he could not scratch some
well sense into it.--i've get within a single letter, brother Toby, cry
my father, of Erasmus his mystic meaning.--you be near enough, brother,
reply my uncle, in all conscience.--pshaw! cry my father, scratch
on--I might as well be seven mile off.--I've do it--say my father,
snap his finger--see, my dear brother Toby, how I have mend the
sense.--But you have marr'd a word, reply my uncle Toby.--My father put
on his spectacle--bit his lip--and tear out the leaf in a passion.
chapter 2.XXXI.
o Slawkenbergius! thou faithful analyzer of my Disgrazias--thou sad
foreteller of so many of the whip and short turn which on one stage or
other of my life have come slap upon I from the shortness of my nose, and
no other cause, that I be conscious of.--Tell I, Slawkenbergius! what
secret impulse be it? what intonation of voice? whence come it? how do it
sound in thy ears?--art thou sure thou heard'st it?--which first cry out
to thee--go--go, Slawkenbergius! dedicate the labour of thy life--neglect
thy pastime--call forth all the power and faculty of thy nature--
macerate thyself in the service of mankind, and write a grand Folio for
they, upon the subject of their nose.
how the communication be convey into Slawkenbergius's sensorium--so that
Slawkenbergius should know whose finger touch'd the key--and whose hand it
be that blow the bellow--as Hafen Slawkenbergius have be dead and lay
in his grave above fourscore and ten year--we can only raise conjecture.
Slawkenbergius be play'd upon, for aught I know, like one of Whitefield's
disciple--that be, with such a distinct intelligence, Sir, of which of the
two master it be that have be practise upon his instrument--as to make
all reasoning upon it needless.
--for in the account which Hafen Slawkenbergius give the world of his
motif and occasion for writing, and spend so many year of his life

should have come first--but the bookbinder have most injudiciously place it
betwixt the analytical content of the book, and the book itself--he
inform his reader, that ever since he have arrive at the age of
discernment, and be able to sit down cooly, and consider within himself

understand, quoth Slawkenbergius, any thing--or rather what be what--and
could perceive that the point of long nose have be too loosely handle by
all who have go before;--have I Slawkenbergius, feel a strong impulse,
with a mighty and unresistible call within I, to gird up myself to this
undertaking.
and to do justice to Slawkenbergius, he have enter the list with a
strong lance, and take a much large career in it than any one man who
have ever enter it before he--and indeed, in many respect, deserve to
be en-nich'd as a prototype for all writer, of voluminous work at least,
to model their book by--for he have take in, Sir, the whole subject--
examine every part of it dialectically--then bring it into full day;
dilucidate it with all the light which either the collision of his own
natural part could strike--or the profound knowledge of the science
have impowere he to cast upon it--collate, collecting, and compiling--
begging, borrowing, and steal, as he go along, all that have be write
or wrangle thereupon in the school and portico of the learn: so that
Slawkenbergius his book may properly be consider, not only as a model--
but as a thorough-stitch Digest and regular institute of nose,
comprehend in it all that be or can be needful to be know about they.
for this cause it be that I forbear to speak of so many (otherwise)
valuable book and treatise of my father's collecting, write either, plump
upon nose--or collaterally touch them;--such for instance as Prignitz,
now lie upon the table before I, who with infinite learning, and from
the most candid and scholar-like examination of above four thousand
different skull, in upwards of twenty charnel-house in Silesia, which he
have rummage--have inform we, that the mensuration and configuration of
the osseous or bony part of human nose, in any give tract of country,
except Crim Tartary, where they be all crush'd down by the thumb, so that
no judgment can be form upon they--be much near alike, than the world
imagines;--the difference amongst they be, he say, a mere trifle, not
worth take notice of;--but that the size and jollity of every individual
nose, and by which one nose rank above another, and bear a high price,
be owe to the cartilaginous and muscular part of it, into whose duct
and sinuse the blood and animal spirit be impell'd and drive by the
warmth and force of the imagination, which be but a step from it (bat
the case of idiot, whom Prignitz, who have live many year in Turky,
suppose under the more immediate tutelage of Heaven)--it so happen, and
ever must, say Prignitz, that the excellency of the nose be in a direct
arithmetical proportion to the excellency of the wearer's fancy.
it be for the same reason, that is, because 'tis all comprehend in
Slawkenbergius, that I say nothing likewise of Scroderus (Andrea) who, all
the world know, set himself to oppugn Prignitz with great violence--
prove it in his own way, first logically, and then by a series of
stubborn fact, 'that so far be Prignitz from the truth, in affirm that
the fancy begat the nose, that on the contrary--the nose begat the fancy.'
--The learn suspect Scroderus of an indecent sophism in this--and
Prignitz cry out aloud in the dispute, that Scroderus have shift the
idea upon he--but Scroderus go on, maintain his thesis.
my father be just balance within himself, which of the two side he
should take in this affair; when Ambrose Paraeus decide it in a moment,
and by overthrow the system, both of Prignitz and Scroderus, drive my
father out of both side of the controversy at once.
be witness--
I donot acquaint the learn reader--in say it, I mention it only to
shew the learned, I know the fact myself--
that this Ambrose Paraeus be chief surgeon and nose-mender to Francis the
ninth of France, and in high credit with he and the two precede, or
succeed king (I know not which)--and that, except in the slip he make
in his story of Taliacotius's nose, and his manner of set they on--he
be esteem by the whole college of physician at that time, as more
know in matter of nose, than any one who have ever take they in hand.
now Ambrose Paraeus convince my father, that the true and efficient cause
of what have engage so much the attention of the world, and upon which
Prignitz and Scroderus have waste so much learning and fine part--be
neither this nor that--but that the length and goodness of the nose be
owe simply to the softness and flaccidity in the nurse's breast--as the
flatness and shortness of puisne nose be to the firmness and elastic
repulsion of the same organ of nutrition in the hale and lively--which,
tho' happy for the woman, be the undoing of the child, inasmuch as his
nose be so snubb'd, so rebuff'd, so rebate, and so refrigerate thereby,
as never to arrive ad mensuram suam legitimam;--but that in case of the
flaccidity and softness of the nurse or mother's breast--by sink into
it, quoth Paraeus, as into so much butter, the nose be comfort,
nourish'd, plump'd up, refresh'd, refocillate, and set a growing for ever.
I have but two thing to observe of Paraeus; first, that he prove and
explain all this with the utmost chastity and decorum of expression:--for
which may his soul for ever rest in peace!
and, secondly, that besides the system of Prignitz and Scroderus, which
Ambrose Paraeus his hypothesis effectually overthrew--it overthrow at the
same time the system of peace and harmony of our family; and for three day
together, not only embroil matter between my father and my mother, but
turn'd likewise the whole house and every thing in it, except my uncle
Toby, quite upside down.
such a ridiculous tale of a dispute between a man and his wife, never
surely in any age or country get vent through the key-hole of a street-
door.
my mother, you must know--but I have fifty thing more necessary to let you
know first--I have a hundred difficulty which I have promise to clear
up, and a thousand distress and domestick misadventure crowd in upon
I thick and threefold, one upon the neck of another. a cow break in
(tomorrow morning) to my uncle Toby's fortification, and eat up two
ration and a half of dry grass, tear up the sod with it, which face
his horn-work and cover way.--Trim insist upon be try by a court-
martial--the cow to be shoot--Slop to be crucifix'd--myself to be tristram'd
and at my very baptism make a martyr of;--poor unhappy devil that we all
are!--I want swaddling--but there be no time to be lose in exclamation--I
have leave my father lie across his bed, and my uncle Toby in his old
fringed chair, sit beside he, and promise I would go back to they in
half an hour; and five-and-thirty minute be laps'd already.--of all the
perplexity a mortal author be ever see in--this certainly be the
great, for I have Hafen Slawkenbergius's folio, Sir, to finish--a
dialogue between my father and my uncle Toby, upon the solution of
Prignitz, Scroderus, Ambrose Paraeus, Panocrates, and Grangousier to
relate--a tale out of Slawkenbergius to translate, and all this in five
minute less than no time at all;--such a head!--would to Heaven my enemy
only see the inside of it!
chapter 2.xxxii.
there be not any one scene more entertaining in our family--and to do it
justice in this point;--and I here put off my cap and lie it upon the table
close beside my ink-horn, on purpose to make my declaration to the world
concern this one article the more solemn--that I believe in my soul
(unless my love and partiality to my understanding blind I) the hand of
the supreme Maker and first designer of all thing never make or put a
family together (in that period at least of it which I have sit down to
write the story of)--where the character of it be cast or contrast
with so dramatick a felicity as ours be, for this end; or in which the
capacity of afford such exquisite scene, and the power of shift
they perpetually from morning to night, be lodge and intrust with so
unlimited a confidence, as in the Shandy Family.
not any one of these be more divert, I say, in this whimsical theatre
of our--than what frequently arise out of this self-same chapter of long
nose--especially when my father's imagination be heat with the enquiry,
and nothing would serve he but to heat my uncle Toby's too.
my uncle Toby would give my father all possible fair play in this attempt;
and with infinite patience would sit smoke his pipe for whole hour
together, whilst my father be practise upon his head, and try every
accessible avenue to drive Prignitz and Scroderus's solution into it.
whether they be above my uncle Toby's reason--or contrary to it--or that
his brain be like damp timber, and no spark could possibly take hold--or
that it be so full of sap, mine, blind, curtin, and such military
disqualification to his seeing clearly into Prignitz and Scroderus's
doctrine--I say not--let schoolman--scullion, anatomist, and engineer,
fight for it among themselves--
'twas some misfortune, I make no doubt, in this affair, that my father have
every word of it to translate for the benefit of my uncle Toby, and render
out of Slawkenbergius's Latin, of which, as he be no great master, his
translation be not always of the pure--and generally least so where
'twa most wanted.--this naturally open'd a door to a second misfortune;--
that in the warm paroxysm of his zeal to open my uncle Toby's eye--my
father's idea run on as much fast than the translation, as the
translation outmove my uncle Toby's--neither the one or the other add
much to the perspicuity of my father's lecture.
chapter 2.xxxiii.
the gift of ratiocination and make syllogism--I mean in man--for in
superior class of being, such as angel and spirits--'tis all do, may
it please your worship, as they tell I, by Intuition;--and being
inferior, as your worship all know--syllogize by their nose: though
there be an island swimming in the sea (though not altogether at its ease)
whose inhabitant, if my intelligence deceive I not, be so wonderfully
gift, as to syllogize after the same fashion, and oft-time to make very
well out too:--but thatbe neither here nor there--
the gift of do it as it should be, amongst we, or--the great and
principal act of ratiocination in man, as logician tell we, be the finding
out the agreement or disagreement of two idea one with another, by the
intervention of a third (call the medius terminus); just as a man, as
Locke well observe, by a yard, find two men nine-pin-alley to be of the
same length, which could not be bring together, to measure their
equality, by juxta-position.
have the same great reasoner look on, as my father illustrate his system
of nose, and observe my uncle Toby's deportment--what great attention he
give to every word--and as oft as he take his pipe from his mouth, with
what wonderful seriousness he contemplate the length of it--survey it
transversely as he hold it betwixt his finger and his thumb--then fore-
right--then this way, and then that, in all its possible direction and
fore-shortening--he would have conclude my uncle Toby have get hold of the
medius terminus, and be syllogize and measure with it the truth of
each hypothesis of long nose, in order, as my father lay they before he. 
this, by-the-bye, be more than my father want--his aim in all the pain
he be at in these philosophick lecture--be to enable my uncle Toby not
to discuss--but comprehend--to hold the grain and scruple of learning--
not to weigh them.--My uncle Toby, as you will read in the next chapter,
do neither the one or the other.
chapter 2.xxxiv.
'tis a pity, cry my father one winter's night, after a three hour
painful translation of Slawkenbergius--'tis a pity, cry my father,
put my mother's threadpaper into the book for a mark, as he speak--that
truth, brother Toby, should shut herself up in such impregnable fastness,
and be so obstinate as not to surrender herself sometimes up upon the
close siege.--
now it happen then, as indeed it have often do before, that my uncle
Toby's fancy, during the time of my father's explanation of Prignitz to
he--have nothing to stay it there, have take a short flight to the
bowl-green;--his body might as well have take a turn there too--so that
with all the semblance of a deep school-man intent upon the medius
terminus--my uncle Toby be in fact as ignorant of the whole lecture, and
all its pro and con, as if my father have be translate Hafen
Slawkenbergius from the latin tongue into the Cherokee. but the word
siege, like a talismanic power, in my father's metaphor, waft back my
uncle Toby's fancy, quick as a note could follow the touch--he open'd his
ear--and my father observe that he take his pipe out of his mouth, and
shuffle his chair nearer the table, as with a desire to profit--my father
with great pleasure begin his sentence again--change only the plan, and
drop the metaphor of the siege of it, to keep clear of some danger my
father apprehend from it.
'tis a pity, say my father, that truth can only be on one side, brother
Toby--consider what ingenuity these learn man have all shew in their
solution of noses.--can nose be dissolve? reply my uncle Toby.
--My father thrust back his chair--rise up--put on his hat--take four long
stride to the door--jerk it open--thrust his head half way out--shut the
door again--take no notice of the bad hinge--return to the table--pluck'd
my mother's thread-paper out of Slawkenbergius's book--go hastily to his
bureau--walk slowly back--twist my mother's thread-paper about his
thumb--unbutton'd his waistcoat--throw my mother's thread-paper into the
fire--bit her sattin pin-cushion in two, fill'd his mouth with bran--
confound it;--but mark!--the oath of confusion be levell'd at my uncle
Toby's brain--which be e'en confuse enough already--the curse come
charge only with the bran--the bran, may it please your honour, be no
more than powder to the ball.
'twas well my father's passion last not long; for so long as they do
last, they lead he a busy life on't; and it be one of the most
unaccountable problem that ever I meet with in my observation of human
nature, that nothing should prove my father's mettle so much, or make his
passion go off so like gun-powder, as the unexpected stroke his science
meet with from the quaint simplicity of my uncle Toby's questions.--Had ten
dozen of hornet sting he behind in so many different place all at one
time--he could not have exert more mechanical function in few seconds-
-or start half so much, as with one single quaere of three word
unseasonably pop in full upon he in his hobby-horsical career.
'twas all one to my uncle Toby--he smoke his pipe on with unvaried
composure--his heart never intend offence to his brother--and as his head
could seldom find out where the sting of it lay--he always give my father
the credit of cool by himself.--He be five minute and thirty-five
second about it in the present case.
by all thatbe good! say my father, swearing, as he come to himself, and
take the oath out of Ernulphus's digest of curses--(though to do my
father justice it be a fault (as he tell Dr. Slop in the affair of
Ernulphus) which he as seldom commit as any man upon earth)--by all
thatbe good and great! brother Toby, say my father, if it be not for the
aid of philosophy, which befriend one so much as they do--you would put a
man beside all temper.--why, by the solution of nose, of which I be
tell you, I mean, as you might have know, have you favour I with one
grain of attention, the various account which learn man of different
kind of knowledge have give the world of the cause of short and long
noses.--there be no cause but one, reply my uncle Toby--why one man's
nose be long than another's, but because that God please to have it so.-
-That be Grangousier's solution, say my father.--'Tis he, continue my
uncle Toby, look up, and not regard my father's interruption, who
make we all, and frame and put we together in such form and
proportion, and for such end, as be agreeable to his infinite wisdom,.--
'tis a pious account, cry my father, but not philosophical--there be more
religion in it than sound science. 'twa no inconsistent part of my uncle
Toby's character--that he fear God, and reverence religion.--so the
moment my father finish his remark--my uncle Toby fall a whistle
Lillabullero with more zeal (though more out of tune) than usual.--
what be become of my wife's thread-paper?
chapter 2.xxxv.
no matter--as an appendage to seamstressy, the thread-paper might be of
some consequence to my mother--of none to my father, as a mark in
Slawkenbergius. Slawkenbergius in every page of he be a rich treasure of
inexhaustible knowledge to my father--he could not open he amiss; and he
would often say in close the book, that if all the art and science in
the world, with the book which treat of they, be lose--should the
wisdom and policy of government, he would say, through disuse, ever
happen to be forget, and all that statesman have write or cause to be
write, upon the strong or the weak side of court and kingdom, should
they be forget also--and Slawkenbergius only leave--there would be enough in
he in all conscience, he would say, to set the world a-go again. a
treasure therefore be he indeed! an institute of all that be necessary to
be know of nose, and every thing else--at matin, noon, and vesper be
Hafen Slawkenbergius his recreation and delight: 'twas for ever in his
hand--you would have swear, Sir, it have be a canon's prayer-book--so
wear, so glaze, so contrited and attrite be it with finger and with
thumb in all its part, from one end even unto the other.
I be not such a bigot to Slawkenbergius as my father;--there be a fund in
he, no doubt: but in my opinion, the good, I donot say the most
profitable, but the most amusing part of Hafen Slawkenbergius, be his
tale--and, consider he be a german, many of they tell not without
fancy:--these take up his second book, contain nearly one half of his
folio, and be comprehend in ten decad, each decad contain ten tales-
-philosophy be not build upon tale; and therefore 'twa certainly wrong in
Slawkenbergius to send they into the world by that name!--there be a few
of they in his eighth, ninth, and tenth decad, which I own seem rather
playful and sportive, than speculative--but in general they be to be
look upon by the learn as a detail of so many independent fact, all of
they turn round somehow or other upon the main hinge of his subject,
and add to his work as so many illustration upon the doctrine of nose.
as we have leisure enough upon our hand--if you give I leave, madam, Iwill
tell you the ninth tale of his tenth decad.
Slawkenbergii Fabella (as Hafen Slawkenbergius de Nasis be extremely
scarce, it may not be unacceptable to the learned reader to see the
speciman of a few page of his original; I will make no reflection upon it,
but that his story-tell Latin be much more concise than his philosophic-
-and, I think, have more of Latinity in it.)
Vespera quadam frigidula, posteriori in parte mensis Augusti, peregrinu,
mulo fusco colore inciden, mantica a tergo, paucis indusiis, binis
calceis, braccisque sericis coccineis repleta, Argentoratum ingressus est.
Militi eum percontanti, quum portus intraret dixit, se apud Nasorum
promontorium fuisse, Francofurtum proficisci, et Argentoratum, transitu ad
fine Sarmatiae mensis intervallo, reversurum.
Miles peregrini in faciem suspexit--Di boni, nova forma nasi!
at multum mihi profuit, inquit peregrinu, carpum amento extrahen, e quo
pependit acinace: Loculo manum inseruit; et magna cum urbanitate, pilei
parte anteriore tacta manu sinistra, ut extendit dextram, militi florinum
dedit et processit.
Dolet mihi, ait miles, tympanistam nanum et valgum alloquens, virum adeo
urbanum vaginam perdidisse: itinerari haud poterit nuda acinaci; neque
vaginam toto Argentorato, habilem inveniet.--Nullam unquam habui, respondit
peregrinu respicien--seque comiter inclinan--hoc more gesto, nudam
acinacem elevan, mulo lento progrediente, ut nasum tueri possim.
Non immerito, benigne peregrine, respondit mile.
Nihili aestimo, ait ille tympanista, e pergamena factitius est.
prout christianus sum, inquit mile, nasus ille, ni sextie major fit, meo
esset conformis.
Crepitare audivi ait tympanista.
Mehercule! sanguinem emisit, respondit mile.
Miseret I, inquit tympanista, qui non ambo tetigimus!
Eodem temporis puncto, quo haec res argumentata fuit inter militem et
tympanistam, disceptabatur ibidem tubicine et uxore sua qui tunc
accesserunt, et peregrino praetereunte, restiterunt.
Quantus nasus! aeque longus est, ait tubicina, ac tuba.
et ex eodem metallo, ait tubicen, velut sternutamento audias.
Tantum abest, respondit illa, quod fistulam dulcedine vincit.
Aeneus est, ait tubicen.
Nequaquam, respondit uxor.
Rursum affirmo, ait tubicen, quod aeneus est.
rem penitus explorabo; prius, enim digito tangam, ait uxor, quam dormivero,
Mulus peregrini gradu lento progressus est, ut unumquodque verbum
controversiae, non tantum inter militem et tympanistam, verum etiam inter
tubicinem et uxorum eju, audiret.
Nequaquam, ait ille, in muli collum fraena demitten, et manibus ambabus in
pectus positis, (mulo lente progrediente) nequaquam, ait ille respicien,
non necesse est ut re isthaec dilucidata foret. Minime gentium! meus
nasus nunquam tangetur, dum spiritus hos reget artus--ad quid agendum? air
uxor burgomagistri.
Peregrinus illi non respondit. Votum faciebat tunc temporis sancto
Nicolao; quo facto, sinum dextrum inseren, e qua negligenter pependit
acinace, lento gradu processit per plateam Argentorati latam quae ad
diversorium templo ex adversum ducit.
Peregrinus mulo descenden stabulo includi, et manticam inferri jussit: 
qua aperta et coccineis sericis femoralibus extractis cum argento laciniato
(Greek), his sese induit, statimque, acinaci in manu, ad forum deambulavit.
Quod ubi peregrinus esset ingressus, uxorem tubicinis obviam euntem
aspicit; illico cursum flectit, metuens ne nasus suus exploraretur, atque
ad diversorium regressus est--exuit se vestibus; braccas coccinea sericas
manticae imposuit mulumque educi jussit.
Francofurtum proficiscor, ait ille, et Argentoratum quatuor abhinc
hebdomadis revertar.
Bene curasti hoc jumentam? (ait) muli faciem manu demulcen--I,
manticamque meam, plus sexcentis mille passibus portavit.
Longa via est! respondet hospe, nisi plurimum esset negoti.--enimvero, ait
peregrinu, a Nasorum promontorio redii, et nasum speciosissimum,
egregiosissimumque quem unquam quisquam sortitus est, acquisivi?
Dum peregrinu hanc miram rationem de seipso reddit, hospe et uxor eju,
oculis intentis, peregrini nasum contemplantur--per sanctos sanctasque
omne, ait hospitis uxor, nasis duodecim maximis in toto Argentorato major
est!--estne, ait illa mariti in aurem insusurrans, nonne est nasus
praegrandis?
Dolus inest, anime mi, ait hospe--nasus est falsus.
Verus est, respondit uxor--
Ex abiete factus est, ait ille, terebinthinum olet--
Carbunculus inest, ait uxor.
Mortuus est nasus, respondit hospe.
Vivus est ait illa,--et si ipsa vivam tangam.
Votum feci sancto Nicolao, ait peregrinu, nasum meum intactum fore usque
ad--Quodnam tempus? illico respondit illa.
Minimo tangetur, inquit ille (manibus in pectus compositis) usque ad illam
horam--Quam horam? ait illa--Nullam, respondit peregrinus, donec pervenio
ad--Quem locum,--obsecro? ait illa--Peregrinus nil responden mulo
conscenso discessit.
Slawkenbergius's Tale
it be one cool refresh evening, at the close of a very sultry day, in
the latter end of the month of August, when a stranger, mount upon a dark
mule, with a small cloak-bag behind he, contain a few shirt, a pair of
shoe, and a crimson-sattin pair of breech, enter the town of
Strasburg.
he tell the centinel, who question he as he enter the gate, that he
have be at the Promontory of Noses--be go on to Frankfort--and should
be back again at Strasburg that day month, in his way to the border of
Crim Tartary.
the centinel look up into the stranger's face--he never see such a nose
in his life!
--I have make a very good venture of it, quoth the stranger--so slip
his wrist out of the loop of a black ribbon, to which a short scymetar be
hung, he put his hand into his pocket, and with great courtesy touch the
fore part of his cap with his left hand, as he extend his right--he put a
florin into the centinel's hand, and pass on.
it grieve, I, say the centinel, speak to a little dwarfish bandy-
legg'd drummer, that so courteous a soul should have lose his scabbard--he
cannot travel without one to his scymetar, and will not be able to get a
scabbard to fit it in all Strasburg.--I never have one, reply the
stranger, look back to the centinel, and put his hand up to his cap
as he speak--I carry it, continue he, thus--hold up his naked scymetar,
his mule move on slowly all the time--on purpose to defend my nose.
it be well worth it, gentle stranger, reply the centinel.
--'ti not worth a single stiver, say the bandy-legg'd drummer--'tis a
nose of parchment.
as I be a true catholic--except that it be six time as big--'tis a nose,
say the centinel, like my own.
--I hear it crackle, say the drummer.
by dunder, say the centinel, I see it bleed.
what a pity, cry the bandy-legg'd drummer, we do not both touch it!
at the very time that this dispute be maintain by the centinel and the
drummer--be the same point debate betwixt a trumpeter and a trumpeter's
wife, who be just then come up, and have stop to see the stranger
pass by.
benedicity!--what a nose! 'tis as long, say the trumpeter's wife, as a
trumpet.
and of the same metal say the trumpeter, as you hear by its sneezing.
'tis as soft as a flute, say she.
--'Tis brass, say the trumpeter.
--'Tis a pudding's end, say his wife.
I tell thee again, say the trumpeter, 'tis a brazen nose,
Iwill know the bottom of it, say the trumpeter's wife, for I will touch it
with my finger before I sleep.
the stranger's mule move on at so slow a rate, that he hear every word of
the dispute, not only betwixt the centinel and the drummer, but betwixt the
trumpeter and trumpeter's wife.
no! say he, drop his rein upon his mule's neck, and lay both his
hand upon his breast, the one over the other in a saint-like position (his
mule go on easily all the time) no! say he, look up--I be not such a
debtor to the world--slander and disappoint as I have be--as to give
it that conviction--no! say he, my nose shall never be touch whilst
Heaven give I strength--to do what? say a burgomaster's wife.
the stranger take no notice of the burgomaster's wife--he be make a vow
to Saint Nicolas; which do, have uncross his arm with the same
solemnity with which he cross they, he take up the rein of his bridle
with his left-hand, and put his right hand into his bosom, with the
scymetar hang loosely to the wrist of it, he ride on, as slowly as one
foot of the mule could follow another, thro' the principal street of
Strasburg, till chance bring he to the great inn in the market-place
over-against the church.
the moment the stranger alight, he order his mule to be lead into the
stable, and his cloak-bag to be bring in; then opening, and take out of
it his crimson-sattin breech, with a silver-fringed--(appendage to they,
which I dare not translate)--he put his breech, with his fringe cod-
piece on, and forth-with, with his short scymetar in his hand, walk out
to the grand parade.
the stranger have just take three turn upon the parade, when he perceive
the trumpeter's wife at the opposite side of it--so turn short, in pain
lest his nose should be attempt, he instantly go back to his inn--
undressed himself, pack up his crimson-sattin breech, &c. in his cloak-
bag, and call for his mule.
I be go forwards, say the stranger, for Frankfort--and shall be back at
Strasburg this day month.
I hope, continue the stranger, stroke down the face of his mule with his
leave hand as he be go to mount it, that you have be kind to this
faithful slave of mine--it have carry I and my cloak-bag, continue he,
tap the mule's back, above six hundred league.
--'tis a long journey, Sir, reply the master of the inn--unless a man have
great business.--tut! tut! say the stranger, I have be at the promontory
of Noses; and have get I one of the goodly, thank Heaven, that ever
fall to a single man's lot.
Whilst the stranger be give this odd account of himself, the master of
the inn and his wife keep both their eye fix full upon the stranger's
nose--by saint Radagunda, say the inn-keeper's wife to herself, there be
more of it than in any dozen of the large nose put together in all
Strasburg! be it not, say she, whisper her husband in his ear, be it
not a noble nose?
'tis an imposture, my dear, say the master of the inn--'tis a false nose.
'tis a true nose, say his wife.
'tis make of fir-tree, say he, I smell the turpentine.--
therebe a pimple on it, say she.
'tis a dead nose, reply the inn-keeper.
'tis a live nose, and if I be alive myself, say the inn-keeper's, wife, I
will touch it.
I have make a vow to saint Nicolas this day, say the stranger, that my
nose shall not be touch till--here the stranger suspend his voice,
look up.--till when? say she hastily.
it never shall be touch, say he, clasp his hand and bring they
close to his breast, till that hour--what hour? cry the inn keeper's
wife.--Never!--never! say the stranger, never till I be get--for Heaven's
sake, into what place? say she--the stranger ride away without say a
word.
the stranger have not get half a league on his way towards Frankfort before
all the city of Strasburg be in an uproar about his nose. the Compline
bell be just ring to call the Strasburgers to their devotion, and
shut up the duty of the day in prayer:--no soul in all Strasburg hear
them--the city be like a swarm of bee--man, woman, and child, (the
Compline bell tinkle all the time) fly here and there--in at one
door, out at another--this way and that way--long way and cross way--up
one street, down another street--in at this alley, out of that--do you see
it? do you see it? do you see it? o! do you see it?--who see it? who
do see it? for mercy's sake, who see it?
Alack o'day! I be at vespers!--I be wash, I be starch, I be
scour, I be quilt--God help I! I never see it--I never touch'd
it!--would I have be a centinel, a bandy-legg'd drummer, a trumpeter, a
trumpeter's wife, be the general cry and lamentation in every street and
corner of Strasburg.
whilst all this confusion and disorder triumph throughout the great city
of Strasburg, be the courteous stranger go on as gently upon his mule
in his way to Frankfort, as if he have no concern at all in the affair--
talk all the way he ride in broken sentence, sometimes to his mule--
sometimes to himself--sometimes to his Julia.
o Julia, my lovely Julia!--nay I cannot stop to let thee bite that thistle-
-that ever the suspect tongue of a rival should have rob I of
enjoyment when I be upon the point of taste it.--
--Pugh!--'tis nothing but a thistle--never mind it--thou shalt have a
well supper at night.
--banish'd from my country--my friend--from thee.--
Poor devil, thou'rt sadly tired with thy journey!--come--get on a little
fast--therebe nothing in my cloak-bag but two shirt--a crimson-sattin
pair of breech, and a fringed--Dear Julia!
--but why to frankfort?--is it that there be a hand unfelt, which secretly
be conduct I through these meander and unsuspected tract?
--stumbling! by saint Nicolas! every step--why at this rate we shall be all
night in get in--
--To happiness--or be I to be the sport of fortune and slander--destine to
be drive forth unconvicted--unheard--untouch'd--if so, why do I not stay
at Strasburg, where justice--but I have swear! come, thou shalt drink--to
St. Nicolas--o julia!--what dost thou prick up thy ear at?--'ti nothing
but a man, &c.
the stranger ride on commune in this manner with his mule and Julia--till
he arrive at his inn, where, as soon as he arrive, he alight--see his
mule, as he have promise it, take good care of--take off his cloak-bag,
with his crimson-sattin breech, &c. in it--call for an omelet to his
supper, go to his bed about twelve o'clock, and in five minute fall fast
asleep.
it be about the same hour when the tumult in Strasburg be abate for
that night,--the Strasburgers have all get quietly into their bed--but not
like the stranger, for the rest either of their mind or body; queen Mab,
like an elf as she be, have take the stranger's nose, and without
reduction of its bulk, have that night be at the pain of slit and
divide it into as many nose of different cut and fashion, as there
be head in Strasburg to hold they. the abbess of Quedlingberg, who with
the four great dignitary of her chapter, the prioress, the deaness, the
sub-chantress, and senior canonness, have that week come to Strasburg to
consult the university upon a case of conscience relate to their placket-
hole--be ill all the night.
the courteous stranger's nose have get perch upon the top of the pineal
gland of her brain, and make such rousing work in the fancy of the four
great dignitary of her chapter, they could not get a wink of sleep the
whole night thro' for it--there be no keep a limb still amongst them--
in short, they get up like so many ghost.
the penitentiary of the third order of saint Francis--the nun of mount
Calvary--the Praemonstratenses--the Clunienses (Hafen Slawkenbergius mean
the Benedictine nun of Cluny, found in the year 940, by Odo, abbe de
cluny.)--the Carthusians, and all the severer order of nun, who lie that
night in blanket or hair-cloth, be still in a bad condition than the
abbess of Quedlingberg--by tumble and tossing, and tossing and tumbling
from one side of their bed to the other the whole night long--the several
sisterhood have scratch'd and maul'd themselves all to death--they get out
of their bed almost flay'd alive--every body think saint Antony have
visit they for probation with his fire--they have never once, in short,
shut their eye the whole night long from vesper to matin.
the nun of saint Ursula act the wise--they never attempt to go to
bed at all.
the dean of Strasburg, the prebendary, the capitular and domiciliar
(capitularly assemble in the morning to consider the case of butter'd
bun) all wish they have follow the nun of saint Ursula's example.--
in the hurry and confusion every thing have be in the night before, the

in one eternal commotion--such a cause of restlessness and disquietude, and
such a zealous inquiry into that cause of the restlessness, have never
happen in Strasburg, since Martin Luther, with his doctrine, have turn
the city upside down.
if the stranger's nose take this liberty of thrust himself thus into the

now what might add, if any thing may be think necessary to add, to so

the trumpeter, the trumpeter's wife, the burgomaster's widow, the master of

the heart in jeopardy (have his nose not stand in his way) of every virgin
who have cast her eye upon he.
I call not upon that heart which be a stranger to the throb and yearning

long than three day.
the centinel and bandy-legg'd drummer!--nothing on this side of old Athens
could equal they! they read their lecture under the city-gate to comer

the master of the inn, with his ostler on his left-hand, read his also in

'tis worth remarking, for the benefit of all demonstrator in natural

they reasoned--
not one profession have throw more light upon this subject than the
Faculty--have not all their dispute about it run into the affair of Wens

it be demonstrate however very satisfactorily, that such a ponderous mass
of heterogenous matter could not be congest and conglomerate to the

balance of the foetus, and throw it plump upon its head nine month
before the time.--
--The opponent grant the theory--they deny the consequence.
and if a suitable provision of vein, artery, &c. say they, be not lay
in, for the due nourishment of such a nose, in the very first stamina and
rudiment of its formation, before it come into the world (bat the case
of Wens) it could not regularly grow and be sustain afterwards.
this be all answer by a dissertation upon nutriment, and the effect
which nutriment have in extend the vessel, and in the increase and
prolongation of the muscular part to the great growth and expansion
imaginable--in the triumph of which theory, they go so far as to affirm,
that there be no cause in nature, why a nose might not grow to the size of
the man himself.
the respondent satisfy the world this event could never happen to they
so long as a man have but one stomach and one pair of lung--for the
stomach, say they, be the only organ destine for the reception of
food, and turn it into chyle--and the lung the only engine of
sanguification--it could possibly work off no more, than what the appetite
bring it: or admit the possibility of a man's overload his
stomach, nature have set bound however to his lung--the engine be of a
determine size and strength, and could elaborate but a certain quantity in
a give time--that is, it could produce just as much blood as be
sufficient for one single man, and no more; so that, if there be as much
nose as man--they prove a mortification must necessarily ensue; and
forasmuch as there could not be a support for both, that the nose must
either fall off from the man, or the man inevitably fall off from his nose.
Nature accommodate herself to these emergency, cry the opponent--else
what do you say to the case of a whole stomach--a whole pair of lung, and
but half a man, when both his leg have be unfortunately shoot off?
he die of a plethora, say they--or must spit blood, and in a fortnight or
three week go off in a consumption.--
--it happen otherwise--reply the opponents.--
it ought not, say they.
the more curious and intimate inquirer after nature and her doing, though
they go hand in hand a good way together, yet they all divide about the
nose at last, almost as much as the Faculty itself
they amicably lay it down, that there be a just and geometrical
arrangement and proportion of the several part of the human frame to its
several destination, office, and function, which could not be
transgress but within certain limit--that nature, though she sported--
she sport within a certain circle;--and they could not agree about the
diameter of it.
the logicians stick much close to the point before they than any of the
class of the literati;--they begin and end with the word Nose; and have
it not be for a petitio principii, which one of the ablest of they run

a nose, argue the logician, cannot bleed without blood--and not only
blood--but blood circulate in it to supply the phaenomenon with a
succession of drops--(a stream being but a quick succession of drop,
that be include, say he.)--now death, continue the logician, be
nothing but the stagnation of the blood--
I deny the definition--death be the separation of the soul from the body,
say his antagonist--then we donot agree about our weapon, say the
logician--then there be an end of the dispute, reply the antagonist.
the civilian be still more concise: what they offer be more in the

such a monstrous nose, say they, have it be a true nose, could not
possibly have be suffer in civil society--and if false--to impose upon

the only objection to this be, that if it prove any thing, it prove the

this left room for the controversy to go on. it be maintain by the

it happen--I must say unluckily for Truth, because they be give she a

the popish doctor have undertake to demonstrate a priori, that from the
necessary influence of the planet on the twenty-second day of October
1483--when the moon be in the twelfth house, Jupiter, Mars, and Venus in

by inspection into his horoscope, where five planet be in coition all at

the little objection of the Lutheran doctor to this, be, that it must

(--I must break off my translation for a moment; for if I do not, I know I

the two university of Strasburg be hard tugging at this affair of

'twas a square cap with a silver tassel upon the crown of it--to a nut-

'tis above reason, cry the doctor on one side.
'tis below reason, cry the other.
'tis faith, cry one.
'tis a fiddle-stick, say the other.
'tis possible, cry the one.
'tis impossible, say the other.
God's power be infinite, cry the Nosarians, he can do any thing.
he can do nothing, reply the Anti-nosarian, which imply
contradiction.
he can make matter think, say the Nosarians.
as certainly as you can make a velvet cap out of a sow's ear, reply the
Anti-nosarians.
he cannot make two and two five, reply the Popish doctors.--'Tis false,
say their other opponents.--
Infinite power be infinite power, say the doctor who maintain the
reality of the nose.--it extend only to all possible thing, reply the
Lutherans.
by God in heaven, cry the popish doctor, he can make a nose, if he
think fit, as big as the steeple of Strasburg.
now the steeple of Strasburg be the big and the tall church-
steeple to be see in the whole world, the Anti-nosarians deny that a
nose of 575 geometrical foot in length could be wear, at least by a middle-
siz'd man--the popish doctor swear it could--the Lutheran doctor say
No;--it could not.
this at once start a new dispute, which they pursue a great way, upon
the extent and limitation of the moral and natural attribute of God--that
controversy lead they naturally into Thomas Aquinas, and Thomas Aquinas to
the devil.
the stranger's nose be no more hear of in the dispute--it just serve as
a frigate to launch they into the gulph of school-divinity--and then they
all sail before the wind.
Heat be in proportion to the want of true knowledge.
the controversy about the attribute, &c. instead of cool, on the

about it--they be leave in all the distress of desire unsatisfie--see

--The poor Strasburgers leave upon the beach!
--What be to be done?--no delay--the uproar increase--every one in
disorder--the city gate set open.--
Unfortunate Strasbergers! be there in the store-house of nature--be there
in the lumber-room of learn--be there in the great arsenal of chance,
one single engine leave undrawn forth to torture your curiosity, and
stretch your desire, which be not point by the hand of Fate to play
upon your hearts?--i dip not my pen into my ink to excuse the surrender of
yourselves--'tis to write your panegyrick. shew I a city so macerate
with expectation--who neither eat, or drank, or sleep, or pray, or
hearken to the call either of religion or nature, for seven-and-twenty
day together, who could have hold out one day long.
on the twenty-eighth the courteous stranger have promise to return to
Strasburg.
seven thousand coach (Slawkenbergius must certainly have make some

Haste we now towards the catastrophe of my tale--I say Catastrophe (cry
Slawkenbergius) inasmuch as a tale, with part rightly dispose, not only
rejoiceth (gaudet) in the Catastrophe and Peripeitia of a Drama, but
rejoiceth moreover in all the essential and integrant part of it--it have
its Protasis, Epitasis, Catastasis, its Catastrophe or Peripeitia grow
one out of the other in it, in the order Aristotle first plant them--
without which a tale have well never be tell at all, say Slawkenbergius,
but be keep to a man's self.
in all my ten tale, in all my ten decade, have I Slawkenbergius tie down
every tale of they as tightly to this rule, as I have do this of the
stranger and his nose.
--From his first parley with the centinel, to his leave the city of
Strasburg, after pull off his crimson-sattin pair of breech, be the
Protasis or first entrance--where the character of the Personae Dramatis
be just touch in, and the subject slightly begun.
the Epitasis, wherein the action be more fully enter upon and heighten,
till it arrive at its state or height call the Catastasis, and which
usually take up the 2d and 3d act, be include within that busy period of
my tale, betwixt the first night's uproar about the nose, to the conclusion
of the trumpeter's wife's lecture upon it in the middle of the grand
parade: and from the first embarking of the learn in the dispute--to the
doctor finally sail away, and leave the Strasburgers upon the beach
in distress, be the Catastasis or the ripening of the incident and
passion for their burst forth in the fifth act.
this commence with the setting out of the Strasburgers in the Frankfort
road, and terminate in unwind the labyrinth and bring the hero out
of a state of agitation (as Aristotle call it) to a state of rest and
quietness.
this, say Hafen Slawkenbergius, constitute the Catastrophe or Peripeitia
of my tale--and that be the part of it I be go to relate.
we leave the stranger behind the curtain asleep--he enter now upon the
stage.
--what dost thou prick up thy ear at?--'ti nothing but a man upon a
horse--be the last word the stranger utter to his mule. it be not
proper then to tell the reader, that the mule take his master's word for
it; and without any more if or and, let the traveller and his horse pass
by.
the traveller be hasten with all diligence to get to Strasburg that
night. what a fool be i, say the traveller to himself, when he have ride
about a league far, to think of get into Strasburg this night.--
Strasburg!--the great Strasburg!--Strasburg, the capital of all Alsatia! 
Strasburg, an imperial city! Strasburg, a sovereign state! Strasburg,
garrison with five thousand of the good troop in all the world!--ala!
if I be at the gate of Strasburg this moment, I could not gain admittance
into it for a ducat--nay a ducat and half--'tis too much--well go back to
the last inn I have pass--than lie I know not where--or give I know not
what. the traveller, as he make these reflection in his mind, turn his
horse's head about, and three minute after the stranger have be conduct
into his chamber, he arrive at the same inn.
--We have bacon in the house, say the host, and bread--and till eleven
o'clock this night have three egg in it--but a stranger, who arrive an
hour ago, have have they dress into an omelet, and we have nothing.--
Alas! say the traveller, harass as I be, I want nothing but a bed.--i
have one as soft as be in Alsatia, say the host.
--the stranger, continue he, should have sleep in it, for 'tis my good
bed, but upon the score of his nose.--he have get a defluxion, say the
traveller.--not that I know, cry the host.--But 'tis a camp-bed, and
Jacinta, say he, look towards the maid, imagine there be not room in
it to turn his nose in.--why so? cry the traveller, start back.--it be
so long a nose, reply the host.--the traveller fix his eye upon
Jacinta, then upon the ground--kneel upon his right knee--have just get
his hand lay upon his breast--trifle not with my anxiety, say he rise
up again.--'ti no trifle, say Jacinta, 'tis the most glorious nose!--The
traveller fall upon his knee again--lay his hand upon his breast--then,
say he, look up to heaven, thou hast conduct I to the end of my
pilgrimage--'Tis Diego.
the traveller be the brother of the Julia, so often invoke that night by
the stranger as he ride from Strasburg upon his mule; and be come, on her
part, in quest of he. he have accompany his sister from Valadolid across
the pyrenean mountain through France, and have many an entangled skein to
wind off in pursuit of he through the many meander and abrupt turning of
a lover's thorny track.
--Julia have sink under it--and have not be able to go a step far than
to Lyons, where, with the many disquietude of a tender heart, which all
talk of--but few feel--she sicken'd, but have just strength to write a
letter to Diego; and have conjure her brother never to see her face till
he have find he out, and put the letter into his hand, Julia take to her
bed.
Fernandez (for that be her brother's name)--tho' the camp-bed be as soft
as any one in Alsace, yet he could not shut his eye in it.--as soon as it
be day he rise, and hear Diego be rise too, he enter his chamber,
and discharge his sister's commission.
the letter be as follow:
'Seig. Diego,
'whether my suspicion of your nose be justly excited or not--'tis not
now to inquire--it be enough I have not have firmness to put they to far
tryal.
'how could I know so little of myself, when I send my Duenna to forbid your
come more under my lattice? or how could I know so little of you, Diego,
as to imagine you would not have staid one day in Valadolid to have give
ease to my doubts?--was I to be abandon, Diego, because I be deceive?
or be it kind to take I at my word, whether my suspicion be just or
no, and leave I, as you do, a prey to much uncertainty and sorrow?
'in what manner Julia have resent this--my brother, when he put this
letter into your hand, will tell you; he will tell you in how few moment
she repent of the rash message she have send you--in what frantic haste
she fly to her lattice, and how many day and night together she lean
immoveably upon her elbow, look through it towards the way which Diego
be willnot to come.
'he will tell you, when she hear of your departure--how her spirit
desert she--how her heart sicken'd--how piteously she mourn--how low
she hang her head. o Diego! how many weary step have my brother's pity lead
I by the hand languish to trace out your; how far have desire carry
I beyond strength--and how oft have I faint by the way, and sink into
his arm, with only power to cry out--o my Diego!
'if the gentleness of your carriage have not belie your heart, you will fly
to I, almost as fast as you flee from I--haste as you will--you will
arrive but to see I expire.--'Tis a bitter draught, Diego, but oh! 'tis
embitter still more by die un. . .--'
she could proceed no far.
Slawkenbergius suppose the word intend be unconvinced, but her strength
would not enable she to finish her letter.
the heart of the courteous Diego over-flow as he read the letter--he
order his mule forthwith and Fernandez's horse to be saddle; and as no
vent in prose be equal to that of poetry in such conflict--chance, which
as often direct we to remedy as to disease, have throw a piece of
charcoal into the window--Diego avail himself of it, and whilst the
hostler be get ready his mule, he ease his mind against the wall as
follow.
Ode.
harsh and untuneful be the note of love,
unless my Julia strike the key,
her hand alone can touch the part,
whose dulcet movement charm the heart,
and govern all the man with sympathetick sway.

o Julia!
the line be very natural--for they be nothing at all to the purpose,
say Slawkenbergius, and 'tis a pity there be no more of they; but
whether it be that Seig. Diego be slow in compose verse--or the
hostler quick in saddle mule--be not aver; certain it be, that
Diego's mule and Fernandez's horse be ready at the door of the inn,
before Diego be ready for his second stanza; so without stay to finish
his ode, they both mount, sally forth, pass the Rhine, traverse
Alsace, shape their course towards Lyons, and before the Strasburgers and
the abbess of Quedlingberg have set out on their cavalcade, have Fernandez,
Diego, and his Julia, cross the pyrenean mountain, and get safe to
Valadolid.
'tis needless to inform the geographical reader, that when Diego be in
Spain, it be not possible to meet the courteous stranger in the Frankfort
road; it be enough to say, that of all restless desire, curiosity be
the strong--the Strasburgers feel the full force of it; and that for
three day and night they be toss to and fro in the Frankfort road,
with the tempestuous fury of this passion, before they could submit to
return home.--When alas! an event be prepare for they, of all other, the
most grievous that could befal a free people.
as this revolution of the Strasburgers affair be often speak of, and
little understand, I will, in ten word, say Slawkenbergius, give the
world an explanation of it, and with it put an end to my tale.
every body know of the grand system of Universal Monarchy, write by order
of Mons. Colbert, and put in manuscript into the hand of Lewis the
fourteenth, in the year 1664.
'tis as well know, that one branch out of many of that system, be the
get possession of Strasburg, to favour an entrance at all time into
Suabia, in order to disturb the quiet of Germany--and that in consequence
of this plan, Strasburg unhappily fall at length into their hand.
it be the lot of a few to trace out the true spring of this and such like
revolution--the vulgar look too high for they--statesman look too low--
truth (for once) lie in the middle.
what a fatal thing be the popular pride of a free city! cry one
historian--the Strasburgers deem it a diminution of their freedom to
receive an imperial garrison--so fall a prey to a french one.
the fate, say another, of the Strasburgers, may be a warning to all free
people to save their money.--they anticipate their revenue--bring
themselves under taxis, exhaust their strength, and in the end become so
weak a people, they have not strength to keep their gate shut, and so the
French push they open.
alas! alas! cry Slawkenbergius, 'twa not the French,--'twas Curiosity
push they open--the French indeed, who be ever upon the catch, when they
see the Strasburgers, man, woman and child, all march out to follow
the stranger's nose--each man follow his own, and march in.
Trade and manufacture have decay and gradually grow down ever since--
but not from any cause which commercial head have assign; for it be
owe to this only, that nose have ever so run in their head, that the
Strasburgers could not follow their business.
alas! alas! cry Slawkenbergius, make an exclamation--it be not the
first--and I fear will not be the last fortress that have be either won--
or lose by nose.
the end of Slawkenbergius's Tale.

the Intricacies of Diego and Julia.
Heavens! thou art a strange creature, Slawkenbergius! what a whimsical view
of the involution of the heart of woman hast thou open! how this can
ever be translate, and yet if this speciman of Slawkenbergius's tale, and
the exquisitiveness of his moral, should please the world--translate shall
a couple of volume be.--Else, how this can ever be translate into good
English, I have no sort of conception--there seem in some passage to want
a sixth sense to do it rightly.--what can he mean by the lambent
pupilability of slow, low, dry chat, five note below the natural tone--
which you know, madam, be little more than a whisper? the moment I
pronounce the word, I could perceive an attempt towards a vibration in
the string, about the region of the heart.--The brain make no
acknowledgment.--therebe often no good understanding betwixt them--I feel as
if I understand it.--I have no ideas.--The movement could not be without
cause.--I'm lose. I can make nothing of it--unless, may it please your
worship, the voice, in that case be little more than a whisper,
unavoidably force the eye to approach not only within six inch of each
other--but to look into the pupil--be not that dangerous?--but it cannot be
avoid--for to look up to the cieling, in that case the two chin
unavoidably meet--and to look down into each other's lap, the forehead
come to immediate contact, which at once put an end to the conference--I
mean to the sentimental part of it.--what be leave, madam, be not worth
stoop for.

Chapter 2.xlv.
be it not a shame to make two chapter of what pass in go down one

the deuce of any other rule have I to govern myself by in this affair--and

and the cause demand it--a pretty story! be a man to follow rule--or
rule to follow he?
now this, you must know, be my chapter upon chapter, which I promise
to write before I go to sleep, I think it meet to ease my conscience
entirely before I lay down, by tell the world all I know about the

necessary as the shifting of scene--with fifty other cold conceit, enough

be a puff at the fire of Diana's temple--you must read Longinus--read away-
-if you be not a jot the wiser by read he the first time over--never
fear--read he again--Avicenna and Licetus read Aristotle's metaphysick
forty time through a-piece, and never understand a single word.--but mark
the consequence--Avicenna turn out a desperate writer at all kind of
writing--for he write books de omni scribili; and for Licetus (Fortunio)
though all the world know he be bear a foetus, (Ce Foetus n'etoit pas
plus grand que la paume de la main; mais son pere l'ayant examine en
qualite de Medecin, & ayant trouve que c'etoit quelque choose de plus qu'un
Embryon, le fit transporter tout vivant a Rapallo, ou il le fit voir a
Jerome Bardi & a d'autres Medecins du lieu. on trouva qu'il ne lui
manquoit rien d'essentiel a la vie; & son pere pour faire voir un essai de
son experience, entreprit d'achever l'ouvrage de la Nature, & de travailler
a la formation de l'Enfant avec le meme artifice que celui donot on se sert
pour faire ecclorre les Poulets en Egypte. Il instruisit une Nourisse de
tout ce qu'elle avoit a faire, & ayant fait mettre son fil dans un pour
proprement accommode, il reussit a l'elever & a lui faire prendre s
accroissemen necessaire, par l'uniformite d'une chaleur etrangere mesuree
exactement sur le degre d'un Thermometre, ou d'un autre instrument
equivalent. (Vide Mich. Giustinian, ne gli Scritt. Liguri a 223. 488.) on
auroit toujour ete tre satisfait de l'industrie d'un pere si experimente
dan l'Art de la Generation, quand il n'auroit pu prolonger la vie a son
fil que pour Puelques mois, ou pour peu d'annee. Mais quand on se
represente que l'Enfant a vecu pre de quatre-vingts an, & qu'il a compose
quatre-vingts Ouvrages different tous fruit d'une longue lecture--il faut
convenir que tout ce qui est incroyable n'est pas toujour faux, & que la
Vraisemblance n'est pas toujour du cote la Verite. Il n'avoit que dix
neuf ans lorsqu'il composa Gonopsychanthropologia de Origine Animae
humanae. (Les Enfans celebre, revus & corrige par M. de la Monnoye de
l'Academie Francoise.)) of no more than five inch and a half in length,
yet he grow to that astonishing height in literature, as to write a book
with a title as long as himself--the learn know I mean his
Gonopsychanthropologia, upon the origin of the human soul.
so much for my chapter upon chapter, which I hold to be the good chapter

I will not finish that sentence till I have make an observation upon the
strange state of affair between the reader and myself, just as thing
stand at present--an observation never applicable before to any one
biographical writer since the creation of the world, but to myself--and I
believe, will never hold good to any other, until its final destruction--
and therefore, for the very novelty of it alone, it must be worth your
worship attend to.
I be this month one whole year old than I be this time twelve-month; and
have get, as you perceive, almost into the middle of my third volume

364 time fast than I should write--it must follow, an' please your
worship, that the more I write, the more I shall have to write--and
consequently, the more your worship read, the more your worship will have
to read.
will this be good for your worship eye?
it will do well for mine; and, be it not that my opinion will be the

but stay--thou art a leaky vessel, Susannah, add my father; canst thou
carry Trismegistus in thy head, the length of the gallery without
scattering?--can I? cry Susannah, shut the door in a huff.--if she
can, Iwill be shoot, say my father, bounce out of bed in the dark, and
groping for his breech.
Susannah run with all speed along the gallery.
my father make all possible speed to find his breech.
Susannah get the start, and keep it--'Tis Tris--something, cry susannah--
there be no christian-name in the world, say the curate, begin with
Tris--but Tristram. then 'tis Tristram-gistus, quoth Susannah.
--there be no gistus to it, noodle!--'tis my own name, reply the curate,
dip his hand, as he speak, into the bason--Tristram! say he, &c. &c.
&c. &c.--so Tristram be I call, and Tristram shall I be to the day of my
death.
my father follow Susannah, with his night-gown across his arm, with
nothing more than his breech on, fasten through haste with but a single
button, and that button through haste thrust only half into the button-
hole.
--She have not forget the name, cry my father, half open the door?--no,
no, say the curate, with a tone of intelligence.--And the child be well,
cry susannah.--and how do your mistress? as well, say Susannah, as
can be expected.--pish! say my father, the button of his breech slip
out of the button-hole--so that whether the interjection be level at
Susannah, or the button-hole--whether Pish be an interjection of contempt
or an interjection of modesty, be a doubt, and must be a doubt till I shall
have time to write the three follow favourite chapter, that is, my
chapter of chamber-maid, my chapter of pishe, and my chapter of button-
hole.
all the light I be able to give the reader at present be this, that the
moment my father cry Pish! he whisk'd himself about--and with his
breech hold up by one hand, and his night-gown throw across the arm of
the other, he turn along the gallery to bed, something slow than he
come.
chapter 2.L.
I wish I could write a chapter upon sleep.
a fitter occasion could never have present itself, than what this moment
offer, when all the curtain of the family be draw--the candle put out-
-and no creature's eye be open but a single one, for the other have be
shut these twenty year, of my mother's nurse.
it be a fine subject.
and yet, as fine as it be, I would undertake to write a dozen chapter upon
button-hole, both quick and with more fame, than a single chapter upon
this.
Button-hole! there be something lively in the very idea of them--and trust
I, when I get amongst them--you gentry with great beard--look as grave as
you will--i'll make merry work with my button-hole--I shall have them all
to myself--'tis a maiden subject--I shall run foul of no man's wisdom or
fine saying in it.
but for sleep--I know I shall make nothing of it before I begin--I be no
dab at your fine saying in the first place--and in the next, I cannot for
my soul set a grave face upon a bad matter, and tell the world--'tis the
refuge of the unfortunate--the enfranchisement of the prisoner--the downy
lap of the hopeless, the weary, and the break-hearted; nor could I set out
with a lye in my mouth, by affirm, that of all the soft and delicious
function of our nature, by which the great author of it, in his bounty,
have be pleased to recompence the suffering wherewith his justice and his
good pleasure have weary we--that this be the chief (I know pleasure
worth ten of it); or what a happiness it be to man, when the anxiety and
passion of the day be over, and he lie down upon his back, that his soul
shall be so seated within he, that whichever way she turn her eye, the
heavens shall look calm and sweet above she--no desire--or fear--or doubt
that trouble the air, nor any difficulty past, present, or to come, that
the imagination may not pass over without offence, in that sweet secession.
'God's blessing,' say Sancho Panca, 'be upon the man who first invent
this self-same thing call sleep--it cover a man all over like a cloak.' 
now there be more to I in this, and it speak warm to my heart and
affection, than all the dissertation squeez'd out of the head of the
learn together upon the subject.
--not that I altogether disapprove of what Montaigne advance upon it--'tis
admirable in its way--(i quote by memory.)
the world enjoy other pleasure, say he, as they do that of sleep,
without taste or feel it as it slip and pass by.--We should study
and ruminate upon it, in order to render proper thank to he who grant it
to us.--For this end I cause myself to be disturb in my sleep, that I may
the well and more sensibly relish it.--and yet I see few, say he again,
who live with less sleep, when need require; my body be capable of a firm,
but not of a violent and sudden agitation--I evade of late all violent
exercise--I be never weary with walk--but from my youth, I never look
to ride upon pavement. I love to lie hard and alone, and even without my
wife--this last word may stagger the faith of the world--but remember, 'La
Vraisemblance' (as Bayle say in the affair of Liceti) 'n'est pas toujour
du Cote de la Verite.' and so much for sleep.
Chapter 2.LI.
if my wife will but venture he--brother Toby, Trismegistus shall be

together.--
--Go, tell Susannah, Obadiah, to step here.
she be run up stair, answer Obadiah, this very instant, sob and

we shall have a rare month of it, say my father, turn his head from

--for he spake in the sweetest modulation--and take down his hat with the

Chapter 2.lii.
when the misfortune of my Nose fall so heavily upon my father's head;--the
reader remember that he walk instantly up stair, and cast himself down
upon his bed; and from hence, unless he have a great insight into human
nature, he will be apt to expect a rotation of the same ascending and
descend movement from he, upon this misfortune of my Name;--no.
the different weight, dear Sir--nay even the different package of two
vexation of the same weight--make a very wide difference in our manner of
bear and get through with them.--It be not half an hour ago, when
(in the great hurry and precipitation of a poor devil's writing for daily
bread) I throw a fair sheet, which I have just finish, and carefully write
out, slap into the fire, instead of the foul one.
instantly I snatch'd off my wig, and throw it perpendicularly, with all
imaginable violence, up to the top of the room--indeed I catch it as it
fall--but there be an end of the matter; nor do I think any think else in
Nature would have give such immediate ease: she, dear Goddess, by an
instantaneous impulse, in all provoke case, determine we to a sally of
this or that member--or else she thrust we into this or that place, or
posture of body, we know not why--but mark, madam, we live amongst riddle
and mystery--the most obvious thing, which come in our way, have dark
side, which the quick sight cannot penetrate into; and even the
clear and most exalt understanding amongst we find ourselves puzzle
and at a loss in almost every cranny of nature's work: so that this, like
a thousand other thing, fall out for we in a way, which tho' we cannot
reason upon it--yet we find the good of it, may it please your reverence
and your worship--and thatbe enough for we.
now, my father could not lie down with this affliction for his life--nor
could he carry it up stair like the other--he walk composedly out with
it to the fish-pond.
have my father lean his head upon his hand, and reason an hour which way
to have go--reason, with all her force, could not have direct he to
any think like it: there be something, Sir, in fish-pond--but what it be,
I leave to system-builder and fish-pond-digger betwixt them to find out--
but there be something, under the first disorderly transport of the
humour, so unaccountably becalm in an orderly and a sober walk towards
one of they, that I have often wonder that neither Pythagoras, nor Plato,
nor Solon, nor Lycurgus, nor Mahomet, nor any one of your noted lawgiver,
ever give order about they.
chapter 2.liii.
your honour, say Trim, shut the parlour-door before he begin to speak,

--for my own part, Trim, though I can see little or no difference betwixt

my name be Alexander, I could have do no more at Namur than my duty.--
bless your honour! cry Trim, advance three step as he speak, do a
man think of his christian-name when he go upon the attack?--or when he
stand in the trench, Trim? cry my uncle Toby, look firm.--or when he
enter a breach? say Trim, push in between two chairs.--or force the
line? cry my uncle, rise up, and push his crutch like a pike.--or
face a platoon? cry Trim, present his stick like a firelock.--or
when he march up the glacis? cry my uncle Toby, look warm and
set his foot upon his stool.--
Chapter 2.liv.
my father be return from his walk to the fish-pond--and open the
parlour-door in the very height of the attack, just as my uncle Toby be
march up the glacis--Trim recover his arm--never be my uncle Toby
catch in rid at such a desperate rate in his life! alas! my uncle Toby!
have not a weighty matter call forth all the ready eloquence of my

my father hang up his hat with the same air he take it down; and after

against my uncle Toby, he sit down in it, and as soon as the tea-thing
