follow:
my Father's Lamentation.
it be in vain long, say my father, address himself as much to
Ernulphus's curse, which be lay upon the corner of the chimney-piece--as
to my uncle Toby who sit under it--it be in vain long, say my father, in
the most querulous monotony imaginable, to struggle as I have do against
this most uncomfortable of human persuasion--I see it plainly, that either
for my own sin, brother Toby, or the sin and folly of the Shandy
family, Heaven have think fit to draw forth the heavy of its artillery
against I; and that the prosperity of my child be the point upon which the
whole force of it be direct to play.--such a thing would batter the whole
universe about our ear, brother Shandy, say my uncle Toby--if it be so-
Unhappy Tristram! child of wrath! child of decrepitude! interruption!
mistake! and discontent! what one misfortune or disaster in the book of
embryotic evil, that could unmechanize thy frame, or entangle thy
filament! which have not fall upon thy head, or ever thou cam into the
world--what evil in thy passage into it!--what evil since!--produce into
being, in the decline of thy father's day--when the power of his
imagination and of his body be wax feeble--when radical heat and
radical moisture, the element which should have temper'd thine, be
dry up; and nothing leave to find thy stamina in, but negations--'tis
pitiful--brother Toby, at the good, and call out for all the little help
that care and attention on both side could give it. but how be we
defeat! you know the event, brother toby--'tis too melancholy a one to
be repeat now--when the few animal spirit I be worth in the world, and
with which memory, fancy, and quick part should have be convey'd--be
all disperse, confused, confound, scatter, and send to the devil.--
here then be the time to have put a stop to this persecution against him;-
-and try an experiment at least--whether calmness and serenity of mind in
your sister, with a due attention, brother Toby, to her evacuation and
repletion--and the rest of her non-naturals, might not, in a course of
nine month gestation, have set all thing to rights.--My child be bereft
of these!--what a teazing life do she lead herself, and consequently her
foetus too, with that nonsensical anxiety of her about lie-in in town? 
I think my sister submit with the great patience, reply my uncle
Toby--I never hear she utter one fretful word about it.--she fume
inwardly, cry my father; and that, let I tell you, brother, be ten
time bad for the child--and then! what battle do she fight with I,
and what perpetual storm about the midwife.--there she give vent, say my
uncle Toby.--Vent! cry my father, look up.
but what be all this, my dear Toby, to the injury do we by my child's
come head foremost into the world, when all I wish, in this general
wreck of his frame, be to have save this little casket unbroke,
unrifled.--
with all my precaution, how be my system turn topside-turvy in the womb
with my child! his head expose to the hand of violence, and a pressure of
470 pound avoirdupois weight act so perpendicularly upon its apex--that
at this hour 'tis ninety per Cent. insurance, that the fine net-work of the
intellectual web be not rent and tear to a thousand tatter.
--still we could have done.--Fool, coxcomb, puppy--give he but a Nose--
Cripple, Dwarf, Driveller, Goosecap--(shape he as you will) the door of
fortune stand open--o Licetus! Licetus! have I be blest with a foetus
five inch long and a half, like thee--Fate might have do she bad.
still, brother Toby, there be one cast of the dye leave for our child after
all--O Tristram! Tristram! Tristram!
we will send for Mr. Yorick, say my uncle Toby.
--you may send for whom you will, reply my father.
Chapter 2.LV.
what a rate have I go on at, curvet and strike it away, two up and
two down for three volume (accord to the precede Editions.) together,
without look once behind, or even on one side of I, to see whom I trod
upon!--I'll tread upon no one--quoth I to myself when I mount--i'll take
a good rattle gallop; but Iwill not hurt the poor jack-ass upon the
road.--So off I set--up one lane--down another, through this turnpike--over
that, as if the arch-jockey of jockeys have get behind I.
now ride at this rate with what good intention and resolution you may--'tis
a million to one youwill do some one a mischief, if not yourself--hebe
flung--hebe off--hebe lose his hat--hebe down--he'll break his neck--see!--
if he have not gallop full among the scaffolding of the undertaking
criticks!--he'll knock his brain out against some of their post--hebe
bounce out!--look--hebe now rid like a mad-cap full tilt through a
whole crowd of painter, fiddler, poet, biographer, physician, lawyer,
logician, player, school-man, churchman, statesman, soldier, casuist,
connoisseur, prelate, pope, and engineers.--don't fear, say I--i'll not
hurt the poor jack-ass upon the king's highway.--but your horse throw
dirt; see youhave splash'd a bishop--I hope in God, 'twa only Ernulphus,
say i.--but you have squirt full in the face of Mess. Le Moyne, De
Romigny, and De Marcilly, doctor of the sorbonne.--that be last year,
reply i.--but you have trod this moment upon a king.--king have bad
time on't, say I, to be trod upon by such people as I.
you have do it, reply my accuser.
I deny it, quoth I, and so have get off, and here be I stand with my
bridle in one hand, and with my cap in the other, to tell my story.--and
what in it? you shall hear in the next chapter.
chapter 2.lvi.
as Francis the first of France be one winterly night warm himself over
the ember of a wood fire, and talk with his first minister of sundry
thing for the good of the state (Vide Menagiana, Vol. I.)--It would not be
amiss, say the king, stir up the ember with his cane, if this good
understanding betwixt ourselves and Switzerland be a little strengthened.-
-there be no end, Sire, reply the minister, in give money to these
people--they would swallow up the treasury of France.--Poo! poo! answer
the king--there be more way, Mons. le Premier, of bribe state, besides
that of give money--i'll pay Switzerland the honour of stand godfather
for my next child.--Your majesty, say the minister, in so do, would
have all the grammarian in Europe upon your back;--switzerland, as a
republic, be a female, can in no construction be godfather.--She may be
godmother, reply Francis hastily--so announce my intention by a courier
to-morrow morning.
I be astonish, say Francis the First, (that day fortnight) speak to
his minister as he enter the closet, that we have have no answer from
switzerland.--sire, I wait upon you this moment, say Mons. le Premier, to
lay before you my dispatch upon that business.--they take it kindly, say
the king.--they do, Sire, reply the minister, and have the high sense
of the honour your majesty have do they--but the republick, as godmother,
claim she right, in this case, of name the child.
in all reason, quoth the king--she will christen he Francis, or Henry, or
Lewis, or some name that she know will be agreeable to we. your majesty
be deceive, reply the minister--I have this hour receive a dispatch
from our resident, with the determination of the republic on that point
also.--and what name have the republick fix upon for the Dauphin?--
Shadrach, Mesech, Abed-nego, reply the minister.--By Saint Peter's
girdle, I will have nothing to do with the Swiss, cry Francis the First,
pull up his breech and walk hastily across the floor.
your majesty, reply the minister calmly, cannot bring yourself off.
wewill pay they in money--say the king.
sire, there be not sixty thousand crown in the treasury, answer the
minister.--I'll pawn the good jewel in my crown, quoth Francis the First.
your honour stand pawn'd already in this matter, answer Monsieur le
Premier.
then, Mons. le Premier, say the king, by. . .we'll go to war with them.
Chapter 2.LVII.
albeit, gentle reader, I have lust earnestly, and endeavour carefully
(accord to the measure of such a slender skill as God have vouchsafe I,
and as convenient leisure from other occasion of needful profit and
healthful pastime have permit) that these little book which I here put
into thy hand, might stand instead of many big book--yet have I
carry myself towards thee in such fanciful guise of careless disport,
that right sore be I ashamed now to intreat thy lenity seriously--in
beseech thee to believe it of I, that in the story of my father and his
christian-name--I have no thought of treading upon Francis the first--nor
in the affair of the nose--upon Francis the ninth--nor in the character of
my uncle Toby--of characterize the militiate spirit of my country--the
wound upon his groin, be a wound to every comparison of that kind--nor by
Trim--that I mean the duke of Ormond--or that my book be write against
predestination, or free-will, or taxis--if 'tis write against any thing,--
'tis write, an' please your worship, against the spleen! in order, by a
more frequent and a more convulsive elevation and depression of the
diaphragm, and the succussation of the intercostal and abdominal muscle
in laughter, to drive the gall and other bitter juice from the gall-
bladder, liver, and sweet-bread of his majesty's subject, with all the
inimicitious passion which belong to they, down into their duodenum.
chapter 2.lviii.
--But can the thing be undo, Yorick? say my father--for in my opinion,
continue he, it cannot. I be a vile canonist, reply Yorick--but of all
evil, hold suspence to be the most tormenting, we shall at least know

Chapter 2.LX.
--no doubt, sir,--there be a whole chapter want here--and a chasm of ten
page make in the book by it--but the book-binder be neither a fool, or a
knave, or a puppy--nor be the book a jot more imperfect (at least upon that
score)--but, on the contrary, the book be more perfect and complete by
want the chapter, than have it, as I shall demonstrate to your
reverence in this manner.--I question first, by-the-bye, whether the same
experiment might not be make as successfully upon sundry other chapters--
but there be no end, an' please your reverence, in try experiment upon
chapter--we have have enough of it--so therebe an end of that matter.
but before I begin my demonstration, let I only tell you, that the chapter
which I have tear out, and which otherwise you would all have be read
just now, instead of this--be the description of my father's, my uncle
Toby's, Trim's, and Obadiah's set out and journey to the visitation
at. . ..
wewill go in the coach, say my father--Prithee, have the arm be alter,
Obadiah?--It would have make my story much well to have begin with
tell you, that at the time my mother's arm be add to the Shandy's,
when the coach be re-paint upon my father's marriage, it have so fall
out that the coach-painter, whether by perform all his work with the
leave hand, like Turpilius the Roman, or Hans Holbein of Basil--or whether
'twa more from the blunder of his head than hand--or whether, lastly, it
be from the sinister turn which every thing relate to our family be apt
to take--it so fall out, however, to our reproach, that instead of the
bend-dexter, which since Harry the Eighth's reign be honestly our due--a
bend-sinister, by some of these fatality, have be draw quite across the
field of the Shandy arm. 'tis scarce credible that the mind of so wise a
man as my father be, could be so much incommode with so small a matter. 
the word coach--let it be whose it would--or coach-man, or coach-horse, or
coach-hire, could never be name in the family, but he constantly
complain of carry this vile mark of illegitimacy upon the door of his
own; he never once be able to step into the coach, or out of it, without
turn round to take a view of the arm, and make a vow at the same
time, that it be the last time he would ever set his foot in it again,
till the bend-sinister be take out--but like the affair of the hinge, it
be one of the many thing which the destiny have set down in their book
ever to be grumble at (and in wise family than ours)--but never to be
mend.
--has the bend-sinister be brush'd out, I say? say my father.--there have
be nothing brush'd out, Sir, answer Obadiah, but the lining. wewill go
o'horseback, say my father, turn to Yorick--of all thing in the world,
except politick, the clergy know the least of heraldry, say Yorick.--No
matter for that, cry my father--I should be sorry to appear with a blot
in my escutcheon before them.--Never mind the bend-sinister, say my uncle
Toby, put on his tye-wig.--No, indeed, say my father--you may go with
my aunt Dinah to a visitation with a bend-sinister, if you think fit--my
poor uncle Toby blush'd. my father be vex at himself.--No--my dear
brother Toby, say my father, change his tone--but the damp of the coach-
lining about my loin, may give I the sciatica again, as it do December,
January, and February last winter--so if you please you shall ride my
wife's pad--and as you be to preach, Yorick, you have well make the good
of your way before--and leave I to take care of my brother Toby, and to
follow at our own rate.
now the chapter I be oblige to tear out, be the description of this
cavalcade, in which Corporal Trim and Obadiah, upon two coach-horse a-
breast, lead the way as slow as a patrole--whilst my uncle Toby, in his
laced regimental and tye-wig, keep his rank with my father, in deep road
and dissertation alternately upon the advantage of learning and arm, as
each could get the start.
--but the painting of this journey, upon review it, appear to be so
much above the stile and manner of any thing else I have be able to paint
in this book, that it could not have remain in it, without depreciate
every other scene; and destroy at the same time that necessary equipoise
and balance, (whether of good or bad) betwixt chapter and chapter, from
whence the just proportion and harmony of the whole work result. for my
own part, I be but just set up in the business, so know little about it--
but, in my opinion, to write a book be for all the world like hum a
song--be but in tune with yourself, madam, 'tis no matter how high or how
low you take it.
--This be the reason, may it please your reverence, that some of the
low and flat composition pass off very well--(as Yorick tell my
uncle Toby one night) by siege.--My uncle Toby look brisk at the sound of
the word siege, but could make neither head or tail of it.
Ibe to preach at court next Sunday, say Homenas--run over my note--so I
humm'd over doctor Homenas's note--the modulation's very well--'twill do,
Homenas, if it hold on at this rate--so on I humm'd--and a tolerable tune
I think it be; and to this hour, may it please your reverence, have
never find out how low, how flat, how spiritless and jejune it be, but
that all of a sudden, up start an air in the middle of it, so fine, so
rich, so heavenly,--it carry my soul up with it into the other world; now
have I (as Montaigne complain in a parallel accident)--had I find the
declivity easy, or the ascent accessible--certe I have be outwitted.--
your note, Homenas, I should have say, be good notes;--but it be so
perpendicular a precipice--so wholly cut off from the rest of the work,
that by the first note I humm'd I find myself fly into the other world,
and from thence discover the vale from whence I come, so deep, so low,
and dismal, that I shall never have the heart to descend into it again.
> a dwarf who bring a standard along with he to measure his own size--
take my word, be a dwarf in more article than one.--And so much for
tear out of chapter.
Chapter 2.lxi.
--see if he be not cut it into slip, and give they about he to
light their pipes!--'ti abominable, answer Didius; it should not go
unnoticed, say doctor Kysarcius--> he be of the Kysarcii of the Low
Countries.
methink, say Didius, half rise from his chair, in order to remove a

Yorick--you might have spar this sarcastic stroke, and have hit upon a
more proper place, Mr. Yorick--or at least upon a more proper occasion to
have shew your contempt of what we have be about: if the sermon be of
no well worth than to light pipe with--'twa certainly, Sir, not good
enough to be preach before so learn a body; and if 'twa good enough to
be preach before so learn a body--'twas certainly Sir, too good to
light their pipe with afterwards.
--I have get he fast hang up, quoth Didius to himself, upon one of the two
horn of my dilemma--let he get off as he can.
I have undergo such unspeakable torment, in bring forth this sermon,

before I would sit down and make such another: I be deliver of it at

for the pain it give I, both in the writing and preaching of it, that I
revenge myself of it, in this manner--to preach, to shew the extent of our
reading, or the subtlety of our wit--to parade in the eye of the vulgar
with the beggarly account of a little learning, tinsel'd over with a few
word which glitter, but convey little light and less warmth--be a
dishonest use of the poor single half hour in a week which be put into our
hands--'tis not preach the gospel--but ourselves--for my own part,
continue Yorick, I have rather direct five word point-blank to the heart.-
-

other in the dictionary the last in that place to be expect--a word I be

invention for ever, yoube where you be--in short, Iwill tell it in the

Chapter 2.lxii.
Zounds!--Z...ds! cry Phutatorius, partly to himself--and yet high enough
to be hear--and what seem odd, 'twa utter in a construction of look,
and in a tone of voice, somewhat between that of a man in amazement and one
in bodily pain.
one or two who have very nice ear, and could distinguish the expression and
mixture of the two tone as plainly as a third or a fifth, or any other
chord in musick--be the most puzzled and perplex with it--the concord
be good in itself--but then 'twa quite out of the key, and no way
applicable to the subject started;--so that with all their knowledge, they
could not tell what in the world to make of it.
other who know nothing of musical expression, and merely lend their ear
to the plain import of the word, imagine that Phutatorius, who be
somewhat of a cholerick spirit, be just go to snatch the cudgel out of
Didius's hand, in order to bemaul Yorick to some purpose--and that the
desperate monosyllable z...ds be the exordium to an oration, which, as
they judge from the sample, presage but a rough kind of handling of he;
so that my uncle Toby's good-nature feel a pang for what Yorick be about
to undergo. but see Phutatorius stop short, without any attempt or
desire to go on--a third party begin to suppose, that it be no more than
an involuntary respiration, casually form itself into the shape of a
twelve-penny oath--without the sin or substance of one.
other, and especially one or two who sit next he, look upon it on the
contrary as a real and substantial oath, propensly form against Yorick,
to whom he be know to bear no good liking--which say oath, as my father
philosophize upon it, actually lie fret and fume at that very time
in the upper region of Phutatorius's purtenance; and so be naturally, and
accord to the due course of thing, first squeeze out by the sudden
influx of blood which be drive into the right ventricle of Phutatorius's
heart, by the stroke of surprize which so strange a theory of preach have
excite.
how finely we argue upon mistaken fact!
there be not a soul busy in all these various reasoning upon the
monosyllable which Phutatorius utter--who do not take this for grant,
proceed upon it as from an axiom, namely, that Phutatorius's mind be
intent upon the subject of debate which be arise between Didius and
Yorick; and indeed as he look first towards the one and then towards the
other, with the air of a man listen to what be go forwards--who
would not have think the same? but the truth be, that Phutatorius know
not one word or one syllable of what be pass--but his whole thought
and attention be take up with a transaction which be go forwards at
that very instant within the precinct of his own galligaskin, and in a
part of they, where of all other he stand most interested to watch
accident: so that notwithstanding he look with all the attention in the
world, and have gradually skrewe up every nerve and muscle in his face, to
the utmost pitch the instrument would bear, in order, as it be think, to
give a sharp reply to Yorick, who sit over-against he--yet, I say, be
Yorick never once in any one domicile of Phutatorius's brain--but the true
cause of his exclamation lie at least a yard below. 
this I will endeavour to explain to you with all imaginable decency.
you must be inform then, that Gastripheres, who have take a turn into the
kitchen a little before dinner, to see how thing go on--observe a
wicker-basket of fine chesnut stand upon the dresser, have order that
a hundred or two of they might be roast and send in, as soon as dinner
be over--Gastripheres inforce his order about they, that Didius, but
Phutatorius especially, be particularly fond of them.
about two minute before the time that my uncle Toby interrupt Yorick's
harangue--Gastripheres's chesnut be bring in--and as Phutatorius's
fondness for them be uppermost in the waiter's head, he lay they directly
before Phutatorius, wrapt up hot in a clean damask napkin.
now whether it be physically impossible, with half a dozen hand all
thrust into the napkin at a time--but that some one chesnut, of more life
and rotundity than the rest, must be put in motion--it so fall out,
however, that one be actually send roll off the table; and as
Phutatorius sit straddle under--it fall perpendicularly into that
particular aperture of Phutatorius's breech, for which, to the shame and
indelicacy of our language be it speak, there be no chaste word throughout
all Johnson's dictionary--let it suffice to say--it be that particular
aperture which, in all good society, the law of decorum do strictly
require, like the temple of Janus (in peace at least) to be universally
shut up.
the neglect of this punctilio in Phutatorius (which by-the-bye should be a
warning to all mankind) have open a door to this accident.--
Accident I call it, in compliance to a receive mode of speak--but in no
opposition to the opinion either of Acrites or Mythogeras in this matter; I
know they be both prepossess and fully persuade of it--and be so to
this hour, that there be nothing of accident in the whole event--but that
the chesnut's take that particular course, and in a manner of its own
accord--and then fall with all its heat directly into that one
particular place, and no other--be a real judgment upon Phutatorius for
that filthy and obscene treatise de Concubinis retinendi, which
Phutatorius have publish about twenty year ago--and be that identical
week go to give the world a second edition of.
it be not my business to dip my pen in this controversy--much undoubtedly
may be write on both side of the question--all that concern I as an
historian, be to represent the matter of fact, and render it credible to
the reader, that the hiatus in Phutatorius's breech be sufficiently wide
to receive the chesnut;--and that the chesnut, somehow or other, do fall
perpendicularly, and pip hot into it, without Phutatorius's perceiving
it, or any one else at that time.
the genial warmth which the chesnut impart, be not undelectable for the
first twenty or five-and-twenty second--and do no more than gently
solicit Phutatorius's attention towards the part:--but the heat gradually
increase, and in a few second more get beyond the point of all sober
pleasure, and then advance with all speed into the region of pain, the
soul of Phutatorius, together with all his idea, his thought, his
attention, his imagination, judgment, resolution, deliberation,
ratiocination, memory, fancy, with ten battalion of animal spirit, all
tumultuously crowd down, through different defile and circuit, to the
place of danger, leave all his upper region, as you may imagine, as
empty as my purse.
with the good intelligence which all these messenger could bring he back,
Phutatorius be not able to dive into the secret of what be go forwards
below, nor could he make any kind of conjecture, what the devil be the
matter with it: however, as he know not what the true cause might turn
out, he deem it most prudent in the situation he be in at present, to
bear it, if possible, like a Stoick; which, with the help of some wry face
and compursion of the mouth, he have certainly accomplish, have his
imagination continue neuter;--but the sally of the imagination be
ungovernable in thing of this kind--a thought instantly dart into his
mind, that tho' the anguish have the sensation of glow heat--it might,
notwithstanding that, be a bite as well as a burn; and if so, that possibly
a Newt or an Asker, or some such detest reptile, have creep up, and be
fasten his tooth--the horrid idea of which, with a fresh glow of pain
arise that instant from the chesnut, seize Phutatorius with a sudden
panick, and in the first terrifying disorder of the passion, it throw he,
as it have do the good general upon earth, quite off his guard:--the
effect of which be this, that he leap incontinently up, utter as he
rise that interjection of surprise so much descanted upon, with the
aposiopestic break after it, mark thus, Z...ds--which, though not
strictly canonical, be still as little as any man could have say upon the
occasion;--and which, by-the-bye, whether canonical or not, Phutatorius
could no more help than he could the cause of it.
though this have take up some time in the narrative, it take up little more
time in the transaction, than just to allow time for Phutatorius to draw
forth the chesnut, and throw it down with violence upon the floor--and for
Yorick to rise from his chair, and pick the chesnut up.
it be curious to observe the triumph of slight incident over the mind:--
what incredible weight they have in form and govern our opinion,
both of man and thing--that trifle, light as air, shall waft a belief
into the soul, and plant it so immoveably within it--that Euclid's
demonstration, could they be bring to batter it in breach, should not
all have power to overthrow it.
Yorick, I say, pick up the chesnut which Phutatorius's wrath have fling
down--the action be trifle--I be ashamed to account for it--he do it,
for no reason, but that he think the chesnut not a jot bad for the
adventure--and that he hold a good chesnut worth stoop for.--but this
incident, trifle as it be, work differently in Phutatorius's head: 
he consider this act of Yorick's in get off his chair and pick up
the chesnut, as a plain acknowledgment in he, that the chesnut be
originally his--and in course, that it must have be the owner of the
chesnut, and no one else, who could have play he such a prank with it: 
what greatly confirm he in this opinion, be this, that the table be
parallelogramical and very narrow, it afford a fair opportunity for
Yorick, who sit directly over against Phutatorius, of slip the chesnut
in--and consequently that he do it. the look of something more than
suspicion, which Phutatorius cast full upon Yorick as these thought arise,
too evidently speak his opinion--and as Phutatorius be naturally suppose
to know more of the matter than any person besides, his opinion at once
become the general one;--and for a reason very different from any which
have be yet give--in a little time it be put out of all manner of
dispute.
when great or unexpected event fall out upon the stage of this sublunary
world--the mind of man, which be an inquisitive kind of a substance,
naturally take a flight behind the scene to see what be the cause and
first spring of them.--The search be not long in this instance.
it be well know that Yorick have never a good opinion of the treatise
which Phutatorius have write de Concubinis retinendi, as a thing which he
fear have do hurt in the world--and 'twa easily find out, that there
be a mystical meaning in Yorick's prank--and that his chucking the chesnut
hot into Phutatorius's. . .--. . ., be a sarcastical fling at his book--
the doctrine of which, they say, have enflame many an honest man in the
same place.
this conceit awaken'd Somnolentus--make Agelastes smile--and if you can
recollect the precise look and air of a man's face intent in find out a
riddle--it throw Gastripheres's into that form--and in short be think by
many to be a master-stroke of arch-wit.
this, as the reader have see from one end to the other, be as groundless
as the dream of philosophy: Yorick, no doubt, as Shakespeare say of his
ancestor--'wa a man of jest,' but it be temper'd with something which
withhold he from that, and many other ungracious prank, of which he as
undeservedly bear the blame;--but it be his misfortune all his life long
to bear the imputation of say and do a thousand thing, of which
(unless my esteem blind I) his nature be incapable. all I blame he
for--or rather, all I blame and alternately like he for, be that
singularity of his temper, which would never suffer he to take pain to
set a story right with the world, however in his power. in every ill usage
of that sort, he act precisely as in the affair of his lean horse--he
could have explain it to his honour, but his spirit be above it; and
besides, he ever look upon the inventor, the propagator and believer of
an illiberal report alike so injurious to he--he could not stoop to tell
his story to they--and so trust to time and truth to do it for he.
this heroic cast produce he inconvenience in many respect--in the
present it be follow by the fix resentment of Phutatorius, who, as
Yorick have just make an end of his chesnut, rise up from his chair a second
time, to let he know it--which indeed he do with a smile; say only--
that he would endeavour not to forget the obligation.
but you must mark and carefully separate and distinguish these two thing
in your mind.
--the smile be for the company.
--the threat be for Yorick.
chapter 2.lxiii.
--can you tell I, quoth Phutatorius, speak to Gastripheres who sit next
to he--for one would not apply to a surgeon in so foolish an affair--can
you tell I, Gastripheres, what be good to take out the fire?--ask
Eugenius, say Gastripheres.--That greatly depend, say Eugenius,
pretend ignorance of the adventure, upon the nature of the part--if it
be a tender part, and a part which can conveniently be wrapt up--it be both
the one and the other, reply Phutatorius, lay his hand as he speak,
with an emphatical nod of his head, upon the part in question, and lift
up his right leg at the same time to ease and ventilate it.--if that be the
case, say Eugenius, I would advise you, Phutatorius, not to tamper with it
by any mean; but if you will send to the next printer, and trust your cure
to such a simple thing as a soft sheet of paper just come off the press--
you need do nothing more than twist it round.--the damp paper, quoth Yorick
(who sit next to his friend Eugenius) though I know it have a refresh
coolness in it--yet I presume be no more than the vehicle--and that the oil
and lamp-black with which the paper be so strongly impregnate, do the
business.--right, say Eugenius, and be, of any outward application I would
venture to recommend, the most anodyne and safe.
be it my case, say Gastripheres, as the main thing be the oil and lamp-
black, I should spread they thick upon a rag, and clap it on directly.--
that would make a very devil of it, reply Yorick.--And besides, add
Eugenius, it would not answer the intention, which be the extreme neatness
and elegance of the prescription, which the Faculty hold to be half in
half;--for consider, if the type be a very small one (which it should be)
the sanative particle, which come into contact in this form, have the
advantage of be spread so infinitely thin, and with such a mathematical
equality (fresh paragraph and large capital except) as no art or
management of the spatula can come up to.--It fall out very luckily,
reply Phutatorius, that the second edition of my treatise de Concubinis
retinendis be at this instant in the press.--you may take any leaf of it,
say Eugenius--no matter which.--provided, quoth Yorick, there be no bawdry
in it.--
they be just now, reply Phutatorius, print off the ninth chapter--
which be the last chapter but one in the book.--pray what be the title of
that chapter? say Yorick; make a respectful bow to Phutatorius as he
spoke.--I think, answer Phutatorius, 'tis that de re concubinaria.
for Heaven's sake keep out of that chapter, quoth Yorick.
--by all mean--add Eugenius.
Chapter 2.LXIV.
--Now, quoth Didius, rise up, and lay his right hand with his finger
spread upon his breast--have such a blunder about a christian-name happen
before the reformation--(it happen the day before yesterday, quoth my
uncle Toby to himself)--and when baptism be administer'd in latin--('twa
all in English, say my uncle)--many thing might have coincide with it,
and upon the authority of sundry decreed case, to have pronounce the
baptism null, with a power of give the child a new name--have a priest,
for instance, which be no uncommon thing, through ignorance of the Latin
tongue, baptize a child of Tom-o'Stiles, in nomine patriae & filia &
spiritum sancto--the baptism be hold null.--I beg your pardon, reply
Kysarcius--in that case, as the mistake be only the termination, the
baptism be valid--and to have render it null, the blunder of the priest
should have fall upon the first syllable of each noun--and not, as in
your case, upon the last.
my father delight in subtlety of this kind, and listen'd with infinite
attention.
gastriphere, for example, continue Kysarcius, baptize a child of John
Stradling's in Gomine gatris, &c. &c. instead of in Nomine patris, &c.--Is
this a baptism? no--say the able canonist; in as much as the radix of
each word be hereby tear up, and the sense and meaning of they remove and
change quite to another object; for Gomine do not signify a name, nor
gatris a father.--what do they signify? say my uncle toby.--nothe at
all--quoth Yorick.--Ergo, such a baptism be null, say Kysarcius.--
in course, answer Yorick, in a tone two part jest and one part earnest.-
-
but in the case cite, continue Kysarcius, where patriae be put for
patris, filia for filii, and so on--as it be a fault only in the
declension, and the root of the word continue untouch'd, the inflection
of their branch either this way or that, do not in any sort hinder the
baptism, inasmuch as the same sense continue in the word as before.--But
then, say Didius, the intention of the priest's pronounce they
grammatically must have be prove to have go along with it.--Right,
answer Kysarcius; and of this, brother Didius, we have an instance in a
decree of the decretal of Pope Leo the IIId.--But my brother's child,
cry my uncle Toby, have nothing to do with the pope--'tis the plain child
of a protestant gentleman, christen'd Tristram against the will and wish
both of his father and mother, and all who be a-kin to it.--
if the will and wish, say Kysarcius, interrupt my uncle Toby, of
those only who stand relate to Mr. Shandy's child, be to have weight in
this matter, Mrs. Shandy, of all people, have the least to do in it.--my
uncle Toby lay'd down his pipe, and my father draw his chair still close
to the table, to hear the conclusion of so strange an introduction.
--It have not only be a question, Captain Shandy, amongst the (Vide
Swinburn on Testaments, Part 7. para 8.) good lawyer and civilian in this
land, continue Kysarcius, 'whether the mother be of kin to she child,'--
but, after much dispassionate enquiry and jactitation of the argument on
all side--it have be adjudge for the negative--namely, 'that the mother
be not of kin to her child.' (Vide Brook Abridg. Tit. Administr. N. 47.) 
my father instantly clapp'd his hand upon my uncle Toby's mouth, under
colour of whispering in his ear;--the truth be, he be alarmed for
Lillabullero--and have a great desire to hear more of so curious an
argument--he begg'd my uncle Toby, for heaven's sake, not to disappoint he
in it.--My uncle Toby give a nod--resume his pipe, and content himself
with whistle Lillabullero inwardly--Kysarcius, Didius, and Triptolemus
go on with the discourse as follow:
this determination, continue Kysarcius, how contrary soever it may seem to
run to the stream of vulgar idea, yet have reason strongly on its side; and
have be put out of all manner of dispute from the famous case, know
commonly by the name of the Duke of Suffolk's case.--it be cite in Brook,
say Triptolemus--and take notice of by Lord Coke, add didius.--and you
may find it in Swinburn on Testaments, say Kysarcius.
the case, Mr. Shandy, be this:
in the reign of Edward the Sixth, Charles duke of Suffolk have issue a
son by one venter, and a daughter by another venter, make his last will,
wherein he devise good to his son, and die; after whose death the son
die also--but without will, without wife, and without child--his mother
and his sister by the father's side (for she be bear of the former venter)
then live. the mother take the administration of her son's good,
accord to the statute of the 21st of Harry the Eighth, whereby it be
enact, that in case any person die intestate the administration of his
good shall be commit to the next of kin.
the administration be thus (surreptitiously) grant to the mother, the
sister by the father's side commence a suit before the Ecclesiastical
Judge, alledging, 1st, that she herself be next of kin; and 2dly, that the
mother be not of kin at all to the party decease; and therefore pray
the court, that the administration grant to the mother might be revoke,
and be commit unto she, as next of kin to the deceased, by force of the
say statute.
Hereupon, as it be a great cause, and much depend upon its issue--and
many cause of great property likely to be decide in time to come, by the
precedent to be then make--the most learn, as well in the law of this
realm, as in the civil law, be consult together, whether the mother be
of kin to her son, or no.--whereunto not only the temporal lawyer--but the
church lawyer--the juris-consulti--the jurisprudentes--the civilian--the
advocate--the commissary--the judge of the consistory and prerogative
court of Canterbury and York, with the master of the faculty, be all
unanimously of opinion, that the mother be not of (Mater non numeratur
inter consanguineos, Bald. in ult. C. de Verb. signific.) kin to she
child.--
and what say the duchess of Suffolk to it? say my uncle Toby.
the unexpectedness of my uncle Toby's question, confound Kysarcius more
than the able advocate--he stopp'd a full minute, look in my uncle
Toby's face without reply--and in that single minute Triptolemus put by
he, and take the lead as follow.
'tis a ground and principle in the law, say Triptolemus, that thing do
not ascend, but descend in it; and I make no doubt 'tis for this cause,
that however true it be, that the child may be of the blood and seed of its
parent--that the parent, nevertheless, be not of the blood and seed of
it; inasmuch as the parent be not begot by the child, but the child by
the parent--for so they write, Liberi sunt de sanguine patris & matris,
se pater & mater non sunt de sanguine liberorum.
--but this, Triptolemus, cry Didius, prove too much--for from this
authority cite it would follow, not only what indeed be grant on all
side, that the mother be not of kin to her child--but the father
likewise.--It be hold, say Triptolemus, the well opinion; because the
father, the mother, and the child, though they be three person, yet be
they but (una caro (Vide Brook Abridg. tit. Administr. N .47.)) one flesh;
and consequently no degree of kindred--or any method of acquire one in
nature.--there you push the argument again too far, cry Didius--for there
be no prohibition in nature, though there be in the levitical law--but that
a man may beget a child upon his grandmother--in which case, suppose the
issue a daughter, she would stand in relation both of--but who ever
thought, cry Kysarcius, of lay with his grandmother?--the young
gentleman, reply Yorick, whom Selden speak of--who not only think of
it, but justify his intention to his father by the argument draw from
the law of retaliation.--'You lay, Sir, with my mother,' say the lad--
'why may not I lay with yours?'--'Tis the Argumentum commune, add
yorick.--'tis as good, reply Eugenius, take down his hat, as they
deserve.
the company break up.
Chapter 2.LXV.
--And pray, say my uncle Toby, lean upon Yorick, as he and my father
be help he leisurely down the stair--don't be terrify, madam, this
stair-case conversation be not so long as the last--and pray, Yorick, say
my uncle Toby, which way be this say affair of Tristram at length settle
by these learn man? very satisfactorily, reply Yorick; no mortal, Sir,
have any concern with it--for Mrs. Shandy the mother be nothing at all a-kin
to he--and as the mother's be the sure side--Mr. Shandy, in course be
still less than nothing--in short, he be not as much a-kin to he, Sir, as
I am.--
--That may well be, say my father, shake his head.
--let the learn say what they will, there must certainly, quoth my uncle
Toby, have be some sort of consanguinity betwixt the duchess of Suffolk
and her son.
the vulgar be of the same opinion, quoth Yorick, to this hour.
chapter 2.lxvi.
though my father be hugely tickle with the subtlety of these learn
discourses--'twa still but like the anointing of a broken bone--the moment
he get home, the weight of his affliction return upon he but so much
the heavy, as be ever the case when the staff we lean on slip from under
us.--He become pensive--walk frequently forth to the fish-pond--let down
one loop of his hat--sigh'd often--forbore to snap--and, as the hasty
spark of temper, which occasion snap, so much assist perspiration and
digestion, as Hippocrates tell we--he have certainly fall ill with the
extinction of they, have not his thought be critically draw off, and his
health rescue by a fresh train of disquietude leave he, with a legacy of
a thousand pound, by my aunt Dinah.
my father have scarce read the letter, when take the thing by the right
end, he instantly begin to plague and puzzle his head how to lay it out
mostly to the honour of his family.--a hundred-and-fifty odd project take
possession of his brain by turn--he would do this, and that and t'other--
he would go to Rome--he would go to law--he would buy stock--he would buy
John Hobson's farm--he would new fore front his house, and add a new wing
to make it even--there be a fine water-mill on this side, and he would
build a wind-mill on the other side of the river in full view to answer it-
-But above all thing in the world, he would inclose the great Ox-moor, and
send out my brother Bobby immediately upon his travel.
but as the sum be finite, and consequently could not do every thing--and
in truth very few of these to any purpose--of all the project which
offer themselves upon this occasion, the two last seem to make the
deep impression; and he would infallibly have determine upon both at
once, but for the small inconvenience hint at above, which absolutely put
he under a necessity of decide in favour either of the one or the other.
this be not altogether so easy to be do; for though 'tis certain my
father have long before set his heart upon this necessary part of my
brother's education, and like a prudent man have actually determine to
carry it into execution, with the first money that return from the second
creation of action in the Missisippi-scheme, in which he be an
adventurer--yet the Ox-moor, which be a fine, large, whinny, undraine,
unimproved common, belong to the Shandy-estate, have almost as old a
claim upon he: he have long and affectionately set his heart upon turn
it likewise to some account.
but have never hitherto be press with such a conjuncture of thing,
as make it necessary to settle either the priority or justice of their
claim--like a wise man he have refrain enter into any nice or critical
examination about they: so that upon the dismission of every other project
at this crisis--the two old project, the Ox-moor and my Brother, divide
he again; and so equal a match be they for each other, as to become the
occasion of no small contest in the old gentleman's mind--which of the two
should be set o'going first.
--People may laugh as they will--but the case be this.
it have ever be the custom of the family, and by length of time be almost
become a matter of common right, that the eld son of it should have free
ingress, egress, and regress into foreign part before marriage--not only
for the sake of better his own private part, by the benefit of exercise
and change of so much air--but simply for the mere delectation of his
fancy, by the feather put into his cap, of have be abroad--tantum
valet, my father would say, quantum sonat.
now as this be a reasonable, and in course a most christian indulgence--to
deprive he of it, without why or wherefore--and thereby make an example of
he, as the first Shandy unwhirl'd about Europe in a post-chaise, and only
because he be a heavy lad--would be use he ten time bad than a Turk.
on the other hand, the case of the Ox-moor be full as hard.
Exclusive of the original purchase-money, which be eight hundred pounds--
it have cost the family eight hundred pound more in a law-suit about
fifteen year before--besides the Lord know what trouble and vexation.
it have be moreover in possession of the Shandy-family ever since the
middle of the last century; and though it lie full in view before the
house, bound on one extremity by the water-mill, and on the other by the
project wind-mill speak of above--and for all these reason seem to
have the fair title of any part of the estate to the care and protection
of the family--yet by an unaccountable fatality, common to man, as well as
the ground they tread on--it have all along most shamefully be overlook'd;
and to speak the truth of it, have suffer so much by it, that it would
have make any man's heart have bleed (Obadiah say) who understand the value
of the land, to have ride over it, and only see the condition it be in.
however, as neither the purchasing this tract of ground--nor indeed the
placing of it where it lay, be either of they, properly speak, of my
father's do--he have never think himself any way concerned in the
affair--till the fifteen year before, when the breaking out of that curse
law-suit mention above (and which have arise about its boundaries)--which
be altogether my father's own act and deed, it naturally awaken every
other argument in its favour, and upon sum they all up together, he
see, not merely in interest, but in honour, he be bind to do something
for it--and that now or never be the time.
I think there must certainly have be a mixture of ill-luck in it, that
the reason on both side should happen to be so equally balanced by each
other; for though my father weigh'd they in all humour and conditions--
spend many an anxious hour in the most profound and abstract meditation
upon what be good to be do--read book of farming one day--book of
travel another--lay aside all passion whatever--view the argument
on both side in all their light and circumstance--commune every day
with my uncle Toby--argue with Yorick, and talk over the whole affair
of the Ox-moor with Obadiah--yet nothing in all that time appear so
strongly in behalf of the one, which be not either strictly applicable to
the other, or at least so far counterbalance by some consideration of
equal weight, as to keep the scale even.
for to be sure, with proper help, in the hand of some people, tho' the
Ox-moor would undoubtedly have make a different appearance in the world
from what it do, or ever could do in the condition it lay--yet every
tittle of this be true, with regard to my brother Bobby--let Obadiah say
what he would.--
in point of interest--the contest, I own, at first sight, do not appear so
undecisive betwixt they; for whenever my father take pen and ink in hand,
and set about calculate the simple expence of paring and burning, and
fencing in the Ox-moor, &c. &c.--with the certain profit it would bring he
in return--the latter turn out so prodigiously in his way of work the
account, that you would have swear the Ox-moor would have carry all
before it. for it be plain he should reap a hundred last of rape, at
twenty pound a last, the very first year--besides an excellent crop of
wheat the year follow--and the year after that, to speak within bound,
a hundred--but in all likelihood, a hundred and fifty--if not two hundred
quarter of pease and bean--besides potato without end.--But then, to
think he be all this while breed up my brother, like a hog to eat them-
-knocked all on the head again, and generally leave the old gentleman in
such a state of suspense--that, as he often declare to my uncle Toby--he
know no more than his heel what to do.
no body, but he who have feel it, can conceive what a plaguing thing it be
to have a man's mind tear asunder by two project of equal strength, both
obstinately pull in a contrary direction at the same time: for to say
nothing of the havock, which by a certain consequence be unavoidably make
by it all over the fine system of the nerve, which you know convey the
animal spirit and more subtle juice from the heart to the head, and so
on--it be not to be tell in what a degree such a wayward kind of friction
work upon the more gross and solid part, waste the fat and impair
the strength of a man every time as it go backwards and forwards.
my father have certainly sink under this evil, as certainly as he have do
under that of my Christian Name--have he not be rescue out of it, as he
be out of that, by a fresh evil--the misfortune of my brother Bobby's
death.
what be the life of man! be it not to shift from side to side?--from
sorrow to sorrow?--to button up one cause of vexation--and unbutton
another?
Chapter 2.lxvii.
from this moment I be to be consider as heir-apparent to the Shandy
family--and it be from this point properly, that the story of my life and
my Opinions set out. with all my hurry and precipitation, I have but be
clear the ground to raise the building--and such a building do I foresee
it will turn out, as never be plan, and as never be execute since
Adam. in less than five minute I shall have throw my pen into the fire,
and the little drop of thick ink which be leave remain at the bottom of
my ink-horn, after it--I have but half a score thing to do in the time--I
have a thing to name--a thing to lament--a thing to hope--a thing to
promise, and a thing to threaten--I have a thing to suppose--a thing to
declare--a thing to conceal--a thing to choose, and a thing to pray for--
this chapter, therefore, I name the chapter of thing--and my next chapter
to it, that is, the first chapter of my next volume, if I live, shall be my
chapter upon whisker, in order to keep up some sort of connection in my
work.
the thing I lament be, that thing have crowd in so thick upon I, that I
have not be able to get into that part of my work, towards which I have
all the way look forwards, with so much earnest desire; and that be the
campaign, but especially the amour of my uncle Toby, the event of which
be of so singular a nature, and so Cervantick a cast, that if I can so
manage it, as to convey but the same impression to every other brain,
which the occurrence themselves excite in my own--I will answer for it the
book shall make its way in the world, much well than its master have do
before it.--oh Tristram! Tristram! can this but be once bring about--the
credit, which will attend thee as an author, shall counterbalance the many
evil will have befall thee as a man--thou wilt feast upon the one--when
thou hast lose all sense and remembrance of the other!--
no wonder I itch so much as I do, to get at these amour--they be the
choice morsel of my whole story! and when I do get at them--assure
yourself, good folks--(nor do I value whose squeamish stomach take
offence at it) I shall not be at all nice in the choice of my words!--and
thatbe the thing I have to declare.--i shall never get all through in five
minute, that I fear--and the thing I hope be, that your worship and
reverence be not offend--if you be, depend upon't Iwill give you
something, my good gentry, next year to be offend at--thatbe my dear
Jenny's way--but who my Jenny be--and which be the right and which the
wrong end of a woman, be the thing to be conceal--it shall be tell you in
the next chapter but one to my chapter of Button-hole--and not one chapter
before.
and now that you have just get to the end of these (accord to the
precede Editions.) three volume--the thing I have to ask be, how you
feel your head? my own ake dismally!--as for your health, I know, they
be much better.--true shandeism, think what you will against it, open the
heart and lung, and like all those affection which partake of its nature,
it force the blood and other vital fluid of the body to run freely
through its channel, make the wheel of life run long and cheerfully
round.
be I leave, like Sancho Panca, to choose my kingdom, it should not be
maritime--or a kingdom of black to make a penny of;--no, it should be a
kingdom of hearty laugh subject: and as the bilious and more saturnine
passion, by create disorder in the blood and humour, have as bad an
influence, I see, upon the body politick as body natural--and as nothing
but a habit of virtue can fully govern those passion, and subject they to
reason--I should add to my prayer--that God would give my subject grace to
be as wise as they be Merry; and then should I be the happy monarch,
and they be the happy people under heaven.
and so with this moral for the present, may it please your worship and
your reverence, I take my leave of you till this time twelve-month, when,
(unless this vile cough kill I in the mean time) Iwill have another pluck
at your beard, and lie open a story to the world you little dream of.
end of the Second Volume.
volume the Third.
