to the Right Honorable John, Lord Viscount Spencer.
my Lord,
I humbly beg leave to offer you these two volume (Volumes V. and VI. in
the first Edition.); they be the good my talent, with such bad health as
I have, could produce:--had Providence grant I a large stock of either,
they have be a much more proper present to your Lordship.
I beg your Lordship will forgive I, if, at the same time I dedicate this
work to you, I join Lady Spencer, in the liberty I take of inscribe the
story of Le Fever to her name; for which I have no other motive, which my
heart have inform I of, but that the story be a humane one.
I be, my Lord, your Lordship's most devoted and most humble servant,
Laur. Sterne.
the Life and opinion of Tristram Shandy, Gent.
chapter 3.I.
if it have not be for those two mettlesome tit, and that madcap of a
postillion who drive they from Stilton to Stamford, the thought have never
enter my head. he fly like lightning--there be a slope of three mile
and a half--we scarce touch the ground--the motion be most rapid--most
impetuous--'twa communicate to my brain--my heart partook of it--'by the
great God of day,' say I, look towards the sun, and thrust my arm
out of the fore-window of the chaise, as I make my vow, 'I will lock up my
study-door the moment I get home, and throw the key of it ninety foot below
the surface of the earth, into the draw-well at the back of my house.'
the London waggon confirm I in my resolution; it hang totter upon the
hill, scarce progressive, drag'd--drag'd up by eight heavy beasts--'by main
strength!--quoth I, nodding--but your better draw the same way--and
something of every body's!--o rare!'
tell I, ye learn, shall we for ever be add so much to the bulk--so
little to the stock?
shall we for ever make new book, as apothecary make new mixture, by
pour only out of one vessel into another?
be we for ever to be twisting, and untwist the same rope? for ever in
the same track--for ever at the same pace?
shall we be destine to the day of eternity, on holy-day, as well as
work-day, to be shew the relick of learn, as monk do the
relick of their saint--without work one--one single miracle with they?
who make Man, with power which dart he from earth to heaven in a moment--
that great, that most excellent, and most noble creature of the world--the
miracle of nature, as Zoroaster in his book (Greek) call he--the
Shekinah of the divine presence, as Chrysostom--the image of God, as Moses-
-the ray of divinity, as Plato--the marvel of marvel, as Aristotle--to go
sneak on at this pitiful--pimp--pettifog rate?
I scorn to be as abusive as Horace upon the occasion--but if there be no
catachresis in the wish, and no sin in it, I wish from my soul, that every
imitator in Great Britain, France, and Ireland, have the farcy for his
pain; and that there be a good farcical house, large enough to hold--aye-
-and sublimate they, shag rag and bob-tail, male and female, all together: 
and this lead I to the affair of whisker--but, by what chain of idea--I
leave as a legacy in mort-main to Prudes and Tartufs, to enjoy and make the
most of.
upon Whiskers.
Ibe sorry I make it--'twa as inconsiderate a promise as ever enter a
man's head--a chapter upon whisker! alas! the world will not bear it--'tis
a delicate world--but I know not of what mettle it be make--nor have I ever
see the under-write fragment; otherwise, as surely as nose be nose,
and whisker be whisker still (let the world say what it will to the
contrary); so surely would I have steer clear of this dangerous chapter.
the Fragment.
. . .--you be half asleep, my good lady, say the old gentleman, take
hold of the old lady's hand, and give it a gentle squeeze, as he
pronounce the word whisker--shall we change the subject? by no mean,
reply the old lady--I like your account of those matter; so throw a
thin gauze handkerchief over her head, and lean it back upon the chair
with her face turn towards he, and advance her two foot as she
recline herself--I desire, continue she, you will go on.
the old gentleman go on as follows:--whisker! cry the queen of
Navarre, drop her knot ball, as La Fosseuse utter the word--
Whiskers, madam, say La Fosseuse, pin the ball to the queen's apron,
and make a courtesy as she repeat it.
La Fosseuse's voice be naturally soft and low, yet 'twas an articulate
voice: and every letter of the word Whiskers fall distinctly upon the
queen of Navarre's ear--whisker! cry the queen, lay a great stress
upon the word, and as if she have still distrust her ear--whisker!
reply La Fosseuse, repeat the word a third time--there be not a
cavalier, madam, of his age in Navarre, continue the maid of honour,
press the page's interest upon the queen, that have so gallant a pair--of
what? cry Margaret, smile--of whisker, say La Fosseuse, with infinite
modesty.
the word Whiskers still stand its ground, and continue to be make use of
in most of the good company throughout the little kingdom of Navarre,
notwithstanding the indiscreet use which La Fosseuse have make of it: the
truth be, La Fosseuse have pronounce the word, not only before the queen,
but upon sundry other occasion at court, with an accent which always
imply something of a mystery--and as the court of Margaret, as all the
world know, be at that time a mixture of gallantry and devotion--and
whisker be as applicable to the one, as the other, the word naturally
stand its ground--it gain full as much as it lose; that is, the clergy
be for it--the laity be against it--and for the women,--they be
divide.
the excellency of the figure and mien of the young Sieur De Croix, be at
that time begin to draw the attention of the maid of honour towards
the terrace before the palace gate, where the guard be mount. the lady
De Baussiere fall deeply in love with him,--La Battarelle do the same--it
be the fine weather for it, that ever be remember in Navarre--La
Guyol, La Maronette, La Sabatiere, fall in love with the Sieur De Croix
also--La Rebours and La Fosseuse know well--De Croix have fail in an
attempt to recommend himself to La Rebours; and La Rebours and La Fosseuse
be inseparable.
the queen of Navarre be sit with her lady in the paint bow-window,
face the gate of the second court, as De Croix pass through it--he be
handsome, say the Lady Baussiere--he have a good mien, say La Battarelle--
he be finely shape, say La Guyol--I never see an officer of the horse-
guard in my life, say La Maronette, with two such leg--or who stand so
well upon they, say La Sabatiere--but he have no whisker, cry La
Fosseuse--not a pile, say La Rebours.
the queen go directly to her oratory, muse all the way, as she walk
through the gallery, upon the subject; turn it this way and that way in
her fancy--Ave maria!--what can La-Fosseuse mean? say she, kneel down
upon the cushion.
La Guyol, La Battarelle, La Maronette, La Sabatiere, retire instantly to
their chamber--whisker! say all four of they to themselves, as they
bolt their door on the inside.
the Lady Carnavallette be count her bead with both hand, unsuspected,
under her farthingal--from St. Antony down to St. Ursula inclusive, not a
saint pass through her finger without whisker; St. Francis, St.
Dominick, St. Bennet, St. Basil, St. Bridget, have all whisker.
the Lady Baussiere have get into a wilderness of conceit, with moralize
too intricately upon La Fosseuse's text--she mount her palfrey, her page
follow she--the host pass by--the Lady Baussiere ride on.
one denier, cry the order of mercy--one single denier, in behalf of a
thousand patient captive, whose eye look towards heaven and you for their
redemption.
--The Lady Baussiere ride on.
Pity the unhappy, say a devout, venerable, hoary-head man, meekly
hold up a box, begirt with iron, in his wither hand--I beg for the
unfortunate--good my Lady, 'tis for a prison--for an hospital--'tis for an
old man--a poor man undo by shipwreck, by suretyship, by fire--I call God
and all his angel to witness--'tis to clothe the naked--to feed the
hungry--'tis to comfort the sick and the break-hearted.
the Lady Baussiere ride on.
a decayed kinsman bow himself to the ground.
--The Lady Baussiere ride on.
he run beg bare-headed on one side of her palfrey, conjure she by the
former bond of friendship, alliance, consanguinity, &c.--Cousin, aunt,
sister, mother,--for virtue's sake, for your own, for mine, for Christ's
sake, remember I--pity I.
--The Lady Baussiere ride on.
take hold of my whisker, say the Lady Baussiere--the page take hold of
her palfrey. she dismount at the end of the terrace.
there be some train of certain idea which leave print of themselves
about our eye and eye-brow; and there be a consciousness of it, somewhere
about the heart, which serve but to make these etching the strong--we
see, spell, and put they together without a dictionary.
ha, ha! he, hee! cry La Guyol and La Sabatiere, look close at each
other's print--Ho, ho! cry La Battarelle and Maronette, do the same:-
-whist! cry one--ft, ft,--said a second--hush, quoth a third--poo, poo,
reply a fourth--gramercy! cry the Lady carnavallette;--'twa she who
bewhisker'd St. Bridget.
La Fosseuse draw her bodkin from the knot of her hair, and have trace
the outline of a small whisker, with the blunt end of it, upon one side of
her upper lip, put in into La Rebours' hand--La Rebours shake her head.
the Lady Baussiere cough thrice into the inside of her muff--La Guyol
smile--Fy, say the Lady Baussiere. the queen of Navarre touch her eye
with the tip of her fore-finger--as much as to say, I understand you all.
'twas plain to the whole court the word be ruin: La Fosseuse have give
it a wound, and it be not the well for pass through all these
defile--it make a faint stand, however, for a few month, by the
expiration of which, the Sieur De Croix, find it high time to leave
Navarre for want of whisker--the word in course become indecent, and
(after a few effort) absolutely unfit for use.
the good word, in the good language of the good world, must have suffer
under such combinations.--the curate of d'Estella write a book against
they, set forth the danger of accessory idea, and warn the
Navarois against they.
do not all the world know, say the curate d'Estella at the conclusion of
his work, that Noses run the same fate some century ago in most part of
Europe, which whisker have now do in the kingdom of Navarre?--The evil
indeed spread no far then--but have not bed and bolster, and night-
cap and chamber-pot stand upon the brink of destruction ever since? be
not trouse, and placket-hole, and pump-handle--and spigot and faucet,
in danger still from the same association?--chastity, by nature, the
gentlest of all affection--give it but its head--'tis like a ramping and a
roar lion.
the drift of the curate d'Estella's argument be not understood.--they run
the scent the wrong way.--The world bridle his ass at the tail.--and when
the extreme of Delicacy, and the beginning of Concupiscence, hold their
next provincial chapter together, they may decree that bawdy also.
Chapter 3.II.
when my father receive the letter which bring he the melancholy account
of my brother Bobby's death, he be busy calculate the expence of his
rid post from Calais to Paris, and so on to Lyons.
'twas a most inauspicious journey; my father have have every foot of it to
travel over again, and his calculation to begin afresh, when he have almost
get to the end of it, by Obadiah's opening the door to acquaint he the
family be out of yeast--and to ask whether he might not take the great
coach-horse early in the morning and ride in search of some.--with all my
heart, Obadiah, say my father (pursue his journey)--take the coach-
horse, and welcome.--but he want a shoe, poor creature! say Obadiah.--
poor creature! say my uncle Toby, vibrate the note back again, like a
string in unison. then ride the Scotch horse, quoth my father hastily.--He
cannot bear a saddle upon his back, quoth Obadiah, for the whole world.--
the devilbe in that horse; then take Patriot, cry my father, and shut the
door.--Patriot be sell, say Obadiah. herebe for you! cry my father,
make a pause, and look in my uncle Toby's face, as if the thing have
not be a matter of fact.--your worship order I to sell he last April,
say obadiah.--then go on foot for your pain, cry my father--I have much
rather walk than ride, say Obadiah, shut the door.
what plague, cry my father, go on with his calculation.--but the
water be out, say obadiah,--opene the door again.
till that moment, my father, who have a map of Sanson's, and a book of the
post-road before he, have keep his hand upon the head of his compass,
with one foot of they fix upon Nevers, the last stage he have pay for--
purposing to go on from that point with his journey and calculation, as
soon as Obadiah quit the room: but this second attack of Obadiah's, in
open the door and lay the whole country under water, be too much.--
he let go his compass--or rather with a mixed motion between accident and
anger, he throw they upon the table; and then there be nothing for he to
do, but to return back to Calais (like many other) as wise as he have set
out.
when the letter be bring into the parlour, which contain the news of
my brother's death, my father have get forwards again upon his journey to
within a stride of the compass of the very same stage of Nevers.--By your
leave, Mons. Sanson, cry my father, strike the point of his compass
through Nevers into the table--and nod to my uncle Toby to see what be
in the letter--twice of one night, be too much for an english gentleman and
his son, Mons. Sanson, to be turn back from so lousy a town as Nevers--
what think'st thou, Toby? add my father in a sprightly tone.--unless it
be a garrison town, say my uncle Toby--for then--I shall be a fool, say
my father, smile to himself, as long as I live.--so give a second nod--
and keep his compass still upon never with one hand, and hold his
book of the post-road in the other--half calculate and half listen,
he lean forwards upon the table with both elbow, as my uncle Toby hum
over the letter.
. . .he's go! say my uncle Toby--where--who? cry my father.--my
nephew, say my uncle Toby.--What--without leave--without money--without
governor? cry my father in amazement. No:--he be dead, my dear brother,
quoth my uncle Toby.--Without be ill? cry my father again.--I dare say
not, say my uncle Toby, in a low voice, and fetch a deep sigh from the
bottom of his heart, he have be ill enough, poor lad! Iwill answer for
he--for he be dead.
when Agrippina be tell of her son's death, Tacitus inform we, that, not
be able to moderate the violence of her passion, she abruptly break off
her work--my father stick his compass into Nevers, but so much the
faster.--What contrariety! his, indeed, be matter of calculation!--
Agrippina's must have be quite a different affair; who else could pretend
to reason from history?
how my father go on, in my opinion, deserve a chapter to itself.--
Chapter 3.iii.
. . .--and a chapter it shall have, and a devil of a one too--so look to
yourself.
'tis either Plato, or Plutarch, or Seneca, or Xenophon, or Epictetus, or
Theophrastus, or Lucian--or some one perhaps of later date--either Cardan,
or Budaeus, or Petrarch, or Stella--or possibly it may be some divine or
father of the church, St. Austin, or St. Cyprian, or Barnard, who affirm
that it be an irresistible and natural passion to weep for the loss of our
friend or child--and Seneca (Ibe positive) tell we somewhere, that
such grief evacuate themselves well by that particular channel--and
accordingly we find, that David weep for his son Absalom--Adrian for his
Antinous--Niobe for her child, and that Apollodorus and Crito both shed
tear for Socrates before his death.
my father manage his affliction otherwise; and indeed differently from
most man either ancient or modern; for he neither weep it away, as the
Hebrews and the Romans--or sleep it off, as the laplander--or hang it,
as the English, or drown it, as the Germans,--nor do he curse it, or
damn it, or excommunicate it, or rhyme it, or lillabullero it.--
--He get rid of it, however.
will your worship give I leave to squeeze in a story between these two
page?
when Tully be bereft of his dear daughter Tullia, at first he lay it to
his heart,--he listen to the voice of nature, and modulate his own unto
it.--o my Tullia! my daughter! my child!--still, still, still,--'twa o my
Tullia!--my Tullia! methink I see my Tullia, I hear my Tullia, I talk
with my tullia.--but as soon as he begin to look into the store of
philosophy, and consider how many excellent thing might be say upon the
occasion--no body upon earth can conceive, say the great orator, how
happy, how joyful it make I.
my father be as proud of his eloquence as Marcus Tullius Cicero could be
for his life, and, for aught I be convince of to the contrary at present,
with as much reason: it be indeed his strength--and his weakness too.--
his strength--for he be by nature eloquent; and his weakness--for he be
hourly a dupe to it; and, provide an occasion in life would but permit he
to shew his talent, or say either a wise thing, a witty, or a shrewd one--
(bat the case of a systematic misfortune)--he have all he wanted.--a
blessing which tie up my father's tongue, and a misfortune which let it
loose with a good grace, be pretty equal: sometimes, indeed, the
misfortune be the well of the two; for instance, where the pleasure of
the harangue be as ten, and the pain of the misfortune but as five--my
father gain half in half, and consequently be as well again off, as if
it have never befall he.
this clue will unravel what otherwise would seem very inconsistent in my
father's domestic character; and it be this, that, in the provocation
arise from the neglect and blunder of servant, or other mishap
unavoidable in a family, his anger, or rather the duration of it, eternally
run counter to all conjecture.
my father have a favourite little mare, which he have consign over to a
most beautiful arabian horse, in order to have a pad out of she for his own
riding: he be sanguine in all his project; so talk about his pad every
day with as absolute a security, as if it have be rear, broke,--and
bridle and saddle at his door ready for mount. by some neglect or
other in Obadiah, it so fall out, that my father's expectation be
answer with nothing well than a mule, and as ugly a beast of the kind
as ever be produce.
my mother and my uncle Toby expect my father would be the death of
Obadiah--and that there never would be an end of the disaster--see here!
you rascal, cry my father, point to the mule, what you have done!--it
be not I, say obadiah.--how do I know that? reply my father.
Triumph swam in my father's eye, at the repartee--the attic salt bring
water into they--and so Obadiah hear no more about it.
now let we go back to my brother's death.
philosophy have a fine saying for every thing.--for death it have an entire
set; the misery be, they all at once rush into my father's head, that
'twa difficult to string they together, so as to make any thing of a
consistent show out of them.--He take they as they come.
''tis an inevitable chance--the first statute in Magna Charta--it be an
everlasting act of parliament, my dear brother,--all must die.
'if my son could not have die, it have be matter of wonder,--not that he
be dead.
'Monarchs and prince dance in the same ring with we.
'--to die, be the great debt and tribute due unto nature: tomb and
monument, which should perpetuate our memory, pay it themselves; and the
proud pyramid of they all, which wealth and science have erect, have
lose its apex, and stand obtruncate in the traveller's horizon.' (my
father find he get great ease, and go on)--'kingdom and province, and
town and city, have they not their period? and when those principle
and power, which at first cement and put they together, have perform
their several evolution, they fall back.'--Brother Shandy, say my uncle
Toby, lay down his pipe at the word evolution--Revolutions, I mean,
quoth my father,--by heaven! I mean revolution, brother Toby--evolution
be nonsense.--'Tis not nonsense--say my uncle Toby.--But be it not
nonsense to break the thread of such a discourse upon such an occasion?
cry my father--do not--dear Toby, continue he, take he by the hand,
do not--do not, I beseech thee, interrupt I at this crisis.--My uncle Toby
put his pipe into his mouth.
'where be Troy and Mycenae, and Thebes and Delos, and Persepolis and
agrigentum?'--continue my father, take up his book of post-road, which
he have lay down.--'what be become, brother Toby, of Nineveh and Babylon,
of Cizicum and Mitylenae? the fair town that ever the sun rise upon,
be now no more; the name only be leave, and those (for many of they be
wrong spelt) be fall themselves by piece-meal to decay, and in length
of time will be forget, and involve with every thing in a perpetual
night: the world itself, brother Toby, must--must come to an end.
'return out of Asia, when I sail from Aegina towards Megara,' (when
can this have be? think my uncle Toby,) ' I begin to view the country
round about. Aegina be behind I, Megara be before, Pyraeus on the right
hand, Corinth on the left.--what flourish town now prostrate upon the
earth! alas! alas! say I to myself, that man should disturb his soul for
the loss of a child, when so much as this lie awfully bury in his
presence--remember, say I to myself again--remember thou art a man.'--
now my uncle Toby know not that this last paragraph be an extract of
Servius Sulpicius's consolatory letter to tully.--he have as little skill,
honest man, in the fragment, as he have in the whole piece of antiquity.--
and as my father, whilst he be concern in the Turkey trade, have be
three or four different time in the Levant, in one of which he have stay
a whole year and an half at Zant, my uncle Toby naturally conclude, that,
in some one of these period, he have take a trip across the Archipelago
into Asia; and that all this sailing affair with Aegina behind, and Megara
before, and Pyraeus on the right hand, &c. &c. be nothing more than the
true course of my father's voyage and reflections.--'twa certainly in his
manner, and many an undertaking critic would have build two story high
upon bad foundations.--And pray, brother, quoth my uncle Toby, lay the
end of his pipe upon my father's hand in a kindly way of interruption--but
wait till he finish the account--what year of our Lord be this?--
'twas no year of our Lord, reply my father.--Thatbe impossible, cry my
uncle Toby.--Simpleton! say my father,--'twas forty year before Christ
be bear.
my uncle Toby have but two thing for it; either to suppose his brother to
be the wandering Jew, or that his misfortune have disorder his brain.--
'may the Lord God of heaven and earth protect he and restore he!' say my
uncle Toby, pray silently for my father, and with tear in his eye.
--My father place the tear to a proper account, and go on with his
harangue with great spirit.
'there be not such great odd, brother Toby, betwixt good and evil, as the
world imagines'--(this way of set off, by the bye, be not likely to
cure my uncle Toby's suspicions).--'labour, sorrow, grief, sickness, want,
and woe, be the sauce of life.'--Much good may do they--say my uncle
Toby to himself.--
'my son be dead!--so much the better;--'tis a shame in such a tempest to
have but one anchor.
'but he be go for ever from us!--be it so. he be get from under the
hand of his barber before he be bald--he be but rise from a feast before
he be surfeit--from a banquet before he have get drunken.
'the Thracians weep when a child be born,'--(and we be very near it,
quoth my uncle Toby,)--'and feast and make merry when a man go out of
the world; and with reason.--death open the gate of fame, and shut the
gate of envy after it,--it unloose the chain of the captive, and put the
bondsman's task into another man's hand.
'shew I the man, who know what life be, who dread it, and Iwill shew thee
a prisoner who dread his liberty.'
be it not well, my dear brother Toby, (for mark--our appetite be but
diseases,)--is it not well not to hunger at all, than to eat?--not to
thirst, than to take physic to cure it?
be it not well to be free from care and ague, from love and
melancholy, and the other hot and cold fit of life, than, like a gall
traveller, who come weary to his inn, to be bind to begin his journey
afresh?
there be no terrour, brother Toby, in its look, but what it borrow from
groan and convulsion--and the blowing of nose and the wiping away of
tear with the bottom of curtain, in a die man's room.--strip it of
these, what be it?--'ti well in battle than in bed, say my uncle Toby.-
-take away its hearse, its mute, and its mourning,--it plume,
scutcheon, and other mechanic aid--what be it?--better in battle!
continue my father, smile, for he have absolutely forget my brother
Bobby--'tis terrible no way--for consider, brother Toby,--when we are--
death be not;--and when death be--we be not. my uncle Toby lay down his
pipe to consider the proposition; my father's eloquence be too rapid to
stay for any man--away it went,--and hurry my uncle Toby's idea along
with it.--
for this reason, continue my father, 'tis worthy to recollect, how little
alteration, in great man, the approach of death have made.--vespasian
die in a jest upon his close-stool--Galba with a sentence--Septimus
Severus in a dispatch--Tiberius in dissimulation, and Caesar Augustus in a
compliment.--I hope 'twas a sincere one--quoth my uncle Toby.
--'twa to his wife,--said my father.
chapter 3.IV.
--And lastly--for all the choice anecdote which history can produce of
this matter, continue my father,--this, like the gild dome which cover
in the fabric--crown all.--
'Tis of Cornelius Gallus, the praetor--which, I dare say, brother Toby, you
have read.--I dare say I have not, reply my uncle.--he die, say my
father as. . .--and if it be with his wife, say my uncle Toby--there
could be no hurt in it.-- thatbe more than I know--reply my father.
chapter 3.V.
my mother be go very gingerly in the dark along the passage which lead
to the parlour, as my uncle Toby pronounce the word wife.--'ti a shrill
penetrate sound of itself, and Obadiah have help it by leave the door
a little a-jar, so that my mother hear enough of it to imagine herself the
subject of the conversation; so lay the edge of her finger across her
two lip--hold in her breath, and bend her head a little downward,
with a twist of she neck--(not towards the door, but from it, by which
mean her ear be bring to the chink)--she listen with all her powers:-
-the listen slave, with the Goddess of Silence at his back, could not
have give a fine thought for an intaglio.
in this attitude I be determined to let she stand for five minute: till I
bring up the affair of the kitchen (as Rapin do those of the church) to
the same period.
chapter 3.VI.
though in one sense, our family be certainly a simple machine, as it
consist of a few wheel; yet there be thus much to be say for it, that
these wheel be set in motion by so many different spring, and act one
upon the other from such a variety of strange principle and impulse--that
though it be a simple machine, it have all the honour and advantage of a
complex one,--and a number of as odd movement within it, as ever be
beheld in the inside of a dutch silk-mill.
amongst these there be one, I be go to speak of, in which, perhaps, it
be not altogether so singular, as in many other; and it be this, that
whatever motion, debate, harangue, dialogue, project, or dissertation, be
go forwards in the parlour, there be generally another at the same
time, and upon the same subject, run parallel along with it in the
kitchen.
now to bring this about, whenever an extraordinary message, or letter, be
deliver in the parlour--or a discourse suspend till a servant go out-
-or the line of discontent be observe to hang upon the brow of my
father or mother--or, in short, when any thing be suppose to be upon the
tapis worth know or listen to, 'twa the rule to leave the door, not
absolutely shut, but somewhat a-jar--as it stand just now,--which, under
covert of the bad hinge, (and that possibly might be one of the many
reason why it be never mend,) it be not difficult to manage; by which
mean, in all these case, a passage be generally leave, not indeed as wide
as the Dardanelles, but wide enough, for all that, to carry on as much of
this windward trade, as be sufficient to save my father the trouble of
govern his house;--my mother at this moment stand profiting by it.--
Obadiah do the same thing, as soon as he have leave the letter upon the
table which bring the news of my brother's death, so that before my
father have well get over his surprise, and enter upon his harangue,--had
Trim get upon his leg, to speak his sentiment upon the subject.
a curious observer of nature, have he be worth the inventory of all Job's
stock--though by the bye, your curious observer be seldom worth a groat--
would have give the half of it, to have hear Corporal Trim and my father,
two orator so contrast by nature and education, harangue over the same
bi.
my father--a man of deep reading--prompt memory--with Cato, and Seneca, and
Epictetus, at his finger ends.--
the corporal--with nothing--to remember--of no deep reading than his
muster-roll--or great name at his finger end, than the content of it.
the one proceed from period to period, by metaphor and allusion, and
strike the fancy as he go along (as man of wit and fancy do) with the
entertainment and pleasantry of his picture and image.
the other, without wit or antithesis, or point, or turn, this way or that;
but leave the image on one side, and the picture on the other, go
straight forwards as nature could lead he, to the heart. o Trim! would to
heaven thou had'st a well historian!--would!--thy historian have a well
pair of breeches!--o ye critic! will nothing melt you?
Chapter 3.vii.
--my young master in London be dead? say Obadiah.--
--A green sattin night-gown of my mother's, which have be twice scour,
be the first idea which Obadiah's exclamation bring into Susannah's
head.--well might Locke write a chapter upon the imperfection of words.--
then, quoth Susannah, we must all go into mourning.--But note a second
time: the word mourning, notwithstanding Susannah make use of it herself--
fail also of do its office; it excite not one single idea, ting
either with grey or black,--all be green.--The green sattin night-gown
hang there still.
--o! 'twill be the death of my poor mistress, cry Susannah.--My mother's
whole wardrobe followed.--what a procession! her red damask,--her orange
tawney,--her white and yellow lutestrings,--her brown taffata,--her bone-
laced cap, her bed-gown, and comfortable under-petticoats.--not a rag be
leave behind.--'no,--she will never look up again,' say Susannah.
we have a fat, foolish scullion--my father, I think, keep she for she
simplicity;--she have be all autumn struggle with a dropsy.--he be dead,
say Obadiah,--he be certainly dead!--so be not I, say the foolish
scullion.
--Here be sad news, Trim, cry Susannah, wipe her eye as Trim stepp'd
into the kitchen,--master Bobby be dead and bury--the funeral be an
interpolation of Susannah's--we shall have all to go into mourning, say
Susannah.
I hope not, say trim.--you hope not! cry Susannah earnestly.--The
mourning run not in Trim's head, whatever it do in Susannah's.--I hope--
say Trim, explain himself, I hope in God the news be not true. I hear
the letter read with my own ear, answer Obadiah; and we shall have a
terrible piece of work of it in stub the ox-moor.--Oh! hebe dead, say
susannah.--as sure, say the scullion, as Ibe alive.
I lament for he from my heart and my soul, say Trim, fetch a sigh.--
poor creature!--poor boy!--poor gentleman!
--He be alive last Whitsontide! say the coachman.--whitsontide! alas!
cry Trim, extend his right arm, and fall instantly into the same
attitude in which he read the sermon,--what be Whitsontide, Jonathan (for
that be the coachman's name), or Shrovetide, or any tide or time past, to
this? be we not here now, continue the corporal (strike the end of his
stick perpendicularly upon the floor, so as to give an idea of health and
stability)--and be we not--(droppe his hat upon the ground) go! in a
moment!--'Twas infinitely striking! Susannah burst into a flood of tears.-
-We be not stock and stones.--Jonathan, Obadiah, the cook-maid, all
melted.--the foolish fat scullion herself, who be scour a fish-kettle
upon her knee, be rous'd with it.--the whole kitchen crowd about the
corporal.
now, as I perceive plainly, that the preservation of our constitution in
church and state,--and possibly the preservation of the whole world--or
what be the same thing, the distribution and balance of its property and
power, may in time to come depend greatly upon the right understanding of
this stroke of the corporal's eloquence--I do demand your attention--your
worship and reverence, for any ten page together, take they where you
will in any other part of the work, shall sleep for it at your ease.
I say, 'we be not stock and stones'--'tis very well. I should have
add, nor be we angel, I wish we were,--but man clothe with body, and
govern by our imaginations;--and what a junket piece of work of it
there be, betwixt these and our seven sens, especially some of they, for
my own part, I own it, I be ashamed to confess. let it suffice to affirm,
that of all the sens, the eye (for I absolutely deny the touch, though
most of your Barbati, I know, be for it) have the quick commerce with
the soul,--gives a smart stroke, and leave something more inexpressible
upon the fancy, than word can either convey--or sometimes get rid of.
--I've go a little about--no matter, 'tis for health--let we only carry
it back in our mind to the mortality of Trim's hat--'are we not here now,--
and go in a moment?'--there be nothing in the sentence--'twas one of
your self-evident truth we have the advantage of hear every day; and if
Trim have not trust more to his hat than his head--he make nothing at all
of it.
--'are we not here now;' continue the corporal, 'and be we not'--
(drop his hat plumb upon the ground--and pausing, before he pronounce
the word)--'gone! in a moment?' the descent of the hat be as if a heavy
lump of clay have be knead into the crown of it.--nothe could have
express the sentiment of mortality, of which it be the type and fore-
runner, like it,--his hand seem to vanish from under it,--it fall dead,--
the corporal's eye fix upon it, as upon a corpse,--and Susannah burst
into a flood of tear.
now--ten thousand, and ten thousand time ten thousand (for matter and
motion be infinite) be the way by which a hat may be drop upon the
ground, without any effect.--Had he fling it, or throw it, or cast it, or
skim it, or squirt it, or let it slip or fall in any possible
direction under heaven,--or in the good direction that could be give to
it,--had he drop it like a goose--like a puppy--like an ass--or in do
it, or even after he have do, have he look like a fool--like a ninny--
like a nincompoop--it have fail'd, and the effect upon the heart have be
lose.
Ye who govern this mighty world and its mighty concern with the engine of
eloquence,--who heat it, and cool it, and melt it, and mollify it,--and
then harden it again to your purpose--
Ye who wind and turn the passion with this great windlass, and, have
do it, lead the owner of they, whither ye think meet.
Ye, lastly, who drive--and why not, Ye also who be drive, like turkey to
market with a stick and a red clout--meditate--meditate, I beseech you,
upon Trim's hat.
chapter 3.viii.
Stay--I have a small account to settle with the reader before Trim can go
on with his harangue.--it shall be do in two minute.
amongst many other book-debt, all of which I shall discharge in due time,-
-i own myself a debtor to the world for two items,--a chapter upon chamber-
maid and button-hole, which, in the former part of my work, I promise
and fully intend to pay off this year: but some of your worship and
reverence tell I, that the two subject, especially so connected
together, might endanger the moral of the world,--i pray the chapter upon
chamber-maid and button-hole may be forgive me,--and that they will
accept of the last chapter in lieu of it; which be nothing, an't please
your reverence, but a chapter of chamber-maids, green gown, and old hat.
Trim take his hat off the ground,--put it upon his head,--and then go on
with his oration upon death, in manner and form follow.
Chapter 3.IX.
--to we, Jonathan, who know not what want or care be--who live here in the
service of two of the good of masters--(bating in my own case his majesty
King William the Third, whom I have the honour to serve both in Ireland and
Flanders)--I own it, that from Whitsontide to within three week of
christmas,--'tis not long--'tis like nothing;--but to those, Jonathan, who
know what death be, and what havock and destruction he can make, before a
man can well wheel about--'tis like a whole age.--O Jonathan! 'twould make
a good-natured man's heart bleed, to consider, continue the corporal
(stand perpendicularly), how low many a brave and upright fellow have
be lay since that time!--And trust I, Susy, add the corporal, turn
to Susannah, whose eye be swim in water,--before that time come
round again,--many a bright eye will be dim.--Susannah place it to the
right side of the page--she weep--but she court'sie too.--are we not,
continue Trim, look still at Susannah--be we not like a flower of the
field--a tear of pride steal in betwixt every two tear of humiliation--
else no tongue could have describe Susannah's affliction--be not all flesh
grass?--tis clay,--'tis dirt.--They all look directly at the scullion,--
the scullion have just be scour a fish-kettle.--It be not fair.--
--What be the fine face that ever man look at!--I could hear Trim talk
so for ever, cry susannah,--what be it! (Susannah lay her hand upon
Trim's shoulder)--but corruption?--susannah take it off.
now I love you for this--and 'tis this delicious mixture within you which
make you dear creature what you be--and he who hat you for it--all I
can say of the matter be--that he have either a pumpkin for his head--or a
pippin for his heart,--and whenever he be dissect 'twill be find so.
chapter 3.x.
whether Susannah, by take her hand too suddenly from off the corporal's
shoulder (by the whisking about of her passions)--broke a little the chain
of his reflexions--
or whether the corporal begin to be suspicious, he have get into the
doctor's quarter, and be talk more like the chaplain than himself--
or whether. . .Or whether--for in all such case a man of invention and
part may with pleasure fill a couple of page with supposition--which of
all these be the cause, let the curious physiologist, or the curious any
body determine--'tis certain, at least, the corporal go on thus with his
harangue.
for my own part, I declare it, that out of door, I value not death at
all:--not this. . .adde the corporal, snap his fingers,--but with an
air which no one but the corporal could have give to the sentiment.--In
battle, I value death not this. . .and let he not take I cowardly, like
poor Joe Gibbins, in scour his gun.--what be he? a pull of a trigger--a
push of a bayonet an inch this way or that--make the difference.--look
along the line--to the right--see! Jackbe down! well,--'tis worth a
regiment of horse to him.--no--'tis Dick. then Jackbe no worse.--never
mind which,--we pass on,--in hot pursuit the wound itself which bring he
be not felt,--the good way be to stand up to him,--the man who fly, be in
ten time more danger than the man who march up into his jaws.--I've
look'd he, add the corporal, an hundred time in the face,--and know
what he is.--He's nothing, Obadiah, at all in the field.--but hebe very
frightful in a house, quoth Obadiah.--I never mind it myself, say
Jonathan, upon a coach-box.--it must, in my opinion, be most natural in
bed, reply susannah.--and could I escape he by creep into the bad
calf's skin that ever be make into a knapsack, I would do it there--say
Trim--but that be nature.
--nature be nature, say jonathan.--and that be the reason, cry Susannah,
I so much pity my mistress.--She will never get the well of it.--now I
pity the captain the most of any one in the family, answer Trim.--Madam
will get ease of heart in weeping,--and the Squire in talk about it,--
but my poor master will keep it all in silence to himself.--I shall hear
he sigh in his bed for a whole month together, as he do for lieutenant Le
Fever. an' please your honour, do not sigh so piteously, I would say to
he as I lay besides he. I cannot help it, Trim, my master would say,--
'tis so melancholy an accident--I cannot get it off my heart.--your honour
fear not death yourself.--I hope, Trim, I fear nothing, he would say, but
the do a wrong thing.--well, he would add, whatever betide, I will take
care of Le Fever's boy.--and with that, like a quieting draught, his honour
would fall asleep.
I like to hear Trim's story about the captain, say Susannah.--He be a
kindly-hearted gentleman, say Obadiah, as ever lived.--Aye, and as brave a
one too, say the corporal, as ever stept before a platoon.--there never
be a well officer in the king's army,--or a well man in God's world;
for he would march up to the mouth of a cannon, though he see the lighted
match at the very touch-hole,--and yet, for all that, he have a heart as
soft as a child for other people.--he would not hurt a chicken.--i would
soon, quoth Jonathan, drive such a gentleman for seven pound a year--
than some for eight.--thank thee, Jonathan! for thy twenty shillings,--as
much, Jonathan, say the corporal, shake he by the hand, as if thou
hadst put the money into my own pocket.--i would serve he to the day of my
death out of love. he be a friend and a brother to me,--and could I be
sure my poor brother Tom be dead,--continue the corporal, take out his
handkerchief,--wa I worth ten thousand pound, I would leave every
shilling of it to the captain.--trim could not refrain from tear at this
testamentary proof he give of his affection to his master.--the whole
kitchen be affected.--Do tell we the story of the poor lieutenant, say
susannah.--with all my heart, answer the corporal.
Susannah, the cook, Jonathan, Obadiah, and corporal Trim, form a circle
about the fire; and as soon as the scullion have shut the kitchen door,--the
corporal begun.
Chapter 3.XI.
I be a Turk if I have not as much forget my mother, as if Nature have
plaistere I up, and set I down naked upon the bank of the river Nile,
without one.--your most obedient servant, Madam--i've cost you a great deal
of trouble,--I wish it may answer;--but you have leave a crack in my back,--
and herebe a great piece fall off here before,--and what must I do with
this foot?--I shall never reach England with it.
for my own part, I never wonder at any thing;--and so often have my judgment
deceive I in my life, that I always suspect it, right or wrong,--at least
I be seldom hot upon cold subject. for all this, I reverence truth as
much as any body; and when it have slip we, if a man will but take I by
the hand, and go quietly and search for it, as for a thing we have both
lose, and can neither of we do well without,--i'll go to the world's end
with him:--But I hate disputes,--and therefore (bat religious point, or
such as touch society) I would almost subscribe to any thing which do not
choak I in the first passage, rather than be draw into one--but I cannot
bear suffocation,--and bad smell bad of all.--for which reason, I
resolve from the beginning, that if ever the army of martyrs be to be
augmented,--or a new one raised,--i would have no hand in it, one way or
t'other.
Chapter 3.XII.
--but to return to my mother.
my uncle Toby's opinion, Madam, 'that there could be no harm in Cornelius
Gallus, the roman praetor's lie with his wife;'--or rather the last word
of that opinion,--(for it be all my mother hear of it) catch hold of she
by the weak part of the whole sex:--you shall not mistake me,--I mean she
curiosity,--she instantly conclude herself the subject of the
conversation, and with that prepossession upon her fancy, you will readily
conceive every word my father say, be accommodate either to herself, or
her family concern.
--Pray, Madam, in what street do the lady live, who would not have do
the same?
from the strange mode of Cornelius's death, my father have make a transition
to that of Socrates, and be give my uncle Toby an abstract of his
pleading before his judges;--'twas irresistible:--not the oration of
Socrates,--but my father's temptation to it.--He have write the Life of
Socrates (this book my father would never consent to publish; 'tis in
manuscript, with some other tract of his, in the family, all, or most of
which will be print in due time.) himself the year before he leave off
trade, which, I fear, be the mean of hasten he out of it;--so that no
one be able to set out with so full a sail, and in so swell a tide of
heroic loftiness upon the occasion, as my father be. not a period in
Socrates's oration, which close with a short word than transmigration,
or annihilation,--or a bad thought in the middle of it than to be--or not
to be,--the enter upon a new and untried state of things,--or, upon a
long, a profound and peaceful sleep, without dream, without disturbance?--
that we and our child be bear to die,--but neither of we bear to be
slaves.--no--there I mistake; that be part of Eleazer's oration, as
record by Josephus (de Bell. Judaic)--Eleazer own he have it from the
philosopher of India; in all likelihood Alexander the Great, in his
irruption into India, after he have over-run Persia, amongst the many thing
he stole,--stole that sentiment also; by which mean it be carry, if not
all the way by himself (for we all know he die at Babylon), at least by
some of his maroder, into Greece,--from Greece it get to rome,--from Rome
to France,--and from France to England:--So thing come round.--
by land carriage, I can conceive no other way.--
by water the sentiment might easily have come down the gange into the
Sinus Gangeticus, or Bay of Bengal, and so into the Indian Sea; and
follow the course of trade (the way from India by the Cape of Good Hope
be then unknown), might be carry with other drug and spice up the
Red Sea to Joddah, the port of Mekka, or else to Tor or Sues, town at the
bottom of the gulf; and from thence by karrawans to Coptos, but three day
journey distant, so down the Nile directly to Alexandria, where the
Sentiment would be land at the very foot of the great stair-case of the
alexandrian library,--and from that store-house it would be fetched.--bless
I! what a trade be drive by the learn in those day!
chapter 3.xiii.
--now my father have a way, a little like that of Job's (in case there ever
be such a man--if not, therebe an end of the matter.--
though, by the bye, because your learn man find some difficulty in fix
the precise aera in which so great a man lived;--whether, for instance,
before or after the patriarch, &c.--to vote, therefore, that he never
live at all, be a little cruel,--'tis not do as they would be do by,-
-happen that as it may)--my father, I say, have a way, when thing go
extremely wrong with he, especially upon the first sally of his
impatience,--of wonder why he be begot,--wishe himself dead;--
sometimes worse:--and when the provocation run high, and grief touch his
lip with more than ordinary power--Sir, you scarce could have
distinguish he from Socrates himself.--every word would breathe the
sentiment of a soul disdain life, and careless about all its issue;
for which reason, though my mother be a woman of no deep reading, yet the
abstract of Socrates's oration, which my father be give my uncle Toby,
be not altogether new to her.--She listen to it with compose
intelligence, and would have do so to the end of the chapter, have not my
father plunge (which he have no occasion to have do) into that part of
the pleading where the great philosopher reckon up his connection, his
alliance, and child; but renounce a security to be so win by work
upon the passion of his judges.--'I have friend--I have relations,--i
have three desolate children,'--say Socrates.--
--Then, cry my mother, open the door,--you have one more, Mr. Shandy,
than I know of.
by heaven! I have one less,--said my father, get up and walk out of
the room.
Chapter 3.XIV.
--They be Socrates's child, say my uncle Toby. he have be dead a
hundred year ago, reply my mother.
my uncle Toby be no chronologer--so not care to advance one step but
upon safe ground, he lay down his pipe deliberately upon the table, and
rise up, and take my mother most kindly by the hand, without say
another word, either good or bad, to she, he lead she out after my father,
that he might finish the ecclaircissement himself.
chapter 3.XV.
have this volume be a farce, which, unless every one's life and opinion
be to be look upon as a farce as well as mine, I see no reason to
suppose--the last chapter, Sir, have finish the first act of it, and then
this chapter must have set off thus.
Ptr. . .r. . .r. . .e--twe--twang--prut--trut--'tis a curse bad
fiddle.--do you know whether my fiddle's in tune or no?--trut. . .prut. .
.--They should be fifths.--'Tis wickedly string--tr. . .a.e.i.o.u.-twang.--
the bridge be a mile too high, and the sound post absolutely down,--else--
trut. . .prut--hark! tis not so bad a tone.--diddle diddle, diddle diddle,
diddle diddle, dum. there be nothing in play before good judges,--but
therebe a man there--no--not he with the bundle under his arm--the grave
man in black.--'Sdeath! not the gentleman with the sword on.--Sir, I have
rather play a Caprichio to calliope herself, than draw my bow across my
fiddle before that very man; and yet Iwill stake my Cremona to a Jew's
trump, which be the great musical odd that ever be lay, that I will
this moment stop three hundred and fifty league out of tune upon my
fiddle, without punish one single nerve that belong to he--Twaddle
diddle, tweddle diddle,--twiddle diddle,--twoddle diddle,--twuddle diddle,-
-prut trut--krish--krash--krush.--I've undo you, sir,--but you see hebe
no worse,--and be Apollo to take his fiddle after I, he can make he no
well.
Diddle diddle, diddle diddle, diddle diddle--hum--dum--drum.
--Your worship and your reverence love music--and God have make you all
with good ear--and some of you play delightfully yourself--trut-prut,--
prut-trut.
o! there be--whom I could sit and hear whole days,--whose talent lie in
make what he fiddle to be felt,--who inspire I with his joy and
hope, and put the most hidden spring of my heart into motion.--if you
would borrow five guineas of I, sir,--which be generally ten guineas more
than I have to spare--or you Messrs. Apothecary and Taylor, want your bill
paying,--thatbe your time.
chapter 3.xvi.
the first thing which enter my father's head, after affair be a little
settle in the family, and Susanna have get possession of my mother's green
sattin night-gown,--wa to sit down coolly, after the example of Xenophon,
and write a Tristra-paedia, or system of education for I; collect first
for that purpose his own scatter thought, counsel, and notion; and
bind they together, so as to form an Institute for the government of my
childhood and adolescence. I be my father's last stake--he have lose my
brother Bobby entirely,--he have lose, by his own computation, full three-
fourth of I--that is, he have be unfortunate in his three first great
cast for I--my geniture, nose, and name,--there be but this one leave;
and accordingly my father give himself up to it with as much devotion as
ever my uncle Toby have do to his doctrine of projectils.--the difference
between they be, that my uncle Toby draw his whole knowledge of projectil
from Nicholas Tartaglia--my father spin his, every thread of it, out of his
own brain,--or reel and cross-twisted what all other spinner and
spinster have spin before he, that 'twa pretty near the same torture to
he.
in about three year, or something more, my father have get advance almost
into the middle of his work.--like all other writer, he meet with
disappointments.--He imagine he should be able to bring whatever he have to
say, into so small a compass, that when it be finish and bind, it might
be roll up in my mother's hussive.--matter grow under our hands.--let no
man say,--'come--i'll write a duodecimo.'
my father give himself up to it, however, with the most painful diligence,
proceed step by step in every line, with the same kind of caution and
circumspection (though I cannot say upon quite so religious a principle) as
be use by John de la Casse, the lord archbishop of Benevento, in
compass his galatea; in which his grace of Benevento spend near forty
year of his life; and when the thing come out, it be not of above half
the size or the thickness of a Rider's almanack.--how the holy man manage
the affair, unless he spend the great part of his time in comb his
whisker, or play at primero with his chaplain,--would pose any mortal
not let into the true secret;--and therefore 'tis worth explain to the
world, be it only for the encouragement of those few in it, who write not
so much to be feed--as to be famous.
I own have John de la Casse, the archbishop of Benevento, for whose memory
(notwithstanding his Galatea,) I retain the high veneration,--had he
be, Sir, a slender clerk--of dull wit--slow part--costive head, and so
forth,--he and his Galatea might have jog on together to the age of
Methuselah for me,--the phaenomenon have not be worth a parenthesis.--
but the reverse of this be the truth: John de la Casse be a genius of
fine part and fertile fancy; and yet with all these great advantage of
nature, which should have prick he forwards with his galatea, he lie
under an impuissance at the same time of advance above a line and a half
in the compass of a whole summer's day: this disability in his grace arise
from an opinion he be afflict with,--which opinion be this,--viz. that
whenever a Christian be write a book (not for his private amusement,
but) where his intent and purpose be, bona fide, to print and publish it
to the world, his first thought be always the temptation of the evil
one.--This be the state of ordinary writer: but when a personage of
venerable character and high station, either in church or state, once
turn author,--he maintain, that from the very moment he take pen in
hand--all the devil in hell break out of their hole to cajole him.--'twa
term-time with them,--every thought, first and last, be captious;--how
specious and good soever,--'twa all one;--in whatever form or colour it
present itself to the imagination,--'twas still a stroke of one or other
of them levell'd at he, and be to be fence off.--so that the life of a
writer, whatever he might fancy to the contrary, be not so much a state of
composition, as a state of warfare; and his probation in it, precisely that
of any other man militant upon earth,--both depend alike, not half so
much upon the degree of his wit--as his Resistance.
my father be hugely pleased with this theory of John de la Casse,
archbishop of Benevento; and (have it not cramp he a little in his creed)
I believe would have give ten of the good acre in the Shandy estate, to
have be the broacher of it.--how far my father actually believe in the
devil, will be see, when I come to speak of my father's religious notion,
in the progress of this work: 'tis enough to say here, as he could not
have the honour of it, in the literal sense of the doctrine--he take up
with the allegory of it; and would often say, especially when his pen be a
little retrograde, there be as much good meaning, truth, and knowledge,
couch under the veil of John de la Casse's parabolical representation,--
as be to be find in any one poetic fiction or mystic record of
antiquity.--prejudice of education, he would say, be the devil,--and the
multitude of they which we suck in with our mother's milk--be the devil
and all.--We be haunt with they, brother Toby, in all our lucubration
and research; and be a man fool enough to submit tamely to what they
obtrude upon him,--what would his book be? Nothing,--he would add,
throw his pen away with a vengeance,--nothing but a farrago of the clack
of nurse, and of the nonsense of the old woman (of both sex) throughout
the kingdom.
this be the good account I be determined to give of the slow progress my
father make in his Tristra-paedia; at which (as I say) he be three year,
and something more, indefatigably at work, and, at last, have scarce
complete, by this own reckoning, one half of his undertaking: the
misfortune be, that I be all that time totally neglect and abandon to
my mother; and what be almost as bad, by the very delay, the first part of
the work, upon which my father have spend the most of his pain, be
render entirely useless,--every day a page or two become of no
consequence.--
--certainly it be ordain as a scourge upon the pride of human wisdom,
that the wise of we all should thus outwit ourselves, and eternally
forego our purpose in the intemperate act of pursue they.
in short my father be so long in all his act of resistance,--or in other
words,--he advanced so very slow with his work, and I begin to live and get
forward at such a rate, that if an event have not happened,--which, when we
get to it, if it can be tell with decency, shall not be conceal a moment
from the reader--I verily believe, I have put by my father, and leave he
draw a sundial, for no well purpose than to be bury under ground.
chapter 3.XVII.
--'twa nothing,--I do not lose two drop of blood by it--'twa not worth
call in a surgeon, have he live next door to we--thousand suffer by
choice, what I do by accident.--doctor Slop make ten time more of it,
than there be occasion:--some man rise, by the art of hang great
weight upon small wires,--and I be this day (August the 10th, 1761) pay
part of the price of this man's reputation.--o 'twould provoke a stone, to
see how thing be carry on in this world!--the chamber-maid have leave no
....... ... under the bed:--cannot you contrive, master, quoth Susannah,
lift up the sash with one hand, as she speak, and help I up into the
window-seat with the other,--cannot you manage, my dear, for a single time,
to .... ... .. ... ......?
I be five year old.--Susannah do not consider that nothing be well hung
in our family,--so slap come the sash down like lightning upon us;--Nothing
be left,--crie susannah,--nothing be leave--for I, but to run my country.-
-
my uncle Toby's house be a much kind sanctuary; and so Susannah flee to
it.
chapter 3.xviii.
when Susannah tell the corporal the misadventure of the sash, with all the
circumstance which attend the murder of me,--(as she call it,)--the
blood forsook his cheeks,--all accessary in murder be principals,--
Trim's conscience tell he he be as much to blame as Susannah,--and if the
doctrine have be true, my uncle Toby have as much of the bloodshed to
answer for to heaven, as either of 'em;--so that neither reason or
instinct, separate or together, could possibly have guide Susannah's step
to so proper an asylum. it be in vain to leave this to the Reader's
imagination:--to form any kind of hypothesis that will render these
proposition feasible, he must cudgel his brain sore,--and to do it
without,--he must have such brain as no reader ever have before him.--Why
should I put they either to trial or to torture? 'tis my own affair: Iwill
explain it myself.
Chapter 3.xix.
'tis a pity, Trim, say my uncle Toby, rest with his hand upon the
corporal's shoulder, as they both stand survey their works,--that we
have not a couple of field-piece to mount in the gorge of that new
redoubt;--'twould secure the line all along there, and make the attack on
that side quite complete:--get I a couple cast, Trim.
your honour shall have they, reply Trim, before tomorrow morning.
it be the joy of Trim's heart, nor be his fertile head ever at a loss for
expedient in do it, to supply my uncle Toby in his campaign, with
whatever his fancy call for; have it be his last crown, he would have
sate down and hammer it into a paderero, to have prevent a single wish
in his master. the corporal have already,--what with cut off the end
of my uncle Toby's spout--hack and chisel up the side of his leaden
gutters,--melte down his pewter shaving-bason,--and go at last, like
Lewis the Fourteenth, on to the top of the church, for spare end, &c.--he
have that very campaign bring no less than eight new battering cannon,
besides three demi-culverin, into the field; my uncle Toby's demand for
two more piece for the redoubt, have set the corporal at work again; and no
well resource offering, he have take the two leaden weight from the
nursery window: and as the sash pullie, when the lead be go, be of
no kind of use, he have take they away also, to make a couple of wheel for
one of their carriage.
he have dismantle every sash-window in my uncle Toby's house long before,
in the very same way,--though not always in the same order; for sometimes
the pullie have be want, and not the lead,--so then he begin with the
pullies,--and the pullie be pick out, then the lead become useless,--
and so the lead go to pot too.
--a great Moral might be pick handsomely out of this, but I have not
time--'tis enough to say, wherever the demolition begin, 'twa equally
fatal to the sash window.
chapter 3.XX.
the corporal have not take his measure so badly in this stroke of
artilleryship, but that he might have keep the matter entirely to himself,
and leave Susannah to have sustain the whole weight of the attack, as she
could;--true courage be not content with come off so.--the corporal,
whether as general or comptroller of the train,--'twas no matter,--had do
that, without which, as he imagine, the misfortune could never have
happened,--at least in Susannah's hands;--how would your honour have
behaved?--he determine at once, not to take shelter behind Susannah,--but
to give it; and with this resolution upon his mind, he march upright into
the parlour, to lay the whole manoeuvre before my uncle Toby.
my uncle Toby have just then be give Yorick an account of the Battle of
Steenkirk, and of the strange conduct of count Solmes in order the foot
to halt, and the horse to march where it could not act; which be directly
contrary to the king's command, and prove the loss of the day.
there be incident in some family so pat to the purpose of what be go
to follow,--they be scarce exceed by the invention of a dramatic
writer;--i mean of ancient days.--
Trim, by the help of his fore-finger, lay flat upon the table, and the
edge of his hand strike across it at right angle, make a shift to tell
his story so, that priest and virgin might have listen to it;--and the
story be told,--the dialogue go on as follow.
Chapter 3.XXI.
--I would be picquette to death, cry the corporal, as he conclude
Susannah's story, before I would suffer the woman to come to any harm,--
'twa my fault, an' please your honour,--not she's.
Corporal Trim, reply my uncle Toby, put on his hat which lie upon the
table,--if any thing can be say to be a fault, when the service absolutely
require it should be done,--'tis I certainly who deserve the blame,--you
obey your order.
have count Solmes, Trim, do the same at the battle of Steenkirk, say
Yorick, drolle a little upon the corporal, who have be run over by a
dragoon in the retreat,--he have save thee;--save! cry Trim,
interrupt Yorick, and finish the sentence for he after his own
fashion,--he have save five battalion, an' please your reverence, every
soul of them:--there be cutt's,--continue the corporal, clap the
forefinger of his right hand upon the thumb of his left, and count round
his hand,--there be Cutt's,--Mackay's,--Angus's,--Graham's,--and Leven's,
all cut to pieces;--and so have the english life-guard too, have it not be
for some regiment upon the right, who march up boldly to their relief,
and receive the enemy's fire in their face, before any one of their own
platoon discharge a musket,--they'll go to heaven for it,--added Trim.--
Trim be right, say my uncle Toby, nod to Yorick,--he's perfectly
right. what signify his marching the horse, continue the corporal,
where the ground be so strait, that the French have such a nation of
hedge, and copse, and ditch, and fell'd tree lay this way and that to
cover they (as they always have).--Count Solmes should have send us,--we
would have fire muzzle to muzzle with they for their lives.--there be
nothing to be do for the horse:--he have his foot shoot off however for his
pain, continue the corporal, the very next campaign at Landen.--Poor Trim
get his wound there, quoth my uncle toby.--'twa owe, an' please your
honour, entirely to count Solmes,--had he drub they soundly at
Steenkirk, they would not have fight we at Landen.--Possibly not,--Trim,
say my uncle Toby;--though if they have the advantage of a wood, or you
give they a moment's time to intrench themselves, they be a nation which
will pop and pop for ever at you.--there be no way but to march coolly up
to them,--receive their fire, and fall in upon they, pell-mell--Ding dong,
add trim.--horse and foot, say my uncle Toby.--Helter Skelter, say
Trim.--Right and leave, cry my uncle Toby.--Blood an' ound, shout the
corporal;--the battle raged,--yorick draw his chair a little to one side
for safety, and after a moment's pause, my uncle Toby sink his voice a
note,--resume the discourse as follow.
chapter 3.xxii.
King William, say my uncle Toby, address himself to Yorick, be so
terribly provoke at count Solmes for disobey his order, that he would
not suffer he to come into his presence for many month after.--i fear,
answer Yorick, the squire will be as much provoke at the corporal, as
the King at the count.--but 'twould be singularly hard in this case,
continue be, if corporal Trim, who have behave so diametrically opposite
to count Solmes, should have the fate to be reward with the same
disgrace:--too oft in this world, do thing take that train.--I would
spring a mine, cry my uncle Toby, rise up,--and blow up my
fortification, and my house with they, and we would perish under their
ruin, ere I would stand by and see it.--trim direct a slight,--but a
grateful bow towards his master,--and so the chapter end.
chapter 3.xxiii.
--then, Yorick, reply my uncle Toby, you and I will lead the way
abreast,--and do you, corporal, follow a few pace behind us.--And
Susannah, an' please your honour, say Trim, shall be put in the rear.--
'twas an excellent disposition,--and in this order, without either drum
beating, or colour fly, they march slowly from my uncle Toby's house
to Shandy-hall.
--I wish, say Trim, as they enter the door,--instead of the sash
weight, I have cut off the church spout, as I once think to have done.--
you have cut off spout enow, reply Yorick.
Chapter 3.XXIV.
as many picture as have be give of my father, how like he soever in
different air and attitudes,--not one, or all of they, can ever help the
reader to any kind of preconception of how my father would think, speak, or
act, upon any untried occasion or occurrence of life.--there be that
infinitude of oddity in he, and of chance along with it, by which
handle he would take a thing,--it baffle, Sir, all calculations.--The
truth be, his road lie so very far on one side, from that wherein most man
travelled,--that every object before he present a face and section of
itself to his eye, altogether different from the plan and elevation of it
see by the rest of mankind.--In other word, 'twa a different object, and
in course be differently consider:
this be the true reason, that my dear Jenny and I, as well as all the world
besides we, have such eternal squabble about nothing.--She look at her
outside,--I, at she in. . .. how be it possible we should agree about her
value?
Chapter 3.xxv.
'tis a point settled,--and I mention it for the comfort of Confucius, (Mr
Shandy be suppose to mean. . ., Esq; member for. . .,--and not the Chinese
Legislator.) who be apt to get entangle in tell a plain story--that
provide he keep along the line of his story,--he may go backwards and
forwards as he will,--'tis still hold to be no digression.
this be premise, I take the benefit of the act of go backwards
myself.
chapter 3.xxvi.
fifty thousand panni load of devils--(not of the Archbishop of
Benevento's--I mean of Rabelais's devil), with their tail chop off by
their rump, could not have make so diabolical a scream of it, as I did--
when the accident befel I: it summon up my mother instantly into the
nursery,--so that Susannah have but just time to make her escape down the
back stair, as my mother come up the fore.
now, though I be old enough to have tell the story myself,--and young
enough, I hope, to have do it without malignity; yet Susannah, in pass
by the kitchen, for fear of accident, have leave it in short-hand with the
cook--the cook have tell it with a commentary to Jonathan, and Jonathan to
Obadiah; so that by the time my father have ring the bell half a dozen
time, to know what be the matter above,--was Obadiah enable to give he
a particular account of it, just as it have happened.--I think as much,
say my father, tuck up his night-gown;--and so walk up stair.
one would imagine from this--(though for my own part I somewhat question
it)--that my father, before that time, have actually write that remarkable
character in the Tristra-paedia, which to I be the most original and
entertain one in the whole book;--and that be the chapter upon sash-
window, with a bitter Philippick at the end of it, upon the forgetfulness
of chamber-maids.--I have but two reason for think otherwise.
first, have the matter be take into consideration, before the event
happen, my father certainly would have nail up the sash window for good
an' all;--which, consider with what difficulty he compose books,--he
might have do with ten time less trouble, than he could have write the
chapter: this argument I foresee hold good against his writing a chapter,
even after the event; but 'tis obviate under the second reason, which I
have the honour to offer to the world in support of my opinion, that my
father do not write the chapter upon sash-window and chamber-pot, at the
time supposed,--and it be this.
--That, in order to render the Tristra-paedia complete,--I write the
chapter myself.
Chapter 3.XXVII.
my father put on his spectacle--looked,--took they off,--put they into the
case--all in less than a statutable minute; and without open his lip,
turn about and walk precipitately down stair: my mother imagine he
have step down for lint and basilicon; but see he return with a
couple of folio under his arm, and Obadiah follow he with a large
reading-desk, she take it for grant 'twas an herbal, and so draw he a
chair to the bedside, that he might consult upon the case at his ease.
--if it be but right done,--said my father, turn to the section--de sede
vel subjecto circumcisionis,--for he have bring up Spenser de Legibus
Hebraeorum Ritualibus--and Maimonides, in order to confront and examine we
altogether.--
--if it be but right do, quoth he:--only tell we, cry my mother,
interrupt he, what herbs?--for that, reply my father, you must send
for Dr. Slop.
my mother go down, and my father go on, read the section as follow,
. . .--Very well,--said my father,. . .--nay, if it have that convenience--
and so without stop a moment to settle it first in his mind, whether
the Jews have it from the Egyptians, or the Egyptians from the Jews,--he
rise up, and rub his forehead two or three time across with the palm
of his hand, in the manner we rub out the footstep of care, when evil have
trod light upon we than we foreboded,--he shut the book, and walk down
stairs.--nay, say he, mention the name of a different great nation upon
every step as he set his foot upon it--if the Egyptians,--the Syrians,--the
Phoenicians,--the Arabians,--the cappadocians,--if the Colchi, and
Troglodytes do it--if Solon and Pythagoras submitted,--what be Tristram?--
who be I, that I should fret or fume one moment about the matter?
chapter 3.xxviii.
Dear Yorick, say my father smile (for Yorick have break his rank with my
uncle Toby in come through the narrow entry, and so have stept first into
the parlour)--this Tristram of our, I find, come very hardly by all his
religious rites.--never be the son of Jew, Christian, Turk, or Infidel
initiate into they in so oblique and slovenly a manner.--But he be no
bad, I trust, say Yorick.--There have be certainly, continue my
father, the deuce and all to do in some part or other of the ecliptic, when
this offspring of mine be formed.--that, you be a well judge of than I,
reply Yorick.--Astrologers, quoth my father, know well than we both:--
the trine and sextil aspect have jump awry,--or the opposite of their
ascendent have not hit it, as they should,--or the lord of the geniture
(as they call they) have be at bo-peep,--or something have be wrong
above, or below with we.
'tis possible, answer yorick.--but be the child, cry my uncle Toby, the
worse?--the troglodyte say not, reply my father. and your theologist,
Yorick, tell we--Theologically? say Yorick,--or speak after the manner
of apothecary? (footnote in Greek Philo.)--statesmen? (footnote in
Greek)--or washer-woman? (footnote in Greek Bochart.)
--I'm not sure, reply my father,--but they tell we, brother Toby, hebe
the well for it.--provide, say Yorick, you travel he into Egypt.--Of
that, answer my father, he will have the advantage, when he see the
pyramids.--
now every word of this, quoth my uncle Toby, be arabic to me.--I wish, say
Yorick, 'twa so, to half the world.
--ilus, (footnote in Greek Sanchuniatho.) continue my father, circumcise
his whole army one morning.--not without a court martial? cry my uncle
Toby.--Though the learn, continue he, take no notice of my uncle
Toby's remark, but turn to Yorick,--are greatly divide still who Ilus
was;--some say saturn;--some the Supreme Being;--others, no more than a
brigadier general under Pharaoh-neco.--Let he be who he will, say my

chapter 3.xxix.
--'which word be hear by all the soldier which be there, diver of

he forthwith launch himself aloft into the air, and place both his foot
together upon the saddle, stand upright, with his back turn towards
his horse's head,--now, (say he) my case go forward. then suddenly in
the same posture wherein he be, he fetch a gambol upon one foot, and
turn to the left-hand, fail not to carry his body perfectly round,
just into his former position, without miss one jot.--Ha! say Tripet, I
will not do that at this time,--and not without cause. well, say Gymnast,
I have failed,--i will undo this leap; then with a marvellous strength and
agility, turn towards the right-hand, he fetch another striking gambol
as before; which do, he set his right hand thumb upon the bow of the

and whirl himself about three time: at the fourth, reverse his body,
and overturn it upside down, and foreside back, without touch any

himself a jerk swing, he seat himself upon the crupper--'
(this cannot be fight, say my uncle Toby.--The corporal shake his head

'then (Tripet) pass'd his right leg over his saddle, and place himself en

Chapter 3.XXX.

chapter 3.xxxi.

my father give Trim a nod, to snuff the candle, and resume his discourse.
--I enter upon this speculation, say my father carelessly, and half

1st, by marriage.
2d, by adoption.
3d, by legitimation.

'the whole secret of health depend upon the due contention for mastery

in say this, my father shut the book,--not as if he resolve to read no

chapter 3.xxxv.
'the two great cause, which conspire with each other to shorten life, say
lord Verulam, be first--
'the internal spirit, which like a gentle flame waste the body down to
death:--And secondly, the external air, that parch the body up to ashes:-
-which two enemy attack we on both side of our body together, at
length destroy our organ, and render they unfit to carry on the function
of life.'
this be the state of the case, the road to longevity be plain; nothing
more be require, say his lordship, but to repair the waste commit
by the internal spirit, by make the substance of it more thick and dense,
by a regular course of opiate on one side, and by refrigerate the heat
of it on the other, by three grain and a half of salt-petre every morning
before you get up.--
still this frame of our be leave expose to the inimical assault of the
air without;--but this be fence off again by a course of greasy unction,
which so fully saturate the pore of the skin, that no spicula could
enter;--nor could any one get out.--thi put a stop to all perspiration,
sensible and insensible, which be the cause of so many scurvy
distemper--a course of clyster be requisite to carry off redundant
humours,--and render the system complete.
what my father have to say to my lord of Verulam's opiate, his salt-petre,

forwards--you shall read the chapter at your leisure (if you chuse it), as
soon as ever the Tristra-paedia be published.--
Sufficeth it, at present to say, my father level the hypothesis with the

Chapter 3.xxxvi.
the whole secret of health, say my father, begin the sentence again,
depend evidently upon the due contention betwixt the radical heat and
radical moisture within us;--the least imaginable skill have be sufficient
to have maintain it, have not the school-man confound the task, merely
(as Van Helmont, the famous chymist, have prove) by all along mistake the
radical moisture for the tallow and fat of animal body.
now the radical moisture be not the tallow or fat of animal, but an oily
and balsamous substance; for the fat and tallow, as also the phlegm or
watery part, be cold; whereas the oily and balsamous part be of a
lively heat and spirit, which account for the observation of Aristotle,
'Quod omne animal post coitum est triste.'
now it be certain, that the radical heat live in the radical moisture, but
whether vice versa, be a doubt: however, when the one decay, the other
decay also; and then be produce, either an unnatural heat, which cause
an unnatural dryness--or an unnatural moisture, which cause dropsies.--So
that if a child, as he grow up, can but be teach to avoid run into
fire or water, as either of them threaten his destruction,--'twill be all
that be needful to be do upon that head.--

the description of the siege of Jericho itself, could not have engage the

aside.--. . .. it be at the siege of Limerick, an' please your honour,

chapter 3.xxxviii.

well--say my father, with a full aspiration, and pause a while after the

chapter 3.XL.
the city of Limerick, the siege of which be begin under his majesty king

and what conclusion dost thou draw, corporal Trim, cry my father, from
all these premise?
I infer, an' please your worship, reply Trim, that the radical moisture
be nothing in the world but ditch-water--and that the radical heat, of

ditch-water--and a dram of geneva--and give we but enough of it, with a

chapter 3.xlii.
--Five year with a bib under his chin;
four year in travel from Christ-cross-row to Malachi;
a year and a half in learn to write his own name;
seven long year and more (greek)-e it, at Greek and Latin;
four year at his probation and his negation--the fine statue still lie

sharpen to hew it out!--'tis a piteous delay!--was not the great Julius
Scaliger within an ace of never get his tool sharpen at all?--Forty-

imagine he intend to be an advocate in the other world: no wonder, when
