dispute and enquire concern wisdom,--what time will he have to make
use of it?
Yorick listen to my father with great attention; there be a seasoning of
wisdom unaccountably mix up with his strange whim, and he have
sometimes such illumination in the darkest of his eclipse, as almost
atone for them:--be wary, Sir, when you imitate he.
I be convince, Yorick, continue my father, half read and half
discoursing, that there be a north-west passage to the intellectual world;
and that the soul of man have short way of go to work, in furnish
itself with knowledge and instruction, than we generally take with it.--
but, alack! all field have not a river or a spring run besides them;--
every child, Yorick, have not a parent to point it out.
--the whole entirely depend, add my father, in a low voice, upon the
auxiliary verbs, Mr. Yorick.
have Yorick trod upon Virgil's snake, he could not have look more

reckon it as one of the great calamity which ever befel the republic
of letter, that those who have be entrust with the education of our
child, and whose business it be to open their mind, and stock they
early with idea, in order to set the imagination loose upon they, have
make so little use of the auxiliary verb in do it, as they have done--
so that, except Raymond Lullius, and the eld Pelegrini, the last of which
arrive to such perfection in the use of them, with his topic, that, in a
few lesson, he could teach a young gentleman to discourse with
plausibility upon any subject, pro and con, and to say and write all that
could be speak or write concern it, without blot a word, to the
admiration of all who behold him.--I should be glad, say Yorick,
interrupt my father, to be make to comprehend this matter. you shall,
say my father.
the high stretch of improvement a single word be capable of, be a high
metaphor,--for which, in my opinion, the idea be generally the bad, and
not the better;--but be that as it may,--when the mind have do that with
it--there be an end,--the mind and the idea be at rest,--until a second
idea enters;--and so on.
now the use of the Auxiliaries be, at once to set the soul a-go by
herself upon the material as they be bring she; and by the versability
of this great engine, round which they be twisted, to open new tract of
enquiry, and make every idea engender million.
you excite my curiosity greatly, say Yorick.
for my own part, quoth my uncle Toby, I have give it up.--The Danes, an'
please your honour, quoth the corporal, who be on the left at the siege
of Limerick, be all auxiliaries.--and very good one, say my uncle
toby.--but the auxiliary, Trim, my brother be talk about,--I conceive
to be different things.--
--you do? say my father, rise up.
chapter 3.xliii.
my father take a single turn across the room, then sit down, and finish
the chapter.
the verb auxiliary we be concern in here, continue my father, be, be;
be; have; have; do; do; make; make; suffer; shall; should; will; would;
can; could; owe; ought; use; or be wont.--and these vary with tense,
present, past, future, and conjugate with the verb see,--or with these
question add to them;--is it? be it? will it be? would it be? may it
be? might it be? and these again put negatively, be it not? be it not?
ought it not?--Or affirmatively,--it be; it be; it ought to be. or
chronologically,--has it be always? lately? how long ago?--Or
hypothetically,--if it be? if it be not? what would follow?--if the
French should beat the English? if the Sun go out of the Zodiac?
now, by the right use and application of these, continue my father, in
which a child's memory should be exercise, there be no one idea can enter
his brain, how barren soever, but a magazine of conception and conclusion
may be draw forth from it.--didst thou ever see a white bear? cry my
father, turn his head round to Trim, who stand at the back of his
chair:--no, an' please your honour, reply the corporal.--but thou couldst
discourse about one, Trim, say my father, in case of need?--how be it
possible, brother, quoth my uncle Toby, if the corporal never see one?--
'tis the fact I want, reply my father,--and the possibility of it be as
follow.
a White Bear! very well. have I ever see one? might I ever have see
one? be I ever to see one? ought I ever to have see one? or can I ever
see one?
would I have see a white bear! (for how can I imagine it?)
if I should see a white bear, what should I say? if I should never see a
white bear, what then?
if I never have, can, must, or shall see a white bear alive; have I ever
see the skin of one? do I ever see one painted?--described? have I
never dream of one?
do my father, mother, uncle, aunt, brother or sister, ever see a white
bear? what would they give? how would they behave? how would the white
bear have behave? be he wild? Tame? terrible? rough? Smooth?
--is the white bear worth seeing?--
--is there no sin in it?--
be it well than a black one?
chapter 3.xliv.
--We'll not stop two moment, my dear sir,--only, as we have get through
these five volume (in the first edition, the sixth volume begin with this
chapter.), (do, Sir, sit down upon a set--they be well than nothing) let
we just look back upon the country we have pass'd through.--
--What a wilderness have it be! and what a mercy that we have not both of
we be lose, or devour by wild beast in it!
do you think the world itself, Sir, have contain such a number of Jack
Asses?--How they view'd and review'd we as we pass over the rivulet at
the bottom of that little valley!--and when we climb over that hill, and
be just get out of sight--good God! what a braying do they all set
up together!
--prithee, shepherd! who keep all those Jack Asses?. . ..
--heaven be their comforter--what! be they never curried?--are they never
take in in winter?--Bray bray--bray. bray on,--the world be deeply your
debtor;--louder still--thatbe nothing:--in good sooth, you be ill-used:--
be I a Jack Asse, I solemnly declare, I would bray in G-sol-re-ut from
morning, even unto night.
Chapter 3.XLV.
when my father have dance his white bear backwards and forwards through
half a dozen page, he close the book for good an' all,--and in a kind of
triumph redelivere it into Trim's hand, with a nod to lay it upon the
'scrutoire, where he find it.--tristram, say he, shall be make to
conjugate every word in the dictionary, backwards and forwards the same
way;--every word, Yorick, by this mean, you see, be convert into a
thesis or an hypothesis;--every thesis and hypothesis have an off-spring of
propositions;--and each proposition have its own consequence and
conclusion; every one of which lead the mind on again, into fresh track
of enquiry and doubtings.--the force of this engine, add my father, be
incredible in open a child's head.--'ti enough, brother Shandy, cry
my uncle Toby, to burst it into a thousand splinters.--
I presume, say Yorick, smiling,--it must be owe to this,--(for let
logician say what they will, it be not to be account for sufficiently
from the bare use of the ten predicaments)--that the famous Vincent
Quirino, amongst the many other astonishing feat of his childhood, of
which the Cardinal Bembo have give the world so exact a story,--should be
able to paste up in the public school at Rome, so early as in the eighth
year of his age, no less than four thousand five hundred and fifty
different thesis, upon the most abstruse point of the most abstruse
theology;--and to defend and maintain they in such sort, as to cramp and
dumbfound his opponents.--what be that, cry my father, to what be tell we
of Alphonsus Tostatus, who, almost in his nurse's arm, learn all the
science and liberal art without be teach any one of them?--what shall
we say of the great Piereskius?--Thatbe the very man, cry my uncle Toby,
I once tell you of, brother Shandy, who walk a matter of five hundred
mile, reckon from Paris to Shevling, and from shevle back again,
merely to see Stevinus's fly chariot.--he be a very great man! add my
uncle Toby (mean Stevinus)--He be so, brother Toby, say my father
(mean Piereskius)--and have multiply his idea so fast, and increase
his knowledge to such a prodigious stock, that, if we may give credit to an
anecdote concern he, which we cannot withhold here, without shake the
authority of all anecdote whatever--at seven year of age, his father
commit entirely to his care the education of his young brother, a boy
of five year old,--with the sole management of all his concerns.--was the
father as wise as the son? quoth my uncle Toby:--I should think not, say
yorick:--but what be these, continue my father--(breaking out in a kind
of enthusiasm)--what be these, to those prodigy of childhood in Grotius,
Scioppius, Heinsius, Politian, Pascal, Joseph Scaliger, Ferdinand de
Cordoue, and other--some of which leave off their substantial form at nine
year old, or soon, and go on reasoning without them;--other go
through their classic at seven;--wrote tragedy at eight;--Ferdinand de
Cordoue be so wise at nine,--'twas think the Devil be in him;--and at
Venice give such proof of his knowledge and goodness, that the monk
imagine he be Antichrist, or nothing.--other be master of fourteen
language at ten,--finished the course of their rhetoric, poetry, logic,
and ethic, at eleven,--put forth their commentary upon Servius and
Martianus Capella at twelve,--and at thirteen receive their degree in
philosophy, law, and divinity:--but you forget the great Lipsius, quoth
Yorick, who compose a work (nous aurion quelque interet, say Baillet, de
montr qu'il n'a rien de ridicule s'il etoit veritable, au moins dans le
sen enigmatique que Nicius Erythraeus a ta he de lui donner. Cet auteur
dit que pour comprendre comme Lipse, il a pu composer un ouvrage le premier
jour de sa vie, il faut s'imaginer, que ce premier jour n'est pas celui de
sa naissance charnelle, mais celui au quel il a commence d'user de la
raison; il veut que c'ait ete a l'age de neuf ans; et il nous veut
persuader que ce fut en cet age, que Lipse fit un poeme.--Le tour est
ingenieux, &c. &c.) the day he be born:--they should have wipe it up,
say my uncle Toby, and say no more about it.
chapter 3.xlvi.
when the cataplasm be ready, a scruple of decorum have unseasonably rise up
in Susannah's conscience, about hold the candle, whilst Slop tie it on;
Slop have not treat Susannah's distemper with anodynes,--and so a quarrel
have ensue betwixt they.
--oh! oh!--said Slop, cast a glance of undue freedom in Susannah's face,
as she decline the office;--then, I think I know you, madam--you know I,
Sir! cry Susannah fastidiously, and with a toss of her head, level
evidently, not at his profession, but at the doctor himself,--you know I!
cry Susannah again.--doctor Slop clap his finger and his thumb
instantly upon his nostrils;--Susannah's spleen be ready to burst at it;--
'tis false, say susannah.--come, come, Mrs. Modesty, say Slop, not a
little elated with the success of his last thrust,--if you willnot hold the
candle, and look--you may hold it and shut your eyes:--Thatbe one of your
popish shift, cry susannah:--'ti well, say Slop, with a nod, than no
shift at all, young woman;--i defy you, Sir, cry Susannah, pull she
shift sleeve below her elbow.
it be almost impossible for two person to assist each other in a surgical
case with a more splenetic cordiality.
Slop snatch up the cataplasm--Susannah snatch up the candle;--a little
this way, say Slop; Susannah look one way, and row another,
instantly set fire to Slop's wig, which be somewhat bushy and unctuous
withal, be burn out before it be well kindled.--you impudent whore!
cry slop,--(for what be passion, but a wild beast?)--you impudent whore,
cry Slop, get upright, with the cataplasm in his hand;--i never be
the destruction of any body's nose, say Susannah,--which be more than you
can say:--is it? cry Slop, throw the cataplasm in her face;--yes, it
be, cry Susannah, return the compliment with what be leave in the pan.
chapter 3.XLVII.
Doctor Slop and Susannah file cross-bill against each other in the
parlour; which do, as the cataplasm have fail, they retire into the
kitchen to prepare a fomentation for me;--and whilst that be do, my
father determine the point as you will read.
chapter 3.xlviii.
you see 'tis high time, say my father, address himself equally to my
uncle Toby and Yorick, to take this young creature out of these woman's
hand, and put he into those of a private governor. Marcus Antoninus
provide fourteen governor all at once to superintend his son Commodus's
education,--and in six week he cashier five of them;--i know very well,
continue my father, that Commodus's mother be in love with a gladiator at
the time of her conception, which account for a great many of Commodus's
cruelty when he become emperor;--but still I be of opinion, that those
five whom Antoninus dismiss, do Commodus's temper, in that short time,
more hurt than the other nine be able to rectify all their life long.
now as I consider the person who be to be about my son, as the mirror in
which he be to view himself from morning to night, by which he be to adjust
his look, his carriage, and perhaps the inmost sentiment of his heart;--i
would have one, Yorick, if possible, polish at all point, fit for my
child to look into.--this be very good sense, quoth my uncle Toby to
himself.
--there be, continue my father, a certain mien and motion of the body and
all its part, both in act and speaking, which argue a man well within;
and I be not at all surprised that Gregory of Nazianzum, upon observe the
hasty and untoward gesture of Julian, should foretel he would one day
become an apostate;--or that St. Ambrose should turn his Amanuensis out of
door, because of an indecent motion of his head, which go backwards and
forwards like a flail;--or that Democritus should conceive Protagoras to be
a scholar, from see he bind up a faggot, and thrusting, as he do it,
the small twig inwards.--there be a thousand unnoticed opening,
continue my father, which let a penetrate eye at once into a man's soul;
and I maintain it, add he, that a man of sense do not lay down his hat
in come into a room,--or take it up in go out of it, but something
escape, which discover he.
it be for these reason, continue my father, that the governor I make
choice of shall neither (Vid. Pellegrina.) lisp, or squint, or wink, or
talk loud, or look fierce, or foolish;--or bite his lip, or grind his
tooth, or speak through his nose, or pick it, or blow it with his fingers.-
-
he shall neither walk fast,--or slow, or fold his arms,--for that be
laziness;--or hang they down,--for that be folly; or hide they in his
pocket, for that be nonsense.--
he shall neither strike, or pinch, or tickle--or bite, or cut his nail, or
hawk, or spit, or snift, or drum with his foot or finger in company;--nor
(accord to Erasmus) shall he speak to any one in make water,--nor
shall he point to carrion or excrement.--now this be all nonsense again,
quoth my uncle Toby to himself.--
I will have he, continue my father, cheerful, facete, jovial; at the same
time, prudent, attentive to business, vigilant, acute, argute, inventive,
quick in resolve doubt and speculative questions;--he shall be wise, and
judicious, and learned:--and why not humble, and moderate, and gentle-
temper, and good? say yorick:--and why not, cry my uncle Toby, free,
and generous, and bountiful, and brave?--He shall, my dear Toby, reply my
father, get up and shake he by his hand.--Then, brother Shandy,
answer my uncle Toby, raise himself off the chair, and lay down his
pipe to take hold of my father's other hand,--I humbly beg I may recommend
poor Le Fever's son to you;--a tear of joy of the first water sparkle in
my uncle Toby's eye, and another, the fellow to it, in the corporal's, as
the proposition be made;--you will see why when you read Le Fever's
story:--fool that I be! nor can I recollect (nor perhaps you) without
turn back to the place, what it be that hinder I from let the
corporal tell it in his own words;--but the occasion be lost,--i must tell
it now in my own.
chapter 3.xlix.
the Story of Le Fever.
it be some time in the summer of that year in which Dendermond be take
by the allies,--which be about seven year before my father come into the
country,--and about as many, after the time, that my uncle Toby and Trim
have privately decamp from my father's house in town, in order to lay some
of the fine siege to some of the fine fortified city in Europe--when
my uncle Toby be one evening get his supper, with Trim sit behind
he at a small sideboard,--i say, sit--for in consideration of the
corporal's lame knee (which sometimes give he exquisite pain)--when my
uncle Toby din or sup alone, he would never suffer the corporal to
stand; and the poor fellow's veneration for his master be such, that, with
a proper artillery, my uncle Toby could have take Dendermond itself, with
less trouble than he be able to gain this point over he; for many a time
when my uncle Toby suppose the corporal's leg be at rest, he would look
back, and detect he stand behind he with the most dutiful respect: 
this breed more little squabble betwixt they, than all other cause for
five-and-twenty year together--but this be neither here nor there--why do
I mention it?--Ask my pen,--it govern me,--I govern not it.
he be one evening sit thus at his supper, when the landlord of a
little inn in the village come into the parlour, with an empty phial in his
hand, to beg a glass or two of sack; 'tis for a poor gentleman,--I think,
of the army, say the landlord, who have be take ill at my house four
day ago, and have never hold up his head since, or have a desire to taste
any thing, till just now, that he have a fancy for a glass of sack and a
thin toast,--i think, say he, take his hand from his forehead, it would
comfort me.--
--If I could neither beg, borrow, or buy such a thing--add the landlord,-
-I would almost steal it for the poor gentleman, he be so ill.--i hope in
God he will still mend, continue he,--we be all of we concern for he.
thou art a good-natured soul, I will answer for thee, cry my uncle Toby;
and thou shalt drink the poor gentleman's health in a glass of sack
thyself,--and take a couple of bottle with my service, and tell he he be
heartily welcome to they, and to a dozen more if they will do he good.
though I be persuade, say my uncle Toby, as the landlord shut the door,
he be a very compassionate fellow--Trim,--yet I cannot help entertain a
high opinion of his guest too; there must be something more than common in
he, that in so short a time should win so much upon the affection of his
host;--and of his whole family, add the corporal, for they be all
concerned for him,.--step after he, say my uncle Toby,--do Trim,--and ask
if he know his name.
--I have quite forget it truly, say the landlord, come back into the
parlour with the corporal,--but I can ask his son again:--Has he a son with
he then? say my uncle toby.--a boy, reply the landlord, of about eleven
or twelve year of age;--but the poor creature have taste almost as little
as his father; he do nothing but mourn and lament for he night and day:-
-He have not stir from the bed-side these two day.
my uncle Toby lay down his knife and fork, and thrust his plate from
before he, as the landlord give he the account; and Trim, without be
order, take away, without say one word, and in a few minute after
bring he his pipe and tobacco.
--Stay in the room a little, say my uncle Toby.
trim!--said my uncle Toby, after he light his pipe, and smoak'd about a
dozen whiffs.--trim come in front of his master, and make his bow;--my
uncle Toby smoak'd on, and say no more.--corporal! say my uncle Toby--the
corporal make his bow.--My uncle Toby proceed no far, but finish
his pipe.
Trim! say my uncle Toby, I have a project in my head, as it be a bad
night, of wrap myself up warm in my roquelaure, and pay a visit to
this poor gentleman.--your honour's roquelaure, reply the corporal, have
not once be have on, since the night before your honour receive your
wound, when we mount guard in the trench before the gate of St.
Nicholas;--and besides, it be so cold and rainy a night, that what with the
roquelaure, and what with the weather, 'twill be enough to give your honour
your death, and bring on your honour's torment in your groin. I fear so,
reply my uncle Toby; but I be not at rest in my mind, Trim, since the
account the landlord have give me.--i wish I have not know so much of this
affair,--adde my uncle Toby,--or that I have know more of it:--how shall
we manage it? leave it, an't please your honour, to I, quoth the
corporal;--i'll take my hat and stick and go to the house and reconnoitre,
and act accordingly; and I will bring your honour a full account in an
hour.--Thou shalt go, Trim, say my uncle Toby, and herebe a shilling for
thee to drink with his servant.--i shall get it all out of he, say the
corporal, shut the door.
my uncle Toby fill his second pipe; and have it not be, that he now and
then wander from the point, with consider whether it be not full as
well to have the curtain of the tennaile a straight line, as a crooked
one,--he might be say to have think of nothing else but poor Le Fever
and his boy the whole time he smoake it.
chapter 3.L.
the Story of Le Fever continue.
it be not till my uncle Toby have knock the ash out of his third pipe,
that corporal Trim return from the inn, and give he the follow
account.
I despair, at first, say the corporal, of be able to bring back your
honour any kind of intelligence concern the poor sick lieutenant--be he
in the army, then? say my uncle Toby--he be, say the corporal--and in
what regiment? say my uncle Toby--i'll tell your honour, reply the
corporal, every thing straight forwards, as I learn it.--Then, Trim, Iwill
fill another pipe, say my uncle Toby, and not interrupt thee till thou
hast do; so sit down at thy ease, Trim, in the window-seat, and begin thy
story again. the corporal make his old bow, which generally speak as plain
as a bow could speak it--your honour be good:--And have do that, he sit
down, as he be ordered,--and begin the story to my uncle Toby over again
in pretty near the same word.
I despair at first, say the corporal, of be able to bring back any
intelligence to your honour, about the lieutenant and his son; for when I
ask where his servant be, from whom I make myself sure of know every
thing which be proper to be asked,--Thatbe a right distinction, Trim, say
my uncle Toby--I be answer, an' please your honour, that he have no
servant with him;--that he have come to the inn with hire horse, which,
upon find himself unable to proceed (to join, I suppose, the regiment),
he have dismiss the morning after he came.--If I get well, my dear, say
he, as he give his purse to his son to pay the man,--we can hire horse
from hence.--but alas! the poor gentleman will never get from hence, say
the landlady to me,--for I hear the death-watch all night long;--and when
he die, the youth, his son, will certainly die with he; for he be broken-
hearte already.
I be hear this account, continue the corporal, when the youth come
into the kitchen, to order the thin toast the landlord speak of;--but I
will do it for my father myself, say the youth.--pray let my save you the
trouble, young gentleman, say I, take up a fork for the purpose, and
offer he my chair to sit down upon by the fire, whilst I do it.--i
believe, Sir, say he, very modestly, I can please he good myself.--i be
sure, say I, his honour will not like the toast the bad for be
toast by an old soldier.--the youth take hold of my hand, and instantly
burst into tears.--poor youth! say my uncle Toby,--he have be breed up
from an infant in the army, and the name of a soldier, Trim, sound in his
ear like the name of a friend;--i wish I have he here.
--I never, in the long march, say the corporal, have so great a mind to
my dinner, as I have to cry with he for company:--What could be the matter
with I, an' please your honour? nothing in the world, Trim, say my uncle
Toby, blow his nose,--but that thou art a good-natured fellow.
when I give he the toast, continue the corporal, I think it be proper
to tell he I be captain Shandy's servant, and that your honour (though a
stranger) be extremely concerned for his father;--and that if there be
any thing in your house or cellar--(And thou might'st have add my purse
too, say my uncle Toby),--he be heartily welcome to it:--He make a very
low bow (which be mean to your honour), but no answer--for his heart be
full--so he go up stair with the toast;--I warrant you, my dear, say I,
as I open the kitchen-door, your father will be well again.--mr. Yorick's
curate be smoke a pipe by the kitchen fire,--but say not a word good or
bad to comfort the youth.--I think it wrong; add the corporal--I think
so too, say my uncle Toby.
when the lieutenant have take his glass of sack and toast, he feel himself
a little revive, and send down into the kitchen, to let I know, that in
about ten minute he should be glad if I would step up stairs.--i believe,
say the landlord, he be go to say his prayers,--for there be a book
lay upon the chair by his bed-side, and as I shut the door, I see his son
take up a cushion.--
I think, say the curate, that you gentleman of the army, Mr. Trim, never
say your prayer at all.--I hear the poor gentleman say his prayer last
night, say the landlady, very devoutly, and with my own ear, or I could
not have believe it.--are you sure of it? reply the curate.--a soldier,
an' please your reverence, say I, pray as often (of his own accord) as a
parson;--and when he be fight for his king, and for his own life, and
for his honour too, he have the most reason to pray to God of any one in the
whole world--'twa well say of thee, Trim, say my uncle Toby.--But when a
soldier, say I, an' please your reverence, have be stand for twelve
hour together in the trench, up to his knee in cold water,--or engage,
say I, for month together in long and dangerous marches;--harasse,
perhaps, in his rear to-day;--harasse other to-morrow;--detache here;--
countermand there;--reste this night out upon his arms;--beat up in his
shirt the next;--benumbed in his joints;--perhap without straw in his tent
to kneel on;--must say his prayer how and when he can.--i believe, say
I,--for I be pique, quoth the corporal, for the reputation of the army,--
I believe, an' please your reverence, say I, that when a soldier get time
to pray,--he pray as heartily as a parson,--though not with all his fuss
and hypocrisy.--Thou shouldst not have say that, Trim, say my uncle
toby,--for God only know who be a hypocrite, and who be not:--At the great
and general review of we all, corporal, at the day of judgment (and not
till then)--it will be see who have do their duty in this world,--and
who have not; and we shall be advance, Trim, accordingly.--I hope we shall,
say trim.--it be in the scripture, say my uncle Toby; and I will shew it
thee to-morrow:--in the mean time we may depend upon it, Trim, for our
comfort, say my uncle Toby, that God Almighty be so good and just a
governor of the world, that if we have but do our duty in it,--it will
never be enquire into, whether we have do they in a red coat or a black
one:--I hope not, say the corporal--but go on, Trim, say my uncle Toby,
with thy story.
when I go up, continue the corporal, into the lieutenant's room, which I
do not do till the expiration of the ten minutes,--he be lie in his bed
with his head raise upon his hand, with his elbow upon the pillow, and a
clean white cambrick handkerchief beside it:--the youth be just stoop
down to take up the cushion, upon which I suppose he have be kneeling,--
the book be lay upon the bed,--and, as he rise, in take up the cushion
with one hand, he reach out his other to take it away at the same time.--
let it remain there, my dear, say the lieutenant.
he do not offer to speak to I, till I have walk up close to his bed-
side:--if you be captain Shandy's servant, say he, you must present my
thank to your master, with my little boy's thank along with they, for his
courtesy to me;--if he be of Levens's--say the lieutenant.--I tell he
your honour be--then, say he, I serve three campaign with he in
Flanders, and remember him,--but 'tis most likely, as I have not the honour
of any acquaintance with he, that he know nothing of me.--you will tell
he, however, that the person his good-nature have lay under obligation to
he, be one Le Fever, a lieutenant in Angus's--but he know I not,--said
he, a second time, musing;--possibly he may my story--add he--pray tell
the captain, I be the ensign at Breda, whose wife be most unfortunately
kill with a musket-shot, as she lie in my arm in my tent.--i remember
the story, an't please your honour, say I, very well.--do you so? say he,
wipe his eye with his handkerchief--then well may i.--in say this, he
draw a little ring out of his bosom, which seem tie with a black ribband
about his neck, and kiss'd it twice--here, Billy, say he,--the boy fly
across the room to the bed-side,--and fall down upon his knee, take the
ring in his hand, and kiss it too,--then kiss his father, and sit down
upon the bed and wept.
I wish, say my uncle Toby, with a deep sigh,--i wish, Trim, I be asleep.
your honour, reply the corporal, be too much concerned;--shall I pour
your honour out a glass of sack to your pipe?--do, Trim, say my uncle
Toby.
I remember, say my uncle Toby, sigh again, the story of the ensign and
his wife, with a circumstance his modesty omitted;--and particularly well
that he, as well as she, upon some account or other (I forget what) be
universally pity by the whole regiment;--but finish the story thou art
upon:--'Tis finish already, say the corporal,--for I could stay no
longer,--so wish his honour a good night; young Le Fever rise from off
the bed, and see I to the bottom of the stair; and as we go down
together, tell I, they have come from Ireland, and be on their route to
join the regiment in flanders.--but alas! say the corporal,--the
lieutenant's last day's march be over.--Then what be to become of his poor
boy? cry my uncle Toby.
Chapter 3.LI.
the Story of Le Fever continue.
it be to my uncle Toby's eternal honour,--though I tell it only for the
sake of those, who, when coop'd in betwixt a natural and a positive law,
know not, for their soul, which way in the world to turn themselves--that
notwithstanding my uncle Toby be warmly engage at that time in carry
on the siege of Dendermond, parallel with the ally, who press theirs on
so vigorously, that they scarce allow he time to get his dinner--that
nevertheless he give up Dendermond, though he have already make a lodgment
upon the counterscarp;--and bent his whole thought towards the private
distress at the inn; and except that he order the garden gate to be
bolt up, by which he might be say to have turn the siege of Dendermond
into a blockade,--he leave Dendermond to itself--to be relieve or not by
the french king, as the french king think good; and only consider how
he himself should relieve the poor lieutenant and his son.
--That kind being, who be a friend to the friendless, shall recompence thee
for this.
Thou hast leave this matter short, say my uncle Toby to the corporal, as he
be put he to bed,--and I will tell thee in what, Trim.--In the first
place, when thou made an offer of my service to Le Fever,--as sickness
and travel be both expensive, and thou know he be but a poor
lieutenant, with a son to subsist as well as himself out of his pay,--that
thou didst not make an offer to he of my purse; because, have he stand in
need, thou know, Trim, he have be as welcome to it as myself.--Your
honour know, say the corporal, I have no orders;--true, quoth my uncle
toby,--thou didst very right, Trim, as a soldier,--but certainly very wrong
as a man.
in the second place, for which, indeed, thou hast the same excuse,
continue my uncle Toby,--when thou offeredst he whatever be in my
house,--thou shouldst have offer he my house too:--A sick brother
officer should have the good quarter, Trim, and if we have he with us,--we
could tend and look to him:--Thou art an excellent nurse thyself, Trim,--
and what with thy care of he, and the old woman's and his boy's, and mine
together, we might recruit he again at once, and set he upon his legs.--
--In a fortnight or three week, add my uncle Toby, smiling,--he might
march.--He will never march; an' please your honour, in this world, say
the corporal:--he will march; say my uncle Toby, rise up from the side
of the bed, with one shoe off:--an' please your honour, say the corporal,
he will never march but to his grave:--he shall march, cry my uncle Toby,
march the foot which have a shoe on, though without advance an inch,--
he shall march to his regiment.--he cannot stand it, say the corporal;--he
shall be support, say my uncle toby;--he'll drop at last, say the
corporal, and what will become of his boy?--He shall not drop, say my
uncle Toby, firmly.--A-well-o'day,--do what we can for he, say Trim,
maintain his point,--the poor soul will die:--He shall not die, by G..,
cry my uncle Toby.
--The Accusing Spirit, which fly up to heaven's chancery with the oath,
blush'd as he give it in;--and the Recording Angel, as he write it down,
dropp'd a tear upon the word, and blot it out for ever.
Chapter 3.lii.
--my uncle Toby go to his bureau,--put his purse into his breech pocket,
and have order the corporal to go early in the morning for a
physician,--he go to bed, and fall asleep.
chapter 3.liii.
the Story of Le Fever continue.
the sun look bright the morning after, to every eye in the village but Le
Fever's and his afflicted son's; the hand of death press heavy upon his
eye-lids,--and hardly could the wheel at the cistern turn round its
circle,--when my uncle Toby, who have rise up an hour before his wonte
time, enter the lieutenant's room, and without preface or apology, sit
himself down upon the chair by the bed-side, and, independently of all
mode and custom, open the curtain in the manner an old friend and
brother officer would have do it, and ask he how he did,--how he have
rest in the night,--what be his complaint,--where be his pain,--and
what he could do to help him:--and without give he time to answer any
one of the enquiry, go on, and tell he of the little plan which he have
be concert with the corporal the night before for him.--
--you shall go home directly, Le Fever, say my uncle Toby, to my house,--
and wewill send for a doctor to see whatbe the matter,--and wewill have an
apothecary,--and the corporal shall be your nurse;--and Iwill be your
servant, Le Fever.
there be a frankness in my uncle toby,--not the effect of familiarity,--
but the cause of it,--which let you at once into his soul, and shew you
the goodness of his nature; to this there be something in his look, and
voice, and manner, superadded, which eternally beckon to the unfortunate
to come and take shelter under he, so that before my uncle Toby have half
finish the kind offer he be make to the father, have the son
insensibly press up close to his knee, and have take hold of the breast
of his coat, and be pull it towards him.--the blood and spirit of Le
Fever, which be wax cold and slow within he, and be retreat to
their last citadel, the heart--rally back,--the film forsook his eye for
a moment,--he look up wishfully in my uncle Toby's face,--then cast a
look upon his boy,--and that ligament, fine as it was,--wa never broken.--
nature instantly ebb'd again,--the film return to its place,--the pulse
flutter--stopp'd--go on--throbb'd--stopp'd again--move--stopp'd--shall
I go on?--no.
Chapter 3.LIV.
I be so impatient to return to my own story, that what remain of young Le
Fever's, that is, from this turn of his fortune, to the time my uncle Toby
recommend he for my preceptor, shall be tell in a very few word in the
next chapter.--all that be necessary to be add to this chapter be as
follows.--
that my uncle Toby, with young Le Fever in his hand, attend the poor
lieutenant, as chief mourner, to his grave.
that the governor of Dendermond pay his obsequie all military honours,--
and that Yorick, not to be behind-hand--pay he all ecclesiastic--for he
bury he in his chancel:--and it appear likewise, he preach a funeral
sermon over he--I say it appears,--for it be Yorick's custom, which I
suppose a general one with those of his profession, on the first leaf of
every sermon which he compose, to chronicle down the time, the place, and
the occasion of its being preach: to this, he be ever willnot to add some
short comment or stricture upon the sermon itself, seldom, indeed, much to
its credit:--for instance, this sermon upon the jewish dispensation--I
donot like it at all;--though I own there be a world of Water-Landish
knowledge in it;--but 'tis all tritical, and most tritically put together.-
-this be but a flimsy kind of a composition; what be in my head when I
make it?
--N.B. the excellency of this text be, that it will suit any sermon,--and
of this sermon,--that it will suit any text.--
--for this sermon I shall be hanged,--for I have steal the great part
of it. Doctor Paidagunes find I out. > Set a thief to catch a thief.--
on the back of half a dozen I find write, so, so, and no more--and upon a
couple Moderato; by which, as far as one may gather from Altieri's italian
dictionary,--but mostly from the authority of a piece of green whipcord,
which seem to have be the unravelling of Yorick's whip-lash, with which
he have leave we the two sermon mark Moderato, and the half dozen of so,
so, tie fast together in one bundle by themselves,--one may safely suppose
he mean pretty near the same thing.
there be but one difficulty in the way of this conjecture, which be this,
that the moderato's be five time well than the so, so's;--show ten
time more knowledge of the human heart;--have seventy time more wit and
spirit in them;--(and, to rise properly in my climax)--discovere a
thousand time more genius;--and to crown all, be infinitely more
entertaining than those tie up with them:--for which reason, whene'er
Yorick's dramatic sermon be offer to the world, though I shall admit
but one out of the whole number of the so, sobe, I shall, nevertheless,
adventure to print the two moderato's without any sort of scruple.
what Yorick could mean by the word lentamente,--tenute,--grave,--and
sometimes adagio,--as apply to theological composition, and with which
he have characterise some of these sermon, I dare not venture to guess.--I
be more puzzled still upon find a l'octava alta! upon one;--Con strepito
upon the back of another;--Scicilliana upon a third;--alla capella upon a
fourth;--Con l'arco upon this;--Senza l'arco upon that.--all I know be,
that they be musical term, and have a meaning;--and as he be a musical
man, I will make no doubt, but that by some quaint application of such
metaphor to the composition in hand, they impress very distinct idea
of their several character upon his fancy,--whatever they may do upon that
of other.
amongst these, there be that particular sermon which have unaccountably lead
I into this digression--the funeral sermon upon poor Le Fever, write out
very fairly, as if from a hasty copy.--I take notice of it the more,
because it seem to have be his favourite composition--it be upon
mortality; and be tie length-way and cros-way with a yarn thrum, and
then roll up and twist round with a half-sheet of dirty blue paper,
which seem to have be once the cast cover of a general review, which to
this day smell horribly of horse drugs.--whether these mark of
humiliation be designed,--i something doubt;--because at the end of the
sermon (and not at the beginning of it)--very different from his way of
treat the rest, he have write--Bravo!
--though not very offensively,--for it be at two inch, at least, and a
half's distance from, and below the conclude line of the sermon, at the
very extremity of the page, and in that right hand corner of it, which, you
know, be generally cover with your thumb; and, to do it justice, it be
write besides with a crow's quill so faintly in a small italian hand, as
scarce to solicit the eye towards the place, whether your thumb be there or
not,--so that from the manner of it, it stand half excuse; and be
write moreover with very pale ink, dilute almost to nothing,--'tis more
like a ritratto of the shadow of vanity, than of Vanity herself--of the
two; resemble rather a faint thought of transient applause, secretly
stir up in the heart of the composer; than a gross mark of it, coarsely
obtrude upon the world.
with all these extenuation, I be aware, that in publish this, I do no
service to Yorick's character as a modest man;--but all man have their
failing! and what lessen this still far, and almost wipe it away, be
this; that the word be strike through sometime afterwards (as appear from
a different tint of the ink) with a line quite across it in this manner,
BRAVO (cross out)--as if he have retract, or be ashamed of the opinion
he have once entertain of it.
these short character of his sermon be always write, except in
this one instance, upon the first leaf of his sermon, which serve as a
cover to it; and usually upon the inside of it, which be turn towards
the text;--but at the end of his discourse, where, perhaps, he have five or
six page, and sometimes, perhaps, a whole score to turn himself in,--he
take a large circuit, and, indeed, a much more mettlesome one;--as if he
have snatch the occasion of unlace himself with a few more frolicksome
stroke at vice, than the straitness of the pulpit allowed.--These, though
hussar-like, they skirmish lightly and out of all order, be still
auxiliary on the side of virtue;--tell I then, Mynheer Vander
Blonederdondergewdenstronke, why they should not be print together?
chapter 3.LV.
when my uncle Toby have turn every thing into money, and settle all
account betwixt the agent of the regiment and Le Fever, and betwixt Le
Fever and all mankind,--there remain nothing more in my uncle Toby's
hand, than an old regimental coat and a sword; so that my uncle Toby find
little or no opposition from the world in take administration. the coat
my uncle Toby give the corporal;--wear it, Trim, say my uncle Toby, as
long as it will hold together, for the sake of the poor lieutenant--and
this,--said my uncle Toby, take up the sword in his hand, and draw it
out of the scabbard as he speak--and this, Le Fever, Iwill save for thee,--
'tis all the fortune, continue my uncle Toby, hang it up upon a crook,
and point to it,--'tis all the fortune, my dear Le Fever, which God have
leave thee; but if he have give thee a heart to fight thy way with it in the
world,--and thou doest it like a man of honour,--'tis enough for we.
as soon as my uncle Toby have lay a foundation, and teach he to inscribe
a regular polygon in a circle, he send he to a public school, where,
except Whitsontide and Christmas, at which time the corporal be
punctually dispatch for him,--he remain to the spring of the year,
seventeen; when the story of the emperor's send his army into Hungary
against the Turks, kindle a spark of fire in his bosom, he leave his greek
and Latin without leave, and throw himself upon his knee before my
uncle Toby, beg his father's sword, and my uncle Toby's leave along with
it, to go and try his fortune under Eugene.--Twice do my uncle Toby forget
his wound and cry out, Le Fever! I will go with thee, and thou shalt fight
beside I--and twice he lay his hand upon his groin, and hang down his
head in sorrow and disconsolation.--
my uncle Toby take down the sword from the crook, where it have hung
untouched ever since the lieutenant's death, and deliver it to the
corporal to brighten up;--and have detain Le Fever a single fortnight
to equip he, and contract for his passage to Leghorn,--he put the sword
into his hand.--if thou art brave, Le Fever, say my uncle Toby, this will
not fail thee,--but Fortune, say he (muse a little),--fortune may--and
if she does,--adde my uncle Toby, embrace he, come back again to I, Le
Fever, and we will shape thee another course.
the great injury could not have oppress the heart of Le Fever more
than my uncle Toby's paternal kindness;--he part from my uncle Toby, as
the good of son from the good of father--both drop tear--and as my
uncle Toby give he his last kiss, he slip sixty guineas, tie up in an
old purse of his father's, in which be his mother's ring, into his hand,--
and bid God bless he.
chapter 3.lvi.
Le Fever get up to the imperial army just time enough to try what metal his
sword be make of, at the defeat of the Turks before Belgrade; but a series
of unmerited mischance have pursue he from that moment, and trod close
upon his heel for four year together after; he have withstand these
buffeting to the last, till sickness overtake he at Marseilles, from
whence he write my uncle Toby word, he have lose his time, his service, his
health, and, in short, every thing but his sword;--and be wait for the
first ship to return back to he.
as this letter come to hand about six week before Susannah's accident, Le
Fever be hourly expect; and be uppermost in my uncle Toby's mind all
the time my father be give he and yorick a description of what kind of
a person he would chuse for a preceptor to I: but as my uncle Toby
think my father at first somewhat fanciful in the accomplishment he
require, he forbore mention Le Fever's name,--till the character, by
Yorick's inter-position, end unexpectedly, in one, who should be gentle-
temper, and generous, and good, it impress the image of Le Fever, and
his interest, upon my uncle Toby so forcibly, he rise instantly off his
chair; and lay down his pipe, in order to take hold of both my father's
hand--I beg, brother Shandy, say my uncle Toby, I may recommend poor Le
Fever's son to you--I beseech you do, add Yorick--he have a good heart,
say my uncle Toby--and a brave one too, an' please your honour, say the
corporal.
--the good heart, Trim, be ever the brave, reply my uncle Toby.--And
the great coward, an' please your honour, in our regiment, be the
great rascal in it.--there be serjeant Kumber, and ensign--
--we'll talk of they, say my father, another time.
chapter 3.lvii.
what a jovial and a merry world would this be, may it please your worship,
but for that inextricable labyrinth of debt, care, woe, want, grief,
discontent, melancholy, large jointure, imposition, and lie!
Doctor Slop, like a son of a w. . ., as my father call he for it,--to
exalt himself,--debase I to death,--and make ten thousand time more of
Susannah's accident, than there be any ground for; so that in a week's
time, or less, it be in every body's mouth, that poor Master Shandy. .
.entirely.--And Fame, who love to double every thing,--in three day more,
have swear, positively she see it,--and all the world, as usual, give credit
to she evidence--'that the nursery window have not only. . .;--but that. .
.'s also.'
could the world have be sue like a Body-Corporate,--my father have
bring an action upon the case, and trounce it sufficiently; but to fall
foul of individual about it--as every soul who have mention the affair,
do it with the great pity imaginable;--'twa like fly in the very
face of his good friends:--and yet to acquiesce under the report, in
silence--be to acknowledge it openly,--at least in the opinion of one half
of the world; and to make a bustle again, in contradict it,--wa to
confirm it as strongly in the opinion of the other half.--
--was ever poor devil of a country gentleman so hamper? say my father.
I would shew he publickly, say my uncle Toby, at the market cross.
--'twill have no effect, say my father.
chapter 3.lviii.
--i'll put he, however, into breech, say my father,--let the world say

Chapter 3.lix.
there be a thousand resolution, Sir, both in church and state, as well as
in matter, Madam, of a more private concern;--which, though they have
carry all the appearance in the world of be take, and enter upon in
a hasty, hare-brain, and unadvised manner, be, notwithstanding this,
(and could you or I have get into the cabinet, or stand behind the curtain,
we should have find it be so) weigh, poize, and perpende--argue
upon--canvass through--enter into, and examine on all side with so
much coolness, that the Goddess of Coolness herself (I do not take upon I
to prove her existence) could neither have wish it, or do it well.
of the number of these be my father's resolution of put I into
breech; which, though determine at once,--in a kind of huff, and a
defiance of all mankind, have, nevertheless, be pro'd and conn'd, and
judicially talk over betwixt he and my mother about a month before, in
two several bed of justice, which my father have hold for that purpose. I
shall explain the nature of these bed of justice in my next chapter; and
in the chapter follow that, you shall step with I, Madam, behind the
curtain, only to hear in what kind of manner my father and my mother
debate between themselves, this affair of the breeches,--from which you
may form an idea, how they debate all less matter.
Chapter 3.LX.
the ancient goth of Germany, who (the learn Cluverius be positive) be
first seat in the country between the Vistula and the Oder, and who
afterwards incorporate the Herculi, the Bugians, and some other Vandallick
clan to them--have all of they a wise custom of debate every thing of
importance to their state, twice, that is,--once drunk, and once sober:--
Drunk--that their council might not want vigour;--and sober--that they
might not want discretion.
now my father be entirely a water-drinker,--wa a long time gravel
almost to death, in turn this as much to his advantage, as he do every
other thing which the ancient do or say; and it be not till the seventh
year of his marriage, after a thousand fruitless experiment and device,
that he hit upon an expedient which answer the purpose;--and that be,
when any difficult and momentous point be to be settle in the family,
which require great sobriety, and great spirit too, in its determination,-
-he fix and set apart the first Sunday night in the month, and the
Saturday night which immediately precede it, to argue it over, in bed with
my mother: by which contrivance, if you consider, Sir, with yourself,. .
..
these my father, humorously enough, call his bed of justice;--for from
the two different counsel take in these two different humour, a middle
one be generally find out which touch the point of wisdom as well, as
if he have get drunk and sober a hundred time.
I must not be make a secret of to the world, that this answer full as well
in literary discussion, as either in military or conjugal; but it be not
every author that can try the experiment as the Goths and Vandals do it--
or, if he can, may it be always for his body's health; and to do it, as my
father do it,--am I sure it would be always for his soul's.
my way be this:--
in all nice and ticklish discussions,--(of which, heaven know, there be
but too many in my book)--where I find I cannot take a step without the
danger of have either their worship or their reverence upon my back--I
write one-half full,--and t'other fasting;--or write it all full,--and
correct it fasting;--or write it fasting,--and correct it full, for they
all come to the same thing:--so that with a less variation from my father's
plan, than my father's from the Gothick--I feel myself upon a par with he
in his first bed of justice,--and no way inferior to he in his second.--
these different and almost irreconcileable effect, flow uniformly from the
wise and wonderful mechanism of nature,--of which,--be shebe the honour.--
all that we can do, be to turn and work the machine to the improvement and
well manufactory of the art and sciences.--
now, when I write full,--i write as if I be never to write fast again
as long as I live;--that be, I write free from the care as well as the
terror of the world.--I count not the number of my scars,--nor do my
fancy go forth into dark entry and bye-corner to ante-date my stabs.--In
a word, my pen take its course; and I write on as much from the fulness of
my heart, as my stomach.--
but when, an' please your honour, I indite fast, 'tis a different
history.--I pay the world all possible attention and respect,--and have as
great a share (whilst it last) of that under strap virtue of
discretion as the good of you.--So that betwixt both, I write a careless
kind of a civil, nonsensical, good-humour shandean book, which will do
all your heart good--
--And all your head too,--provide you understand it.
Chapter 3.lxi.
we should begin, say my father, turn himself half round in bed, and

we should begin to think, Mrs. Shandy, of put this boy into breeches.--
we should so,--said my mother.--we defer it, my dear, quoth my father,

I think we do, Mr. Shandy,--said my mother.
--not but the child look extremely well, say my father, in his vest and

--He do look very well in them,--replie my mother.--
--And for that reason it would be almost a sin, add my father, to take

--it would so,--said my mother:--But indeed he be grow a very tall lad,-
-rejoine my father.
--He be very tall for his age, indeed,--said my mother.--
--I can not (make two syllable of it) imagine, quoth my father, who the
deuce he take after.--
I cannot conceive, for my life, say my mother.--
Humph!--said my father.
(the dialogue cease for a moment.)
--(I be very short myself,--continue my father gravely.
you be very short, Mr. Shandy,--said my mother.
Humph! quoth my father to himself, a second time: in mutter which, he
pluck his pillow a little far from my mother's,--and turn about
again, there be an end of the debate for three minute and a half.
--When he get these breech make, cry my father in a high tone, hewill
look like a beast in them.
he will be very awkward in they at first, reply my mother.
--And 'twill be lucky, if thatbe the bad on't, add my father.
it will be very lucky, answer my mother.
I suppose, reply my father,--make some pause first,--he'll be exactly
like other people's children.--
exactly, say my mother.--
--Though I shall be sorry for that, add my father: and so the debate
stopp'd again.--

they will last he, say my mother, the long.
but he can have no lining to them, reply my father.--
he cannot, say my mother.
'twere well to have they of fustian, quoth my father.
nothing can be well, quoth my mother.--

to them,--they should have where to secure it.--
order it as you please, Mr. Shandy, reply my mother.--
--But donot you think it right? add my father, press the point home to
she.
perfectly, say my mother, if it please you, Mr. Shandy.--
--therebe for you! cry my father, lose his temper--please me!--you

betwixt a point of pleasure and a point of convenience.--this be on the

Chapter 3.lxii.
after my father have debate the affair of the breech with my mother,--he
consult Albertus Rubenius upon it; and Albertus Rubenius use my father
ten time bad in the consultation (if possible) than even my father have
use my mother: for as Rubenius have write a quarto express, De re
Vestiaria Veterum,--it be Rubenius's business to have give my father some
lights.--on the contrary, my father might as well have think of
extract the seven cardinal virtue out of a long beard,--as of
extract a single word out of Rubenius upon the subject.
upon every other article of ancient dress, Rubenius be very communicative
to my father;--gave he a full satisfactory account of
the Toga, or loose gown.
the Chlamys.
the Ephod.
the Tunica, or Jacket.
the Synthesis.
the Paenula.
the Lacema, with its Cucullus.
the Paludamentum.
the Praetexta.
the Sagum, or soldier's jerkin.
the Trabea: of which, accord to Suetonius, there be three kinds.--
--but what be all these to the breech? say my father.
Rubenius throw he down upon the counter all kind of shoe which have be
in fashion with the romans.--
there be,
the open shoe.
the close shoe.
the slip shoe.
the wooden shoe.
the soc.
the buskin.
and the military shoe with hobnail in it, which Juvenal take notice of.
there be,
the clog.
the pattin.
the pantoufle.
the brogue.
the sandal, with latchet to they.
there be,
the feel shoe.
the linen shoe.
the laced shoe.
the braided shoe.
the calceus incisus.
and the calceus rostratus.
Rubenius shew my father how well they all fitted,--in what manner they
lace on,--with what point, strap, thong, latchet, ribband, jaggs, and
ends.--
--But I want to be inform about the breech, say my father.
Albertus Rubenius inform my father that the Romans manufacture stuff of
various fabrics,--some plain,--some striped,--others diapere throughout
the whole contexture of the wool, with silk and gold--that linen do not
begin to be in common use till towards the declension of the empire, when
the Egyptians come to settle amongst they, bring it into vogue.
--that person of quality and fortune distinguish themselves by the
fineness and whiteness of their clothe; which colour (next to purple,
which be appropriate to the great office) they most affect, and wear
on their birth-day and public rejoicings.--That it appear from the good
historian of those time, that they frequently send their clothe to the
fuller, to be clean'd and whitened:--but that the inferior people, to avoid
that expence, generally wear brown clothe, and of a something coarser
texture,--till towards the beginning of Augustus's reign, when the slave
dress like his master, and almost every distinction of habiliment be
lose, but the Latus Clavus.
and what be the Latus Clavus? say my father.
Rubenius tell he, that the point be still litigate amongst the
learned:--that Egnatius, Sigonius, Bossius Ticinensis, Bayfius Budaeus,
Salmasius, Lipsius, Lazius, Isaac Casaubon, and Joseph Scaliger, all
differ from each other,--and he from they: that some take it to be the
button,--some the coat itself,--other only the colour of it;--that the
great Bayfuis in his Wardrobe of the Ancients, chap. 12--honestly say, he
know not what it was,--whether a tibula,--a stud,--a button,--a loop,--a
buckle,--or clasp and keepers.--
--My father lose the horse, but not the saddle--they be hook and eye,
say my father--and with hook and eye he order my breech to be make.
chapter 3.lxiii.
we be now go to enter upon a new scene of events.--

leave we my mother--(tru of all the Poco-curante's of her sex!)--
careless about it, as about every thing else in the world which concern

Leave we Slop likewise to the full profit of all my dishonours.--

-And last of all,--because the hard of all--
let we leave, if possible, myself:--But 'tis impossible,--i must go along
with you to the end of the work.
Chapter 3.lxiv.
if the reader have not a clear conception of the rood and the half of ground
which lie at the bottom of my uncle Toby's kitchen-garden, and which be
the scene of so many of his delicious hours,--the fault be not in me,--but
in his imagination;--for I be sure I give he so minute a description, I
be almost ashamed of it.
when Fate be look forwards one afternoon, into the great transaction
of future times,--and recollect for what purpose this little plot, by a
decree fast bind down in iron, have be destined,--she give a nod to
nature,--'twa enough--nature throw half a spade full of her kindliest
compost upon it, with just so much clay in it, as to retain the form of
angle and indentings,--and so little of it too, as not to cling to the
spade, and render work of so much glory, nasty in foul weather.
my uncle Toby come down, as the reader have be inform, with plan along
with he, of almost every fortified town in Italy and Flanders; so let the
duke of Marlborough, or the ally, have set down before what town they
please, my uncle Toby be prepare for they.
his way, which be the simple one in the world, be this; as soon as ever
a town be invested--(but soon when the design be know) to take the
plan of it (let it be what town it would), and enlarge it upon a scale to
the exact size of his bowling-green; upon the surface of which, by mean of
a large role of packthread, and a number of small piquet drive into the
ground, at the several angle and redan, he transfer the line from his
paper; then take the profile of the place, with its work, to determine
the depth and slope of the ditches,--the talus of the glacis, and the
precise height of the several banquet, parapet, &c.--he set the corporal
to work--and sweetly go it on:--the nature of the soil,--the nature of
the work itself,--and above all, the good-nature of my uncle Toby sit
by from morning to night, and chat kindly with the corporal upon past-
do deeds,--left Labour little else but the ceremony of the name.
when the place be finish in this manner, and put into a proper posture
of defence,--it be invested,--and my uncle Toby and the corporal begin to
run their first parallel.--I beg I may not be interrupt in my story, by
be tell, that the first parallel should be at least three hundred toise
distant from the main body of the place,--and that I have not leave a single
inch for it;--for my uncle Toby take the liberty of incroache upon his
kitchen-garden, for the sake of enlarge his work on the bowling-green,
and for that reason generally run his first and second parallel betwixt
two row of his cabbage and his cauliflower; the convenience and
inconvenience of which will be consider at large in the history of my
uncle Toby's and the corporal's campaign, of which, this Ibe now write
be but a sketch, and will be finish, if I conjecture right, in three
page (but there be no guessing)--the campaign themselves will take up as
many book; and therefore I apprehend it would be hang too great a
weight of one kind of matter in so flimsy a performance as this, to
rhapsodize they, as I once intend, into the body of the work--surely they
have well be print apart,--we'll consider the affair--so take the
following sketch of they in the mean time.
Chapter 3.lxv.
when the town, with its work, be finish, my uncle Toby and the corporal
begin to run their first parallel--not at random, or any how--but from the
same point and distance the ally have begin to run theirs; and
regulate their approach and attack, by the account my uncle Toby
receive from the daily papers,--they go on, during the whole siege, step
by step with the ally.
when the duke of Marlborough make a lodgment,--my uncle Toby make a
lodgment too.--And when the face of a bastion be batter down, or a
defence ruined,--the corporal take his mattock and do as much,--and so
on;--gaine ground, and make themselves master of the work one after
another, till the town fall into their hand.
to one who take pleasure in the happy state of others,--there could not
have be a great sight in world, than on a post morning, in which a
practicable breach have be make by the duke of Marlborough, in the main
body of the place,--to have stand behind the horn-beam hedge, and observe
the spirit with which my uncle Toby, with Trim behind he, sally forth;--
the one with the Gazette in his hand,--the other with a spade on his
shoulder to execute the contents.--what an honest triumph in my uncle
Toby's look as he march up to the rampart! what intense pleasure
swimming in his eye as he stand over the corporal, read the paragraph
ten time over to he, as he be at work, lest, peradventure, he should
make the breach an inch too wide,--or leave it an inch too narrow.--But
when the chamade be beat, and the corporal help my uncle up it, and
follow with the colour in his hand, to fix they upon the ramparts--
Heaven! earth! sea!--but what avail apostrophes?--with all your element,
wet or dry, ye never compound so intoxicate a draught.
in this track of happiness for many year, without one interruption to it,
except now and then when the wind continue to blow due west for a week or
ten day together, which detain the Flanders mail, and keep they so long
in torture,--but still 'twa the torture of the happy--in this track, I
say, do my uncle Toby and Trim move for many year, every year of which,
and sometimes every month, from the invention of either the one or the
other of they, add some new conceit or quirk of improvement to their
operation, which always open fresh spring of delight in carry they
on.
the first year's campaign be carry on from begin to end, in the
plain and simple method Ihave relate.
in the second year, in which my uncle Toby take Liege and Ruremond, he
think he might afford the expence of four handsome draw-bridge; of two
of which I have give an exact description in the former part of my work.
at the latter end of the same year he add a couple of gate with port-

thing; and during the winter of the same year, my uncle Toby, instead of a
new suit of clothe, which he always have at Christmas, treat himself with
a handsome sentry-box, to stand at the corner of the bowling-green, betwixt
which point and the foot of the glacis, there be leave a little kind of an
esplanade for he and the corporal to confer and hold council of war upon.
--the sentry-box be in case of rain.
all these be paint white three time over the ensue spring, which
enable my uncle Toby to take the field with great splendour.
my father would often say to Yorick, that if any mortal in the whole
universe have do such a thing except his brother Toby, it would have be
look upon by the world as one of the most refined satire upon the parade
and prance manner in which Lewis XIV. from the beginning of the war, but
particularly that very year, have take the field--but 'tis not my brother
Toby's nature, kind soul! my father would add, to insult any one.
--but let we go on.
chapter 3.lxvi.
I must observe, that although in the first year's campaign, the word town
be often mentioned,--yet there be no town at that time within the polygon;
that addition be not make till the summer follow the spring in which
the bridge and sentry-box be paint, which be the third year of my
uncle Toby's campaigns,--when upon his take Amberg, Bonn, and Rhinberg,
and Huy and Limbourg, one after another, a thought come into the corporal's
head, that to talk of take so many town, without one Town to shew for
it,--wa a very nonsensical way of go to work, and so propose to my
uncle Toby, that they should have a little model of a town build for them,-
-to be run up together of slit deal, and then paint, and clap within
the interior polygon to serve for all.
my uncle Toby feel the good of the project instantly, and instantly agree
to it, but with the addition of two singular improvement, of which he be
almost as proud as if he have be the original inventor of the project
itself.
the one be, to have the town build exactly in the style of those of which
it be most likely to be the representative:--with grate window, and the
gable end of the house, face the street, &c. &c.--as those in Ghent
and bruge, and the rest of the town in Brabant and Flanders.
the other be, not to have the house run up together, as the corporal
propose, but to have every house independent, to hook on, or off, so as to
form into the plan of whatever town they please. this be put directly
into hand, and many and many a look of mutual congratulation be exchange
between my uncle Toby and the corporal, as the carpenter do the work.
--It answer prodigiously the next summer--the town be a perfect Proteus-
-It be Landen, and Trerebach, and Santvliet, and Drusen, and hagenau,--and
then it be Ostend and Menin, and Aeth and Dendermond.
--surely never do any Town act so many part, since Sodom and Gomorrah, as
my uncle Toby's town do.
in the fourth year, my uncle Toby think a town look foolishly without
a church, add a very fine one with a steeple.--trim be for have bell
in it;--my uncle Toby say, the metal have well be cast into cannon.
this lead the way the next campaign for half a dozen brass field-piece, to

in a short time, these lead the way for a train of somewhat larger,--and so
on--(as must always be the case in hobby-horsical affair) from piece of
half an inch bore, till it come at last to my father's jack boot.
the next year, which be that in which Lisle be besiege, and at the close
of which both Ghent and bruge fall into our hands,--my uncle Toby be
sadly put to it for proper ammunition;--i say proper ammunition--because
his great artillery would not bear powder; and 'twa well for the Shandy
family they would not--for so full be the paper, from the beginning to
the end of the siege, of the incessant firing keep up by the besiegers,--
and so heated be my uncle Toby's imagination with the account of they,
that he have infallibly shoot away all his estate.
something therefore be want as a succedaneum, especially in one or two
of the more violent paroxysm of the siege, to keep up something like a
continual firing in the imagination,--and this something, the corporal,
whose principal strength lie in invention, supply by an entire new system
of batter of his own,--without which, this have be object to by
military critic, to the end of the world, as one of the great desiderata
of my uncle Toby's apparatus.
this will not be explain the bad, for set off, as I generally do,
at a little distance from the subject.

as this be the most memorable attack in the whole war,--the most gallant

--gracious power! which erst have open the lip of the dumb in his
distress, and make the tongue of the stammerer speak plain--when I shall
arrive at this dread page, deal not with I, then, with a stint hand.
chapter 3.lxix.
the corporal, who the night before have resolve in his mind to supply the

upon turn it this way, and that, a little in his mind, he soon begin to

--Let no man say from what taggs and jaggs hint may not be cut out for the

I beg the reader will assist I here, to wheel off my uncle Toby's ordnance

vain science! thou assistest we in no case of this kind--and thou puzzl

there be, Madam, in my uncle Toby, a singleness of heart which mislead he
so far out of the little serpentine track in which thing of this nature
usually go on; you can--you can have no conception of it: with this, there
be a plainness and simplicity of thinking, with such an unmistrusting

Chapter 3.LXXIV.
amongst the many ill consequence of the treaty of Utrecht, it be within a
point of give my uncle Toby a surfeit of siege; and though he recover
his appetite afterwards, yet Calais itself leave not a deep scar in Mary's
heart, than Utrecht upon my uncle Toby's. to the end of his life he never
could hear Utrecht mention upon any account whatever,--or so much as read
an article of news extract out of the Utrecht Gazette, without fetch a
sigh, as if his heart would break in twain.
my father, who be a great Motive-Monger, and consequently a very dangerous
person for a man to sit by, either laugh or crying,--for he generally
know your motive for do both, much well than you know it yourself--
would always console my uncle Toby upon these occasion, in a way, which
shewed plainly, he imagine my uncle Toby grieve for nothing in the whole
affair, so much as the loss of his hobby-horse.--never mind, brother Toby,
he would say,--by God's blessing we shall have another war break out again
some of these day; and when it does,--the belligerent power, if they
would hang themselves, cannot keep we out of play.--i defy them, my dear
Toby, he would add, to take country without take towns,--or town
without siege.
my uncle Toby never take this back-stroke of my father's at his hobby-horse
kindly.--He think the stroke ungenerous; and the more so, because in
strike the horse he hit the rider too, and in the most dishonourable part
a blow could fall; so that upon these occasion, he always lay down his
pipe upon the table with more fire to defend himself than common.
I tell the reader, this time two year, that my uncle Toby be not
eloquent; and in the very same page give an instance to the contrary:--I
repeat the observation, and a fact which contradict it again.--He be not
eloquent,--it be not easy to my uncle Toby to make long harangues,--and he
hat florid one; but there be occasion where the stream overflow the
man, and run so counter to its usual course, that in some part my uncle
Toby, for a time, be at least equal to Tertullus--but in other, in my own
opinion, infinitely above he.
my father be so highly pleased with one of these apologetical oration of

that he write it down before he go to bed.
I have have the good fortune to meet with it amongst my father's paper,
with here and there an insertion of his own, betwixt two crook, thus (. .
.), and be endorse,
my Brother Toby's Justification of his own Principles and Conduct in
wish to continue the War.
I may safely say, I have read over this apologetical oration of my uncle
Toby's a hundred time, and think it so fine a model of defence,--and shew
so sweet a temperament of gallantry and good principle in he, that I give
it the world, word for word (interlineation and all), as I find it.
chapter 3.lxxv.
my Uncle Toby's Apologetical Oration.
I be not insensible, brother Shandy, that when a man whose profession be
arm, wish, as I have do, for war,--it have an ill aspect to the world;-
-and that, how just and right soever his motif the intention may be,--he
stand in an uneasy posture in vindicate himself from private view in
do it.
for this cause, if a soldier be a prudent man, which he may be without
be a jot the less brave, he will be sure not to utter his wish in the
hearing of an enemy; for say what he will, an enemy will not believe him.--
he will be cautious of do it even to a friend,--lest he may suffer in
his esteem:--but if his heart be overcharge, and a secret sigh for arm
must have its vent, he will reserve it for the ear of a brother, who know
his character to the bottom, and what his true notion, disposition, and
principle of honour be: what, I hope, I have be in all these, brother
Shandy, would be unbecoming in I to say:--much bad, I know, have I be
than I ought,--and something bad, perhaps, than I think: but such as I
be, you, my dear brother Shandy, who have suck the same breast with me,-
-and with whom I have be bring up from my cradle,--and from whose
knowledge, from the first hour of our boyish pastime, down to this, I
have conceal no one action of my life, and scarce a thought in it--such
as I be, brother, you must by this time know I, with all my vice, and
with all my weakness too, whether of my age, my temper, my passion, or
my understanding.
tell I then, my dear brother Shandy, upon which of they it be, that when I
condemn the peace of Utrecht, and grieve the war be not carry on with
vigour a little long, you should think your brother do it upon unworthy
view; or that in wish for war, he should be bad enough to wish more of
his fellow-creature slain,--more slave make, and more family drive
from their peaceful habitation, merely for his own pleasure:--tell I,
brother Shandy, upon what one deed of mine do you ground it? (the devil a
deed do I know of, dear Toby, but one for a hundred pound, which I lend
thee to carry on these cursed siege.)
if, when I be a school-boy, I could not hear a drum beat, but my heart
beat with it--be it my fault?--Did I plant the propensity there?--Did I
sound the alarm within, or nature?
when Guy, Earl of Warwick, and Parismus and Parismenus, and Valentine and
Orson, and the Seven Champions of England, be hand around the school,--
be they not all purchase with my own pocket-money? be that selfish,
brother Shandy? when we read over the siege of Troy, which last ten
year and eight months,--though with such a train of artillery as we have at
Namur, the town might have be carry in a week--be I not as much
concerned for the destruction of the Greeks and Trojans as any boy of the
whole school? have I not three stroke of a ferula give I, two on my
right hand, and one on my left, for call Helena a bitch for it? do any
one of you shed more tear for Hector? and when king Priam come to the
camp to beg his body, and return weep back to Troy without it,--you
know, brother, I could not eat my dinner.--
--Did that bespeak I cruel? or because, brother Shandy, my blood fly out
into the camp, and my heart pant for war,--wa it a proof it could not
ache for the distress of war too?
o brother! 'tis one thing for a soldier to gather laurels,--and 'tis
another to scatter cypress.--(Who tell thee, my dear Toby, that cypress be
use by the antient on mournful occasion?)
--'Tis one thing, brother Shandy, for a soldier to hazard his own life--to
leap first down into the trench, where he be sure to be cut in pieces:--
'tis one thing, from public spirit and a thirst of glory, to enter the
breach the first man,--to stand in the foremost rank, and march bravely on
with drum and trumpet, and colour fly about his ears:--'tis one
thing, I say, brother Shandy, to do this,--and 'tis another thing to
reflect on the misery of war;--to view the desolation of whole
country, and consider the intolerable fatigue and hardship which the
soldier himself, the instrument who work they, be force (for sixpence a
day, if he can get it) to undergo.
need I be tell, dear Yorick, as I be by you, in Le Fever's funeral sermon,
that so soft and gentle a creature, bear to love, to mercy, and kindness,
as man be, be not shape for this?--But why do you not add, Yorick,--if
not by Nature--that he be so by Necessity?--For what be war? what be it,
Yorick, when fight as ours have be, upon principle of liberty, and upon
principle of honour--what be it, but the get together of quiet and
harmless people, with their sword in their hand, to keep the ambitious
and the turbulent within bound? and heaven be my witness, brother Shandy,
that the pleasure I have take in these things,--and that infinite delight,
in particular, which have attend my siege in my bowling-green, have arise
within I, and I hope in the corporal too, from the consciousness we both
have, that in carry they on, we be answer the great end of our
creation.

I tell the christian reader--I say Christian--hop he be one--and if he

Chapter 3.LXXVII.
I tell the christian reader in the beginning of the chapter which precede

fortification and harbour of Dunkirk be demolish, accord to
stipulation.
the French be so backwards all that summer in set about that affair,

only her thunderbolt to fall upon the martial work, which might have
