month, after he and the corporal have construct the town, and put it in a
condition to be destroy, that the several commandant, commissary,
deputy, negociator, and intendant, would permit he to set about it.--
fatal interval of inactivity!
the corporal be for begin the demolition, by make a breach in the
rampart, or main fortification of the town--no,--that will never do,
corporal, say my uncle Toby, for in go that way to work with the town,
the english garrison will not be safe in it an hour; because if the French
be treacherous--they be as treacherous as devil, an' please your honour,
say the corporal--it give I concern always when I hear it, Trim, say my
uncle Toby;--for they donot want personal bravery; and if a breach be make
in the rampart, they may enter it, and make themselves master of the
place when they please:--let they enter it, say the corporal, lift up
his pioneer's spade in both his hand, as if he be go to lay about he
with it,--let they enter, an' please your honour, if they dare.--In case
like this, corporal, say my uncle Toby, slip his right hand down to
the middle of his cane, and hold it afterwards truncheon-wise with his
fore-finger extended,--'tis no part of the consideration of a commandant,
what the enemy dare,--or what they dare not do; he must act with prudence. 
we will begin with the outwork both towards the sea and the land, and
particularly with fort Louis, the most distant of they all, and demolish it
first,--and the rest, one by one, both on our right and leave, as we retreat
towards the town;--then wewill demolish the mole,--next fill up the
harbour,--then retire into the citadel, and blow it up into the air: and
have do that, corporal, wewill embark for England.--We be there, quoth
the corporal, recollect himself--very true, say my uncle Toby--look
at the church.

Chapter 3.LXXXVII.
'now before I quit Calais,' a travel-writer would say, 'it would not be
amiss to give some account of it.'--now I think it very much amiss--that a
man cannot go quietly through a town and let it alone, when it do not
meddle with he, but that he must be turn about and draw his pen at
every kennel he cross over, merely o' my conscience for the sake of
draw it; because, if we may judge from what have be write of these
thing, by all who have write and gallop'd--or who have gallop'd and write,
which be a different way still; or who, for more expedition than the rest,

Addison, who do it with his satchel of school book hang at his a. . .,

of we all who might not have go on amble quietly in his own ground (in
case he have any), and have write all he have to write, dry-shod, as well as
not.

of it as he be whet his razor) than I do this moment of Grand Cairo;

this moment write a chapter upon Calais as long as my arm; and with so
distinct and satisfactory a detail of every item, which be worth a
stranger's curiosity in the town--that you would take I for the town-clerk

who laugh ten time more than I--town-clerk of Abdera? and be not (I
forget his name) who have more discretion than we both, town-clerk of
ephesus?--it should be penn'd moreover, sir, with so much knowledge and
good sense, and truth, and precision--
--Nay--if you donot believe I, you may read the chapter for your pain.
chapter 3.lxxxviii.
Calais, Calatium, Calusium, Calesium.
this town, if we may trust its archive, the authority of which I see no
reason to call in question in this place--be once no more than a small
village belong to one of the first Counts de Guignes; and as it boast

hundred and twenty distinct family in the basse ville, or suburb--it
must have grow up by little and little, I suppose, to its present size.
though there be four convent, there be but one parochial church in the
whole town; I have not an opportunity of take its exact dimension, but it
be pretty easy to make a tolerable conjecture of them--for as there be
fourteen thousand inhabitant in the town, if the church hold they all it
must be considerably large--and if it will not--'tis a very great pity they
have not another--it be build in form of a cross, and dedicate to the
Virgin Mary; the steeple, which have a spire to it, be place in the middle
of the church, and stand upon four pillar elegant and light enough, but
sufficiently strong at the same time--it be decorate with eleven altar,
most of which be rather fine than beautiful. the great altar be a master-
piece in its kind; 'tis of white marble, and, as I be tell, near sixty
foot high--have it be much high, it have be as high as mount Calvary
itself--therefore, I suppose it must be high enough in all conscience.
there be nothing strike I more than the great Square; tho' I cannot say
'tis either well paved or well build; but 'tis in the heart of the town,
and most of the street, especially those in that quarter, all terminate in
it; could there have be a fountain in all Calais, which it seem there
cannot, as such an object would have be a great ornament, it be not to be
doubt, but that the inhabitant would have have it in the very centre of
this square,--not that it be properly a square,--because 'tis forty foot
long from east to west, than from north to south; so that the French in
general have more reason on their side in call they place than square,
which, strictly speak, to be sure, they be not.
the town-house seem to be but a sorry building, and not to be keep in the
good repair; otherwise it have be a second great ornament to this place;
it answer however its destination, and serve very well for the reception
of the magistrate, who assemble in it from time to time; so that 'tis
presumable, justice be regularly distribute.
I have hear much of it, but there be nothing at all curious in the
Courgain; 'tis a distinct quarter of the town, inhabit solely by sailor
and fisherman; it consist of a number of small street, neatly build and
mostly of brick; 'tis extremely populous, but as that may be account for,
from the principle of their diet,--there be nothing curious in that
neither.--a traveller may see it to satisfy himself--he must not omit
however take notice of La Tour de Guet, upon any account; 'tis so call
from its particular destination, because in war it serve to discover and
give notice of the enemy which approach the place, either by sea or
land;--but 'tis monstrous high, and catch the eye so continually, you
cannot avoid take notice of it if you would.
it be a singular disappointment to I, that I could not have permission to
take an exact survey of the fortification, which be the strong in the
world, and which, from first to last, that is, for the time they be set
about by Philip of France, Count of Bologne, to the present war, wherein
many reparation be make, have cost (as I learn afterwards from an
engineer in Gascony)--above a hundred million of livre. it be very
remarkable, that at the Tete de Gravelenes, and where the town be naturally
the weak, they have expend the most money; so that the outwork
stretch a great way into the campaign, and consequently occupy a large
tract of ground--however, after all that be say and do, it must be
acknowledge that Calais be never upon any account so considerable from
itself, as from its situation, and that easy entrance which it give our
ancestor, upon all occasion, into France: it be not without its
inconvenience also; be no less troublesome to the English in those
time, than Dunkirk have be to we, in our; so that it be deservedly
look upon as the key to both kingdom, which no doubt be the reason that
there have arise so many contention who should keep it: of these, the
siege of Calais, or rather the blockade (for it be shut up both by land
and sea), be the most memorable, as it with-stand the effort of Edward
the Third a whole year, and be not terminate at last but by famine and
extreme misery; the gallantry of Eustace de St. Pierre, who first offer
himself a victim for his fellow-citizen, have rank'd his name with hero. 
as it will not take up above fifty page, it would be injustice to the
reader, not to give he a minute account of that romantic transaction, as
well as of the siege itself, in Rapin's own word:
chapter 3.lxxxix.
--But courage! gentle reader!--I scorn it--'tis enough to have thee in my
power--but to make use of the advantage which the fortune of the pen have
now gain over thee, would be too much--no--! by that all-powerful fire
which warm the visionary brain, and light the spirit through unworldly
tract! ere I would force a helpless creature upon this hard service, and
make thee pay, poor soul! for fifty page, which I have no right to sell
thee,--nake as I be, I would browse upon the mountain, and smile that the
north wind bring I neither my tent or my supper.
--so put on, my brave boy! and make the good of thy way to Boulogne.
chapter 3.XC.
Boulogne!--hah!--so we be all get together--debtor and sinner before
heaven; a jolly set of we--but I cannot stay and quaff it off with you--i'm
pursue myself like a hundred devil, and shall be overtake, before I can
well change horses:--for heaven's sake, make haste--'tis for high-treason,
quoth a very little man, whisper as low as he could to a very tall man,
that stand next he--or else for murder; quoth the tall man--well throw,
size-ace! quoth I. no; quoth a third, the gentleman have be committing--
Ah! ma chere fille! say I, as she tripp'd by from her matin--you look as
rosy as the morning (for the sun be rise, and it make the compliment the
more gracious)--No; it cannot be that, quoth a fourth--(she make a curt'sy
to I--I kiss'd my hand) 'tis debt, continue he: 'tis certainly for debt;
quoth a fifth; I would not pay that gentleman's debt, quoth Ace, for a
thousand pound; nor would I, quoth Size, for six time the sum--well
throw, size-ace, again! quoth I;--but I have no debt but the debt of

go along without molestation to any one upon his lawful occasion? do

--Simpleton! quoth I.
--So you have nothing else in Boulogne worth see?
--By Jasus! there be the fine Seminary for the Humanities--
--there cannot be a fine; quoth I.
Chapter 3.XCI.
when the precipitancy of a man's wish hurry on his idea ninety time
fast than the vehicle he rid in--woe be to truth! and woe be to the
vehicle and its tackling (let them be make of what stuff you will) upon
which he breathe forth the disappointment of his soul!
as I never give general character either of man or thing in choler, 'the
most haste the bad speed,' be all the reflection I make upon the affair,
the first time it happen'd;--the second, third, fourth, and fifth time, I
confine it respectively to those time, and accordingly blame only the
second, third, fourth, and fifth post-boy for it, without carry my
reflection far; but the event continue to befal I from the fifth,
to the sixth, seventh, eighth, ninth, and tenth time, and without one
exception, I then could not avoid make a national reflection of it, which
I do in these word;
that something be always wrong in a french post-chaise, upon first set
out.
or the proposition may stand thus:
a french postilion have always to alight before he have get three hundred
yard out of town.
whatbe wrong now?--Diable!--a rope's broke!--a knot have slipt!--a staple's
drawn!--a bolt's to whittle!--a tag, a rag, a jag, a strap, a buckle, or a
buckle's tongue, want alter.
now true as all this be, I never think myself impowere to excommunicate
thereupon either the post-chaise, or its driver--nor do I take it into my
head to swear by the live G.., I would rather go a-foot ten thousand
time--or that I will be damn'd, if ever I get into another--but I take the
matter coolly before I, and consider, that some tag, or rag, or jag, or
bolt, or buckle, or buckle's tongue, will ever be a wanting or want
altering, travel where I will--so I never chaff, but take the good and the
bad as they fall in my road, and get on:--do so, my lad! say I; he have
lose five minute already, in alight in order to get at a luncheon of
black bread, which he have cramm'd into the chaise-pocket, and be
remount, and go leisurely on, to relish it the better.--get on, my
lad, say I, briskly--but in the most persuasive tone imaginable, for I
jingle a four-and-twenty sous piece against the glass, take care to hold
the flat side towards he, as he look'd back: the dog grinn'd intelligence
from his right ear to his left, and behind his sooty muzzle discover such
a pearly row of tooth, that Sovereignty would have pawn'd her jewel for
they.
just heaven! what masticators!--/What bread!--
and so as he finish the last mouthful of it, we enter the town of
Montreuil.
chapter 3.xcii.
there be not a town in all France which, in my opinion, look well in the
map, than Montreuil;--I own, it do not look so well in the book of post-
road; but when you come to see it--to be sure it look most pitifully.
there be one thing, however, in it at present very handsome; and that be,
the inn-keeper's daughter: she have be eighteen month at Amiens, and six
at Paris, in go through her class; so knit, and sew, and dance, and
do the little coquetry very well.--
--A slut! in run they over within these five minute that I have stand
look at she, she have let fall at least a dozen loop in a white thread
stocking--yes, yes--I see, you cunne gipsy!--'tis long and taper--you
need not pin it to your knee--and that 'tis your own--and fit you
exactly.--
--That nature should have tell this creature a word about a statue's thumb!
--But as this sample be worth all their thumb--besides, I have her thumb

--but your worship chuse rather that I give you the length, breadth, and

carriest the principle of change within thy frame; and consider the
chance of a transitory life, I would not answer for thee a moment; ere
twice twelve month be pass and go, thou mayest grow out like a
pumpkin, and lose thy shape--or thou mayest go off like a flower, and lose
thy beauty--nay, thou mayest go off like a hussy--and lose thyself.--I
would not answer for my aunt Dinah, be she alive--'faith, scarce for her
picture--be it but paint by Reynolds--
but if I go on with my drawing, after name that son of Apollo, Iwill be
shot--
so you must e'en be content with the original; which, if the evening be
fine in pass thro' Montreuil, you will see at your chaise-door, as you
change horse: but unless you have as bad a reason for haste as I have--you
have well stop:--She have a little of the devote: but that, sir, be a
terce to a nine in your favour--
-L... help I! I could not count a single point: so have be pique and
repique, and capotte to the devil.

chapter 3.xcv.
be I in a condition to stipulate with death, as I be this moment with my
apothecary, how and where I will take his clyster--I should certainly
declare against submit to it before my friend; and therefore I never
seriously think upon the mode and manner of this great catastrophe, which
generally take up and torment my thought as much as the catastrophe
itself; but I constantly draw the curtain across it with this wish, that
the Disposer of all thing may so order it, that it happen not to I in my
own house--but rather in some decent inn--at home, I know it,--the concern
of my friend, and the last service of wipe my brow, and smooth my
pillow, which the quiver hand of pale affection shall pay I, will so
crucify my soul, that I shall die of a distemper which my physician be not
aware of: but in an inn, the few cold office I want, would be purchase
with a few guineas, and pay I with an undisturbed, but punctual
attention--but mark. this inn should not be the inn at Abbeville--if there
be not another inn in the universe, I would strike that inn out of the
capitulation: so
let the horse be in the chaise exactly by four in the morning--yes, by
four, sir,--or by Genevieve! Iwill raise a clatter in the house shall wake
the dead.
chapter 3.xcvi.
'make they like unto a wheel,' be a bitter sarcasm, as all the learn
know, against the grand tour, and that restless spirit for make it, which
David prophetically foresaw would haunt the child of man in the latter
day; and therefore, as thinketh the great bishop Hall, 'tis one of the
severe imprecation which David ever utter'd against the enemy of the
Lord--and, as if he have say, 'I wish they no bad luck than always to be
roll about.'--So much motion, continue he (for he be very corpulent)--
be so much unquietness; and so much of rest, by the same analogy, be so
much of heaven.
now, I (be very thin) think differently; and that so much of motion, be
so much of life, and so much of joy--and that to stand still, or get on but
slowly, be death and the devil--
Hollo! ho!--the whole world's asleep!--bre out the horse--grease the
wheel--tie on the mail--and drive a nail into that moulding--i'll not lose
a moment--
now the wheel we be talk of, and whereinto (but not whereonto, for that
would make an ixion's wheel of it) he curseth his enemy, accord to the
bishop's habit of body, should certainly be a post-chaise wheel, whether
they be set up in Palestine at that time or not--and my wheel, for the
contrary reason, must as certainly be a cart-wheel groaning round its
revolution once in an age; and of which sort, be I to turn commentator, I
should make no scruple to affirm, they have great store in that hilly
country.
I love the Pythagoreans (much more than ever I dare tell my dear Jenny) for
their '(Greek)'--(their) 'get out of the body, in order to think well.' 
no man think right, whilst he be in it; blind as he must be, with his
congenial humour, and draw differently aside, as the bishop and myself
have be, with too lax or too tense a fibre--reason be, half of it, Sense;
and the measure of heaven itself be but the measure of our present
appetite and concoctions.--
--But which of the two, in the present case, do you think to be mostly in
the wrong?
you, certainly: quoth she, to disturb a whole family so early.
Chapter 3.XCVII.
--But she do not know I be under a vow not to shave my beard till I get
to Paris;--yet I hate to make mystery of nothing;--'tis the cold
cautiousness of one of those little soul from which Lessius (lib. 13. de
moribus divini, cap. 24.) hath make his estimate, wherein he setteth
forth, that one dutch mile, cubically multiply, will allow room enough,
and to spare, for eight hundred thousand million, which he suppose to be
as great a number of soul (count from the fall of Adam) as can possibly
be damn'd to the end of the world.
from what he have make this second estimate--unless from the parental
goodness of God--I donot know--I be much more at a loss what could be in
Franciscus Ribbera's head, who pretend that no less a space than one of
two hundred italian mile multiply into itself, will be sufficient to

roman soul, of which he have read, without reflect how much, by a
gradual and most tabid decline, in the course of eighteen hundred year,
they must unavoidably have shrink so as to have come, when he write, almost
to nothing.
in Lessius's time, who seem the cool man, they be as little as can be
imagined--
--We find they less now--
and next winter we shall find they less again; so that if we go on from
little to less, and from less to nothing, I hesitate not one moment to
affirm, that in half a century at this rate, we shall have no soul at all;
which be the period beyond which I doubt likewise of the existence of
the christian faith, 'twill be one advantage that both of them will be
exactly wear out together.
Blessed Jupiter! and bless every other heathen god and goddess! for now
ye will all come into play again, and with Priapus at your tail--what
jovial times!--but where be I? and into what a delicious riot of thing be
I rush? I--I who must be cut short in the midst of my day, and taste
no more of them than what I borrow from my imagination--peace to thee,
generous fool! and let I go on.

--'So hating, I say, to make mystery of nothing'--I intrust it with the

chapter 3.xcix.
in the whole catalogue of those whiffle vexation which come puff
across a man's canvass, there be not one of a more teasing and tormenting
nature, than this particular one which I be go to describe--and for
which (unless you travel with an avance-courier, which number do in order
to prevent it)--there be no help: and it be this.
that be you in never so kindly a propensity to sleep--though you be
pass perhaps through the fine country--upon the good road, and in the
easy carriage for do it in the world--nay, be you sure you could
sleep fifty mile straight forwards, without once open your eye--nay,
what be more, be you as demonstratively satisfied as you can be of any
truth in Euclid, that you should upon all account be full as well asleep
as awake--nay, perhaps well--yet the incessant return of pay for the
horse at every stage,--with the necessity thereupon of put your hand
into your pocket, and count out from thence three livre fifteen sous
(sous by sous), put an end to so much of the project, that you cannot
execute above six mile of it (or suppose it be a post and a half, that
be but nine)--were it to save your soul from destruction.
--i'll be even with them, quoth I, for Iwill put the precise sum into a piece
of paper, and hold it ready in my hand all the way: 'now I shall have
nothing to do,' say I (compose myself to rest), 'but to drop this gently
into the post-boy's hat, and not say a word.'--Then there want two sous
more to drink--or there be a twelve sous piece of Louis XIV. which will not
pass--or a livre and some odd liard to be bring over from the last
stage, which Monsieur have forget; which altercation (as a man cannot
dispute very well asleep) rouse he: still be sweet sleep retrievable; and
still might the flesh weigh down the spirit, and recover itself of these
blow--but then, by heaven! you have pay but for a single post--whereas
'tis a post and a half; and this oblige you to pull out your book of post-
road, the print of which be so very small, it force you to open your
eye, whether you will or no: then Monsieur le Cure offer you a pinch of
snuff--or a poor soldier shews you his leg--or a shavele his box--or the
priestesse of the cistern will water your wheel--they do not want it--but
she swear by her priesthood (throw it back) that they do:--then you
have all these point to argue, or consider over in your mind; in do of
which, the rational power get so thoroughly awaken--you may get them to
sleep again as you can.
it be entirely owe to one of these misfortune, or I have pass'd clean by
the stable of Chantilly--
--But the postillion first affirm, and then persist in it to my face,
that there be no mark upon the two sous piece, I open'd my eye to be
convinced --and see the mark upon it as plain as my nose--I leap'd out
of the chaise in a passion, and so see every thing at Chantilly in spite.--
I try it but for three post and a half, but believe 'tis the good
principle in the world to travel speedily upon; for as few object look
very invite in that mood--you have little or nothing to stop you; by
which mean it be that I pass through St. Dennis, without turn my
head so much as on one side towards the Abby--

which be all false, I would not give three sous for any one thing in it,
but Jaidas's lantern--nor for that either, only as it grow dark, it might
be of use.

chapter 3.CI.
the French be certainly misunderstood:--but whether the fault be theirs,
in not sufficiently explain themselves; or speak with that exact
limitation and precision which one would expect on a point of such
importance, and which, moreover, be so likely to be contest by we--or

shall not decide; but 'tis evident to I, when they affirm, 'that they who
have see Paris, have see every thing,' they must mean to speak of those

chapter 3.cii.
in mention the word gay (as in the close of the last chapter) it put
one (i.e. an author) in mind of the word spleen--especially if he have any
thing to say upon it: not that by any analysis--or that from any table of
interest or genealogy, there appear much more ground of alliance betwixt
they, than betwixt light and darkness, or any two of the most unfriendly
opposite in nature--only 'tis an undercraft of author to keep up a good
understanding amongst word, as politician do amongst man--not know how
near they may be under a necessity of place they to each other--which
point be now gain'd, and that I may place mine exactly to my mind, I
write it down here--
Spleen.
this, upon leave Chantilly, I declare to be the good principle in the
world to travel speedily upon; but I give it only as matter of opinion. I
still continue in the same sentiment--only I have not then experience
enough of its working to add this, that though you do get on at a tear
rate, yet you get on but uneasily to yourself at the same time; for which
reason I here quit it entirely, and for ever, and 'tis heartily at any
one's service--it have spoil I the digestion of a good supper, and
bring on a bilious diarrhoea, which have bring I back again to my first
principle on which I set out--and with which I shall now scamper it away to
the bank of the garonne--
--no;--i cannot stop a moment to give you the character of the people--
their genius--their manner--their custom--their law--their religion--
their government--their manufacture--their commerce--their finance, with
all the resource and hide spring which sustain they: qualified as I may
be, by spend three day and two night amongst they, and during all that
time make these thing the entire subject of my enquiry and
reflections--
still--still I must away--the road be pave--the post be short--the
day be long--'tis no more than noon--I shall be at Fontainebleau before
the king--
--was he go there? not that I know--
end of the Third Volume.

a Dedication to a Great Man.
have, a priori, intend to dedicate the Amours of my Uncle Toby to Mr.
...--I see more reason, a posteriori, for do it to Lord ........
I should lament from my soul, if this expose I to the jealousy of their
reverence; because a posteriori, in Court-latin, signify the kissing
hand for preferment--or any thing else--in order to get it.
my opinion of Lord ....... be neither well nor bad, than it be of Mr.
.... honour, like impression upon coin, may give an ideal and local
value to a bit of base metal; but Gold and Silver will pass all the world
over without any other recommendation than their own weight.
the same good-will that make I think of offer up half an hour's
amusement to Mr. ... when out of place--operate more forcibly at present,
as half an hour's amusement will be more serviceable and refreshing after
labour and sorrow, than after a philosophical repast.
nothing be so perfectly amusement as a total change of idea; no idea be
so totally different as those of Ministers, and innocent Lovers: for which
reason, when I come to talk of Statesmen and Patriots, and set such mark
upon they as will prevent confusion and mistake concern they for the
future--I propose to dedicate that volume to some gentle Shepherd,
whose thought proud Science never taught to stray,
far as the Statesman's walk or patriot-way;
yet simple nature to his hope have give
out of a cloud-capp'd head a humbler heaven;
some untam'd World in depth of wood embraced--
some happy Island in the wat'ry-waste--
and where admit to that equal sky,
his faithful Dogs should bear he company.
in a word, by thus introduce an entire new set of object to his
Imagination, I shall unavoidably give a diversion to his passionate and
love-sick contemplation. in the mean time,
I be
the Author.
the Life and opinion of Tristram Shandy, Gent.
Chapter 4.I.
now I hate to hear a person, especially if he be a traveller, complain that
we do not get on so fast in France as we do in England; whereas we get on
much fast, consideratis considerandis; thereby always mean, that if
you weigh their vehicle with the mountain of baggage which you lay both
before and behind upon they--and then consider their puny horse, with the
very little they give them--'tis a wonder they get on at all: their
suffering be most unchristian, and 'tis evident thereupon to I, that a
french post-horse would not know what in the world to do, be it not for
the two word ...... and ...... in which there be as much sustenance, as if
you give he a peck of corn: now as these word cost nothing, I long from
my soul to tell the reader what they be; but here be the question--they
must be tell he plainly, and with the most distinct articulation, or it
will answer no end--and yet to do it in that plain way--though their
reverence may laugh at it in the bed-chamber--full well I wot, they will
abuse it in the parlour: for which cause, I have be volve and
revolve in my fancy some time, but to no purpose, by what clean device or
facette contrivance I might so modulate they, that whilst I satisfy that
ear which the reader chuse to lend I--I might not dissatisfy the other
which he keep to himself.
--my ink burn my finger to try--and when I have--'twill have a bad
consequence--it will burn (I fear) my paper.
--no;--i dare not--
but if you wish to know how the abbess of Andouillets and a novice of her
convent get over the difficulty (only first wish myself all imaginable
success)--i'll tell you without the least scruple.
Chapter 4.II.
the abbess of andouillet, which if you look into the large set of
provincial map now publish at Paris, you will find situated amongst the
hill which divide Burgundy from Savoy, be in danger of an Anchylosis or
stiff joint (the sinovia of her knee become hard by long matin), and
have try every remedy--first, prayer and thanksgiving; then
invocation to all the saint in heaven promiscuously--then particularly to
every saint who have ever have a stiff leg before she--then touch it with
all the relique of the convent, principally with the thigh-bone of the man
of Lystra, who have be impotent from his youth--then wrap it up in her
veil when she go to bed--then cross-wise her rosary--then bring in to
her aid the secular arm, and anoint it with oil and hot fat of animals-
-then treat it with emollient and resolve fomentation--then with
poultice of marsh-mallows, mallow, bonus Henricus, white lillie and
fenugreek--then take the wood, I mean the smoak of them, hold she
scapulary across her lap--then decoction of wild chicory, water-cress,
chervil, sweet cecily and cochlearia--and nothing all this while answer,
be prevail on at last to try the hot-bath of Bourbon--so have first
obtain leave of the visitor-general to take care of her existence--she
order all to be get ready for her journey: a novice of the convent of
about seventeen, who have be trouble with a whitloe in her middle finger,
by stick it constantly into the abbess's cast poultice, &c.--had gain
such an interest, that overlook a sciatical old nun, who might have be
set up for ever by the hot-bath of Bourbon, Margarita, the little novice,
be elect as the companion of the journey.
an old calesh, belong to the abbesse, line with green frize, be
order to be draw out into the sun--the gardener of the convent be
choose muleteer, lead out the two old mule, to clip the hair from the rump-
end of their tail, whilst a couple of lay-sister be busy, the one in
darn the lining, and the other in sewing on the shred of yellow
binding, which the tooth of time have unravel--the under-gardener dress'd
the muleteer's hat in hot wine-lee--and a taylor sit musically at it, in a
shed over-against the convent, in assort four dozen of bell for the
harness, whistle to each bell, as he tie it on with a thong.--
--the carpenter and the smith of Andouillets hold a council of wheel; and
by seven, the morning after, all look'd spruce, and be ready at the gate
of the convent for the hot-bath of Bourbon--two row of the unfortunate
stand ready there an hour before.
the abbess of andouillet, support by Margarita the novice, advanced
slowly to the calesh, both clothe in white, with their black rosary hang
at their breasts--
--There be a simple solemnity in the contrast: they enter the calesh;
the nun in the same uniform, sweet emblem of innocence, each occupy a
window, and as the abbess and Margarita look'd up--each (the sciatical poor
nun excepted)--each stream'd out the end of her veil in the air--then
kiss'd the lilly hand which let it go: the good abbess and Margarita lay
their hand saint-wise upon their breast--look'd up to heaven--then to
they--and look'd 'God bless you, dear sister.'
I declare I be interested in this story, and wish I have be there.
the gardener, whom I shall now call the muleteer, be a little, hearty,
broad-set, good-natured, chatter, top kind of a fellow, who trouble
his head very little with the hows and whens of life; so have mortgage a
month of his conventical wage in a borrachio, or leathern cask of wine,
which he have dispose behind the calesh, with a large russet-colour
riding-coat over it, to guard it from the sun; and as the weather be hot,
and he not a niggard of his labour, walk ten time more than he rode--
he find more occasion than those of nature, to fall back to the rear of
his carriage; till by frequent coming and go, it have so happen'd, that
all his wine have leak'd out at the legal vent of the borrachio, before one
half of the journey be finish'd.
Man be a creature bear to habitude. the day have be sultry--the evening
be delicious--the wine be generous--the burgundian hill on which it grow
be steep--a little tempt bush over the door of a cool cottage at the
foot of it, hang vibrate in full harmony with the passion--a gentle air
rustle distinctly through the leaves--'come--come, thirsty muleteer,--come
in.'
--the muleteer be a son of Adam, I need not say a word more. he give the
mule, each of them, a sound lash, and look in the abbess's and
Margarita's face (as he do it)--as much as to say 'here I am'--he give a
second good crack--as much as to say to his mule, 'get on'--so slinking
behind, he enter'd the little inn at the foot of the hill.
the muleteer, as I tell you, be a little, joyous, chirp fellow, who
think not of to-morrow, nor of what have go before, or what be to
follow it, provide he get but his scantling of Burgundy, and a little
chit-chat along with it; so enter into a long conversation, as how he
be chief gardener to the convent of Andouillets, &c. &c. and out of
friendship for the abbess and Mademoiselle Margarita, who be only in her
noviciate, he have come along with they from the confine of Savoy, &c. &c.-
-and as how she have get a white swelling by her devotion--and what a
nation of herb he have procure to mollify her humour, &c. &c. and that if
the water of Bourbon do not mend that leg--she might as well be lame of
both--&c. &c. &c.--He so contrive his story, as absolutely to forget the
heroine of it--and with she the little novice, and what be a more ticklish
point to be forget than both--the two mule; who be creature that take
advantage of the world, inasmuch as their parent take it of they--and they
not be in a condition to return the obligation downward (as man and
woman and beast are)--they do it side-way, and long-way, and back-ways--
and up hill, and down hill, and which way they can.--philosopher, with all
their ethick, have never consider this rightly--how should the poor
muleteer, then in his cup, consider it at all? he do not in the least--
'tis time we do; let we leave he then in the vortex of his element, the
happy and most thoughtless of mortal man--and for a moment let we look
after the mule, the abbess, and Margarita.
by virtue of the muleteer's two last stroke the mule have go quietly on,
follow their own conscience up the hill, till they have conquer'd about
one half of it; when the elder of they, a shrewd crafty old devil, at the
turn of an angle, give a side glance, and no muleteer behind them,--
by my fig! say she, swearing, Iwill go no far--and if I do, reply the
other, they shall make a drum of my hide.--
and so with one consent they stopp'd thus--

chapter 4.IV.
we be ruin'd and undo, my child, say the abbess to Margarita,--we shall
be here all night--we shall be plunder'd--we shall be ravished--
--We shall be ravish'd, say Margarita, as sure as a gun.
Sancta Maria! cry the abbess (forget the O!)--why be I govern'd by
this wicked stiff joint? why do I leave the convent of andouillet? and
why didst thou not suffer thy servant to go unpolluted to her tomb?
o my finger! my finger! cry the novice, catch fire at the word
servant--why be I not content to put it here, or there, any where rather
than be in this strait?
Strait! say the abbess.
Strait--say the novice; for terror have strike their understanding--the
one know not what she say--the other what she answer'd.
o my virginity! virginity! cry the abbess.
...inity! ...inity! say the novice, sob.
chapter 4.V.
my dear mother, quoth the novice, come a little to herself,--there be
two certain word, which I have be tell will force any horse, or ass, or
mule, to go up a hill whether he will or no; be he never so obstinate or
ill-will'd, the moment he hear they utter'd, he obey. they be word
magic! cry the abbess in the utmost horror--no; reply Margarita calmly-
-but they be word sinful--what be they? quoth the abbess, interrupt
she: they be sinful in the first degree, answer Margarita,--they be
mortal--and if we be ravish and die unabsolved of they, we shall both-
but you may pronounce they to I, quoth the abbess of andouillet--they
cannot, my dear mother, say the novice, be pronounce at all; they will
make all the blood in one's body fly up into one's face--but you may
whisper they in my ear, quoth the abbess.
Heaven! hadst thou no guardian angel to delegate to the inn at the bottom
of the hill? be there no generous and friendly spirit unemployed--no agent
in nature, by some monitory shivering, creep along the artery which lead
to his heart, to rouse the muleteer from his banquet?--no sweet minstrelsy
to bring back the fair idea of the abbess and Margarita, with their black
rosary!
Rouse! rouse!--but 'tis too late--the horrid word be pronounce this
moment--
--and how to tell they--Ye, who can speak of every thing exist, with
unpollute lip--instruct I--guide me--
Chapter 4.VI.
all sin whatever, quoth the abbess, turn casuist in the distress they
be under, be hold by the confessor of our convent to be either mortal or
venial: there be no further division. now a venial sin be the
slight and least of all sin--be halve--by take either only the
half of it, and leave the rest--or, by take it all, and amicably
halve it betwixt yourself and another person--in course become dilute
into no sin at all.
now I see no sin in say, bou, bou, bou, bou, bou, a hundred time
together; nor be there any turpitude in pronounce the syllable ger, ger,
ger, ger, ger, be it from our matin to our vesper: therefore, my dear
daughter, continue the abbess of andouillet--I will say bou, and thou
shalt say ger; and then alternately, as there be no more sin in fou than in
bou--Thou shalt say fou--and I will come in (like fa, sol, la, re, mi, ut,
at our compline) with ter. and accordingly the abbess, give the pitch
note, set off thus:
Abbess,.....) Bou...bou...bou..
Margarita,..) ---ger,..ger,..ger.
Margarita,..) Fou...fou...fou..
Abbess,.....) ---ter,..ter,..ter.
the two mule acknowledge the note by a mutual lash of their tail; but
it go no further--'twill answer by an' by, say the novice.
Abbess,.....) Bou. bou. bou. bou. bou. bou.
Margarita,..) ---ger, ger, ger, ger, ger, ger.
Quicker still, cry Margarita. Fou, fou, fou, fou, fou, fou, fou, fou,
fou.
Quicker still, cry Margarita. Bou, bou, bou, bou, bou, bou, bou, bou,
bou.
Quicker still--God preserve I; say the abbess--they do not understand we,
cry Margarita--but the Devil do, say the abbess of andouillet.
Chapter 4.VII.
what a tract of country have I run!--how many degree near to the warm
sun be I advance, and how many fair and goodly city have I see, during
the time you have be read and reflect, Madam, upon this story! 
therebe Fontainbleau, and Sens, and Joigny, and Auxerre, and Dijon the
capital of Burgundy, and Challon, and Macon the capital of the Maconese,
and a score more upon the road to Lyons--and now I have run they over--I
might as well talk to you of so many market town in the moon, as tell you
one word about they: it will be this chapter at the least, if not both
this and the next entirely lost, do what I will--
--Why, 'tis a strange story! Tristram.
Alas! Madam, have it be upon some melancholy lecture of the cross--the
peace of meekness, or the contentment of resignation--I have not be
incommode: or have I think of write it upon the purer abstraction of
the soul, and that food of wisdom and holiness and contemplation, upon
which the spirit of man (when separate from the body) be to subsist for
ever--you would have come with a well appetite from it--
--I wish I never have write it: but as I never blot any thing out--let we
use some honest mean to get it out of our head directly.
--Pray reach I my fool's cap--I fear you sit upon it, Madam--'tis under
the cushion--i'll put it on--
bless I! you have have it upon your head this half hour.--there then let it
stay, with a

chapter 4.viii.
--all you need say of Fontainbleau (in case you be ask'd) be, that it
stand about forty mile (south something) from Paris, in the middle of a
large forest--that there be something great in it--that the king go there
once every two or three year, with his whole court, for the pleasure of
the chace--and that, during that carnival of sporting, any english
gentleman of fashion (you need not forget yourself) may be accommodate
with a nag or two, to partake of the sport, take care only not to out-
gallop the king--
though there be two reason why you need not talk loud of this to every
one.
first, because 'twill make the say nag the hard to be get; and
secondly, 'tis not a word of it true.--allon!
as for sen--you may dispatch--in a word--''ti an archiepiscopal see.'
--For joigny--the less, I think, one say of it the well.
but for Auxerre--I could go on for ever: for in my grand tour through
Europe, in which, after all, my father (not care to trust I with any
one) attend I himself, with my uncle Toby, and Trim, and Obadiah, and
indeed most of the family, except my mother, who be take up with a
project of knit my father a pair of large worsted breeches--(the thing
be common sense)--and she not care to be put out of her way, she staid at
home, at Shandy Hall, to keep thing right during the expedition; in which,
I say, my father stop we two day at Auxerre, and his research be
ever of such a nature, that they would have find fruit even in a desert--
he have leave I enough to say upon Auxerre: in short, wherever my father
go--but 'twa more remarkably so, in this journey through France and
Italy, than in any other stage of his life--his road seem to lie so much
on one side of that, wherein all other traveller have go before he--he
see king and court and silk of all colour, in such strange light--and
his remark and reasoning upon the character, the manner, and custom of
the country we pass'd over, be so opposite to those of all other mortal
man, particularly those of my uncle Toby and Trim--(to say nothing of
myself)--and to crown all--the occurrence and scrape which we be
perpetually meet and get into, in consequence of his system and
opiniotry--they be of so odd, so mix'd and tragi-comical a contexture--
that the whole put together, it appear of so different a shade and tint
from any tour of Europe, which be ever execute--that I will venture to
pronounce--the fault must be mine and mine only--if it be not read by all
traveller and travel-reader, till travel be no more,--or which come
to the same point--till the world, finally, take it into its head to stand
still.--
--But this rich bale be not to be open'd now; except a small thread or two
of it, merely to unravel the mystery of my father's stay at Auxerre.
--as I have mention it--'tis too slight to be keep suspend; and when
'tis wove in, there be an end of it.
wewill go, brother Toby, say my father, whilst dinner be coddle--to the
abbey of Saint Germain, if it be only to see these body, of which
Monsieur Sequier have give such a recommendation.--i'll go see any body,
quoth my uncle Toby; for he be all compliance through every step of the
journey--defend I! say my father--they be all mummy--then one need not
shave; quoth my uncle Toby--Shave! no--cry my father--'twill be more like
relation to go with our beard on--so out we sally, the corporal lending
his master his arm, and bring up the rear, to the abbey of Saint
Germain.
every thing be very fine, and very rich, and very superb, and very
magnificent, say my father, address himself to the sacristan, who be a
young brother of the order of Benedictines--but our curiosity have lead we
to see the body, of which Monsieur Sequier have give the world so exact a
description.--The sacristan make a bow, and light a torch first, which
he have always in the vestry ready for the purpose; he lead we into the tomb
of St. Heribald--this, say the sacristan, lay his hand upon the tomb,
be a renowned prince of the house of Bavaria, who under the successive
reign of Charlemagne, Louis le Debonnair, and Charles the Bald, bear a
great sway in the government, and have a principal hand in bring every
thing into order and discipline--
then he have be as great, say my uncle, in the field, as in the cabinet--
I dare say he have be a gallant soldier--he be a monk--say the
sacristan.
my uncle Toby and Trim seek comfort in each other's face--but find it
not: my father clap both his hand upon his cod-piece, which be a way
he have when any thing hugely tickle he: for though he hat a monk and
the very smell of a monk bad than all the devil in hell--yet the shot
hit my uncle Toby and Trim so much hard than he, 'twa a relative
triumph; and put he into the gay humour in the world.
--and pray what do you call this gentleman? quoth my father, rather
sportingly: this tomb, say the young Benedictine, look downward,
contain the bone of Saint Maxima, who come from Ravenna on purpose to
touch the body--
--Of Saint Maximus, say my father, pop in with his saint before him,--
they be two of the great saint in the whole martyrology, add my
father--Excuse I, say the sacristan--'twa to touch the bone of Saint
Germain, the builder of the abbey--and what do she get by it? say my
uncle Toby--what do any woman get by it? say my father--Martyrdome;
reply the young Benedictine, make a bow down to the ground, and
utter the word with so humble, but decisive a cadence, it disarm my
father for a moment. 'Tis suppose, continue the Benedictine, that St.
Maxima have lie in this tomb four hundred year, and two hundred before her
canonization--'tis but a slow rise, brother Toby, quoth my father, in this
self-same army of martyrs.--A desperate slow one, an' please your honour,
say Trim, unless one could purchase--I should rather sell out entirely,
quoth my uncle Toby--I be pretty much of your opinion, brother Toby, say
my father.
--Poor St. Maxima! say my uncle Toby low to himself, as we turn'd from her
tomb: she be one of the fair and most beautiful lady either of Italy
or France, continue the sacristan--but who the duce have get lain down
here, besides she? quoth my father, point with his cane to a large tomb
as we walk on--it be Saint Optat, Sir, answer the sacristan--and
properly be Saint Optat plac'd! say my father: and what be Saint Optat's
story? continue he. Saint Optat, reply the sacristan, be a bishop--
--I think so, by heaven! cry my father, interrupt he--Saint optat!-
-how should Saint Optat fail? so snatch out his pocket-book, and the
young Benedictine hold he the torch as he write, he set it down as a
new prop to his system of christian name, and I will be bold to say, so
disintereste be he in the search of truth, that have he find a treasure
in Saint Optat's tomb, it would not have make he half so rich: 'twas as
successful a short visit as ever be pay to the dead; and so highly be
his fancy pleas'd with all that have pass in it,--that he determine at
once to stay another day in Auxerre.
--i'll see the rest of these good gentry to-morrow, say my father, as we
cross'd over the square--and while you be pay that visit, brother
Shandy, quoth my uncle Toby--the corporal and I will mount the rampart.
chapter 4.IX.
--Now this be the most puzzled skein of all--for in this last chapter, as
far at least as it have help'd I through Auxerre, I have be get
forwards in two different journie together, and with the same dash of the
pen--for I have get entirely out of Auxerre in this journey which I be
write now, and I be get half way out of Auxerre in that which I shall
write hereafter--there be but a certain degree of perfection in every
thing; and by push at something beyond that, I have bring myself into
such a situation, as no traveller ever stand before I; for I be this
moment walk across the market-place of Auxerre with my father and my
uncle Toby, in our way back to dinner--and I be this moment also enter
Lyons with my post-chaise break into a thousand piece--and I be moreover
this moment in a handsome pavillion build by Pringello (the same Don
Pringello, the celebrate spanish architect, of whom my cousin Antony have
make such honourable mention in a scholium to the Tale inscribe to his
name. Vid. p.129, small edit.), upon the bank of the Garonne, which Mons.
Sligniac have lend I, and where I now sit rhapsodise all these affair.
--let I collect myself, and pursue my journey.
chapter 4.x.
I be glad of it, say I, settle the account with myself, as I walk'd into
Lyons--my chaise be all lay higgledy-piggledy with my baggage in a
cart, which be move slowly before I--I be heartily glad, say I, that
'tis all break to piece; for now I can go directly by water to Avignon,
which will carry I on a hundred and twenty mile of my journey, and not
cost I seven livre--and from thence, continue I, bring forwards the
account, I can hire a couple of mule--or ass, if I like, (for nobody
know I,) and cross the plain of Languedoc for almost nothing--I shall
gain four hundred livre by the misfortune clear into my purse: and
pleasure! worth--worth double the money by it. with what velocity,
continue I, clap my two hand together, shall I fly down the rapid
Rhone, with the Vivares on my right hand, and Dauphiny on my left, scarce
see the ancient city of Vienne, Valence, and viviere. what a flame
will it rekindle in the lamp, to snatch a blushing grape from the Hermitage
and Cote roti, as I shoot by the foot of they! and what a fresh spring in
the blood! to behold upon the bank advance and retire, the castle of
romance, whence courteous knight have whilome rescue the distress'd--and
see vertiginous, the rock, the mountain, the cataract, and all the hurry
which nature be in with all her great work about she.
as I go on thus, methought my chaise, the wreck of which look'd stately
enough at the first, insensibly grow less and less in its size; the
freshness of the painting be no more--the gilding lose its lustre--and the
whole affair appear so poor in my eye--so sorry!--so contemptible! and,
in a word, so much bad than the abbess of andouillet' itself--that I be
just open my mouth to give it to the devil--when a pert vamp chaise-
undertaker, step nimbly across the street, demand if Monsieur would
have his chaise refit--no, no, say I, shake my head sideways--would
Monsieur choose to sell it? rejoin the undertaker--with all my soul, say
I--the iron work be worth forty livre--and the glass worth forty more--
and the leather you may take to live on.
what a mine of wealth, quoth I, as he count I the money, have this post-
chaise bring I in? and this be my usual method of book-keeping, at
least with the disaster of life--make a penny of every one of them as
they happen to me--
--Do, my dear Jenny, tell the world for I, how I behave under one, the
most oppressive of its kind, which could befal I as a man, proud as he
ought to be of his manhood--
'tis enough, saidst thou, come close up to I, as I stand with my garter
in my hand, reflect upon what have not pass'd--'tis enough, Tristram, and
I be satisfied, saidst thou, whisper these word in my ear, .... .. ....
... ......;--...... ...--any other man would have sink down to the centre--
--Every thing be good for something, quoth I.
--i'll go into Wales for six week, and drink goat's whey--and Iwill gain
seven year long life for the accident. for which reason I think myself
inexcusable, for blame Fortune so often as I have do, for pelt I
all my life long, like an ungracious duchess, as I call'd she, with so many
small evil: surely, if I have any cause to be angry with she, 'tis that
she have not send I great one--a score of good curse, bounce loss,
would have be as good as a pension to I.
--one of a hundred a year, or so, be all I wish--I would not be at the
plague of pay land-tax for a large.
Chapter 4.XI.
to those who call vexation, vexation, as know what they be, there
could not be a great, than to be the good part of a day at Lyons, the
most opulent and flourish city in France, enrich with the most
fragment of antiquity--and not be able to see it. to be withhold upon any
account, must be a vexation; but to be withhold by a vexation--must
certainly be, what philosophy justly call 
Vexation 
upon 
Vexation.
I have get my two dish of milk coffee (which by the bye be excellently
good for a consumption, but you must boil the milk and coffee together--
otherwise 'tis only coffee and milk)--and as it be no more than eight in
the morning, and the boat do not go off till noon, I have time to see
enough of Lyons to tire the patience of all the friend I have in the world
with it. I will take a walk to the cathedral, say I, look at my list,
and see the wonderful mechanism of this great clock of Lippius of Basil, in
the first place--
now, of all thing in the world, I understand the least of mechanism--I
have neither genius, or taste, or fancy--and have a brain so entirely unapt
for every thing of that kind, that I solemnly declare I be never yet able
to comprehend the principle of motion of a squirrel cage, or a common
knife-grinder's wheel--tho' I have many an hour of my life look'd up with
great devotion at the one--and stand by with as much patience as any
christian ever could do, at the other--
Iwill go see the surprising movement of this great clock, say I, the very
first thing I do: and then I will pay a visit to the great library of the
Jesuits, and procure, if possible, a sight of the thirty volume of the
general history of China, write (not in the tartarean, but) in the chinese
language, and in the chinese character too.
now I almost know as little of the chinese language, as I do of the
mechanism of Lippius's clock-work; so, why these should have jostle
themselves into the two first article of my list--I leave to the curious
as a problem of Nature. I own it look like one of her ladyship's
obliquity; and they who court she, be interested in find out her
humour as much as I.
when these curiosity be see, quoth I, half address myself to my
valet de place, who stand behind me--'twill be no hurt if we go to the
church of St. Irenaeus, and see the pillar to which Christ be tie--and
after that, the house where Pontius Pilate lived--'twa at the next town,
say the valet de place--at Vienne; I be glad of it, say I, rise briskly
from my chair, and walk across the room with stride twice as long as my
usual pace--'for so much the soon shall I be at the Tomb of the two
lover.'
what be the cause of this movement, and why I take such long stride in
utter this--I might leave to the curious too; but as no principle of
clock-work be concern in it--'twill be as well for the reader if I
explain it myself.
Chapter 4.xii.
o! there be a sweet aera in the life of man, when (the brain be tender
and fibrillous, and more like pap than any thing else)--a story read of two
fond lover, separate from each other by cruel parent, and by still more
cruel destiny--
Amandus--he
Amanda--She--
each ignorant of the other's course,
he--east
she--west
Amandus take captive by the Turks, and carry to the emperor of Morocco's
court, where the princess of Morocco fall in love with he, keep he
twenty year in prison for the love of his Amanda.--
She--(Amanda) all the time wander barefoot, and with dishevell'd hair,
o'er rock and mountain, enquire for Amandus!--Amandus! amandus!--make
every hill and valley to echo back his name--
Amandus! Amandus!
at every town and city, sit down forlorn at the gate--have Amandus!--has
my Amandus enter'd?--till,--goe round, and round, and round the world--
chance unexpected bring they at the same moment of the night, though by
different way, to the gate of Lyons, their native city, and each in well-
know accent call out aloud,
be Amandus / be my Amanda still alive?
they fly into each other's arm, and both drop down dead for joy.
there be a soft aera in every gentle mortal's life, where such a story
afford more pabulum to the brain, than all the Frusts, and crust, and
Rusts of antiquity, which traveller can cook up for it.
--'twa all that stick on the right side of the cullender in my own, of
what Spon and other, in their account of Lyons, have strain into it; and
finding, moreover, in some Itinerary, but in what God know--that sacred to
the fidelity of Amandus and Amanda, a tomb be build without the gate,
where, to this hour, lover call upon they to attest their truth--I
never could get into a scrape of that kind in my life, but this tomb of the
lover would, somehow or other, come in at the close--nay such a kind of
empire have it establish'd over I, that I could seldom think or speak of
Lyons--and sometimes not so much as see even a Lyons-waistcoat, but this
remnant of antiquity would present itself to my fancy; and I have often
say in my wild way of run on--tho' I fear with some irreverence--'I
think this shrine (neglect as it be) as valuable as that of Mecca, and
so little short, except in wealth, of the Santa Casa itself, that some time
or other, I would go a pilgrimage (though I have no other business at Lyons)
on purpose to pay it a visit.'
in my list, therefore, of Videnda at Lyons, this, tho' last,--wa not, you
see, least; so take a dozen or two of long stride than usual cross my
room, just whilst it pass my brain, I walk down calmly into the basse
cour, in order to sally forth; and have call for my bill--as it be
uncertain whether I should return to my inn, I have pay it--have moreover
give the maid ten sous, and be just receive the derni compliment of
Monsieur Le Blanc, for a pleasant voyage down the Rhone--when I be stop
at the gate--
chapter 4.xiii.
--'twa by a poor ass, who have just turn in with a couple of large
pannier upon his back, to collect eleemosynary turnip-top and cabbage-
leaf; and stand dubious, with his two fore-foot on the inside of the
threshold, and with his two hinder foot towards the street, as not know
very well whether he be to go in or no.
now, 'tis an animal (be in what hurry I may) I cannot bear to strike--there
be a patient endurance of suffering, write so unaffectedly in his look
and carriage, which plead so mightily for he, that it always disarm I;
and to that degree, that I do not like to speak unkindly to he: on the
contrary, meet he where I will--whether in town or country--in cart or
under pannier--whether in liberty or bondage--I have ever something civil
to say to he on my part; and as one word beget another (if he have as
little to do as I)--I generally fall into conversation with he; and surely
never be my imagination so busy as in frame his response from the
etching of his countenance--and where those carry I not deep enough--in
fly from my own heart into his, and see what be natural for an ass to
think--as well as a man, upon the occasion. in truth, it be the only
creature of all the class of being below I, with whom I can do this: 
for parrot, jackdaw, &c.--I never exchange a word with they--nor with the
ape, &c. for pretty near the same reason; they act by rote, as the other
speak by it, and equally make I silent: nay my dog and my cat, though I
value they both--(and for my dog he would speak if he could)--yet somehow
or other, they neither of they possess the talent for conversation--I can
make nothing of a discourse with they, beyond the proposition, the reply,
and rejoinder, which terminate my father's and my mother's conversation,
in his bed of justice--and those utter'd--therebe an end of the dialogue--
--But with an ass, I can commune for ever.
come, Honesty! say i,--seee it be impracticable to pass betwixt he and
the gate--art thou for come in, or go out?
the ass twist his head round to look up the street--
Well--reply I--we'll wait a minute for thy driver:
--He turn his head thoughtful about, and look wistfully the opposite
way--
I understand thee perfectly, answer I--if thou tak a wrong step in
this affair, he will cudgel thee to death--well! a minute be but a minute,
and if it save a fellow-creature a drubbing, it shall not be set down as
ill-spend.
he be eat the stem of an artichoke as this discourse go on, and in
the little peevish contention of nature betwixt hunger and unsavouriness,
have dropt it out of his mouth half a dozen time, and pick'd it up again--
God help thee, Jack! say I, thou hast a bitter breakfast on't--and many a
bitter day's labour,--and many a bitter blow, I fear, for its wages--'ti
all--all bitterness to thee, whatever life be to others.--And now thy
mouth, if one know the truth of it, be as bitter, I dare say, as soot--(for
he have cast aside the stem) and thou hast not a friend perhaps in all this
world, that will give thee a macaroon.--In say this, I pull'd out a
paper of them, which I have just purchase, and give he one--and at this
moment that I be tell it, my heart smite I, that there be more of
pleasantry in the conceit, of see how an ass would eat a macaroon--than
of benevolence in give he one, which preside in the act.
when the ass have eat his macaroon, I press'd he to come in--the poor
beast be heavy loaded--his leg seem'd to tremble under he--he hang
rather backwards, and as I pull'd at his halter, it break short in my hand-

will, you may'--if I do, say I, Iwill be d....d.
the word be but one-half of it pronounce, like the abbess of

out upon it!

Chapter 4.XIV.
when all be set to right, I come down stair again into the basse cour

lover, &c.--and be a second time stopp'd at the gate--not by the ass--but
by the person who strike he; and who, by that time, have take possession
(as be not uncommon after a defeat) of the very spot of ground where the
ass stand.
it be a commissary send to I from the post-office, with a rescript in his
hand for the payment of some six livre odd sous.
upon what account? say i.--'ti upon the part of the king, reply the
commissary, heave up both his shoulders--
--My good friend, quoth I--as sure as I be I--and you be you--
--And who be you? say he.--don't puzzle I; say I.
Chapter 4.XV.
--but it be an indubitable verity, continue I, address myself to the
commissary, change only the form of my asseveration--that I owe the king
of France nothing but my good will; for he be a very honest man, and I wish
he all health and pastime in the world--
Pardonnez moi--reply the commissary, you be indebted to he six livre
four sous, for the next post from hence to St. Fons, in your route to
Avignon--which be a post royal, you pay double for the horse and
postillion--otherwise 'twould have amount to no more than three livre
two sous--
--But I donot go by land; say I.
--You may if you please; reply the commissary--
your most obedient servant--say I, make he a low bow--
the commissary, with all the sincerity of grave good breeding--make I one,
as low again.--I never be more disconcerted with a bow in my life.
--The devil take the serious character of these people! quoth i--(aside)
they understand no more of Irony than this--
the comparison be stand close by with his pannier--but something

Sir, say I, collect myself--it be not my intention to take post--
--But you may--say he, persist in his first reply--you may take post if
you chuse--
--And I may take salt to my pickle herring, say I, if I chuse--
--But I do not chuse--
--But you must pay for it, whether you do or no.
Aye! for the salt; say I (I know)--
--And for the post too; add he. defend I! cry i--
I travel by water--I be go down the Rhone this very afternoon--my

C'est tout egal--'tis all one; say he.
Bon Dieu! what, pay for the way I go! and for the way I do not go!
--c'est tout egal; reply the commissary--
--The devil it be! say I--but I will go to ten thousand Bastiles first--
o England! England! thou land of liberty, and climate of good sense, thou
tender of mother--and gentle of nurse, cry I, kneel upon one

when the director of Madam Le Blanc's conscience come in at that instant,

-ask'd, if I stand in want of the aid of the church--
I go by Water--say I--and herebe another will be for make I pay for
go by Oil.
Chapter 4.XVI.
as I perceive the commissary of the post-office would have his six livre
four sous, I have nothing else for it, but to say some smart thing upon the
occasion, worth the money:
and so I set off thus:--
--And pray, Mr. Commissary, by what law of courtesy be a defenceless
stranger to be use just the reverse from what you use a Frenchman in this
matter?
by no mean; say he.
excuse I; say I--for you have begin, Sir, with first tear off my
breech-and now you want my pocket--
Whereas--have you first take my pocket, as you do with your own people--and
then leave I bare a..'d after--I have be a beast to have complain'd--
as it is--
--'tis contrary to the law of nature.
--'tis contrary to reason.
--'tis contrary to the Gospel.
but not to this--say he--put a print paper into my hand,
Par le Roy.
--'tis a pithy prolegomenon, quoth I--and so read on. . ..
--by all which it appear, quoth I, have read it over, a little too
rapidly, that if a man set out in a post-chaise from Paris--he must go on
travel in one, all the day of his life--or pay for it.--excuse I,
say the commissary, the spirit of the ordinance be this--that if you set
out with an intention of run post from Paris to Avignon, &c. you shall
not change that intention or mode of travel, without first satisfy
the fermier for two post far than the place you repent at--and 'tis
found, continue he, upon this, that the revenue be not to fall short
through your fickleness--
--O by heaven! cry I--if fickleness be taxable in France--we have
nothing to do but to make the good peace with you we can--
and so the Peace be make;
--and if it be a bad one--as Tristram Shandy lay the corner-stone of it--
nobody but Tristram Shandy ought to be hang.
chapter 4.xvii.
though I be sensible I have say as many clever thing to the commissary

my hand into my coat-pocket for my remarks--(which, by the bye, may be a

Heaven! earth! sea! fire! cry I, call in every thing to my aid but

you dropp'd a good many very singular one; reply he--Pugh! say I, those

Sancho Panca, when he lose his ass's furniture, do not exclaim more
bitterly.
chapter 4.xviii.
when the first transport be over, and the register of the brain be

Chapter 4.XIX.
when we arrive at the chaise-vamper's house, both the house and the shop
be shut up; it be the eighth of September, the nativity of the bless
Virgin Mary, mother of God--
--Tantarra-ra-tan-tivi--the whole world be go out a may-poling--frisk
here--caper there--no body care a button for I or my remark; so I sit
I down upon a bench by the door, philosophate upon my condition: by a
well fate than usually attend I, I have not wait half an hour, when
the mistress come in to take the papilliote from off her hair, before she
go to the May-poles--
the french woman, by the bye, love May-pole, a la folie--that is, as much
as their matin--give them but a May-pole, whether in May, June, July or
September--they never count the time--down it goes--'tis meat, drink,
washing, and lodge to them--and have we but the policy, an' please your
worship (as wood be a little scarce in France), to send they but plenty of
May-poles--
the woman would set they up; and when they have do, they would dance round
they (and the man for company) till they be all blind.
the wife of the chaise-vamper stepp'd in, I tell you, to take the
papilliote from off her hair--the toilet stand still for no man--so she
jerk'd off her cap, to begin with they as she open'd the door, in do
which, one of they fall upon the ground--I instantly see it be my own
writing--
O Seigneur! cry I--you have get all my remark upon your head, Madam!--
J'en suis bien mortifiee, say she--'tis well, think I, they have stick
there--for could they have go deep, they would have make such confusion
in a french woman's noddle--she have well have go with it unfrizle, to
the day of eternity.
Tenez--say she--so without any idea of the nature of my suffering, she
take they from her curl, and put they gravely one by one into my hat--one
be twist this way--another twist that--ey! by my faith; and when they
be publish, quoth I,--
they will be bad twisted still.
chapter 4.XX.
and now for Lippius's clock! say I, with the air of a man, who have get

--And so away I post to the college of the Jesuits.
now it be with the project of get a peep at the history of China in

for all the Jesuits have get the cholic--and to that degree, as never be

chapter 4.xxii.
no matter how, or in what mood--but I fly from the tomb of the lover--or

Prithee, friend, say I, take hold of my mule for a moment--for I want to
