--But Monsieur le Marquis have walk in--
chapter 4.xxiii.
I have now the whole south of France, from the bank of the Rhone to those
of the Garonne, to traverse upon my mule at my own leisure--at my own
leisure--for I have leave death, the Lord know--and he only--how far behind
me--'I have follow many a man thro' France, quoth he--but never at this
mettlesome rate.'--Still he followed,--and still I flee he--but I flee he
cheerfully--still he pursue--but, like one who pursue his prey without
hope--as he lagg'd, every step he lose, soften his look--why should I
fly he at this rate?
so notwithstanding all the commissary of the post-office have say, I
change the mode of my travel once more; and, after so precipitate and
rattle a course as I have run, I flatter my fancy with thinking of my
mule, and that I should traverse the rich plain of Languedoc upon his
back, as slowly as foot could fall.
there be nothing more pleasing to a traveller--or more terrible to travel-
writer, than a large rich plain; especially if it be without great river
or bridge; and present nothing to the eye, but one unvaried picture of
plenty: for after they have once tell you, that 'tis delicious! or
delightful! (as the case happens)--that the soil be grateful, and that
nature pour out all her abundance, &c. . .they have then a large plain
upon their hand, which they know not what to do with--and which be of
little or no use to they but to carry they to some town; and that town,
perhaps of little more, but a new place to start from to the next plain--
and so on.
--This be most terrible work; judge if I donot manage my plain well.
Chapter 4.XXIV.
I have not go above two league and a half, before the man with his gun
begin to look at his priming.
I have three several time loiter'd terribly behind; half a mile at least
every time; once, in deep conference with a drum-maker, who be make
drum for the fair of Baucaira and Tarascone--I do not understand the
principles--
the second time, I cannot so properly say, I stopp'd--for meet a couple
of franciscan straiten more for time than myself, and not be able to
get to the bottom of what I be about--I have turn'd back with them--
the third, be an affair of trade with a gossip, for a hand-basket of
provence fig for four sous; this would have be transact at once; but
for a case of conscience at the close of it; for when the fig be pay
for, it turn'd out, that there be two dozen of egg cover over with
vine-leaf at the bottom of the basket--as I have no intention of buy
egg--I make no sort of claim of they--as for the space they have occupied--
what signify it? I have fig enow for my money--
--But it be my intention to have the basket--it be the gossip's intention
to keep it, without which, she could do nothing with her egg--and unless I
have the basket, I could do as little with my fig, which be too ripe
already, and most of them burst at the side: this bring on a short
contention, which terminate in sundry proposal, what we should both do--
--how we dispose of our egg and fig, I defy you, or the Devil himself,
have he not be there (which I be persuade he be), to form the least
probable conjecture: you will read the whole of it--not this year, for I
be hasten to the story of my uncle Toby's amour--but you will read it
in the collection of those which have arise out of the journey across this
plain--and which, therefore, I call my
Plain Stories.
how far my pen have be fatigue, like those of other traveller, in this
journey of it, over so barren a track--the world must judge--but the trace
of it, which be now all set o' vibrate together this moment, tell I 'tis
the most fruitful and busy period of my life; for as I have make no
convention with my man with the gun, as to time--by stop and talk to
every soul I meet, who be not in a full trot--join all party before
I--wait for every soul behind--hail all those who be come
through cross-road--arrest all kind of beggar, pilgrim, fiddler,
friar--not pass by a woman in a mulberry-tree without commend her
leg, and tempt she into conversation with a pinch of snuff--in short,
by seize every handle, of what size or shape soever, which chance hold
out to I in this journey--I turn my plain into a city--I be always in
company, and with great variety too; and as my mule love society as much
as myself, and have some proposal always on his part to offer to every
beast he meet--I be confident we could have pass through Pall-Mall, or St.
James's-Street, for a month together, with few adventure--and see less
of human nature.
o! there be that sprightly frankness, which at once unpin every plait of a
Languedocian's dress--that whatever be beneath it, it look so like the
simplicity which poet sing of in well day--I will delude my fancy, and
believe it be so.
'twa in the road betwixt Nismes and Lunel, where there be the good
Muscatto wine in all France, and which by the bye belong to the honest
canon of Montpellier--and foul befal the man who have drink it at their
table, who grudge they a drop of it.
--The sun be set--they have do their work; the nymph have tie up their
hair afresh--and the swain be prepare for a carousal--my mule make a
dead point--'tis the fife and tabourin, say I--i'm frighten'd to death,
quoth he--they be run at the ring of pleasure, say I, give he a
prick--by saint Boogar, and all the saint at the backside of the door of
purgatory, say he--(making the same resolution with the abbesse of
Andouillets) Iwill not go a step further--'tis very well, sir, say I--I
never will argue a point with one of your family, as long as I live; so
leap off his back, and kick off one boot into this ditch, and t'other
into that--i'll take a dance, say I--so stay you here.
a sun-burn daughter of Labour rise up from the groupe to meet I, as I
advance towards they; her hair, which be a dark chesnut approach
rather to a black, be tie up in a knot, all but a single tress.
we want a cavalier, say she, hold out both her hand, as if to offer
they--and a cavalier ye shall have; say I, take hold of both of they.
hadst thou, Nannette, be array'd like a duchesse!
--But that curse slit in thy petticoat!
Nannette care not for it.
we could not have do without you, say she, let go one hand, with
self-teach politeness, lead I up with the other.
a lame youth, whom Apollo have recompense with a pipe, and to which he have
add a tabourin of his own accord, run sweetly over the prelude, as he sit
upon the bank--tie I up this tress instantly, say Nannette, put a
piece of string into my hand--it teach I to forget I be a stranger--the
whole knot fall down--we have be seven year acquaint.
the youth strike the note upon the tabourin--his pipe follow, and off we
bounded--'the duce take that slit!'
the sister of the youth, who have steal her voice from heaven, sung
alternately with she brother--'twa a Gascoigne roundelay.
Viva la Joia!
Fidon la Tristessa!
the nymph join'd in unison, and their swain an octave below them--
I would have give a crown to have it sew'd up--Nannette would not have
give a sous--Viva la joia! be in her lip--Viva la joia! be in her eye. 
a transient spark of amity shoot across the space betwixt we--she look'd
amiable!--why could I not live, and end my day thus? just Disposer of our
joy and sorrow, cry I, why could not a man sit down in the lap of
content here--and dance, and sing, and say his prayer, and go to heaven
with this nut-brown maid? capriciously do she bend her head on one side,
and dance up insidious--then 'tis time to dance off, quoth I; so change
only partner and tune, I dance it away from Lunel to Montpellier--from
thence to Pescnas, Beziers--I dance it along through Narbonne, Carcasson,
and Castle Naudairy, till at last I dance myself into Perdrillo's
pavillion, where pull out a paper of black line, that I might go on
straight forwards, without digression or parenthesis, in my uncle Toby's
amours--
I begin thus--
Chapter 4.xxv.
--But softly--for in these sportive plain, and under this genial sun,
where at this instant all flesh be run out piping, fiddle, and
dance to the vintage, and every step thatbe take, the judgment be
surprise by the imagination, I defy, notwithstanding all that have be
say upon straight line (Vid. Vol. III.) in sundry page of my book--I
defy the good cabbage planter that ever exist, whether he plant
backwards or forwards, it make little difference in the account (except
that he will have more to answer for in the one case than in the other)--i
defy he to go on coolly, critically, and canonically, plant his
cabbage one by one, in straight line, and stoical distance, especially
if slit in petticoat be unsew'd up--without ever and anon straddling
out, or sidle into some bastardly digression--in freeze-land, Fog-land,
and some other land I wot of--it may be done--
but in this clear climate of fantasy and perspiration, where every idea,
sensible and insensible, get vent--in this land, my dear Eugenius--in this
fertile land of chivalry and romance, where I now sit, unskrewe my ink-
horn to write my uncle Toby's amour, and with all the meander of Julia's
track in quest of her Diego, in full view of my study window--if thou
com not and takest I by the hand--
what a work it be likely to turn out!
let we begin it.
chapter 4.xxvi.
it be with Love as with cuckoldom--
but now I be talk of begin a book, and have long have a thing upon my
mind to be impart to the reader, which, if not impart now, can never be
impart to he as long as I live (whereas the Comparison may be impart
to he any hour in the day)--i'll just mention it, and begin in good
earnest.
the thing be this.
that of all the several way of begin a book which be now in practice
throughout the known world, I be confident my own way of do it be the
good--i'm sure it be the most religious--for I begin with write the first
sentence--and trust to Almighty God for the second.
'twould cure an author for ever of the fuss and folly of open his
street-door, and call in his neighbour and friend, and kinsfolk, with
the devil and all his imp, with their hammer and engine, &c. only to
observe how one sentence of mine follow another, and how the plan follow
the whole.
I wish you see I half start out of my chair, with what confidence, as I
grasp the elbow of it, I look up--catch the idea, even sometimes before
it half way reach me--
I believe in my conscience I intercept many a thought which heaven intend
for another man.
Pope and his portrait (Vid. Pope's Portrait.) be fool to I--no martyr be
ever so full of faith or fire--I wish I could say of good work too--but I
have no
Zeal or Anger--or
Anger or zeal--
and till god and man agree together to call it by the same name--the

Chapter 4.XXVII.
--Bon jour!--good morrow!--so you have get your cloak on betimes!--but 'tis
a cold morning, and you judge the matter rightly--'tis well to be well
mount, than go o' foot--and obstruction in the gland be dangerous--and
how go it with thy concubine--thy wife,--and thy little one o' both
side? and when do you hear from the old gentleman and lady--your sister,
aunt, uncle, and cousin--I hope they have get well of their cold,
cough, clap, tooth-ache, fever, strangurie, sciatica, swelling, and
sore eye.
--What a devil of an apothecary! to take so much blood--give such a vile
purge--puke--poultice--plaister--night-draught--clyster--blister?--And why
so many grain of calomel? santa Maria! and such a dose of opium! peri-
clitating, pardi! the whole family of ye, from head to tail--by my great-
aunt Dinah's old black velvet mask! I think there be no occasion for it.
now this be a little bald about the chin, by frequently put off and
on, before she be get with child by the coachman--not one of our family
would wear it after. to cover the Mask afresh, be more than the mask be
worth--and to wear a mask which be bald, or which could be half see
through, be as bad as have no mask at all--
this be the reason, may it please your reverence, that in all our numerous
family, for these four generation, we count no more than one archbishop, a
Welch judge, some three or four aldermen, and a single mountebank--
in the sixteenth century, we boast of no less than a dozen alchymist.
chapter 4.xxviii.
'it be with Love as with cuckoldom'--the suffer party be at least the

of all mortal, and immortal man too, if you please, who ever soliloquize

chapter 4.xxix.
why weaver, gardener, and gladiator--or a man with a pined leg
(proceed from some ailment in the foot)--should ever have have some
tender nymph break her heart in secret for they, be point well and
duly settle and account for, by ancient and modern physiologist.
a water-drinker, provide he be a profess'd one, and do it without fraud
or covin, be precisely in the same predicament: not that, at first sight,
there be any consequence, or show of logic in it, 'that a rill of cold
water dribble through my inward part, should light up a torch in my
Jenny's--'
--the proposition do not strike one; on the contrary, it seem to run
opposite to the natural working of cause and effects--
but it shews the weakness and imbecility of human reason.
--'and in perfect good health with it?'
--the most perfect,--Madam, that friendship herself could wish me--
'and drink nothing!--nothing but water?'
--impetuous fluid! the moment thou press against the flood-gate of the
brain--see how they give way!--
in swim Curiosity, beckon to her damsel to follow--they dive into the
center of the current--
Fancy sit muse upon the bank, and with her eye follow the stream,
turn straw and bulrushe into mast and bow-sprit--and Desire, with vest
hold up to the knee in one hand, snatch at they, as they swim by she,
with the other--
o ye water drinker! be it then by this delusive fountain, that ye have so
often govern and turn'd this world about like a mill-wheel--grind the
face of the impotent--bepowdere their rib--bepeppere their nose, and
change sometimes even the very frame and face of nature--
if I be you, quoth Yorick, I would drink more water, Eugenius--and, if I
be you, Yorick, reply Eugenius, so would I.
which shew they have both read Longinus--
for my own part, I be resolve never to read any book but my own, as long
as I live.
chapter 4.xxx.
I wish my uncle Toby have be a water-drinker; for then the thing have be
account for, that the first moment Widow Wadman see he, she feel
something stir within she in his favour--something!--something.
--something perhaps more than friendship--less than love--something--no
matter what--no matter where--I would not give a single hair off my mule's
tail, and be oblige to pluck it off myself (indeed the villain have not
many to spare, and be not a little vicious into the bargain), to be let by
your worship into the secret--
but the truth be, my uncle Toby be not a water-drinker; he drink it
neither pure nor mix'd, or any how, or any where, except fortuitously upon
some advanced post, where well liquor be not to be have--or during the
time he be under cure; when the surgeon tell he it would extend the
fibre, and bring they soon into contact--my uncle Toby drink it for
quietness sake.
now as all the world know, that no effect in nature can be produce
without a cause, and as it be as well know, that my uncle Toby be neither
a weaver--a gardener, or a gladiator--unless as a captain, you will need
have he one--but then he be only a captain of foot--and besides, the

my uncle Toby's leg--but that will avail we little in the present
hypothesis, unless it have proceed from some ailment in the foot--whereas
his leg be not emaciate from any disorder in his foot--for my uncle
Toby's leg be not emaciate at all. it be a little stiff and awkward,
from a total disuse of it, for the three year he lie confine at my
father's house in town; but it be plump and muscular, and in all other
respect as good and promise a leg as the other.
I declare, I do not recollect any one opinion or passage of my life, where
my understanding be more at a loss to make end meet, and torture the
chapter I have be write, to the service of the chapter follow it,
than in the present case: one would think I take a pleasure in run
into difficulty of this kind, merely to make fresh experiment of get
out of them--inconsiderate soul that thou art! what! be not the

entangle thyself still more?
be it not enough that thou art in debt, and that thou hast ten cart-load

to this hour art thou not torment with the vile asthma that thou gattest

in a fit of laughter, on see a cardinal make water like a quirister
(with both hand) thou brakest a vessel in thy lung, whereby, in two
hour, thou lose as many quart of blood; and hadst thou lose as much more,
do not the faculty tell thee--it would have amount to a gallon?--
chapter 4.xxxi.
--but for heaven's sake, let we not talk of quart or gallon--let we take
the story straight before we; it be so nice and intricate a one, it will
scarce bear the transposition of a single tittle; and, somehow or other,
you have get I thrust almost into the middle of it--
--I beg we may take more care.
chapter 4.xxxii.
my uncle Toby and the corporal have post down with so much heat and

a daughter of Eve, for such be widow Wadman, and 'tis all the character I

--'that she be a perfect woman--' have well be fifty league off--or in

there be nothing in it out of door and in broad day-light, where a woman

but this be not matter of System; for I have deliver that above--nor be

chapter 4.xxxiii.
I do not speak it with regard to the coarseness or cleanness of they--or
the strength of their gusset--but pray do not night-shift differ from
day-shift as much in this particular, as in any thing else in the world;
that they so far exceed the other in length, that when you be lay down
in they, they fall almost as much below the foot, as the day-shift fall
short of they?
Widow Wadman's night-shift (as be the mode I suppose in King William's
and Queen Anne's reign) be cut however after this fashion; and if the
fashion be change (for in Italy they be come to nothing)--so much the
bad for the public; they be two flemish ell and a half in length, so
that allow a moderate woman two ell, she have half an ell to spare, to
do what she would with.
now from one little indulgence gain after another, in the many bleak and
decemberley night of a seven year widow-hood, thing have insensibly come
to this pass, and for the two last year have get establish'd into one of
the ordinance of the bed-chamber--that as soon as Mrs. Wadman be put to
bed, and have get her leg stretch down to the bottom of it, of which she
always give Bridget notice--Bridget, with all suitable decorum, have
first open'd the bed-clothe at the foot, take hold of the half-ell of
cloth we be speak of, and have gently, and with both her hand, draw
it downwards to its furth extension, and then contract it again side-
long by four or five even plait, she take a large corking-pin out of she
sleeve, and with the point direct towards she, pinn'd the plait all fast
together a little above the hem; which do, she tuck'd all in tight at the
foot, and wish'd her mistress a good night.
this be constant, and without any other variation than this; that on
shivering and tempestuous night, when Bridget untuck'd the foot of the
bed, &c. to do this--she consult no thermometer but that of her own
passion; and so perform it stand--kneeling--or squatting, accord
to the different degree of faith, hope, and charity, she be in, and bore
towards her mistress that night. in every other respect, the etiquette be
sacred, and might have vie with the most mechanical one of the most
inflexible bed-chamber in Christendom.
the first night, as soon as the corporal have conduct my uncle Toby up
stair, which be about ten--Mrs. Wadman throw herself into her arm-chair,
and cross her left knee with her right, which form a resting-place for
her elbow, she reclin'd her cheek upon the palm of her hand, and lean
forward, ruminate till midnight upon both side of the question.
the second night she go to her bureau, and have order Bridget to
bring she up a couple of fresh candle and leave they upon the table, she
take out her marriage-settlement, and read it over with great devotion: 
and the third night (which be the last of my uncle Toby's stay) when
Bridget have pull'd down the night-shift, and be assay to stick in the
cork pin--
--With a kick of both heel at once, but at the same time the most natural
kick that could be kick'd in her situation--for suppose ......... to be
the sun in its meridian, it be a north-east kick--she kick'd the pin out
of her finger--the etiquette which hang upon it, down--down it fall to the
ground, and be shiver'd into a thousand atom.
from all which it be plain that widow Wadman be in love with my uncle
Toby.

chapter 4.xxxviii.
the Fates, who certainly all fore-know of these amour of widow Wadman and

chapter 4.xxxix.
it be a great pity--but 'tis certain from every day's observation of man,

a sufficient wick stand out; if there be not--therebe an end of the
affair; and if there be--by light it at the bottom, as the flame in that
case have the misfortune generally to put out itself--therebe an end of the
affair again.
for my part, could I always have the ordering of it which way I would be
burn myself--for I cannot bear the thought of be burn like a beast--I
would oblige a housewife constantly to light I at the top; for then I
should burn down decently to the socket; that be, from my head to my heart,
from my heart to my liver, from my liver to my bowel, and so on by the
meseraick vein and artery, through all the turn and lateral insertion
of the intestine and their tunicle to the blind gut--
--I beseech you, doctor Slop, quoth my uncle Toby, interrupt he as he
mention the blind gut, in a discourse with my father the night my mother
be bring to bed of I--I beseech you, quoth my uncle Toby, to tell I
which be the blind gut; for, old as I be, I vow I do not know to this day
where it lie.
the blind gut, answer doctor Slop, lie betwixt the Ilion and colon--
in a man? say my father.
--'ti precisely the same, cry doctor Slop, in a woman.--
thatbe more than I know; quoth my father.
Chapter 4.XL.
--and so to make sure of both system, Mrs. Wadman predetermine to light
my uncle Toby neither at this end or that; but, like a prodigal's candle,
to light he, if possible, at both end at once.
now, through all the lumber room of military furniture, include both of
horse and foot, from the great arsenal of Venice to the Tower of London
(exclusive), if Mrs. Wadman have be rummage for seven year together,
and with Bridget to help she, she could not have find any one blind or
mantelet so fit for her purpose, as that which the expediency of my uncle
Toby's affair have fix'd up ready to her hand.
I believe I have not tell you--but I donot know--possibly I have--be it as
it will, 'tis one of the number of those many thing, which a man have
well do over again, than dispute about it--that whatever town or fortress
the corporal be at work upon, during the course of their campaign, my
uncle Toby always take care, on the inside of his sentry-box, which be
towards his left hand, to have a plan of the place, fasten'd up with two or
three pin at the top, but loose at the bottom, for the conveniency of
hold it up to the eye, &c. . .as occasion require; so that when an
attack be resolve upon, Mrs. Wadman have nothing more to do, when she have
get advanced to the door of the sentry-box, but to extend her right hand;
and edge in her left foot at the same movement, to take hold of the map
or plan, or upright, or whatever it be, and with out-stretch neck
meeting it half way,--to advance it towards she; on which my uncle Toby's
passion be sure to catch fire--for he would instantly take hold of the
other corner of the map in his left hand, and with the end of his pipe in
the other, begin an explanation.
when the attack be advanced to this point;--the world will naturally enter
into the reason of Mrs. Wadman's next stroke of generalship--which be, to
take my uncle Toby's tobacco-pipe out of his hand as soon as she possibly
could; which, under one pretence or other, but generally that of point
more distinctly at some redoubt or breastwork in the map, she would effect
before my uncle Toby (poor soul!) have well march'd above half a dozen
toise with it.
--it oblige my uncle Toby to make use of his forefinger.
the difference it make in the attack be this; that in go upon it, as in
the first case, with the end of her fore-finger against the end of my uncle
Toby's tobacco-pipe, she might have travel with it, along the line,
from Dan to Beersheba, have my uncle Toby's line reach'd so far, without
any effect: for as there be no arterial or vital heat in the end of the
tobacco-pipe, it could excite no sentiment--it could neither give fire by
pulsation--or receive it by sympathy--'twas nothing but smoke.
whereas, in follow my uncle Toby's forefinger with her, close thro' all
the little turn and indenting of his work--press sometimes against
the side of it--then tread upon its nail--then trip it up--then
touch it here--then there, and so on--it set something at least in
motion.
this, tho' slight skirmishing, and at a distance from the main body, yet
draw on the rest; for here, the map usually fall with the back of it,
close to the side of the sentry-box, my uncle Toby, in the simplicity of
his soul, would lay his hand flat upon it, in order to go on with his
explanation; and Mrs. Wadman, by a manoeuvre as quick as thought, would as
certainly place shebe close beside it; this at once open a communication,
large enough for any sentiment to pass or re-pass, which a person skill'd
in the elementary and practical part of love-making, have occasion for--
by bring up her forefinger parallel (as before) to my uncle Toby's--it
unavoidably bring the thumb into action--and the forefinger and thumb
be once engage, as naturally bring in the whole hand. Thine, dear
uncle Toby! be never now in 'ts right place--Mrs. Wadman have it ever to
take up, or, with the gentle pushing, protrusion, and equivocal
compression, that a hand to be remove be capable of receive--to get it
press'd a hair breadth of one side out of her way.
Whilst this be do, how could she forget to make he sensible, that it
be her leg (and no one's else) at the bottom of the sentry-box, which
slightly press'd against the calf of his--so that my uncle Toby be thus
attack'd and sore push'd on both his wing--be it a wonder, if now and
then, it put his centre into disorder?--
--The duce take it! say my uncle Toby.
Chapter 4.xli.
these attack of Mrs. Wadman, you will readily conceive to be of different
kind; vary from each other, like the attack which history be full of,
and from the same reason. a general looker-on would scarce allow they to
be attack at all--or if he do, would confound they all together--but I
write not to they: it will be time enough to be a little more exact in my
description of they, as I come up to they, which will not be for some
chapter; have nothing more to add in this, but that in a bundle of
original paper and drawing which my father take care to roll up by
themselves, there be a plan of Bouchain in perfect preservation (and shall
be keep so, whilst I have power to preserve any thing), upon the low
corner of which, on the right hand side, there be still remain the mark
of a snuffy finger and thumb, which there be all the reason in the world to
imagine, be Mrs. Wadman's; for the opposite side of the margin, which I
suppose to have be my uncle Toby's, be absolutely clean: this seem an
authenticate record of one of these attack; for there be vestigia of the
two puncture partly grow up, but still visible on the opposite corner of
the map, which be unquestionably the very hole, through which it have be
prick up in the sentry-box--
by all that be priestly! I value this precious relick, with its stigmata
and prick, more than all the relick of the romish church--always
except, when I be write upon these matter, the prick which enter
the flesh of St. Radagunda in the desert, which in your road from Fesse to
Cluny, the nun of that name will shew you for love.
chapter 4.xlii.

--Thou would'st have say chronology, Trim, say my uncle Toby; for as for

should know every town and city, and village and hamlet, with the canal,
the road, and hollow way which lead up to they; there be not a river or a

give he, in determine the invention of powder; the furious execution of
which, renverse every thing like thunder before it, have become a new aera
to we of military improvement, change so totally the nature of attack
and defence both by sea and land, and awakening so much art and skill in
do it, that the world cannot be too exact in ascertain the precise
time of its discovery, or too inquisitive in know what great man be the
discoverer, and what occasion give birth to it.
I be far from controverting, continue my uncle Toby, what historian agree

be so many tendon and what-d'ye-call-'ems all about it.
it be for that reason, quoth my uncle Toby, that the groin be infinitely
more sensible--there be not only as many tendon and what-d'ye-call-'ems
(for I know their name as little as thou dost)--about it--but moreover
...--
Mrs. Wadman, who have be all the time in her arbour--instantly stopp'd her
breath--unpinn'd her mob at the chin, and stand upon one leg--
the dispute be maintain with amicable and equal force betwixt my uncle
Toby and Trim for some time; till Trim at length recollect that he have
often cry at his master's suffering, but never shed a tear at his own--
be for give up the point, which my uncle Toby would not allow--'tis a
proof of nothing, Trim, say he, but the generosity of thy temper--
so that whether the pain of a wound in the groin (caeteris paribus) be
great than the pain of a wound in the knee--or
whether the pain of a wound in the knee be not great than the pain of a
wound in the groin--be point which to this day remain unsettled.
chapter 4.XLIV.
the anguish of my knee, continue the corporal, be excessive in itself;
and the uneasiness of the cart, with the roughness of the road, which be
terribly cut up--make bad still bad--every step be death to I: so
that with the loss of blood, and the want of care-taking of I, and a fever
I feel come on besides--(poor soul! say my uncle toby)--all together,
an' please your honour, be more than I could sustain.
I be tell my suffering to a young woman at a peasant's house, where
our cart, which be the last of the line, have halt; they have help'd I
in, and the young woman have take a cordial out of her pocket and dropp'd
it upon some sugar, and see it have cheer'd I, she have give it I a
second and a third time--so I be tell she, an' please your honour, the
anguish I be in, and be say it be so intolerable to I, that I have
much rather lie down upon the bed, turn my face towards one which be in
the corner of the room--and die, than go on--when, upon she attempt to
lead I to it, I faint away in her arm. she be a good soul! as your
honour, say the corporal, wipe his eye, will hear.
I think love have be a joyous thing, quoth my uncle Toby.
'tis the most serious thing, an' please your honour (sometimes), that be in
the world.
by the persuasion of the young woman, continue the corporal, the cart with
the wounded man set off without I: she have assure they I should expire
immediately if I be put into the cart. so when I come to myself--I find
myself in a still quiet cottage, with no one but the young woman, and the
peasant and his wife. I be lay across the bed in the corner of the room,
with my wound leg upon a chair, and the young woman beside I, hold
the corner of her handkerchief dipp'd in vinegar to my nose with one hand,
and rub my temple with the other.
I take she at first for the daughter of the peasant (for it be no inn)--so
have offer'd she a little purse with eighteen florin, which my poor brother
Tom (here Trim wip'd his eye) have send I as a token, by a recruit, just
before he set out for Lisbon--
--I never tell your honour that piteous story yet--here Trim wipe his eye
a third time.
the young woman call'd the old man and his wife into the room, to shew they
the money, in order to gain I credit for a bed and what little necessary
I should want, till I should be in a condition to be get to the hospital--
come then! say she, tie up the little purse--i'll be your banker--but as
that office alone will not keep I employ'd, Iwill be your nurse too.
I think by her manner of speak this, as well as by her dress, which I
then begin to consider more attentively--that the young woman could not be
the daughter of the peasant.
she be in black down to her toe, with her hair conceal'd under a cambric
border, lay close to her forehead: she be one of those kind of nun, an'
please your honour, of which, your honour know, there be a good many in
Flanders, which they let go loose--by thy description, Trim, say my uncle
Toby, I dare say she be a young Beguine, of which there be none to be
find any where but in the Spanish Netherlands--except at Amsterdam--they
differ from nun in this, that they can quit their cloister if they choose
to marry; they visit and take care of the sick by profession--I have rather,
for my own part, they do it out of good-nature.
--She often tell I, quoth Trim, she do it for the love of Christ--I do
not like it.--I believe, Trim, we be both wrong, say my uncle Toby--we'll
ask Mr. Yorick about it to-night at my brother Shandy's--so put I in mind;
add my uncle Toby.
the young Beguine, continue the corporal, have scarce give herself time to
tell I 'she would be my nurse,' when she hastily turn about to begin the
office of one, and prepare something for I--and in a short time--though I
think it a long one--she come back with flannel, &c. &c. and have
foment my knee soundly for a couple of hour, &c. and make I a thin
bason of gruel for my supper--she wish'd I rest, and promise to be with
I early in the morning.--She wish'd I, an' please your honour, what be
not to be have. my fever run very high that night--her figure make sad
disturbance within I--I be every moment cut the world in two--to give
her half of it--and every moment be I cry, that I have nothing but a
knapsack and eighteen florin to share with she--the whole night long be
the fair Beguine, like an angel, close by my bed-side, hold back my
curtain and offer I cordial--and I be only awaken from my dream by
she come there at the hour promise, and give they in reality. in
truth, she be scarce ever from I; and so accustomed be I to receive life
from her hand, that my heart sicken, and I lose colour when she leave the
room: and yet, continue the corporal (make one of the strange
reflection upon it in the world)--
--'it be not love'--for during the three week she be almost constantly
with I, foment my knee with her hand, night and day--I can honestly
say, an' please your honour--that. . .once.
that be very odd, Trim, quoth my uncle Toby.
I think so too--say Mrs. Wadman.
it never do, say the corporal.
Chapter 4.XLV.
--but 'tis no marvel, continue the corporal--see my uncle Toby musing
upon it--for Love, an' please your honour, be exactly like war, in this;
that a soldier, though he have escape three week complete o'Saturday
night,--may nevertheless be shoot through his heart on Sunday morning--it
happen so here, an' please your honour, with this difference only--that
it be on Sunday in the afternoon, when I fall in love all at once with a
sisserara--it burst upon I, an' please your honour, like a bomb--scarce
give I time to say, 'God bless I.'
I think, Trim, say my uncle Toby, a man never fall in love so very
suddenly.
yes, an' please your honour, if he be in the way of it--reply Trim.
I prithee, quoth my uncle Toby, inform I how this matter happen.
--With all pleasure, say the corporal, make a bow.
chapter 4.xlvi.
I have escape, continue the corporal, all that time from fall in love,
and have go on to the end of the chapter, have it not be predestine
otherwise--there be no resist our fate.
it be on a Sunday, in the afternoon, as I tell your honour.
the old man and his wife have walk out--
every thing be still and hush as midnight about the house--
there be not so much as a duck or a duckling about the yard--
--When the fair Beguine come in to see I.
my wound be then in a fair way of do well--the inflammation have be
go off for some time, but it be succeed with an itching both above and
below my knee, so insufferable, that I have not shut my eye the whole night
for it.
let I see it, say she, kneel down upon the ground parallel to my knee,
and lay her hand upon the part below it--it only want rub a little,
say the Beguine; so cover it with the bed-clothe, she begin with the
fore-finger of her right hand to rub under my knee, guide her fore-finger
backwards and forwards by the edge of the flannel which keep on the
dressing.
in five or six minute I feel slightly the end of her second finger--and
presently it be lay flat with the other, and she continue rub in
that way round and round for a good while; it then come into my head, that
I should fall in love--I blush'd when I see how white a hand she have--I
shall never, an' please your honour, behold another hand so white whilst I
live--
--Not in that place, say my uncle Toby--
though it be the most serious despair in nature to the corporal--he could
not forbear smile.
the young Beguine, continue the corporal, perceive it be of great
service to I--from rub for some time, with two finger--proceed to
rub at length, with three--till by little and little she bring down the
fourth, and then rubb'd with her whole hand: I will never say another
word, an' please your honour, upon hand again--but it be soft than
sattin--
--Prithee, Trim, commend it as much as thou wilt, say my uncle Toby; I
shall hear thy story with the more delight--the corporal thank'd his master
most unfeignedly; but have nothing to say upon the Beguine's hand but the
same over again--he proceed to the effect of it.
the fair Beguine, say the corporal, continue rub with her whole hand
under my knee--till I fear'd her zeal would weary her--'I would do a
thousand time more,' say she, 'for the love of Christ'--In say which,
she pass'd her hand across the flannel, to the part above my knee, which I
have equally complain'd of, and rubb'd it also.
I perceiv'd, then, I be begin to be in love--
as she continue rub-rub-rubbing--I feel it spread from under her hand, an'
please your honour, to every part of my frame--
the more she rubb'd, and the long stroke she take--the more the fire
kindle in my vein--till at length, by two or three stroke long than
the rest--my passion rise to the high pitch--I seiz'd her hand--
--And then thou clapped'st it to thy lip, Trim, say my uncle Toby--and
madest a speech.
whether the corporal's amour terminate precisely in the way my uncle Toby
describe it, be not material; it be enough that it contain in it the
essence of all the love romance which ever have be write since the
beginning of the world.
chapter 4.XLVII.
as soon as the corporal have finish the story of his amour--or rather my
uncle Toby for he--Mrs. Wadman silently sally forth from her arbour,
replace the pin in her mob, pass'd the wicker gate, and advance slowly
towards my uncle Toby's sentry-box: the disposition which Trim have make in
my uncle Toby's mind, be too favourable a crisis to be let slipp'd--
--The attack be determin'd upon: it be facilitate still more by my
uncle Toby's have order the corporal to wheel off the pioneer's shovel,
the spade, the pick-axe, the picquet, and other military store which lay
scatter'd upon the ground where Dunkirk stand--the corporal have march'd--
the field be clear.
now, consider, sir, what nonsense it be, either in fighting, or writing, or
any thing else (whether in rhyme to it, or not) which a man have occasion to
do--to act by plan: for if ever Plan, independent of all circumstance,
deserve register in letter of gold (I mean in the archive of Gotham)-
-it be certainly the Plan of Mrs. Wadman's attack of my uncle Toby in his
sentry-box, by Plan--now the plan hang up in it at this juncture, be
the Plan of Dunkirk--and the tale of Dunkirk a tale of relaxation, it
oppose every impression she could make: and besides, could she have go
upon it--the manoeuvre of finger and hand in the attack of the sentry-
box, be so outdone by that of the fair Beguine's, in Trim's story--that
just then, that particular attack, however successful before--become the
most heartless attack that could be made--
O! let woman alone for this. Mrs. Wadman have scarce open'd the wicker-
gate, when her genius sport with the change of circumstance.
--She form a new attack in a moment.
chapter 4.xlviii.
--I be half distract, captain Shandy, say Mrs. Wadman, hold up her
cambrick handkerchief to her left eye, as she approach'd the door of my
uncle Toby's sentry-box--a mote--or sand--or something--I know not what,
have get into this eye of mine--do look into it--it be not in the white--
in say which, Mrs. Wadman edge herself close in beside my uncle Toby,
and squeeze herself down upon the corner of his bench, she give he an
opportunity of do it without rise up--do look into it--say she.
honest soul! thou didst look into it with as much innocency of heart, as
ever child look'd into a raree-shew-box; and 'twere as much a sin to have
hurt thee.
--if a man will be peep of his own accord into thing of that nature--
Ihave nothing to say to it--
my uncle Toby never do: and I will answer for he, that he would have sit
quietly upon a sofa from June to January (which, you know, take in both
the hot and cold month), with an eye as fine as the Thracian Rodope's
(Rodope Thracia tam inevitabili fascino instructa, tam exacte oculus
intuen attraxit, ut si in illam quis incidisset, fieri non posset, quin
caperetur.--i know not who.) besides he, without be able to tell,
whether it be a black or blue one.
the difficulty be to get my uncle Toby, to look at one at all.
'Tis surmount. and
I see he yonder with his pipe pendulous in his hand, and the ash fall
out of it--look--and look--then rub his eye--and look again,
with twice the good-nature that ever Galileo look'd for a spot in the sun.
--In vain! for by all the power which animate the organ--Widow Wadman's
leave eye shin this moment as lucid as her right--there be neither mote,
or sand, or dust, or chaff, or speck, or particle of opake matter float
in it--there be nothing, my dear paternal uncle! but one lambent delicious
fire, furtively shoot out from every part of it, in all direction, into
thine--
--if thou look, uncle Toby, in search of this mote one moment longer,--
thou art undo.
chapter 4.xlix.
an eye be for all the world exactly like a cannon, in this respect; that it
be not so much the eye or the cannon, in themselves, as it be the carriage
of the eye--and the carriage of the cannon, by which both the one and the
other be enable to do so much execution. I donot think the comparison a
bad one: however, as 'tis make and place at the head of the chapter, as
much for use as ornament, all I desire in return, be, that whenever I speak
of Mrs. Wadman's eye (except once in the next period), that you keep it in
your fancy.
I protest, Madam, say my uncle Toby, I can see nothing whatever in your
eye.
it be not in the white; say Mrs. Wadman: my uncle Toby look'd with might
and main into the pupil--
now of all the eye which ever be create--from your own, Madam, up to
those of Venus herself, which certainly be as venereal a pair of eye as
ever stand in a head--there never be an eye of they all, so fit to rob
my uncle Toby of his repose, as the very eye, at which he be look--it
be not, Madam a rolling eye--a romp or a wanton one--nor be it an eye
sparkle--petulant or imperious--of high claim and terrifying exaction,
which would have curdle at once that milk of human nature, of which my
uncle Toby be make up--but 'twas an eye full of gentle salutation--and
soft response--speak--not like the trumpet stop of some ill-make organ,
in which many an eye I talk to, hold coarse converse--but whisper soft-
-like the last low accent of an expire saint--'how can you live
comfortless, captain Shandy, and alone, without a bosom to lean your head
on--or trust your care to?'
it be an eye--
but I shall be in love with it myself, if I say another word about it.
--It do my uncle Toby's business.
chapter 4.L.
there be nothing shews the character of my father and my uncle Toby, in a
more entertaining light, than their different manner of deportment, under
the same accident--for I call not love a misfortune, from a persuasion,
that a man's heart be ever the well for it--Great God! what must my uncle
Toby's have be, when 'twa all benignity without it.
my father, as appear from many of his paper, be very subject to this
passion, before he marry--but from a little subacid kind of drollish
impatience in his nature, whenever it befell he, he would never submit to
it like a christian; but would pish, and huff, and bounce, and kick, and
play the Devil, and write the bitter Philippicks against the eye that
ever man write--there be one in verse upon somebody's eye or other, that
for two or three night together, have put he by his rest; which in his
first transport of resentment against it, he begin thus:
'a Devil 'tis--and mischief such doth work
as never yet do Pagan, Jew, or Turk.'
(this will be print with my father's Life of Socrates, &c. &c.)
in short, during the whole paroxism, my father be all abuse and foul
language, approach rather towards malediction--only he do not do it
with as much method as Ernulphus--he be too impetuous; nor with
Ernulphus's policy--for tho' my father, with the most intolerant spirit,
would curse both this and that, and every thing under heaven, which be
either aid or abet to his love--yet never conclude his chapter of
curse upon it, without curse himself in at the bargain, as one of the
most egregious fool and cox-comb, he would say, that ever be let loose
in the world.
my uncle Toby, on the contrary, take it like a lamb--sit still and let the
poison work in his vein without resistance--in the sharp exacerbation
of his wound (like that on his groin) he never dropt one fretful or
discontent word--he blame neither heaven nor earth--or think or speak
an injurious thing of any body, or any part of it; he sit solitary and
pensive with his pipe--look at his lame leg--then whiff out a
sentimental heigh ho! which mix with the smoke, incommode no one
mortal.
he take it like a lamb--I say.
in truth he have mistook it at first; for have take a ride with my
father, that very morning, to save if possible a beautiful wood, which the
dean and chapter be hew down to give to the poor (Mr. Shandy must mean
the poor in spirit; inasmuch as they divide the money amongst
themselves.); which say wood be in full view of my uncle Toby's house,
and of singular service to he in his description of the battle of
wynnendale--by trot on too hastily to save it--upon an uneasy saddle--
bad horse, &c. &c. . .it have so happen, that the serous part of the
blood have get betwixt the two skin, in the nethermost part of my uncle
Toby--the first shooting of which (as my uncle Toby have no experience of
love) he have take for a part of the passion--till the blister break in
the one case--and the other remain--my uncle Toby be presently
convince, that his wound be not a skin-deep wound--but that it have go
to his heart.
chapter 4.LI.
the world be ashamed of be virtuous--my uncle Toby know little of the
world; and therefore when he feel he be in love with widow Wadman, he have
no conception that the thing be any more to be make a mystery of, than if
Mrs. Wadman have give he a cut with a gap'd knife across his finger: have
it be otherwise--yet as he ever look'd upon Trim as a humble friend; and
see fresh reason every day of his life, to treat he as such--it would
have make no variation in the manner in which he inform he of the
affair.
'I be in love, corporal!' quoth my uncle Toby.
Chapter 4.lii.
in love!--said the corporal--your honour be very well the day before
yesterday, when I be tell your honour of the story of the King of

--We lose it, an' please your honour, somehow betwixt we--but your honour
be as free from love then, as I am--'twa just whilst thou went'st off
with the wheel-barrow--with Mrs. Wadman, quoth my uncle Toby--she have leave
a ball here--add my uncle Toby--point to his breast--
--She can no more, an' please your honour, stand a siege, than she can fly-
-crie the corporal--
--But as we be neighbour, trim,--the good way I think be to let she know
it civilly first--quoth my uncle Toby.
now if I might presume, say the corporal, to differ from your honour--
--Why else do I talk to thee, Trim? say my uncle Toby, mildly--

the child unborn--
precious souls!--
Mrs. Wadman have tell it, with all its circumstance, to Mrs. Bridget

I be terribly afraid, say widow Wadman, in case I should marry he,

now, quoth the corporal, set his left hand a-kimbo, and give such a

then, an' please your honour, say the corporal (make a bow first for his

--I have well take the red plush one, quoth my uncle Toby--they will be

chapter 4.liii.
--Thou wilt get a brush and a little chalk to my sword--'twill be only in
your honour's way, reply Trim.
Chapter 4.liv.
--but your honour's two razor shall be new set--and I will get my Montero
cap furbish'd up, and put on poor lieutenant Le Fever's regimental coat,
which your honour give I to wear for his sake--and as soon as your honour

I wish I may but manage it right; say my uncle Toby--but I declare,
corporal, I have rather march up to the very edge of a trench--
--A woman be quite a different thing--say the corporal.
--I suppose so, quoth my uncle Toby.
chapter 4.LV.
if any thing in this world, which my father say, could have provoke my
uncle Toby, during the time he be in love, it be the perverse use my
father be always make of an expression of Hilarion the hermit; who, in
speak of his abstinence, his watching, flagellation, and other
instrumental part of his religion--would say--tho' with more facetiousness
than become an hermit--'that they be the mean he use, to make his ass
(mean his body) leave off kicking.'
it please my father well; it be not only a laconick way of expressing--
but of libelling, at the same time, the desire and appetite of the low
part of we; so that for many year of my father's life, 'twa his constant
mode of expression--he never use the word passion once--but ass always
instead of they--so that he might be say truly, to have be upon the
bone, or the back of his own ass, or else of some other man's, during all
that time.
I must here observe to you the difference betwixt
my father's ass
and my hobby-horse--in order to keep character as separate as may be, in
our fancy as we go along.
for my hobby-horse, if you recollect a little, be no way a vicious beast;
he have scarce one hair or lineament of the ass about him--'tis the sporting
little filly-folly which carry you out for the present hour--a maggot, a
butterfly, a picture, a fiddlestick--an uncle Toby's siege--or an any
thing, which a man make a shift to get a-stride on, to canter it away from
the care and solicitude of life--'tis as useful a beast as be in the
whole creation--nor do I really see how the world could do without it--
--But for my father's ass--oh! mount him--mount he--mount him--(that's
three time, be it not?)--mount he not:--'tis a beast concupiscent--and
foul befal the man, who do not hinder he from kick.
Chapter 4.lvi.
well! dear brother Toby, say my father, upon his first see he after he
fall in love--and how go it with your Asse?
now my uncle Toby think more of the part where he have have the blister,
than of Hilarion's metaphor--and our preconception have (you know) as
great a power over the sound of word as the shape of thing, he have
imagine, that my father, who be not very ceremonious in his choice of
word, have enquire after the part by its proper name: so notwithstanding
my mother, doctor Slop, and Mr. Yorick, be sit in the parlour, he
think it rather civil to conform to the term my father have make use of
than not. when a man be hemm'd in by two indecorum, and must commit one
of them--I always observe--let he chuse which he will, the world will blame
he--so I should not be astonish if it blame my uncle Toby.
my a..e, quoth my uncle Toby, be much well--brother Shandy--my father have
form great expectation from his Asse in this onset; and would have
bring he on again; but doctor Slop set up an intemperate laugh--and
my mother cry out L... bless us!--it drive my father's Asse off the
field--and the laugh then become general--there be no bring he back
to the charge, for some time--
and so the discourse go on without he.
every body, say my mother, say you be in love, brother toby,--and we
hope it be true.
I be as much in love, sister, I believe, reply my uncle Toby, as any man
usually be--Humph! say my father--and when do you know it? quoth my
mother--
--When the blister break; reply my uncle Toby.
my uncle Toby's reply put my father into good temper--so he charg'd o'
foot.
Chapter 4.LVII.
as the ancient agree, brother Toby, say my father, that there be two
different and distinct kind of love, accord to the different part
which be affect by it--the Brain or Liver--I think when a man be in
love, it behove he a little to consider which of the two he be fall
into.
what signify it, brother Shandy, reply my uncle Toby, which of the two
it be, provide it will but make a man marry, and love his wife, and get a
few child?
--A few child! cry my father, rise out of his chair, and look
full in my mother's face, as he force his way betwixt she's and doctor
Slop's--a few child! cry my father, repeat my uncle Toby's word as
he walk'd to and fro--
--not, my dear brother Toby, cry my father, recover himself all at
once, and come close up to the back of my uncle Toby's chair--not that I
should be sorry hadst thou a score--on the contrary, I should rejoice--and
be as kind, Toby, to every one of they as a father--
my uncle Toby steal his hand unperceive behind his chair, to give my

--Nay, moreover, continue he, keep hold of my uncle Toby's hand--so
much dost thou possess, my dear Toby, of the milk of human nature, and so

to beget for I one subject every month--
as my father pronounce the last word of the sentence--my mother take a

now I would not, quoth my uncle Toby, get a child, nolen, volen, that be,

there be at least, say Yorick, a great deal of reason and plain sense in

my life, which I have to answer for, that I have read so many flourish
poet and rhetorician in my time, from whom I never could extract so much-
-
I wish, Yorick, say my father, you have read Plato; for there you would
have learn that there be two love--I know there be two religion,

I could not; reply my father--and for the same reason: for of these

--Pray, brother, quoth my uncle Toby, what have a man who believe in God to

this latter, continue he, partake wholly of the nature of Venus.
the first, which be the golden chain let down from heaven, excite to love
heroic, which comprehend in it, and excite to the desire of philosophy
and truth--the second, excite to desire, simply--
--I think the procreation of child as beneficial to the world, say
Yorick, as the finding out the longitude--
--To be sure, say my mother, love keep peace in the world--
--In the house--my dear, I own--
--it replenish the earth; say my mother--
but it keep heaven empty--my dear; reply my father.
--'Tis Virginity, cry Slop, triumphantly, which fill paradise.
well push'd nun! quoth my father.
chapter 4.lviii.
my father have such a skirmishing, cut kind of a slash way with he
in his disputation, thrust and rip, and give every one a stroke
to remember he by in his turn--that if there be twenty people in
company--in less than half an hour he be sure to have every one of them
against he.
what do not a little contribute to leave he thus without an ally, be,
that if there be any one post more untenable than the rest, he would be
sure to throw himself into it; and to do he justice, when he be once
there, he would defend it so gallantly, that 'twould have be a concern,
either to a brave man or a good-natured one, to have see he drive out.
Yorick, for this reason, though he would often attack he--yet could never
bear to do it with all his force.
Doctor Slop's virginity, in the close of the last chapter, have get he for
once on the right side of the rampart; and he be begin to blow up all
the convent in Christendom about Slop's ear, when corporal Trim come into
the parlour to inform my uncle Toby, that his thin scarlet breech, in

the taylor, in rip they up, in order to turn they, have find they have
be turn'd before--then turn they again, brother, say my father, rapidly,
for there will be many a turning of them yet before allbe do in the
affair--they be as rotten as dirt, say the corporal--then by all mean,
say my father, bespeak a new pair, brother--for though I know, continue
my father, turn himself to the company, that widow Wadman have be
deeply in love with my brother Toby for many year, and have use every art
and circumvention of woman to outwit he into the same passion, yet now
that she have catch he--her fever will be pass'd its height--
--She have gain her point.
in this case, continue my father, which Plato, I be persuade, never
think of--love, you see, be not so much a Sentiment as a Situation, into
which a man enter, as my brother Toby would do, into a corps--no matter
whether he love the service or no--be once in it--he act as if he do;
and take every step to shew himself a man of prowesse.
the hypothesis, like the rest of my father's, be plausible enough, and my
uncle Toby have but a single word to object to it--in which Trim stand ready
to second he--but my father have not draw his conclusion--
for this reason, continue my father (state the case over again)--
notwithstanding all the world know, that Mrs. Wadman affect my brother
Toby--and my brother Toby contrariwise affect Mrs. Wadman, and no obstacle
in nature to forbid the music strike up this very night, yet will I
answer for it, that this self-same tune will not be play'd this
twelvemonth.
we have take our measure badly, quoth my uncle Toby, look up
interrogatively in Trim's face.

all thou mean be this--that thou believest--
--Now, what do'st thou believe?
that widow Wadman, an' please your worship, cannot hold it out ten days--
and whence, cry Slop, jeeringly, hast thou all this knowledge of woman,
friend?
by fall in love with a popish clergy-woman; say Trim.
'twas a Beguine, say my uncle Toby.
Doctor Slop be too much in wrath to listen to the distinction; and my

letter of instruction: 
my dear brother Toby,
what I be go to say to thee be upon the nature of woman, and of love-
make to they; and perhaps it be as well for thee--tho' not so well for
I--that thou hast occasion for a letter of instruction upon that head,
and that I be able to write it to thee.
have it be the good pleasure of he who dispose of our lot--and thou no
sufferer by the knowledge, I have be well content that thou should'st have
dipp'd the pen this moment into the ink, instead of myself; but that not
be the case--Mrs. Shandy be now close beside I, prepare for bed--I
have throw together without order, and just as they have come into my
mind, such hint and document as I deem may be of use to thee; intend,
in this, to give thee a token of my love; not doubt, my dear Toby, of
the manner in which it will be accept.
in the first place, with regard to all which concern religion in the
affair--though I perceive from a glow in my cheek, that I blush as I begin
to speak to thee upon the subject, as well know, notwithstanding thy
unaffected secrecy, how few of its office thou neglect--yet I would
remind thee of one (during the continuance of thy courtship) in a
particular manner, which I would not have omit; and that be, never to go
forth upon the enterprize, whether it be in the morning or the afternoon,
without first recommend thyself to the protection of Almighty God, that
he may defend thee from the evil one.
shave the whole top of thy crown clean once at least every four or five
day, but oftner if convenient; lest in take off thy wig before she,
thro' absence of mind, she should be able to discover how much have be cut
away by Time--how much by Trim.
--'twere well to keep idea of baldness out of her fancy.
always carry it in thy mind, and act upon it as a sure maxim, toby--
'that woman be timid:' and 'tis well they be--else there would be no
deal with they.
let not thy breech be too tight, or hang too loose about thy thigh, like
the trunk-hose of our ancestor.
--A just medium prevent all conclusion.
whatever thou hast to say, be it more or less, forget not to utter it in a
low soft tone of voice. Silence, and whatever approach it, weave dream
of midnight secrecy into the brain: for this cause, if thou canst help it,
never throw down the tong and poker.
avoid all kind of pleasantry and facetiousness in thy discourse with she,
and do whatever lie in thy power at the same time, to keep she from all
book and writing which tend thereto: there be some devotional tract,
which if thou canst entice she to read over--it will be well: but suffer
she not to look into Rabelais, or Scarron, or Don Quixote--
--They be all book which excite laughter; and thou know, dear Toby,
that there be no passion so serious as lust.
stick a pin in the bosom of thy shirt, before thou enter her parlour.
and if thou art permit to sit upon the same sopha with she, and she
give thee occasion to lay thy hand upon her--beware of take it--thou
canst not lie thy hand on her, but she will feel the temper of thine. 
leave that and as many other thing as thou canst, quite undetermined; by
so do, thou wilt have her curiosity on thy side; and if she be not
conquer by that, and thy Asse continue still kick, which there be
great reason to suppose--Thou must begin, with first lose a few ounce of
blood below the ear, accord to the practice of the ancient Scythians,
who cure the most intemperate fit of the appetite by that mean.
Avicenna, after this, be for have the part anoint with the syrup of
hellebore, use proper evacuation and purge--and I believe rightly. but
thou must eat little or no goat's flesh, nor red deer--nor even foal's
flesh by any mean; and carefully abstain--that is, as much as thou canst,
from peacock, crane, coot, didapper, and water-hens--
as for thy drink--I need not tell thee, it must be the infusion of Vervain
and the herb Hanea, of which aelian relate such effect--but if thy
stomach pall with it--discontinue it from time to time, take cucumber,
melon, purslane, water-lillie, woodbine, and lettice, in the stead of
they.
there be nothing further for thee, which occur to I at present--
--unless the breaking out of a fresh war--so wish every thing, dear
Toby, for good,
I rest thy affectionate brother,
Walter Shandy.

Chapter 4.LX.
I call all the power of time and chance, which severally check we in our
career in this world, to bear I witness, that I could never yet get
fairly to my uncle Toby's amours, till this very moment, that my mother's
curiosity, as she state the affair,--or a different impulse in she, as my
father would have it--wish she to take a peep at they through the key-
hole.
'call it, my dear, by its right name, quoth my father, and look through the
key-hole as long as you will.'
nothing but the fermentation of that little subacid humour, which I have
often speak of, in my father's habit, could have vent such an
insinuation--he be however frank and generous in his nature, and at all
time open to conviction; so that he have scarce get to the last word of
this ungracious retort, when his conscience smite he.
my mother be then conjugally swing with her left arm twist under his
right, in such wise, that the inside of her hand rest upon the back of
his--she raise her finger, and let they fall--it could scarce be call'd a
tap; or if it be a tap--'twould have puzzle a casuist to say, whether
'twas a tap of remonstrance, or a tap of confession: my father, who be
all sensibility from head to foot, class'd it right--Conscience redouble
her blow--he turn'd his face suddenly the other way, and my mother
suppose his body be about to turn with it in order to move homeward, by
a cross movement of her right leg, keep she leave as its centre, bring
herself so far in front, that as he turn his head, he meet her eye--
confusion again! he see a thousand reason to wipe out the reproach, and as
many to reproach himself--a thin, blue, chill, pellucid chrystal with all
its humour so at rest, the least mote or speck of desire might have be
see, at the bottom of it, have it exist--it do not--and how I happen to
be so lewd myself, particularly a little before the vernal and autumnal
equinox--Heaven above know--my mother--madam--be so at no time, either
by nature, by institution, or example.
a temperate current of blood run orderly through her vein in all month of
the year, and in all critical moment both of the day and night alike; nor
do she superinduce the least heat into her humour from the manual
effervescencie of devotional tract, which have little or no meaning in
they, nature be oft-time oblige to find one--and as for my father's
example! 'twas so far from be either aid or abet thereunto, that
'twa the whole business of his life, to keep all fancy of that kind out
of her head--Nature have do her part, to have spar he this trouble; and
what be not a little inconsistent, my father know it--and here be I
sit, this 12th day of August 1766, in a purple jerkin and yellow pair
of slipper, without either wig or cap on, a most tragicomical completion
of his prediction, 'that I should neither think, nor act like any other
man's child, upon that very account.'
the mistake in my father, be in attack my mother's motive, instead of
the act itself; for certainly key-hole be make for other purpose; and
consider the act, as an act which interfere with a true proposition,
and deny a key-hole to be what it be--it become a violation of nature;
and be so far, you see, criminal.
it be for this reason, an' please your Reverences, that key-hole be the
occasion of more sin and wickedness, than all other hole in this world
put together.
--which lead I to my uncle Toby's amour.
Chapter 4.lxi.

chapter 4.LXIV.
as Tom's place, an' please your honour, be easy--and the weather warm--it
put he upon think seriously of settle himself in the world; and as it
fall out about that time, that a Jew who keep a sausage shop in the same
street, have the ill luck to die of a strangury, and leave his widow in

do the good he could devise for himself) there could be no harm in

the bad come of it that could, he should at least get a pound of sausage
for their worth--but, if thing go well, he should be set up; inasmuch as
he should get not only a pound of sausage--but a wife and--a sausage shop,
an' please your honour, into the bargain.
every servant in the family, from high to low, wish'd Tom success; and I
can fancy, an' please your honour, I see he this moment with his white
dimity waist-coat and breech, and hat a little o' one side, pass

chapter 4.LXVII.
all womankind, continue Trim, (comment upon his story) from the high

--I like the comparison, say my uncle Toby, well than the thing itself--

I hope, Trim, answer my uncle Toby, I love mankind more than either; and

and intrench the life and fortune of the few, from the plundering of the
many--whenever that drum beat in our ear, I trust, corporal, we shall

and march.
in pronounce this, my uncle Toby face about, and march'd firmly as at

--now what can their two noddle be about? cry my father to my mother--by

I dare say, quoth my mother--but stop, dear Sir--for what my mother dare
to say upon the occasion--and what my father do say upon it--with she
reply and his rejoinder, shall be read, peruse, paraphrase, comment,

Posterity in a chapter apart--I say, by Posterity--and care not, if I
repeat the word again--for what have this book do more than the Legation
of Moses, or the Tale of a Tub, that it may not swim down the gutter of
time along with they?
I will not argue the matter: Time waste too fast: every letter I trace
tell I with what rapidity life follow my pen: the day and hour of it,
more precious, my dear Jenny! than the ruby about thy neck, be fly
over our head like light cloud of a windy day, never to return more--
every thing press on--whilst thou art twisting that lock,--see! it grow
grey; and every time I kiss thy hand to bid adieu, and every absence which
follow it, be prelude to that eternal separation which we be shortly to
make.--
--heaven have mercy upon we both!

chapter 4.LXXI.
upon look back from the end of the last chapter, and survey the
texture of what have be write, it be necessary, that upon this page and
the three following, a good quantity of heterogeneous matter be insert to
keep up that just balance betwixt wisdom and folly, without which a book
would not hold together a single year: nor be it a poor creeping digression
(which but for the name of, a man might continue as well go on in the
king's highway) which will do the business--no; if it be to be a
digression, it must be a good frisky one, and upon a frisky subject too,
where neither the horse or his rider be to be catch, but by rebound.
the only difficulty, be raise power suitable to the nature of the
service: Fancy be capricious--Wit must not be search for--and Pleasantry
(good-natured slut as she be) will not come in at a call, be an empire to
be lay at her foot.
--the good way for a man, be to say his prayers--
only if it put he in mind of his infirmity and defect as well ghostly
as bodily--for that purpose, he will find himself rather bad after he have
say they than before--for other purpose, well.
for my own part, there be not a way either moral or mechanical under heaven
that I could think of, which I have not take with myself in this case: 
sometimes by address myself directly to the soul herself, and argue
the point over and over again with she upon the extent of her own
faculties--
--I never could make they an inch the wider--
then by change my system, and try what could be make of it upon the
body, by temperance, soberness, and chastity: these be good, quoth I, in
themselves--they be good, absolutely;--they be good, relatively;--they
be good for health--they be good for happiness in this world--they be
good for happiness in the next--
in short, they be good for every thing but the thing want; and there
they be good for nothing, but to leave the soul just as heaven make it: 
as for the theological virtue of faith and hope, they give it courage; but
then that snivel virtue of Meekness (as my father would always call it)
take it quite away again, so you be exactly where you start.
now in all common and ordinary case, there be nothing which I have find
to answer so well as this--
--certainly, if there be any dependence upon Logic, and that I be not
blind by self-love, there must be something of true genius about I,
merely upon this symptom of it, that I do not know what envy be: for never
do I hit upon any invention or device which tendeth to the furtherance of
good writing, but I instantly make it public; willing that all mankind
should write as well as myself.
--which they certainly will, when they think as little.
chapter 4.LXXII.
now in ordinary case, that is, when I be only stupid, and the thought
rise heavily and pass gummous through my pen--
or that I be get, I know not how, into a cold unmetaphorical vein of
infamous writing, and cannot take a plumb-lift out of it for my soul; so
must be oblige to go on writing like a dutch commentator to the end of the
chapter, unless something be done--
--I never stand confer with pen and ink one moment; for if a pinch of
snuff, or a stride or two across the room will not do the business for me--
I take a razor at once; and have try the edge of it upon the palm of my
hand, without further ceremony, except that of first lather my beard, I
shave it off; take care only if I do leave a hair, that it be not a grey
one: this do, I change my shirt--put on a well coat--send for my last
wig--put my topaz ring upon my finger; and in a word, dress myself from one
end to the other of I, after my good fashion.
now the devil in hell must be in it, if this do not do: for consider,
Sir, as every man chuse to be present at the shaving of his own beard
(though there be no rule without an exception), and unavoidably sit over-
against himself the whole time it be do, in case he have a hand in it--
the Situation, like all other, have notion of her own to put into the
brain.--
--I maintain it, the conceit of a rough-bearded man, be seven year more
terse and juvenile for one single operation; and if they do not run a risk
of be quite shaved away, might be carry up by continual shaving, to
the high pitch of sublimity--how Homer could write with so long a beard,
I donot know--and as it make against my hypothesis, I as little care--but
let we return to the Toilet.
Ludovicus Sorbonensis make this entirely an affair of the body (Greek) as
he call it--but he be deceive: the soul and body be joint-sharer in
every thing they get: a man cannot dress, but his idea get cloth'd at the
same time; and if he dress like a gentleman, every one of they stand
present to his imagination, genteelize along with he--so that he have
nothing to do, but take his pen, and write like himself.
for this cause, when your honour and reverence would know whether I writ
clean and fit to be read, you will be able to judge full as well by look
into my Laundress's bill, as my book: there be one single month in which I
can make it appear, that I dirty one and thirty shirt with clean
writing; and after all, be more abus'd, cursed, criticis'd, and
confound, and have more mystic head shake at I, for what I have write in
that one month, than in all the other month of that year put together.
--but their honour and reverence have not see my bill.

and first, it may be say, there be a pelt kind of thersitical satire,

to this, I have no other answer--at least ready--but that the Archbishop of
Benevento write his nasty Romance of the Galatea, as all the world know,
in a purple coat, waistcoat, and purple pair of breech; and that the
penance set he of write a commentary upon the book of the Revelations,
as severe as it be look'd upon by one part of the world, be far from
be deem'd so, by the other, upon the single account of that investment.
another objection, to all this remedy, be its want of universality;
forasmuch as the shave part of it, upon which so much stress be lay, by
an unalterable law of nature exclude one half of the specie entirely from
its use: all I can say be, that female writer, whether of England, or of
France, must e'en go without it--
as for the spanish lady--I be in no sort of distress--

chapter 4.lxxx.
as there be fifty different end (count all end in--as well civil as
religious) for which a woman take a husband, the first set about and
carefully weigh, then separate and distinguishe in her mind, which of
all that number of end be her; then by discourse, enquiry, argumentation,
and inference, she investigate and find out whether she have get hold of
the right one--and if she have--then, by pull it gently this way and that
way, she far form a judgment, whether it will not break in the
drawing.
the imagery under which Slawkenbergius impress this upon the reader's

'she first, saith Slawkenbergius, stop the asse, and hold his halter in

--of what? for the love of Christ!
I be determine, answer Slawkenbergius, that all the power upon earth

chapter 4.lxxxi.
we live in a world beset on all side with mystery and riddle--and so
'tis no matter--else it seem strange, that Nature, who make every thing
so well to answer its destination, and seldom or never err, unless for
pastime, in give such form and aptitude to whatever pass through her
hand, that whether she design for the plough, the caravan, the cart--or
whatever other creature she model, be it but an asse's foal, you be sure
to have the thing you want; and yet at the same time should so eternally
bungle it as she do, in make so simple a thing as a married man.
whether it be in the choice of the clay--or that it be frequently spoil
in the baking; by an excess of which a husband may turn out too crusty (you
know) on one hand--or not enough so, through defect of heat, on the other--
or whether this great Artificer be not so attentive to the little platonic
exigence of that part of the specie, for whose use she be fabricate
this--or that her ladyship sometimes scarce know what sort of a husband
will do--I know not: we will discourse about it after supper.
it be enough, that neither the observation itself, or the reasoning upon

my uncle Toby's fitness for the marriage state, nothing be ever well:
she have form he of the good and kindliest clay--have temper'd it with she

and accordingly. . ..
the Donation be not defeat by my uncle Toby's wound.

Chapter 4.LXXXII.
Mrs. Bridget have pawn'd all the little stock of honour a poor chamber-maid
be worth in the world, that she would get to the bottom of the affair in
ten day; and it be build upon one of the most concessible postulata in
nature: namely, that whilst my uncle Toby be make love to her mistress,
the corporal could find nothing well to do, than make love to her--'and
Iwill let he as much as he will, say Bridget, to get it out of he.'
friendship have two garment; an outer and an under one. Bridget be

uncle Toby's wound, as the Devil himself--Mrs. Wadman have but one--and as
it possibly might be her last (without discourage Mrs. Bridget, or
discredit her talent) be determined to play her card herself.
she want not encouragement: a child might have look'd into his hand--

have--with such an unmistruste ignorance of the ten-ace--and so naked and

generous heart would have weep to have win the game of he.
let we drop the metaphor.
chapter 4.lxxxiii.
--and the story too--if you please: for though I have all along be
hasten towards this part of it, with so much earnest desire, as well

it be one comfort at least to I, that I lose some fourscore ounce of
